It was Kitty who crossed the hall.

She did not fuss. She did not stoop and coo. She simply sat down on the second-to-bottom step of the staircase, so that her face was level with the child's, and said, in a voice Elizabeth had not heard her use before — practical, unhurried — "Carriages can be so tiring. Should you like to sit beside me for a moment?"

The child considered. He walked, on uncertain legs, across the distance between them, and allowed himself to be received onto Kitty's lap. She did not embrace him. She held him in the unassuming manner of a chair. After a moment she took, from her own pocket, a small sugared biscuit that had no business being there — evidence, perhaps, of some private negotiation with Hill earlier in the morning — and broke it in half, and gave him one half without comment.

The crying stopped. The child chewed.

Mrs. Gardiner had paused in the drawing-room doorway. She took a careful inventory of her niece; and then she went in to her tea.

Kitty said, to the top of the child's head, "When you have finished, we might go out of doors. The lawn is very good for hoops, and Maria shall show you. Should that do?"

The child nodded with the solemnity of one concluding a treaty.

Kitty rose — carefully, so as not to disturb him — and carried him into the drawing room to be restored to his mother. Elizabeth, finding herself no longer required, slipped back upstairs to the unfinished trunk.

The tea things were handed out. Hill, released from Mrs. Bennet's surveillance by the consuming interest of the tea, slipped past with an armful of towels and the face of a woman who had been reprieved. 

Soon after, Kitty, with Maria at her shoulder, marshaled the smaller children toward the garden door; there was laughter from the lawn for the better part of an hour. 

Elizabeth did not go down. She closed her trunk at last, and turned the key. In the hall below, Mrs. Gardiner was heard to say — *yes, Mrs. Bennet, I quite agree, the children are far better aired. Kitty is very good with them. I had not known* — and Mrs. Bennet, on a note of discovery, rallied, and found other qualities in Kitty at some length; but Elizabeth had stopped listening.

---

Dinner passed. Mrs. Bennet, with five guests and a fresh audience, was in her element. The children ate with the seriousness of small soldiers. Mr. Gardiner and Mr. Bennet spoke, at their end of the table, of mills and of roads, and afterwards retired with the brandy to the study. Lydia did not come down. Her chair stayed empty, and no one remarked upon it, which was itself a kind of remarking.

The children went up to ready for bed. The ladies withdrew.

The drawing-room fire had been built up against the evening cool. Mrs. Bennet occupied the sofa and the greater part of the conversation, having taken up a grievance concerning a neighbor and a dish not yet returned. Mary, at the pianoforte but not playing, was prepared to supply, at the first opening, an observation upon the obligations of borrowing. Mrs. Gardiner had taken the chair by the fire. Jane sat close beside her — very nearly too close — the way one sits when one has been waiting for a particular listener for a particular purpose.

Elizabeth took up a book and did not read it. She turned a page at reasonable intervals. From the sofa came her mother, clear and uninterrupted, with only poor Kitty forced to respond. From the fire came only fragments, low and pitched for two.

"— all the way to Gracechurch Street —"

"— and to stay not above three minutes —"

"— but Miss Bingley meant well —"

It was not eavesdropping; eavesdropping required an object. Elizabeth only knew what the fragments meant because she knew who they were about; and she knew who they were about because Jane, who had not been able to speak of Mr. Bingley to her in eight days, was able, now, in a low voice by the fire, to speak to their aunt of his sister.

Mrs. Gardiner had been there. She had received the call — or the insufficiency of it — in her own parlor in Gracechurch Street, and had drawn her own conclusions long before tonight. Jane was not telling her anything she did not already know. She was going over it again, the way one returns to a bruise not to learn its shape but to have someone else confirm it is still there. Elizabeth had not known that Miss Bingley had called. She had not known the visit had lasted three minutes or less. Jane had carried all of it, and had not laid it down until she was given a pair of hands that already knew its weight.

Elizabeth sightlessly turned another page.

The sting was small but sharp. She was not owed Jane's confidences; she had been the confidante for a long time, and would be again. But tonight Jane had looked around her own house and had chosen to speak, not to her nearest sister, but to the aunt who had been at Longbourn eight hours. 

She, of all people, had no claim to make one. Elizabeth had a letter folded in her writing-case that Jane had never seen, and could never see, and about which Elizabeth had never spoken.

The fragments at the fire settled into silence; Jane's hand had found Mrs. Gardiner's on the arm of the chair. Mrs. Bennet, at length, discovered the hour, and discovered that she was tired, and delivered both discoveries to the room. They went up.

Jane, by the fire, had spoken of a man who had disappointed her by not coming back. He had not written; he had not called; there was no letter in her writing-case to explain him. Jane's grief was simple. Clean. It asked no questions of her own conduct. It did not require her to reconsider what she had said, at Hunsford, or to whom, or why.

Elizabeth envied her, briefly, the decisiveness of it.

---

Next morning, they all stood on the drive in the pale early light before Elizabeth had quite managed to believe that they were leaving.

The trunks were on; the horses had been brought round; her bonnet had been tied and retied by Jane, who had a better hand for ribbons. Her mother had kissed her three times, each kiss accompanied by a separate instruction concerning her health, her letters, and the folly of eating the food of inns. Lydia had not come down. No one had sent for her, and no one had suggested that anyone send for her.

Mr. Gardiner was settling his wife into the carriage with the small practical attentions of a man who had been handing his wife into carriages for fifteen years and intended to hand her into carriages for fifty more. Mrs. Gardiner arranged the rug over her knees and looked out at Elizabeth with a face that said *come.*

Jane was beside her at the carriage door.

"I shall write," Elizabeth said.

"I know."

"Often."

"I know."

Jane pressed her hand. Then she stepped back, and Elizabeth went up into the carriage, and the door was closed, and the horses shifted in their harness, and the carriage began to move.

The house slid past the window: door, step, the hedge by the gravel sweep. Elizabeth had promised herself she would not seek her father's study window, and she did not. Beyond the hedge lay the front lawn — a pale green square in the early-morning sun.

Kitty was there.

She had tucked up her skirt and was running, in a half-remembered way that suggested she had not run for some years, pacing a hoop across the grass with the stick held out before her like an ensign of office. Maria was shouting direction. William had seated himself on the grass and was offering his observations upon the business. The middle child was trying to overtake Kitty, and was failing. The smallest child stood at the edge of the lawn with his hands in the air and his face tipped back, laughing.

The carriage turned past the hedge, and they were gone — the lawn, the children, Kitty — and Elizabeth sat forward against the cushion and closed her eyes for the length of a breath.

"Well, my dear," said Mr. Gardiner from the opposite seat, mildly. "And now we are away."

"Yes," Elizabeth said.

"A long day's drive," Mr. Gardiner said, opposite. "The Swan tonight, and then north in earnest."

Elizabeth opened her eyes. The hedgerows of Hertfordshire were already beginning to pass. She had not known how much of herself she had mistaken for the house she was leaving; what else she had mistaken, she would have a month to find out.

The carriage bowled on toward the Meryton road.

# Chapter 6: The Park Pale

The road into Derbyshire took Elizabeth and the Gardiners under a sky that had been making up its mind since morning and had at last settled on something low and soft and grey, without temper in it, without any particular intention of rain. By early afternoon the light through the chaise windows was that pale, even spread which makes all greens the same green and all distances the same distance, and which, Elizabeth thought, was perhaps the kindest weather for traveling — the horses neither hot nor chilled, the postboys neither hurried nor sullen, the countryside laid out as if for inspection without vanity. It was the last week of July, though nothing in this afternoon proposed to say so.

They had been five days on the road — an idle afternoon at Kenilworth among its ruins, a morning at Matlock Bath among its waters, on the principle that one might as well see England as cross it. The post-chaise was the fourth they had been in since leaving Meryton, or the fifth, depending on whether one counted the small exchange at the last stage where one of the horses had gone lame within a half-mile of the post-house and they had been obliged to wait while another was brought. Mr. Gardiner had taken this delay with the philosophic calm of a man who had hired a great many horses in his life and knew that a certain proportion of them were bound to disappoint. He had used the hour to stretch his legs, to speak to the ostler about the road beyond Leek, and to come back with the intelligence that the surface was very tolerable now, since the rain of the week before had laid the dust without softening the gravel, which was the most one could reasonably ask of an English highway.

Mrs. Gardiner had listened to all of this with the patient affection of a wife who had heard her husband discuss road conditions for fifteen years of marriage and still enjoyed the sound of him saying *tolerable.* When they were underway again she had pointed out, from her window, the first true hill of the stage — not a Derbyshire hill yet, she said, but one of its cousins — and had begun to speak of the country of her girlhood.

Elizabeth listened, and watched, and did not think of him.

There was the rhythm of the springs, and the way the body learned by the third day to take the jolt of a poor rut in the knees and the shoulders and not at all in the head. There were the hills ahead, taller by the mile, shouldering against one another in a way she had not seen in Hertfordshire, nor indeed anywhere south. There was the ham and the very indifferent cheese at yet another White Lion, and the change of horses at the next stage, and whether the inn at Lambton would have a fire lit in their parlor.

There had been the lame horse at the last stage — its dropped head, the ostler's hand on its neck, a picture that had held her the better part of an hour. That had been real. That had been the proof.

*Proof,* she told herself, *that I am not thinking of him.*

"That stone in the hills, my dear," said Mr. Gardiner. "That's limestone, or has the look of it. I knew a man in Cheapside who bought Derbyshire stone by the boatload — he would have it as good as Portland for a wall that meant to stand fifty years without staining."

"My father would have gone further," said Mrs. Gardiner. "He held it the handsomest stone in England. But he was a Lambton man, and a Lambton man will not admit there is a superior stone anywhere on principle."

Mr. Gardiner laughed. Elizabeth smiled at the window.

They climbed a long, slow rise, and the hills were closer now, and the light on them was still that flat pale afternoon light that made every sheep on every hillside look as if it had been painted and unreal. A stone wall began, some way before the crest, on the right-hand side of the road. It was a wall of the ordinary sort, squared blocks laid without mortar, nothing handsome, nothing striking, breast-high or a little above — the kind of wall one passed a hundred times in a day on a country road and never looked at twice.

"Now here," said Mrs. Gardiner pleasantly, leaning a little toward the window, "is where the Darcys' pale begins. We are on Pemberley ground for the next — oh, I should think four miles, or the nearest thing to it."

Mr. Gardiner, who had been halfway through a remark about the ostler at Leek, stopped and looked. "Four miles? Along the road?"

"Along the road. And a great deal more besides, inland. It is a very considerable park."

"A pale?" Elizabeth said.

"Only the wall that bounds the park. One calls it a pale — I cannot tell you why, except that my father did, and his father before him. I believe in very old times they were made of oak stakes rather than stone, and the word came from that. A park pale is what one calls the enclosure around the whole of a gentleman's ground, where he keeps his deer and his coverts and what you would call his *property,* though I never heard a Derbyshire man use so blunt a word for it."

"A wall round it all,” Mr. Gardiner said.

His wife nodded. "A wall round it all."

Mr. Gardiner made a small impressed noise, being a man of London who measured his own premises in feet and was prepared to be generous about country estates measured in miles. He leaned slightly to see past his wife to the window, and the wall flowed past, unbroken, squared block upon squared block, no gate, no lodge, no indication that anything lay beyond it but the wood and the same hillside it had been cut from.

Elizabeth did not lean. She did not need to. The window on her own side showed her nothing but the long pale rolling by, an arm's length from the wheels, at the steady easy pace of the post-horses — and so the pale rolled and rolled, and did not end.

The name had passed her without catching — Mrs. Gardiner had said it like *the Vicar's garden* or *the river* — and only now, a full minute late, did it come back on her. Then she saw the wall.

It was, as her aunt had said, only a wall. It had none of the grandeur that the imagination would have supplied. There were no obelisks, no iron crests, no piers of dressed stone with carved escutcheons. It was a Derbyshire field wall, a little higher and a little straighter than a field wall was obliged to be, laid in the patient competent hand of men who had been laying walls in these hills since there had been hills to lay them in. It did not announce itself. It did not need to. What announced it was that it did not stop.

A mile, she supposed, was a great many strides of a horse. A mile was the length of the Longbourn lane taken twice, and the lane had always seemed to her a respectable distance, such that one began a letter at one end of it and had finished the first page before one reached the other. They would pass this wall for four miles. Four such lanes, laid end to end, and all of them only the edge of a thing. Beyond the wall she could see, through the gaps between the trees, what must be the park — open grass, and the shoulder of a further rise — but no roof, no chimney, no glint of water from any lake. The house, wherever it sat, sat too deep in its own ground to be glimpsed from the road. Only the pale told her where she was.

She had not, in all the weeks since Hunsford, actually pictured it. She had known, as one knows a fact without looking at it, that the estate existed, and that it was somewhere, and that it was large. The largeness had been a word. Now the largeness was a wall her knee was very nearly brushing against through the glass.

She turned her face to the window and kept it there, and put her gloved hand up under her chin as though she were considering the hills, which she was not. The wall went on. A little ash sapling had taken root between two of its stones, high up, and someone had not yet got round to pulling it out; she watched the sapling approach and pass. A pheasant scuttered along the wall's foot on the outside and was gone. The wall went on.

She found that she had been, all afternoon, and perhaps all morning, and perhaps all the week, thinking of him. The horses and the ham and the shape of the hills had been a screen. The wall had not the courtesy to be a screen. The wall was very plain, and it was very long, and it belonged to him.

"You are very quiet, Lizzy," said her aunt, after a time, not looking round, still cheerful at her own window. 

"I am admiring the country, Aunt," said Elizabeth — which came out even, which surprised her.

"It is worth the admiring. We shall have better of it tomorrow, if the sky clears."

The pale ended. There was no gate where it ended, and no lodge; it simply turned away from the road and went off up the hillside to the northeast, a pale seam in the green, and then dipped out of sight into a fold of the land. The chaise rolled on between ordinary hedges, the hills resuming their ordinary business of being hills, and the country looked, very suddenly, like any other country one had passed through in an afternoon.

Elizabeth did not know whether she was relieved or bereaved. She had never before felt a loss at the ending of a simple wall.

---

Lambton, when they came into it, was a small handsome gray town of the sort that had been gray and handsome since before any of them were born. Sheltered in its valley, at ease with itself, unpersuaded by the century. Her aunt grew visibly younger by the yard. She pointed out the church, and the lane to the rectory, and the corner where Mr. Someone had kept a shop when she was a girl and was perhaps keeping it still if he had not had the discourtesy to die. When the chaise turned at last into the yard of the inn — the Rose, it was called, a broad low building of the same gray stone as the walls, with stables down one side and an ostler already coming to the horses' heads — she named the landlord — Staveley, as she recalled him — and was proved right within a minute by Mr. Staveley himself, who appeared at the door wiping his hands on a cloth and professed himself honored to welcome Miss Shelby home, though she had not been Miss Shelby these twenty years.

This produced, in the yard, among the trunks being handed down and the postboys waiting for their tipping, a small warmth that did the work of several speeches. The landlord's wife came out and curtseyed to Mrs. Gardiner as to a neighbor rather than a stranger, mentioned a cousin in Liverpool whose health had lately improved, and said that the parlor abovestairs had a fire lit since noon against the damp, and that a letter had come for Miss Bennet from Hertfordshire and was laid out upon the mantel.

Elizabeth sat in the well-warmed parlor on a bench beside  the window below which lay the main street of Lambton,  orderly and quiet and wearing its overcast like an old shawl. Elizabeth skimmed Jane’s lines — Longbourn, the twenty-third — while her aunt and uncle consulted with the landlady about supper. The letter contained nothing remarkable — the weather at Longbourn had been as indifferent as the weather here; their mother was well and had turned sharp against the curate; Mary had undertaken a new course of improving passages. *Kitty* *had the Gardiner children in fits of laughter yesterday by a very creditable impression of Lady Lucas.”* Elizabeth read this last sentence twice, and smiled at it, and folded the letter and put it in her pocket, and was grateful beyond any proportion the letter itself could possibly account for. 

Supper came up at seven. Good plain food: a fowl, a pudding, a little dish of something her aunt identified as a Derbyshire cheese and ate with the attention of a connoisseur. They took the meal in the parlor between the fire and the window, with the light gone soft outside into the particular blue-grey of a northern evening under cloud. Mr. Gardiner was mellow. Mrs. Gardiner was happy. Elizabeth was — something. She did not know what she was. 

"Now, Edward," said her aunt, at the point in the meal where she had finished her fowl and begun to think about tomorrow, "what do you say to our taking Lizzy to see Pemberley in the morning?"

Mr. Gardiner set down his glass. "Can we?"

"They show it very handsomely, I am told. I have not seen it within since I was a girl, and then only the hall and the great staircase, because my father knew the then housekeeper and she was good enough to indulge a curious child. They let visitors in, you understand, when the family is not in residence — which, Mr. Staveley tells me, is the case at present. The family is in town."

"In town." Mr. Gardiner was pleased. "Then we may satisfy our curiosity without disturbing anybody."

"Exactly." Mrs. Gardiner turned her bright sensible face to Elizabeth across the cloth. "Lizzy? It is a beautiful house, and a beautiful park, and you have come all this way. Shall we?"

Elizabeth took a breath, steadier than she had any right to expect, and reached for the line she had been keeping in reserve without knowing she had been keeping it.

"Are there no other great houses in the neighborhood?"

It came out, and it sounded, to her own ear, very much as she had meant it to sound. Careless. Faintly droll. The sort of question one might put to one's aunt when one's aunt had become a little too enthusiastic about country seats.

Her aunt only laughed at her, the frank affectionate laugh of a woman who had not heard any of the things that would have made the question mean anything, and who had, instead, heard a tired niece at the end of five days' traveling trying to be witty.

"Oh, my dear, we shall not concern ourselves with any others. There is none within twenty miles half so worth a morning as Pemberley, and I mean to have my morning."

She said it in the tone of a woman claiming a piece of her childhood back. There was no challenge in it. There was scarcely any persuasion in it. Mrs. Gardiner had not seen the question for what it was — and so the question, having nothing to resist, lost its shape.

Elizabeth had not prepared a second line. She had been too confident of the first.

*The family is not at home.*

"Then I suppose," she said, after a pause just long enough to be worth the noticing and not long enough to be remarked upon, "we had better go."

"That's my Lizzy." Her aunt reached across and pressed her hand, warm and open, and then turned to her husband to talk of hours, and of whether they should take the chaise or walk from the lodge, and Mr. Gardiner was all for walking if the sky would only consent, and the conversation went on above Elizabeth's head as comfortably as any conversation she had sat through in her life.

She sipped her Madeira. She looked, by accident, out at the window, where the street was nearly dark now and the overcast, holding the last of the light, lay low and even over the roofs of Lambton. Over her aunt's childhood church, and the landlord's cousin's shop, and the road that ran, she now knew, past four unbroken miles of quiet, competent wall.

*He will not be there.*

The fire made its small sound. Her aunt laughed at something her uncle had said. The letter from Jane was still in her pocket, and her hand had gone, without her permission, to rest upon it. She must recount Jane’s story of Kitty and the children.

*He will not be there.*

Half a promise. Half a prayer.

# Chapter 7: An Evening at Home

*Two evenings earlier, Grosvenor Street, London*

Darcy had read only to the fifth paragraph of his steward's letter before setting it down on his desk. He had read the first four twice and could not, on request, have given the substance of any of them. He picked it up again. The hedges along the south drive wanted cutting. The east pasture had been let for a month to a neighbor whose Herefords were, the steward reported, in better flesh than had been expected after so dry a spring. Darcy read these sentences in the order they had been written. He read them as a man reads a newspaper he has bought only to hold.

Saturday, they were all to go down to Pemberley. So it had been settled a month ago; so it had been settled again last Tuesday, when Bingley had called, and the road, and the rooms at the Bedford, and the hour of the remount, had all been arranged between them with the cheerful precision of a man who enjoys arranging. The party would be six — himself, Georgiana, Bingley, the Hursts, and Miss Caroline Bingley. Three days on the road; two nights at the posting-inns; one arrival at Pemberley in good order, and the summer to begin.

It was now Wednesday evening. He would see it through.

From the pianoforte in the next room came the Mozart poor Georgiana had been working on for a fortnight. She was playing it slowly. She was making the same mistake at the same bar.

He did not think of Kent. 

They had come straight to town, all late spring bustle and noise. As the weeks passed, the neighborhood had emptied around them by degrees, the usual pilgrimage from Grosvenor Street to the wilds in summer. First the Parliamentary men, as the session closed; then the hunting men, for reasons of their own. Then the rest of the Season — trunks, plants, abigails, spinster aunts — going down by degrees to Hertfordshire and Sussex and Hampshire and the Peak. 

Two doors east, the Lindsays' house had been shuttered a week. Across the way, the Penningtons had closed theirs today. His own household had remained. There had been no reason to leave. The Bingleys could not be ready before Saturday, and Georgiana, he had told himself, was better for the extra week at the London pianoforte with the London music master. She had not yet, she said, made friends with the new instrument at Pemberley.

The room was stifling. He had opened the window onto the street an hour since, and there was no breeze to reward him for having done so. Somewhere below, a servant crossed a tiled floor in soft shoes. The clock on the mantel behind him struck the quarter and seemed to consider the matter closed. He had kept only three candles burning. The sun would be down in an hour. He would light the rest then, or he would not.

On the desk at his elbow, under the steward's letter, lay the ordinary furniture of a gentleman's office: a pen-knife his father had used, a blotter creased at the corner, a ledger of estate accounts he had meant to finish by yesterday and had not, a miniature of his mother in an oval frame that had stood there eleven years. He did not look at the miniature. He did not need to. He had noticed some months ago, and not again until this moment, that his sister's mouth was the same mouth; that she had it from the woman in the frame. He set his eyes back on the steward's letter.

Miss Bingley had left a card. It lay on the tray in the hall when he had come in at five. He supposed it was there still. She had been accompanied, the porter had said, by Mrs. Hurst, and they had remained three-quarters of an hour with Miss Darcy in the small parlor before going away again. Darcy had been at his club. He was sorry that he had missed them. No; he was not sorry. He was glad to have missed them, and glad to have taken no greater trouble to have done so.

A note had come up with the card. It lay at the far corner of the desk, where he had put it on coming in. The hand was Miss Bingley's. He had not read it. He did not mean to read it tonight. He was sure he knew, within a thin margin, what was in it. Some courtesy about his sister; some archness about the upcoming journey; some wish, phrased to resemble her brother's, that the weather would hold. He could answer it without reading it. He would answer it, properly, in the morning.

Or not.

He moved it a half-inch to the left with the edge of his hand.

Bingley had not called today. Nor yesterday. Now Darcy considered it, Bingley had been, for some weeks — for some months — a man who did not call where formerly he had called. Who dined at home, with his sisters, when formerly he would have dined out. A man who had lost, without remarking on the loss, some portion of the openness of manner that had been, in other days, among his most agreeable qualities. 

Darcy had marked the decline in April, when he had come up from Kent. He had marked it again in May. He had, by a habit learned in boyhood, declined to think about it further. Or rather, he had thought about it just so far as comfort permitted, and then laid it aside, as one lays aside a letter one does not mean to answer tonight.

In this case, though, it had not stayed put.

*Do you think that any consideration would tempt me to accept the man who has been the means of ruining, perhaps for ever, the happiness of a most beloved sister?*

April, in a parlor at Hunsford, in a voice that had not trembled.

At the time he had been able to answer it. Of course he had. A man in the habit of arranging other people's happiness is seldom without reasons for the arrangement. The lady had not seemed attached. Bingley had been exposed to an imprudent connection. The family had been — he stopped there, because the next word in his mind had too much of his aunt in it, and because another sentence had followed the first.

*Had you behaved in a more gentlemanlike manner.*

He glanced toward the door. Georgiana was still playing. No one had heard his thoughts, or seen his wince. He could no longer look at Bingley's quietness without seeing his own hand in it. A man who has been shown his own hand in a thing prefers, afterwards, not to look at the thing.

If he can.

He glanced at the steward's letter.

The hedges wanted cutting. Yes. He would speak to the steward on Monday about the hedges. No need to write.

Oh, the south drive at Pemberley. The cool green of it in July. The scent of cut grass at evening, which was definitely not a London scent. The river below the house that would be, at this hour, still bright. The light in his sitting-room there. In summer he kept the north window open and, often, a book on the bench beneath. He had not been in that room for — he could not at once reckon. Since February. Five months.

It was not a longing. It was a preference. He had preferences about where he was on a July evening, as any man did.

He drew his attention back to the desk.

The Mozart stumbled at the same bar again. He did not, this time, let himself attend to it. He had attended to it, fitfully, for an hour. He had not been a help.

Then the lid of the pianoforte closed — not firmly, only firmly enough — and then steps in the hall. Georgiana appeared in the doorway.

Taller than she had been last summer. Darcy shut the door on those thoughts with a slam, but not before a tendril of guilt snuck in.

Georgiana did not, this evening, stand in the doorway, as she had sometimes stood, waiting to be invited in. She crossed the room to him, drew out the chair on the other side of his desk, and seated herself in it. She did this with the composure he had watched her acquire across the past long years. There was, tonight, a small tightness at the corner of her mouth — the set of some feeling not quite patience, caught and held. He had not seen her wear it before. Or had not, perhaps, noticed he was seeing it.

"Brother," she said.

"Georgiana."

"I cannot make it come right," she said. 

"The bar?"

"The bar."

"You will. You make it come right tomorrow. You have made it come right before."

"I did it once," she said, the tightness at her mouth now plainer, "and I have been unable to make it come right since. I begin to suspect that it was chance, and that I have learned nothing about the piece at all."

She was tall for her age — tall enough now that he could no longer, with any honesty, think of her as the child who had once fit beneath his arm on the library sofa at Pemberley. Her figure had taken on, almost without his permission, the full and graceful lines of womanhood; her face, less striking than their mother's had been and less marked than his own, was soft where his was severe, with a sweetness about the mouth that made strangers praise her amiability before they had earned the right to judge it. She was handsome enough to be admired, accomplished enough to be paraded, and shy enough to make both facts a burden to her.

Darcy saw all this, and because he loved her, saw also what others missed: the hesitation before she spoke; the careful lowering of her eyes when attention pressed too hard; the way her hands folded themselves into obedience even when her thoughts had begun, lately, to move past it. She had been praised all her life for gentleness. He had encouraged the praise, because gentleness had seemed safe. Tonight, for the first time, he wondered whether he had mistaken safety for happiness.

"That," he said, after a moment, "is the nature of the instrument."

She did not smile. "It is the nature of practice, I think, more than of the instrument. Mrs. Annesley says one cannot practice what one is not attending to. I have not been attending."

He did not, at once, reply.

She folded her hands in her lap. He had seen her fold her hands in her lap, just so, very often in the last ten months. He had come to regard the gesture as evidence of a patience she was teaching herself. It was not impatience. He had not, until this evening, considered that patience taught to oneself was a kind of weapon, held in reserve.

He thought, for the space of one breath, of what another person in the world now knew — had been told, in writing, and had returned to him no word of since — about the reason this sister had been obliged to teach herself any patience at all. A syllable began to form in the back of his mind; the shape of a letter; the beginning of a name. He did not finish it. He did not permit himself the luxury of even the shape.

He moved his hand, instead, a quarter-inch along the edge of the steward's letter.

"Miss Bingley called today," Georgiana said.

"So I see. I was sorry to have missed her."

She looked at him from under her lashes — quickly, to see whether he had meant it — and then she did something she would not have done three months ago. She said, "I do not think you were, brother."

One of his eyebrows went up of its own accord.

"She brought me a book," Georgiana went on, quite evenly. "She said she thought it would do very well for a young lady of my age. It is a novel I had already read, in May, which Mrs. Annesley thought indifferent and which I thought worse. I did not tell Miss Bingley so."

Something moved at the corner of his mouth — the beginning of a smile, too small to call back. 

"I told her I should be glad to take it down to Pemberley with me."

"Where," Darcy said, "nobody will admire it, and you may read it in peace."

"I do not mean to read it," said Georgiana, "at all."

It was the nearest thing to wit he had ever heard from his sister. He had not thought her capable of that particular edge.

She was growing up.

He did not, at once, know whether to be pleased. He found, on consideration, that he was, on the whole, pleased; and that he was also, in some part that was not wholly creditable to himself, alarmed. A girl who could say that, evenly, at little more than sixteen, was a girl whose attention had sharpened in a direction he had not been at liberty, lately, to supervise.

"Bingley did not come with them?" he said.

"Miss Bingley said he was at the club; but I think she says that when she does not wish to say where her brother is."

Sharp eyes. She had been in company with Miss Bingley, he reminded himself, four and five times a week for three months. It was not unreasonable that she should have formed an opinion on Miss Bingley's habits of speech. It was only that he had, by habit, expected her to form her opinions and keep them; and she was, tonight, putting them on the table between them, and waiting to see what he would do with them.

"He has been quiet," Darcy said.

"Yes."

"For some weeks."

"Yes." She did not, this time, soften the observation with any hope as to Bingley's health. She let the word stand.

The clock marked another half-hour. A door clicked shut somewhere below. A carriage went past in the street, the first in an hour, and the sound of its wheels died away toward the square.

"You have been quiet also, brother," said Georgiana. "Since you came back from Kent."

She was watching him when she said it. She had picked this moment; she had kept it in reserve, perhaps for days, perhaps for longer; and she had delivered it, he understood with an unpleasant clarity, at the one moment when he was least armored against it. The moment his guard had come down, when his attention had strayed from the steward's letter and not yet returned to anything else.

He did not answer at once. He could not. He found, with some dismay, that he had been expecting — for how long? for weeks? for the whole of this quiet July? — some form of this question from someone. He had not expected it from her. He had expected, he now thought, to be asked by a man in a club, to whom he could have given a man's answer; or by his cousin, to whom he could have given no answer at all, and been understood. He had not expected to be asked, across his own desk, by his little bit of a sister.

"Our aunt," he said, at last. "You know how she is. There was some business at Rosings that did not resolve itself. It tires one, more than such things ought. I have been a little —"

He hesitated.

"— melancholy, perhaps. It will pass."

He disliked the word the moment he had used it. *Melancholy* was a word for an idle young man in a novel; for a poet who had mislaid a glove and wished to make something of it. He was neither. What he had been, these months, was not melancholy. He did not know what it was. He had several private names for it, each of which he mistrusted.

"Yes," said Georgiana. She did not, this time, say it in the manner of a young lady who was accepting a displacement. She said it in the manner of a young lady who was registering a reply she did not intend to contradict.

"I am well, Georgiana."

She did not press. She had never, in her life, pressed. But he had the novel sensation, tonight, that she had decided not to press, rather than been unable to.

Past time to return to Pemberley. 

Not a retreat, nor a reprieve. An obligation. A duty.

The carriage was to be made ready on Saturday for all of them and their luggage, and another for the servants and Mr. Hurst's dogs. They would be three days on the road. Bingley was not a man who enjoyed travel, but he was a man who could be cheerful through it. Mrs. Hurst would read, or pretend to read. Mr. Hurst would sleep. Miss Bingley would sit in the forward seat, next to Georgiana, and she would speak.

She would speak for three days.

He considered the sound of her speaking for three days, and of himself sitting through it. He considered the sound of it set against the cool green of the south drive at Pemberley, the pine, the grass, the quiet sun.

"I have been considering," he said, "going on ahead."

Georgiana sat up straighter, if that was possible. "Brother?"

"Tomorrow. I have been some time from Pemberley; there are matters I should be glad to put in hand before — before the party arrives. You will come on Saturday with Bingley and his sisters, as we had arranged. Mrs. Annesley will travel with you."

She did not answer at once. For one instant, he saw her turn over in her mind whether she was being sent away, or being left.

"It is," he added, softly, "nothing to do with you, Georgiana."

A pause.

"I did not think it was," she said.

It struck him, after a half-second, that this was not quite reassurance. It was information.

"I shall be only a few days before you."

"Yes." And then — and here she looked at him, and there was the small true smile he had been waiting for all evening without knowing he had been waiting — "The Derbyshire air has always done you good."

Almost always. "Yes," he said. "It has."

She rose. She crossed to him — it was only a step — and for a moment laid her hand on the back of his, where it rested on the desk. She held it a shade longer than she usually did. Then she straightened, and bade him good night.

The door shut softly behind her. He heard her step on the stair. Mrs. Annesley's voice greeting her on the upstairs landing, and his sister's in reply. 

He sat a moment holding his hand still. Feeling the traces of his sister's touch.

Then he took paper and pen.

*Jenkins — the chaise at six.*

He folded this, directed it, and laid it on the tray at his elbow for the footman to carry down.

He took another sheet.

The candle was low; he did not trim it. He wrote quickly, before the pen could begin to make its own arguments.

*Bingley —*

*I find that I must go on ahead of our party. I shall set out tomorrow morning, and shall look for you, my sister, and the Hursts at Pemberley on Monday. Pray excuse the want of ceremony in this note: I have not time to call on you tonight. All will be in readiness by the time you arrive.*

*I remain, &c.,*

*F. Darcy.*

He had written *I remain* because it was what one wrote. He had written nothing else that was untrue, and he had written nothing, also, that was not true. He did not write *why.* He did not give a reason — not the estate, not the steward, not the hedges. Bingley would not ask for one, tonight or ever. It was a great convenience that Bingley took one's word for things.

He folded the note, sealed it, and laid it with the other. Then he stood, stretched, and went to the window.

Grosvenor Street was lit in squares. The lamps had come up while he had been writing. The pavement was lit in stretches, with the darker spaces between. There were few carriages. A man walked a dog, a well-kept grey terrier of the cheerful, incurable sort. A boy with a broom swept something off the step of a house shuttered for the summer. That was all. Two doors east, the Lindsays' windows were dark. Across the way, the Penningtons'. Further down, a house he did not, at this distance, know, showed a single light in an upper room, where perhaps a servant was closing up. The town had thinned, this past week, to the bone.

He rested his hand on the window frame, the wood still warm from the day.

A word began to form itself in his head. A name, or the beginning of one. 

He did not let it finish. 

He set it down, as he had set down the steward's letter, as he had set down Caroline's unopened note, as he had set down, in April, in a parlor at Hunsford, everything he had thought he understood of himself.

*Melancholy,* he thought, and despised the word.

# Chapter 8: The South Drive

The morning at the Rose Inn was already too bright by nine. Sun on the yard, sun on the gray stone of the church across the way, sun on the polished brass of the harness as the chaise was brought round to the door. The cloud of yesterday had gone off in the night and taken the easy gray with it. There was no breeze, even in the yard. The sky had settled into a hard pale blue and the leaves did not move.

Mr. Gardiner came down the inn stairs cheerful — ruddy, square-built, the kind of man whose coat sat well across the shoulders because he walked his own warehouse twice a day. Mrs. Gardiner came down hatted and gloved and very nearly girlish, small and neat and brown-haired under her bonnet, her bright sensible face lit up an extra degree by the morning and the place, having slept the sleep of a woman returned to ground she knew by heart. Elizabeth awaited them in the front passage, just out of the sun, watching the chaise turn under the arch. Asked at breakfast, she had told her aunt she had slept perfectly well. She had not slept well at all, which she had told no one.

Best bonnet tied, neatest gloves on. Holding one thought in the palm of her glove like a small coin: *He will not be there*.

The chaise rolled out under the inn arch and turned into the Lambton street, springs creaking, the smell of warmed leather already gathering inside it. The landlord had said five miles by the post-road, less if they turned in at the lodge. They would do it in an hour, the landlord had said, if the horses were not idle. As they passed slowly through town, keeping the dust they kicked up to a minimum, Mrs. Gardiner gave another potted tour. There, the corner where the old market cross had stood until the year before her wedding. Here, the smithy where she had once held a mare's head while the smith argued with her father about the price of shoeing. Yonder the parsonage paddock she had once been chased out of for a debt of crab-apples. Mr. Gardiner laughed. Elizabeth smiled where she was meant to smile.

The town sank behind them. The road bent north and east, climbing past hedgerows where the bees worked the meadowsweet without hurry. A pheasant stood in the middle of the road and held its ground until the very last moment, then took itself off into the bracken in a fluster of indignation.

Elizabeth turned her face to the open window.

The road, she now understood without being told, was the same road. It ran north and east the same way the wall had run north and east, yesterday, on the other side. The wall was somewhere off to her right, behind the hedges and the shoulder of the rise; it had been there all morning, and would be all morning, and she was now driving alongside the inside of what she had driven alongside the outside of. She had not, when she had agreed to this expedition at her aunt's table last night, thought through this fact in this particular form. She had thought through nothing in any particular form. *He will not be there.* That had been form enough. Her gloved hand was at her throat without permission; she put it down.

The turnoff to the lodge came up on the left without warning — a low gray building with a single chimney, and an old woman in the door who curtsied with calm efficiency. They must have passed it yesterday without noticing it, between the unbroken miles of the wall and her own preoccupation with not looking at it. The gates were already open. The chaise turned in.

The drive ran north through woods. Tall woods, kept clear underneath, the trunks of beeches at neat intervals and the bracken at their feet clearly thinned by deer and not by gardeners. The light through the leaves came down in the sort of dappled coins that one saw in pictures and rarely in life. 

Elizabeth had not, in any of her private picturings of Pemberley, allowed it deer or beeches. She had allowed it severe firs, planted in lines, and gravel raked to a finished level. She corrected herself, privately, in the dappled light, and was annoyed to have to. Each correction was a small admission — and she had been making a great many small admissions, lately, about its owner.

The wood went on. They climbed; they descended; they climbed again. At one point the drive bore right around a great oak that had been there, plainly, since the Restoration; at another it crossed a small bridge over a stream so clear one could count the stones in its bed at the speed of a trotting horse. Elizabeth caught herself counting, and stopped.

Then the woods opened.

Elizabeth gasped.

The prospect was one she had only read about in books. It was a trick of the Capability Brown manner of landscape, where the trees stood back at a precise  angle and gave the visitor the house all at once, framed, a living portrait. She had read about the device in books, and disliked it on the page. In a wood, it did not feel like a device. It felt like a welcome.

Pemberley stood well on rising ground a quarter-mile below them, above the river and backed by a ridge of high woody hills. A handsome stone house, large but not enormous, longer than it was tall, with the look of a building that had been added to over a hundred and fifty years by men who had each had the courtesy to consult the men before them. A south front of pale gold stone in the morning sun. Tall windows. A flat broad terrace before it, and beyond the terrace a slope of grass down to the water, and beyond the water the wooded rise on the far side of the valley, hot and dark and full of summer.

Mr. Gardiner said, "Well." 

Mrs. Gardiner turned to Elizabeth, smiling. “Oh, Lizzy. Just so. Just exactly so.” 

Elizabeth was silent. She had been carrying, since April — perhaps since longer than April, perhaps since the very first uttering of the name *Pemberley* by Mr. Bingley's sisters in a Hertfordshire drawing-room — a picture of this place that was a fortress. Stone piled in such a way as to crow about itself. Avenues laid out to lecture one on perspective. A house whose owner could stand on its terrace and look down at the country and find the country wanting.

The house on its rise was nothing like that. It was settled exactly where the land suggested it. Made of the stone of the hill, and built to look at the river and not at the visitor. There was nothing in it of crowing. There was, on consideration, very little in it of pride, in the sense in which she had used that word about its owner for nine months together.

She looked at it a long minute.

*He had never spoken of this place to her* — not once, not in any of those drawing-rooms last winter.

The chaise began the descent.

When it drew up before the door, on the gravel sweep before the south front,  a man in plain dark livery handed them down with the practiced civility of a servant who had handed down a great many strangers in his time without making any of them feel less like strangers. The door opened of itself, or seemed to, and a woman came out of it.

The housekeeper, Mrs. Reynolds, was a respectable-looking elderly person — much less fine, and more civil, than Elizabeth had had any notion of finding her. The first phrase she put out had Lambton in it without putting the word *Lambton* directly: she had heard, she said, that there was a Lambton lady in the party, and she was very glad they had thought to come up to the house, and the family being away she would be happy to show them whatever they cared to see.

Mrs. Gardiner, smiling, said the name she had had as a girl. Mrs. Reynolds smiled in return — a smile of placement, the kind a woman gives when she has just understood whose family she is speaking to — and took them in.

The hall was quiet and cool. After the heat of the gravel sweep, it was nearly a separate climate. Stone underfoot, plaster overhead, a staircase that turned upon itself in an easy decided manner. The stair had been carrying its family up and down for a hundred and forty years, according to Mrs. Reynolds, winking.

There were no flowers. Elizabeth had pictured flowers — banks of them, suffocating — and there were none. The family was not at home; one did not stage flowers for one's own absence. A great clock in the corner kept the time of the house with the sober regularity of a man who has business to attend to and does not mean to be late for it.

Mrs. Reynolds led them through.

The principal rooms were arrayed in good order: a saloon with windows giving onto the terrace and the river, a dining-room a little darker than the saloon with a long table of dark mahogany unrelieved by any cloth, a library at the corner of the house with two walls of books and a wide low window-seat under the third window — its cushion plain dark wool, the curtains above it heavy linen the color of weak tea. 

Elizabeth, who had been learning all her life that the manner in which a room was lived in could be read from a glance, looked into each, and read each, and found in none of them what she had half-wanted to find — some piece of the man she had known last October, some confirming pomp that would settle her judgment back into its old shape.

The books in the library had been read. She could tell at twenty paces. They stood at the angles of books that had been pulled out and put back, and at the small unevennesses of bindings that had taken their owner's hand many times. The window-seat had a cushion that had been sat on. There was a small table by the window-seat with a single book on it, face down to keep its place, as if the reader had been called away an hour ago and meant to return. (She glanced at the spine, briefly: something on the rotation of crops.) The room clearly was not got up for visitors. 

The saloon was the same. The chairs had been arranged for use, not for show. A workbasket sat beside one of them, with a piece of half-finished embroidery laid across it, the needle still in the cloth — Miss Georgiana Darcy's, the housekeeper said, when Elizabeth's eye lit on it. Miss Darcy was very fond of her work; would the lady care to see the morning room next, and the gallery beyond?

The music room had a view of the spread of the lawn — smooth and unhurried, the way a thing looks when it has been cared for for generations. The music room had a pianoforte, a fine one, with a piece of music open on its stand. Mozart, it looked like. The music room held a small writing-desk near the window, its inkwell capped against dust, and a sheet of paper laid blank on the blotter as though waiting for the lady of the house to come back to her morning's correspondence. There was no lady of the house at the moment, but the house went on as if there were.

Mrs. Reynolds, walking ahead, kept up a steady housekeeperly commentary that was neither boastful nor humble. *This room had been re-papered four years since. The chimneypiece in the dining-room was the third Mr. Darcy's putting in. The library had had its present arrangement from the late master, but the present master had added, oh, a great many books, particularly upon the management of estates, and upon the new methods in agriculture, of which he was a great reader.* "He has added a great many books, ma'am, in the last year or two — he is one of those gentlemen who reads about his fields as well as walks them. "

Mrs. Reynolds liked the master of the house. She had liked him as a boy and liked him still as a man, with no interval of having to be persuaded back to it. Her manner was not one that liked a young master from a distance, on the understanding that one ought to. It was the manner in which one liked the weather of one's own house: as a fact, settled, and on the whole a fortunate one.

Mr. Gardiner cleared his throat. "Forgive a London man's curiosity, Mrs. Reynolds — does the master manage the home grounds himself, or has he a steward to it?"

"Mr. Greaves manages the home farm, sir, but the master is in the office two mornings a week and walks the land more often than that. He was raised to know his own soil, if you will pardon the expression."

"I will pardon it. It is the right expression."

Mrs. Gardiner, behind, said quietly to Elizabeth, "She was here in my mother's time, you know. She was a young woman then." Elizabeth nodded. She did not trust her voice for an answer.

They went up the staircase. Mrs. Reynolds, on the landing, paused before a long room that opened off it.

"The picture-gallery," she said. "If the lady cares to step in."

The lady cared to.

The gallery ran the length of the south front. The morning sun came in along its full length in long warm bars. Portraits down both walls. Darcys in a wig and a starched ruff, Darcys in periwigs, Darcys in regimentals, Darcys in the plain dark coats of country gentlemen who declined to be painted in any costume but their own. 

Elizabeth walked the length of it slowly, behind Mrs. Reynolds, with her uncle and aunt at her shoulder, and looked into face after face that bore, in this jaw or that brow, the family thing — the *Darcy* thing — she had thought, until April, was vanity, and was beginning to think might only be the small accident of bone.

Near the end of the gallery, on the right hand: a tall portrait of a gentleman in a dark coat, perhaps thirty years old, perhaps a little less. The painter had caught him standing easily by a desk, one hand on a book, the other at his side, and had caught also the faint expression at the corner of his mouth that one saw when one had just amused someone without meaning to, and which he had not yet decided whether to permit. 

Elizabeth had not seen that expression on his face since Hertfordshire. Last autumn. She tried to put a particular drawing-room to it, and could not — only that he had looked at her, once or twice, like that, and she had not known what to do with it, and so had decided he was sneering.

"That is the master," said Mrs. Reynolds, beside her, with the proprietary calm of a woman who has watched a boy turn into a man and finds the result entirely satisfactory.

"Yes," said Elizabeth.

She did not move on. She stood in front of it longer than she had meant to, the gallery quiet around her, and looked at the easy hand on the book and the half-finished expression at the mouth, and felt — what? Not quite anything she could name.

Mrs. Reynolds had moved on; her aunt also; their voices, several paces down the gallery, had taken up another portrait. Elizabeth heard, without quite hearing, the housekeeper say, "She was very low last summer, you know — we were sorry for it — but she is more herself now."

Georgiana.

Last summer. Ramsgate. A girl of only fifteen, very nearly carried off by a man Elizabeth had stood in a Hertfordshire ballroom and found witty. The cold flush came up from her stomach as it had in April. She had walked into that wrongness with both eyes wide open. She had liked the man. She had defended him in her own head. She had heard his account of the Darcys and given it room, given it weight; and the girl on the wall in front of her was the girl who had been the price.

She turned, with some difficulty, from Mr. Darcy's portrait, and followed the others.

The miniature of Miss Darcy at twelve was at the gallery's far end. A small oval, gold-framed: a fair girl with her brother's eyes and none of his guarded mouth. All sweetness, was Elizabeth's first thought; all softness; the kind of face that had not yet been required to put a wall up against anything. She felt, looking at it, a small protective tenderness she had not authorized.

"She has grown so tall, ma'am, you would not know her," Mrs. Reynolds was saying. She paused, and added, in the same warm key, "She comes down with the master on Monday — and a good thing, too. The house has stood too quiet this summer."

"On Monday?" Mrs. Gardiner said. "Why, that is the day after tomorrow. Shall you have a houseful?"

"Aye, with the master and the young lady, ma'am, and four others, as well. Miss Darcy is bringing her friends out of London with her. We shall be lively again, ma'am, and very glad of it. They set out from town today, and so we shall have them all by Monday afternoon." Mrs. Reynolds paused, considering, and added in the same domestic key, "He has been longer in town this year than is his custom. We do not often have him so long away from us."

Elizabeth, behind her, did the date arithmetic. February. February to July. Five months. The same arithmetic, she thought without permission, that she had heard in another voice eleven weeks ago in a parlor at Hunsford, when a man had said *I have struggled in vain* and meant *I have been struggling for months.* The five months were the same five months. She had not, before this minute, set the two against each other.

“Is Mr. Darcy much attached to Pemberley, then?” Mrs Gardiner asked, smiling to soften her words. 

“Oh, no! Well, perhaps.” Mrs. Reynolds smiled at the miniature of Georgiana. “He certainly is a doting brother.”

Elizabeth turned her face to the nearest window. The sun on the lawn outside was so bright that for a half-second she could see nothing at all.

Could she have been so wrong? 

After they had seen the rest of the public parts of the house, Mrs. Reynolds walked them back down the staircase to the south door. There she stopped and recommended very prettily that the lady and gentleman, before they left, should walk in the grounds a little. She waved toward the river, toward the bench above the falls, where the prospect of the house was particularly fine; the gardeners were not at work this morning in that quarter and they would have the grounds to themselves. 

Elizabeth listened with a half-attention. Her other half was still in the gallery, in front of a portrait, with a small fond housekeeper's voice in it she had not anticipated and could not quite put down.

“Such a kind offer,” Mrs. Gardiner said. “We would be delighted.”

Elizabeth, who would have taken any open door at this moment — air, sea, anything that was not another room of his — gave thanks to the housekeeper that she hoped were the right thanks, and stepped out into the sun.

At the first step out, the heat hit. After the cool of the rooms it was as if they had stepped into a warm bath. A perfect July day, with heat enough to make the shade an event and sky enough to make the green shine. Mr. Gardiner offered Mrs. Gardiner his arm, and they took the path that led west across the front of the house. They passed the terrace, broad and pale and warm underfoot, and then down by an easy slope to the gravel walk that ran above the river.

Elizabeth followed a step or two behind. She had not been able to say, in any room of that house, what she had felt. She did not, in the open air of the lawn, know exactly what she felt now. The sun on the back of her neck was a warm scarf. Her aunt's voice, ahead, was placid; her uncle's, answering, was pleased. The river below the walk ran over its stones with the soft continuous burbling that sang that it was happy to carry on forever.

Where the path bent south at a clump of beeches, the trees fell back, and they had a new view of the valley: their own bank sloping down to the river, the tributary's small stone bridge just below them, and on the far side, half a mile off through the trunks, the line of the south drive. Mr. Gardiner, ahead, stopped at the bend to admire. Mrs. Gardiner stopped beside him.

Elizabeth let her aunt and uncle stop at the bend, and went on alone the few yards to the rail of the bridge over the tributary, and looked across.

Through the trunks of the beeches on the far side of the river, on the south drive, a horseman was riding.

He was a long way off—a quarter-mile, perhaps; perhaps a little more. Through the gaps between the trees she could see only that he was on a dark horse, that he wore a dark coat, that he sat his horse in the manner of a man who had been on horses since he was four. He was not hurrying. He was crossing the ground at the long swinging walk of a horse being let cool at the end of a piece of road.

Something at the back of her ribs jumped without her permission.

She saw him for, perhaps, three seconds. The trees closed.

She looked again. The trees did not open. There was only the brown and white of the trunks and the green of the leaves, and the line of the drive going off northward through them, and no horseman on it.

Her aunt and uncle had not turned. They were looking the other way, at the bridge over the tributary.

She forced herself to draw a breath, slowly.

It must have been, she informed herself in the privacy of her head, a tenant. A tenant of Mr. Darcy's, riding home on his own business. Or a steward. Or a gardener's son on a borrowed horse. Or a stranger to the place altogether. A traveler taking the south road, who had no notion he was passing through anybody's pale. 

It surely had not been the master of the house, who was setting out from London this very morning, who was leaving with a party of five, who would be brought to Pemberley on Monday afternoon in a procession of carriages, who could not, by any arithmetic in the world, be on his own south drive at twenty minutes past eleven on a hot Saturday in late July.

She had come a hundred and fifty miles north on her aunt's account, not on his. She had not come an hundred and fifty miles to put him into trees that did not contain him.

The crossness was sharper than the moment warranted. She felt that, also, and tried to set it aside.

"Lizzy!" said Mrs. Gardiner, brightly, at the end of the bridge. "Come and see. There is a fish in here as long as your arm."

Elizabeth came, and admired the fish, and contributed an observation about its evident contentment in its situation, and walked on with her aunt and uncle along the gravel walk, and did not look back across the river. The image did not leave her. 

They walked another half-hour in the grounds — a small circuit east of the house, by the shrubbery and the kitchen garden, and back round to the south door — and saw nothing on the south drive but, twice, a deer, and once a kingfisher down at the water. 

Elizabeth looked for another sign of the rider, despite herself; and twice she told herself, with displeasure, to stop looking. She was inventing him out of trees. It must be the heat.

By the time they came round the front of the house, the sun stood high, and the chaise had been brought up, and the same quiet man was holding the door. No one else was around. The beautiful grounds, the beautiful house, was empty.

Mr. Gardiner handed Mrs. Gardiner up. Mrs. Gardiner, in the act of being handed, observed that she had had a perfectly delightful morning and that it had been a very great kindness in the housekeeper, and that they ought, perhaps, to leave a little something for the woman by way of —

The hooves came up the gravel from the south — single, unhurried, a horse and one rider, no carriage behind.

*He will not be there.* She had carried the sentence up to the inn and down from it; she had carried it up the drive and around the gravel sweep; she was carrying it, still, standing, about to set her foot on the carriage step.

Elizabeth’s gloved hand was reaching for the carriage door. She turned her head.

The horseman, tall, dark, on a tall bay, dust on its flanks, sweat at its shoulder. The bay was breathing hard. 

Mr. Darcy.

He saw her at the same moment. 

She saw the surprise reach him. There was a small, distinct alteration in the angle of his head, and his hand on the rein checked, and the horse — taking the change of weight — fell from the walk to a halt, four yards from where she stood. His other hand, gloveless, came up to lift his hat.

The bay blew out. The sound, in the high still noon, was very loud.

Elizabeth had not moved.

He said, "Miss Bennet."

She heard her own voice from somewhere outside her body say, "Mr. Darcy."

Mrs Gardiner made a small startled sound from the carriage. Elizabeth did not look at her.

# Chapter 9: On the Gravel

He swung down — economical, no flourish, the dismount of a man who had been doing it since he could walk. He took two steps toward the carriage and stopped, and she could see that he had not, in the two seconds he had had since seeing her, decided what to do with himself.

Elizabeth would have been hard pressed afterwards to say exactly what he looked like in that first half-minute. She had time, and looking, and all the leisure of a frozen world. Yet the impressions came one at a time, each insisting on being noticed before the next would arrive.

The line under the brim of his hat, where he had taken sun on his face; he was browner than she remembered. The dust on his boots and the dust dark along the seam of his coat-skirts, the way dust laid itself on a man who had been riding fast on a dry road. The one glove still on his hand dark with it where it had held the rein.

He was thinner. She thought it the first moment she had seen him. The moment after, she distrusted the thought. It was the sort of thing a woman wishes to see, some evidence of having struggled, and she had learned recently the danger of seeing what one wished to see.

His eyes had gone, at the moment of speaking her name, to her face; and had, at the second of finding her face there, gone away again — to the bay's neck, to the gravel between them, briefly to her uncle, who had come up beside the carriage door and was waiting with the polite suspended attention of a man who has not yet been introduced and does not intend to be presumptuous about it.

Her uncle. Her aunt. She had forgotten, for the moment the world had given her, that there was anyone else in it.

"Mr. Darcy," she heard herself say again, and was at least relieved that the words came in the order in which words are supposed to come, "may I — Mr. Darcy, my uncle and aunt — Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner, of Gracechurch Street."

He bowed to her uncle with a gravity that was, she registered with a small interior shock, exactly correct. There was no interval in it; there was no measuring. Whatever it might have been to the Mr. Darcy of last October to be presented to a tradesman and his wife on his own gravel sweep — and she could supply that calculation in a half-instant, having watched its arithmetic done in a Hertfordshire ballroom — was not, evidently, what it was to this Mr. Darcy. He acknowledged the introduction. He asked, in a low voice, if they had come up that morning from Lambton; he hoped they had not found the road too dusty in the heat.

Mr. Gardiner, a man of the city who had lived all his life by the principle of taking each new acquaintance at his own face, returned that they had had a most pleasant drive, and that the morning had been a very great pleasure, and that the housekeeper had shown them the house with admirable patience.

"Mrs. Reynolds is patience itself," Mr. Darcy said. The corner of his mouth lifted — the half-finished expression of the portrait, exactly. "She has had practice."

Mr. Gardiner smiled at it. Mrs. Gardiner, leaning a little out of the carriage with her kind face under her bonnet, smiled at it too. She said something pleasant, perhaps about the prospect from the gallery windows.

Elizabeth said nothing. She could not at that moment have produced her own name without practising it first.

She had been ready, somewhere in the back of her mind, for various Mr. Darcys, against any of whom she had a small private armory of replies and silences.

She had not been ready for this one.

As a small boy ran up to take the reins of his horse, Mr. Darcy was speaking to her uncle gravely about the road from Lambton, in the manner of a man who knew the road from Lambton. There was no condescension in it. No patronage, no slight tilt of the head that suggested the speaker was being agreeable to someone he need not be agreeable to. The man who had written that letter, she thought, would not condescend. Here, he was that man.

Or perhaps not. Perhaps he was so disordered that he could not muster condescension. Or this was ordinary courtesy, exactly correctly performed by a gentleman to a gentleman in his own avenue, and she was decorating it because she wished it to be decorated.

She had been wrong about a man's character once before this year, and at such cost. She felt the cold flush come up under her ribs again — the fear, this time, of being about to be wrong in the opposite direction, and at the same kind of cost.

She could not get her feet under her. She tried, and could not.

Mr. Darcy had asked — she had not heard the asking; she had heard her uncle's reply — how long they meant to stay in the country; and Mr. Gardiner was telling him, pleasantly, that they were to be at Lambton four days more, and then to take the road south by way of Birmingham. Mr. Darcy bowed. He asked, after a small pause, after Mr. Bennet's family.

It was directed nominally to her uncle, but the question was for her. She felt the small turn of his shoulders that gave it to her.

"They were all well," she said, "when I last had a letter."

"I am glad."

A silence held for a half-beat, of the kind that, in the drawing-rooms of last winter, she would have been at small social pains to fill. She did not fill it. She did not, this morning, have any small pleasant pains in reserve.

Mr. Gardiner — God bless her uncle — turned a little and said something quiet and easy to Mrs. Gardiner about the time, and the heat of the day, and stepped over to settle Mrs. Gardiner's parasol against the corner of the carriage seat where it would not be in her way. It was the smallest possible piece of social tact, and it gave Elizabeth, for the space of perhaps ten seconds, the gravel between herself and Mr. Darcy with no one immediately attending.

He took half a step nearer. Not toward her, of course. Toward the door to the carriage. His voice, when he spoke, was lower than it had been to her uncle.

"I had not known," he said, "you were to be in Derbyshire."

He did not look at her when he said it. He looked past the carriage, at the sweep of his lawn.

She heard it twice. She heard it in the surface meaning — *I would have warned my household to expect you, I would have given orders that the family was to be from home, I would have spared you this awkwardness on my own gravel.* And she heard it underneath. *I came home in the belief I would not see you. I came north because I would not see you.*

She did not know which had been meant. She suspected — she knew, with the certainty one has about things one cannot prove — that both had been meant. He had not, in the half-second between dismounting and speaking, decided which sentence he was making. He had said the one sentence that did the work of both, and trusted her to hear it.

She was not, this morning, in any condition to be trusted with anything.

What she wanted to say was that she had not known it either until yesterday. That she had agreed to the expedition on the understanding, recited to herself twice in the dark before sleeping that he would be in London until the end of August; that she would not, on any consideration she could presently think of, have set foot in his county had she supposed there was the smallest chance of meeting him on it.

What she said was:

"My uncle had a fancy to see this part of the country. He had not been so far north before."

"It is a fine country."

"Yes."

He looked, for the first time since the bow, at her face. A half-second, perhaps three-quarters. Then his eyes went away again, to the carriage.

"You are at Lambton?"

"At the Rose."

"I — " he began, and stopped. Whatever he had been going to say, he did not say. He bowed, instead, very slightly. "I hope you have not found the morning too warm."

"Not at all."

"I am glad."

Then he turned his head a degree toward Elizabeth. He bowed, and held a hand out.

She took his hand — gloveless still, warm under her own glove — and managed, somehow, to step up into the carriage. Her aunt's hand, quietly, was at her elbow as she sat down. She did not look at her aunt. She did not look at her uncle. She found her seat by the river side, in the small shade of the carriage's hood, and put her gloved hands in her lap, and turned her face, with all the composure she possessed, to the window.

She had not, until this morning, supposed that one could feel mortification of a wholly new kind at twenty years old. She had been wrong about that as she had been wrong about, evidently, a great many things.

The trees of the drive came up around them on either side. Through the small back window of the chaise — looking past her aunt's shoulder, between the brim of her aunt's bonnet and the back of the seat — there was a strip of the sweep visible, narrowing as the chaise drew off.

She did not look at it for the first ten yards. She did not look at it for the first twenty. Then she did.

He had not moved. The hand he had given her at the carriage was at his side again, gloveless still, dust along the side of the thumb.

---

For a quarter of an hour no one spoke.

The lodge gate fell behind. The chaise came out onto the post-road, and the unbroken stone of Pemberley's pale lay between the road and the woods inside it, going north and east the way it had gone yesterday at her right. 

The harness creaked. The hooves clopped on. A fly worked the inside of the glass. Mr. Gardiner, opposite, gazed out the window with the ease of a man who had had a satisfactory morning and was content to let it settle. Mrs. Gardiner, beside her, had her bonnet a degree turned, not quite toward Elizabeth, not quite away. Her hands were folded in her lap in the manner of a woman who is letting a silence do its work.

Elizabeth's gloved hands echoed her aunt’s. Against the printed cotton of her skirt, a trace of dust along the side of her right thumb The spot his bare hand had taken her gloved one to hold her steady on the step was still there. She could see it on the kid. She did not, for the first ten yards, or the first one hundred, brush it off.

His hand had been warm in hers. He had not held her hand a second longer than the courtesy required. He had not held it a second less. The exactness of it — the *care* of the exactness — fluttered through her like a small private weather she had not authorized. She looked at her glove and put the thought down.

"A fine fellow, Lizzy."

Mr. Gardiner said it as a man states a discovered fact he is pleased about. He had been waiting, evidently, for the road to put a half-mile between them and the gates before he would say it; now the half-mile was behind him and the verdict could be released.

"He had not, you know, been quite what I had been led — by my wife, I confess, — to expect. The bow was very civil. The question after your father was prettily put. He had no occasion to be pleasant to me, and he was pleasant to me. That is not nothing, in a man." He considered. "Not nothing, in a great man."

"No, Uncle," said Elizabeth. Somehow, her voice came out steady. Nothing else inside her was.

Mrs. Gardiner turned her bonnet a further degree toward her niece.

"Lizzy — was *that* the Mr. Darcy you met at Meryton?"

Elizabeth turned her face to the open window. The wall, still on her left, kept rolling along.

"Yes?" She sounded unsure even to herself.

"I had been led," said Mrs. Gardiner, in a voice the word *carefully* would not be too heavy for, "to expect — by Jane, you know, this winter — a gentleman of a different quality.”

She left a small pause for Elizabeth to speak into. Elizabeth did not.

“A gentleman who had stood up at a country ball and refused to dance with anyone he had not been introduced to. A gentleman who had said something, within hearing, of the second Miss Bennet, that the second Miss Bennet had not chosen to forget."

Another small pause. Another silence.

"Could Jane truly have been so mistaken?" Mrs. Gardiner sighed. “I always thought her views of people more rosy than they should be.” 

It was perfectly mildly said. There was no edge in it. 

But still, Elizabeth bled a bit inside.

She wanted to say that she had been wrong, last winter, to tell Jane those things.That she had carried, for months, an account of a man's character built on a mere half-hour of seeing him in a country ballroom. She had spent the winter besides taking another man's grievance for the truth. That she had been required, in April, on a path at Rosings, to take her account apart in her own hand and look at it. 

And wince.

What she said was:

"I think — perhaps, Aunt — it was a mistake to take a man's worst evening as his settled character. He had been brought into Hertfordshire against his inclination, by Mr. Bingley. He had not chosen any of the company in that ballroom. This morning, though, even when obviously surprised by us, he chose to be civil to my uncle.” To her. She paused. The wall rolled by. "It is possible to mistake a season's weather for a man's character."

"Just so," said Mr. Gardiner, nodding. "A man on his own ground is seldom the man he is on someone else's." He let the matter rest.
