Mrs. Gardiner did not nod. She did not, however, press. Behind her sharp eyes, she considered.

A mile of road. The silence returned. Mrs. Gardiner, after a decent interval, in a voice so light it might have been an afterthought, said:

"And had you ever met him since, Lizzy? Since Hertfordshire?"

Elizabeth had been waiting, trying to hide and knowing she could not, for the question.

"I had, in fact, in the spring." She kept her own voice at the same light weight as her aunt's. "He was at Rosings during my stay with the Collinses. Lady Catherine de Bourgh is his aunt, you know. I was several times at the great house — with Lady Catherine, and Miss de Bourgh, and a few others."

“A few other?” Mrs. Gardiner’s brows rose. “Among the few others, was he, Lizzy?”

Elizabeth told herself not to shrug. Not to look guilty.

The wall ended. The hedgerows took up. The road bent gently east.

The small angle of his head as he had said *I had not known you were to be in Derbyshire* came back on her as she watched the hedgerow go by. The half-degree to the right; the way his eyes had not been looking at her when he said it. The way he had trusted her to hear the sentence that could mean *I would have warned my household to expect you* and also could mean *I came north because I would not see you*, and to choose between them. Or not to choose, and to know that both were true. 

The hot crowded feeling that had come up under her ribs at the moment was up there again, in the chaise. Only this time without the armor of his being there in front of her, requiring her to be composed. Her gloved hand was at her throat without permission. She put it down.

A second mile. No one spoke.

Yesterday seemed weeks ago.

"Well," said Mr. Gardiner, easily, into the quiet, "I shall be very glad of my luncheon."

He said it with the cheerful candor of a man who had had a long morning and a great deal of fresh air and considered his appetite a plain civic fact. "It must be coming on toward one. And what a morning it has been — my dear, I am very glad indeed that the country has used you so kindly. Derbyshire is a delightful piece of England, and I shall be very glad to come back to it; though I confess I am the kind of man who likes a place the better for knowing London is at the other end of the road. I am the better for the morning." 

He drew out his watch, considered the face of it, and put it back. "I am also hungry."

"My dear," said Mrs. Gardiner. "I had been thinking of just that. There is — or there was, in my time, and is again, by the landlord's account — a small place at the very end of the inn yard, two steps up the lane that goes by the church. A Mrs. Pratt kept it then; her daughter keeps it now.  And she does, the landlord says. The best cold pigeon pie of three parishes. We might walk over, after we have set ourselves to rights at the inn."

"Excellent," said Mr. Gardiner, "I am perfectly agreeable."

"Lizzy?" said her aunt.

"Yes," Elizabeth said. "Yes — of course."

The chaise turned under the arch of the Rose. The same arch. The same yard. The same boy ran up. Mr. Gardiner stepped down, and turned, and handed his wife down, and turned again to hand his niece.

Elizabeth looked at her hands. The dust on the side of her thumb was still there. 

She gave him her other hand. He took it without noticing, and handed her down.

# Chapter 10: After the Surprise

The chaise passed the beech turn and was gone.

For half a minute the road still kept the noise of it — wheels over dry gravel, the creak of leather and jingle of harness, the postilion's call to the near horse — and then the wood took that too. The dust hung where the carriage had been. Then the dust settled.

A beautiful day. 

And now empty.

Darcy stood at the foot of his own steps with one glove in his hand and no recollection of having taken it off.

The boy had the bay by the bridle and was leading him round toward the stable road. Darcy rolled his shoulder and turned to mount the steps. Mrs. Reynolds was already emerging from the doorway.

She did not run. Mrs. Reynolds had not run in this century, and would have considered any report to the contrary an impertinence. But she crossed the portico and came down the first two steps faster than dignity strictly required. She caught herself on the third, and travelled with him back to the landing with her cap and her keys and her countenance all restored to office.

She was a small, upright woman, near sixty. She had been at Pemberley nearly thirty years. She had taken his first fall from the great chestnut as a private grievance against the chestnut, and held it still. In the year after his mother died, he had known Mrs. Reynolds's face better than almost any woman's. The same brown eyes — quick, judicious, devoted — held him pinned now.

"I beg your pardon, sir." She folded her hands at her waist. "Had I known you were so near arriving, I should never have taken a party through the house this morning."

She glanced down the long drive, squinting. "And the others?”

"Monday."

"Of course." She glanced back at him, measuring more than his answer.

"You could not know," he said. "No harm was done."

Her face eased. There was the matter of the Monday arrivals to dispose of, then. She named them as they crossed the portico: the blue chamber for Miss Bingley, the Hursts' old rooms, the chamber over the south door for Mr. Bingley, Mrs. Annesley's room aired and Miss Darcy's apartments prepared. He gave the briefest assent he could give without seeming inattentive.

He knew he did not fool her.

Having recovered her composure, she turned briskly to the business of the house.

"The younger lady was much taken with the library, sir. Which puts me in mind — those books you sent for last spring have come in. There were so many parcels I had them set in the library rather than carried up. I did not think you would wish your desk so heaped."

The younger lady.

His mind supplied the name before he could prevent it. Elizabeth.

He shut it away at once, but not before Mrs. Reynolds paused half a breath — not long enough to be impertinent — and then went on as if she had seen nothing.

"Thank you, Mrs. Reynolds."

"Very good, sir."

Inside, the hall held the cool of the stone and a faint beeswax scent. Mrs. Reynolds walked half a pace behind him, still a little out of breath and too well trained to admit it.

"Mr. Greaves will be glad to hear you are come down early, sir," she said. "He had wondered, with so large a party expected, when he should have his half-hour with you. The estate papers are already in your office."

Darcy glanced at the old walnut longcase clock at the far end of the hall. "Send to him for three o'clock."

"Very good, sir." She paused another half a breath, taking in what he had not said, and then moved on. "A cold tray in the small parlor, then. I’ll see to it now."

The principal rooms were familiar as the order of his own name: gallery, library, morning room, the private stair that led toward the family apartments.

Miss Elizabeth Bennet had passed through some of those rooms not two hours before. She had stood where his mother had stood. She had looked at his books. She had heard his housekeeper speak of him.

He did not turn his head toward any of it. He went directly through to his dressing-room.

Already, someone had set out water, towels, a fresh shirt. Hodges, an under-butler doing the office of valet. Watkins had remained with the rest of the household and would not reach Pemberley until Monday. Darcy let himself be unbuttoned and helped out of his coat. He sluiced cold water across his face and the back of his neck. The gravel-smell of the road slid off in a single grey rinse. He scrubbed the dust from his hands. He scrubbed his right hand twice.

It was not the dust.

A lady accepting assistance into a carriage; a gentleman giving it. An interval so exact that no bystander could have put a name to it. No gentleman, afterwards, had the right to do so.

His palm remembered it.

Inconvenient. He had spent months teaching himself not to think of her. Reinforced the lesson over the past long days on the road. The lesson had gone to nothing in five minutes on the gravel.

Hodges had a clean shirt over his head before he had finished considering the inconvenience. By the time he was buttoned again, a stranger looking at him would have seen only a gentleman newly come in from a short ride; nothing of three days' travel, and nothing of the catastrophe five minutes on the gravel had made of him.

He did not look in the glass.

The estate office was on the ground floor near the steward's door, a small paneled room with shutters half-drawn against the heat. He took the back steps down. Mr. Greaves's first batch of papers waited on the blotter, held by the silver weight. Hedges, leases, repairs, accounts. 

This was work he could set his mind to.

Darcy sat in his father's chair — broad, square, and uncushioned, the arms worn smooth where three generations of Darcys had set their hands. The top sheet concerned the east pasture and the man with the Herefords.

He read the first paragraph. He read the second. Mr. Greaves's dry, slanting hand had not altered in twenty years: *The tenant being desirous of extending the let to Michaelmas…*

He knew the sentence. He had read its brothers all his adult life.

He took up the pen and wrote beneath the line: *Renewed to Michaelmas.*

Three words. A black line. A decision in the world. Easy.

He set the pen down and drew the next sheet toward him.

At three, Mr. Greaves came in with a folio under his arm. A thin, grey man in a perfectly brushed coat and immaculate linen, he had been steward at Pemberley for nearly all of Darcy's father's time. Twelve years a widower. Not a sentimental man, but an exact one. Darcy had inherited him as he had inherited the house and the silver. As with the house and the silver, he was sometimes uncertain whether Greaves served him, or only continued, as the rest of the furniture continued, in his service.

If Greaves had heard there had been visitors in the house that morning, he gave no sign of it. Visitors belonged to Mrs. Reynolds's province; hedges and leases belonged to his.

They worked through the papers.

The Hartside boundary, where an old hedge had been permitted to argue with a new ditch. The mortar wanting work on the stable wall. Adamson leaving the home farm for his brother's place near Sheffield, and deserving, Greaves thought, a recommendation if Mr. Darcy saw no objection. The east pasture. More about the Herefords. The south drive.

Darcy answered. He signed where he was to sign. He gave leave where leave was wanted. He wrote Adamson's character himself, refused the proposed delay on the stable wall, and approved the new gate at the Hartside boundary, which had been wanting these three years.

How far was it to Lambton?

The thought came in between one sheet and the next, with no obvious provocation beyond Greaves's mentioning the Lambton road in another connection. Five miles, give or take. He had ridden it as a boy, as a young man, as an adult last summer with his sister. Five miles was nothing on horseback; a small excursion by carriage. It was not so far that one could not call. It was not so near that calling was inevitable.

Did he dare?

He set his hand flat on the desk while Greaves moved to the next sheet. The signet ring caught the light.

She had no affection for him. He had her own word for that, given in April, in a parlor in Kent, in language he could repeat to himself word by knife-edged word. To present himself at the inn at Lambton merely because she happened to be staying there — to send a note, to send a basket, to send anything — was to require her to receive him with a civility she might not feel. It was the imposition of an acquaintance she had renounced. 

It approached, at least in spirit, the offense she had named at Hunsford.

But that touch.

Her hand had rested in his for the space required by courtesy. Perhaps a shade longer. He could not, in justice, build a single thing upon it. A lady did not refuse the assistance of a gentleman handing her into a carriage. She could not, without insult to him and a public scene, have done other than place her hand where she had placed it. The interval had been the interval prescribed by good manners. It signified nothing.

It signified only that she had not drawn back. It was nothing. It was less than that. 

But it was more than he had had a month ago.

He permitted himself the single word, *hope*. And then took it back, because he had no right to it.

Mr. Gardiner, though, he might write to at once. The freedom of the Pemberley water was a courtesy from gentleman to gentleman, and required no sister's countenance. 

The ladies were another matter. He could not invite Mrs. Gardiner and her niece to so much as a luncheon under his own roof. Georgiana must come down on Monday to issue the invitation in her own person. That was the form. He would observe it, even if it cost him three days.

But what if the Gardiners planned to depart before Monday?

"Sir?" said Mr. Greaves.

Darcy looked up. Greaves had a sheet held out, waiting.

"I beg your pardon.” He touched his temple. “The road, I think. Read it again."

Greaves read it. Darcy heard one sentence in three. He nodded where Greaves's voice paused for nodding, and said *yes* twice, and was, by some grace of inheritance, not understood to have been elsewhere.

The meeting ran past its half-hour. Darcy did not draw it to a close. He was grateful for each new sheet. Each sheet asked a question with a possible answer.

And not one of them required courage.

Mr. Greaves closed the folio at last. He stood, with the aid of the chair’s armrest. "I shall have the men on the stable wall Monday, sir, unless you prefer it delayed while the family is in the house."

"Monday. The wall will not mend itself out of respect for visitors."

Greaves's lips pursed, which in Greaves was laughter. "Very good, sir."

When he had gone, the room enlarged around the empty chair.

On the desk, a blank sheet lay before him. He drew it nearer. *Mr. Gardiner — the freedom of the Pemberley water — tackle to be sent down — a basket from the gardens, with the housekeeper's compliments to the ladies.* *Surely, you are staying the week?* He composed it three times in his head. He did not yet take up the pen.

Tomorrow would do better. Tomorrow the note would have the look of intention rather than impulse. He would not go himself. Nor Monday. On Tuesday he would call at the inn with Georgiana and Mrs. Annesley, precisely as form required. His sister would be introduced to Miss Bennet. Mrs. Annesley would sit beside her. The visit would last the proper length of time. He would say what civility required and not one word beyond his command.

He arranged these determinations in his mind as a man sets stones across a stream in flood.

The absurdity was that it was he who had built the stream.

By evening, the heat had gone out of the glass and nearly out of the wood. The house settled and the sounds softened. A tray set down below. A soft step in the passage. A door latched at the far end of the corridor. The clock on the mantel performing its office with the same grave indifference it had shown to every private disaster since his grandfather's day.

He ate again in the small parlor because Mrs. Reynolds had arranged it, and because not eating would have set the household worrying. She brought in a plain dinner herself, saw that the wine was what it should be, and stood a moment longer than the placing of dishes required.

"Mrs. Annesley's room is aired, sir, and the linen done. The green chamber is in readiness, should Mr. Bingley come up before Monday. Robert has set out the silver as you prefer it."

"Thank you."

"Will you take coffee in the library, sir?"

"No, brandy in the blue sitting-room, thank you."

She inclined her head and left.

He had been listening for the thing she did not say. Whether the young lady had frowned at his portrait, laughed at Mrs. Reynolds's praise, looked bored among his books, looked relieved to leave. Mrs. Reynolds had told him none of it. Of course not.

That was the part he could not pardon himself — that he had wanted to ask.

He did not, at once, go up to his sitting-room. He turned the other way, through the hall and up to the gallery.

Beyond the branched candlestick he carried, the long room was swathed in summer dusk. The portraits hung as they had hung all his life. His grandfather, in court dress, glowering at the painter. His great-aunt with her spaniel, painted twice because the first attempt had not flattered the dog. His mother in white, twenty and not yet anyone's mother. So like Georgiana. His father, three years before he died, the new hunting dog at his feet and a younger man's face still upon him. Darcy paused before each.

He came at last to himself.

He had not chosen to stand for it. He had thought it absurd at the time and thought so still. He had been merely five-and-twenty. The painter had been an obligation acquired through his aunt. The sittings had cost him three entire afternoons.

He had grudged them — until the third. That afternoon, Georgiana had come in, mid-sitting, on some small errand of her own, and stood at his shoulder to ask the painter a serious question about the brush. He had turned his head to her and answered her, what, he could not now recall. Only that she had laughed, and he had felt his own mouth move with hers.

The painter had been quick.

There it was now, by candlelight: a quirk, asmall expression at the corner of the mouth that did not belong to the rest of the face. The likeness was respectable. The expression was not for company.

Mrs. Reynolds was fond of the picture. She must have brought Miss Bennet up before it this morning. He could imagine, with painful distinctness, what the housekeeper had said: that he was the sweetest-tempered, the most generous-hearted master in the world. He could imagine, with painful distinctness, what Miss Bennet had not said. *I have known this gentleman in two aspects, and neither of them was this one.*

Mrs. Reynolds was fond of the picture. She must have brought Miss Bennet up before it this morning. He could imagine, with painful distinctness, what the housekeeper had said: that he was the sweetest-tempered, the most generous-hearted master in the world. He could imagine, with painful distinctness, what Miss Bennet had not said. *That is not the gentleman I knew.*

He turned away.

He left the gallery and went down to the library. The shutters were not closed; the tall west windows held the last of the light. The shelves stood as he had arranged them himself, two summers ago. On the second shelf of the south wall, the new books — perhaps thirty of them — lay flat in neat stacks, still in their paper boards, some with their pages uncut.

He set his hand on the topmost stack and let it rest there. She had stood in this room. She had, surely, looked over the titles. What had she seen?

He would not imagine it.

In the blue sitting-room, the lamp on the small writing desk illuminated the tray with the brandy and a small plate covered with a white napkin. The rest of the room stood in the half-dark, too familiar to distract him and too quiet to be merciful.

He crossed to the window that overlooked the sweep and the beginning of the south drive. Outside, the gravel sweep had faded to a pale blur, and the beginning of the south drive disappeared into trees. 

The lamp behind him transformed the glass into a poor mirror. His face, thinly imposed on Pemberley: brow, mouth, the line beside the mouth deeper than it had been in April. Giving nothing away.

So he hoped.

He set his hand on the window-frame.

Under his palm, beneath the warmth of polished oak, a whisper of the other pressure remained: exact, light, accusing. *Hers.*

He would write to Mr. Gardiner in the morning. He would offer the freedom of the water and send tackle if Mr. Gardiner wished it. He would send the basket. He would not ride to Lambton.

On Monday, the house would fill. On Tuesday, he would call at the inn with his sister. He would introduce them. He would conduct himself as a gentleman conducts himself toward the visiting relations of an acquaintance.

After Tuesday, he would not seek her out.

# Chapter 11: The Note

By eight o'clock, the Rose had already begun its day: boots on the stair, voices in the yard, the smell of toast and wet stone rising together from below. Elizabeth came down to breakfast with the air of a young woman who had slept perfectly well, and the complexion of one who had not slept at all.

She had made, before leaving her chamber, the usual arrangements with her face. A little cold water. A little pinning and repinning of hair. A little straightening of the lace at her cuffs. In the small glass above the washstand, she’d found nothing more damning than paleness, and paleness could be attributed to heat, to travel, to a strange bed, to the Derbyshire air, to any number of innocent causes that had not stood in front of her yesterday on a gravel sweep and said her name.

She had not slept.

Or rather, she had slept in fragments, each one finding its way back to Pemberley. Mr. Darcy on the south drive; Mr. Darcy bowing to her uncle; Mr. Darcy asking after her family; Mr. Darcy's hand, bare and warm under her glove; Mr. Darcy's portrait looking as though it had once known how to be amused; Mrs. Reynolds saying, with all the comfortable certainty of a woman describing the weather, that he was the best landlord and the best master in the world.

The words had behaved badly in the dark. They had not stayed where she put them.

It was one thing to discover that Mr. Wickham had been false. That discovery, though painful, had arranged itself at last into a shape she could understand. She had trusted charm and been deceived by it. It was humiliating, but not incomprehensible. Charm existed to deceive. A handsome countenance and easy manners were traps set in the open field of society, and she had walked into one with both eyes bright and all her judgment asleep.

But Mr. Darcy had not been charming. Mr. Darcy had been everything that should have made judgment easy. Proud. Silent. Disagreeable. Severe in manner, superior in address, almost industrious in making himself disliked. If she had been wrong about *him*, what then had she been using for judgment at all?

She had told Jane, with all the confidence of affection and wit, that he was the last man in the world from whom civility was to be expected. She had told her aunt enough to make yesterday morning's bewilderment possible. She had allowed her father to laugh at him, her mother to abuse him, Lydia to mimic his gravity, Wickham to wound him under her very eyes and be rewarded for the performance with her sympathy. She had carried an account of his character from room to room, from Meryton to Longbourn to Gracechurch Street, and set it down before the people she loved as if she had been bringing them truth.

What if she had given them, not truth, but only her own mortification?

This question had come to her at some hour when the street below the Rose was quiet, when even the ostler's yard had given over its last mutterings. It had come and sat by her pillow like an unwelcome caller.

But by breakfast, Elizabeth was prepared to be composed at any cost.

The public room of the Rose was already busy. Sun entered through two windows that looked onto the yard and laid bright squares upon the flagged floor. A pair of farmers sat near the door over ale and bread, their hats beside them and their voices low. At another table, a commercial traveler was making impressive work of ham and eggs while reading a newspaper two days old. The smell of tea, toast, warm beer, and the faint clean damp of a recently scrubbed floor mingled with the stable-yard scents beyond the open door.

Mr. Staveley had put the Gardiners at a table somewhat apart from the rest, in the angle beneath the smaller window, where the wooden settle made a little enclosure of its own. It was a kindness, or a distinction, or only good innkeeping. Elizabeth was in no state to decide which.

Mrs. Gardiner was already there when Elizabeth came in, neat in her morning dress, her cap arranged with the pretty precision that made everything she wore seem more fashionable than it was. She had a letter open beside her plate, not newly arrived but one of yesterday's, and was smiling over it with the private warmth of a woman who had been returned, by geography, to some younger version of herself. Mr. Gardiner stood at the window, hands behind his back, looking into the yard with the contentment of a man for whom stables, weather, and breakfast were three respectable forms of evidence that the world was good.

"Ah, Lizzy," he said, turning. "You are just in time to prevent your aunt from ordering me another dish merely because Derbyshire air is supposed to require it. I have already eaten enough to satisfy three counties."

"Not three," said Mrs. Gardiner. "Only two, and one of them a small one. Sit down, Lizzy. You look a little tired."

"Do I?" Elizabeth took her place. "Then Derbyshire has used me ill already. I thought it promised better manners."

It was a perfectly acceptable answer. It even sounded, to her own ear, a little like herself. Her aunt's eyes rested on her for half a second longer than comfort allowed, and then moved away.

"Derbyshire manners," said Mr. Gardiner, resuming his seat, "are excellent, if yesterday may be admitted in evidence. Good roads, good prospects, good housekeepers, good cold pigeon pie —"

"The pigeon pie was Mrs. Pratt's daughter's," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"Then Mrs. Pratt's daughter shall stand for the county. I have no objection."

Elizabeth reached for the tea-pot. Her hand was steady. This seemed a considerable achievement and deserved, she thought, more praise than it was likely to receive.

She had just poured when Mr. Staveley appeared at their table with an expression of importance moderated by professional discretion. A broad, civil man with gray at his temples and a manner that could make a guest feel remembered without making that remembrance oppressive. Two days ago, he had welcomed Mrs. Gardiner home under her maiden name; today, he carried a folded note upon a small tray as though the note itself had a rank. Behind him, one of the inn boys bore a covered basket in both arms, its white cloth lifted at one corner by the green of leaves beneath.

"Begging your pardon, Mr. Gardiner, sir," he said, with a slight bow. "A letter. For you. And a basket for the ladies, with Mr. Darcy's compliments."

Elizabeth set the spoon beside her cup with more care than the spoon deserved.

Mr. Gardiner looked up with surprise, then amusement. "For me?"

Mr. Staveley leaned in and lowered his voice by the smallest possible degree. "The boy came from Pemberley with them. You must have made an impression up at the house yesterday, sir."

He placed the basket on the sideboard, where even under its cloth it declared itself too handsome for an ordinary errand, and withdrew with a bow.

There was nothing improper in the words. Indeed, there was scarcely anything in them at all. Yet Elizabeth felt the farmers near the door, the traveler with his newspaper, the yard beyond the window, the whole of Lambton arranging itself around the fact that a note and a basket had come from Pemberley.

When the innkeeper had gone, her uncle broke the seal. Her aunt's brows lifted, not much. Elizabeth laid her spoon against the saucer and did not lift the cup.

"Well," said Mr. Gardiner, after the first line, and his voice had gone pleasantly blank in the way it did when he was deciding how much of his thoughts to show. "This is very civil."

"From Mr. Darcy?" said Mrs. Gardiner.

"Indeed." He read on, and then, after a moment, read aloud: "*Mr. Darcy presents his compliments to Mr. Gardiner, and begs that Mr. Gardiner will command the Pemberley water during his stay at Lambton, if the sport should be agreeable to him. Tackle can be sent down at any hour Mr. Gardiner names.*"

"There," said Mrs. Gardiner, smiling. "Your Derbyshire trout have found you out."

"Or Mr. Darcy has found out my weakness," said Mr. Gardiner. "Which is most alarming, as I have no recollection of confessing it."

"You spoke of the river from the gallery," Mrs. Gardiner reminded him. "And of fish at least twice on the walk."

"Did I? Then I am betrayed by my own conversation. It is a common fate."

Elizabeth kept her eyes on the toast-rack. Mr. Darcy had remembered. That was all. A gentleman had observed another gentleman's interest and responded with a gentleman's courtesy. There was no occasion for agitation in that. There was less than no occasion. If anything, agitation would be vanity.

And yet the courtesy had been so exactly placed. Not to her. Not even first to her aunt. To her uncle, whose trade Mr. Darcy had yesterday acknowledged without a blink, whose company he had not been obliged to cultivate, whose pleasure he had nevertheless remembered before breakfast.

It was mortifying. It was suspicious. It was — she would not call it kind. But civil.

Mr. Gardiner read silently for a few more lines. The amusement in his face altered, not leaving him, but deepening into something more thoughtful.

"There is more?" asked Mrs. Gardiner.

He glanced once at Elizabeth, which was unnecessary and therefore cruel. "*Mr. Darcy also begs that Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet will accept a small basket from the gardens, with his compliments, and hopes Mrs. Gardiner may find something in Derbyshire still equal to her remembrance of it.*"

Her aunt's expression changed at once. The teasing brightness softened. "So prettily said."

"Just," said Mr. Gardiner.

Elizabeth could not disagree. She wished, for the space of one sharp second, that it had been clumsy. A clumsy compliment might have been dismissed as effort. A grand one might have been resented. This was neither. It had the advantage of being addressed to her aunt's history rather than her own feelings, and therefore gave Elizabeth no proper object upon which to exercise displeasure.

"He must have been told," Mrs. Gardiner said, "that I was once a Lambton girl. Mrs. Reynolds perhaps."

"Or Staveley," said Mr. Gardiner. "Staveley has told everyone else."

Mrs. Gardiner laughed.

"We must first determine whether I am a fisherman," said Mr. Gardiner, glancing again at the page.

"I can answer that," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"My dear, you can answer many things, but allow a man one illusion before breakfast."

Elizabeth took up her cup at last and discovered she had forgotten the sugar. She drank it anyway and found it bitter. She ought to say something. A light remark would be expected. Yesterday, in the chaise, she had defended Mr. Darcy to her aunt in phrases that had surprised even herself. Today, when his civility lay on the table between the cups and knives, she could not produce a sentence fit for use.

Her aunt noticed. Of course she noticed. Mrs. Gardiner had not been placed on earth to overlook the discomfort of people she loved.

"Lizzy," she said lightly, "you are very grave over Mr. Darcy's peaches."

"Over his possible peaches, then. One should never be grave over fruit in prospect."

Her uncle chuckled. Elizabeth managed a smile, but it came too late and did not convince anyone who mattered.

"It is only," she said, "a very handsome attention."

"Yes," said her uncle. "And handsomely offered. A very well-judged note."

Well-judged. The word touched precisely where she was tender.

"In the best sense," said Mr. Gardiner. "He gives us room to accept without embarrassment and to decline without offense. That is not so common a talent as it ought to be."

True. Elizabeth knew enough of gentlemen's addresses — formal and otherwise — to know the difference between civility that made space and civility that occupied it. Mr. Collins had never entered a room without filling it to suffocation with his own humility. Mr. Darcy, in three paragraphs, had contrived to make them obliged and free at once.

It was intolerable.

"I see yet another sentence," said Mrs. Gardiner, whose eyes had returned to the note.

Mr. Gardiner turned the page slightly. "Ah. Yes."

He did not read it aloud immediately. That was the first warning.

Mrs. Gardiner leaned nearer. "My dear."

"I am considering whether I am equal to the responsibility of delivering it."

"Then it must be very serious."

"Extremely. It concerns a young lady."

Elizabeth set down her cup too quickly. It touched the saucer with a small, betraying sound.

Her uncle's mouth twitched; her aunt's did not, though her eyes had gone bright.

"*If Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner plan to remain in Lambton until Tuesday,*" Mr. Gardiner read, with admirable gravity, "*Mr. Darcy would be honored to wait upon them at Lambton with his sister, if they would permit the introduction.*"

There was a pause.

Not a long one. Not long enough for any person in the room to have observed it, had any person in the room been attending. At their table, however, it settled like an additional place laid between them.

"His sister," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"So it says."

"Miss Darcy comes on Monday, I think Mrs. Reynolds told us." Mrs. Gardiner's voice remained perfectly mild. "Did she not, Lizzy?"

"Yes." Elizabeth was proud she could get out the word. It was short, but it had stood upright.

"And Mr. Darcy would be honored to bring her to *us*," said Mr. Gardiner.

"To you and my aunt," Elizabeth said, too quickly.

"My dear Lizzy," said her uncle, "your aunt and I are very agreeable people, but I confess I did not know we had become so irresistible to Derbyshire gentry merely by invading their home.”

Her aunt looked down at her plate. It was not enough to hide the smile.

"Perhaps," Elizabeth said, "he is naturally attentive to visitors."

"Perhaps," said Mrs. Gardiner.

Elizabeth touched the handle of her cup. The china clicked once against the saucer. She withdrew her hand.

"Mrs. Reynolds spoke very warmly of Miss Darcy," Mrs. Gardiner continued. "I should like to meet her. A young lady so highly praised by her staff is likely to be worth knowing."

In her mind, Elizabeth saw, not the miniature at twelve, but the story beyond it. The girl of fifteen at Ramsgate, very nearly ruined. The brother writing in bitterness and pride because he had no other way to make the truth known. She had spent half the night with that knowledge moving restlessly inside her, seeking a place to settle. It had found none.

"Yes," she said. "I believe she is."

Her aunt looked at her then. Carefully. Kindly.

Elizabeth found the pattern of the tablecloth suddenly worthy of study.

Her uncle folded the note along its original crease. "So. Do we change our plans and linger a few days longer?"

"We had not fixed our departure," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"We had fixed it in that very firm manner in which travelers fix things, by saying several contradictory things and meaning whichever should prove convenient." He tapped the folded note against the table. "I had some thought of going on tomorrow, if the heat held. Birmingham will not move farther off by our waiting, but neither will it come nearer."

"My dear," said Mrs. Gardiner, "you cannot be offered Pemberley water and refuse it. It would be uncivil to the trout."

He chuckled. "I am glad you put the matter on its proper moral footing."

"And I should like to see the more of the countryside, and down the river, if we may," her aunt added. "Lizzy and I may walk there while you discover whether Derbyshire fish are more obliging than Hertfordshire ones."

"Hertfordshire fish have never had a fair opportunity to disappoint me."

"Then you will write to Mr. Darcy?"

"I shall write to Mr. Darcy," said Mr. Gardiner, "and accept with gratitude. I shall name tomorrow morning for the fishing, if that suits you both. We shall remain until Tuesday at the least."

There it was.

Elizabeth had known it was coming. The whole note had been moving toward it with the quiet inevitability of a carriage approaching the inn door. Still, when her uncle said it, she felt the words close around her.

Remain until Tuesday.

On Tuesday, she would meet Miss Darcy. On Tuesday she would stand before the girl whose history she had carried since April like a sealed letter inside her own chest. Mr. Darcy would come again, not by accident, not on horseback out of trees, but by intention, to this very inn, with his sister beside him.

She could not object. To object would be to explain. To explain would be impossible.

"Lizzy?" said Mrs. Gardiner. "You do not dislike the plan?"

"No," said Elizabeth. Then, because no one believed her, she added, "No, indeed. I should be very glad to meet Miss Darcy."

This was true, which made it no easier to say.

Her uncle's expression softened. He was too kind a man to press when a lesser uncle might have enjoyed himself. "Then it is settled. Mr. Staveley!"

Mr. Staveley appeared with the promptness of a man who had been waiting to be summoned and the dignity of a man who would deny it.

"Sir?"

"If the boy from Pemberley is still below, tell him he shall have an answer directly."

"Very good, sir."

"And Mr. Staveley," Mrs. Gardiner said, glancing toward the sideboard, "may we see what Mr. Darcy has sent before it goes below?"

"Gladly, ma'am."

He crossed to the sideboard and drew back the cloth with a ceremony that would not have disgraced a larger occasion. There were early peaches, currants, cucumbers as fine as any Elizabeth had seen that summer, a little lettuce, herbs, and a small nosegay tucked in at one side.

Mr. Staveley permitted himself a smile. "A very handsome basket, ma'am. Not that one is surprised, from Pemberley. They do things properly up there."

Elizabeth felt the sentence enter the long account being drawn up against her, line by line, witness by witness. Mrs. Reynolds. The portrait. Her uncle's approval. Her aunt's softened face over the mention of Derbyshire remembered. Mr. Staveley, with his napkin over one arm, stating that Pemberley did things properly as though all Lambton had known it for thirty years and only Elizabeth Bennet had required instruction.

"We cannot possibly eat this entire basket by ourselves," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"No," said Mr. Gardiner. "That would be gluttony, and I try to be a moral man, especially on Sundays."

"Mr. Staveley," Mrs. Gardiner said, "would your kitchen be able to make use of some of it for our luncheon? You may keep the rest."

"Gladly, ma'am. Mrs. Staveley will make you a luncheon from it that would do credit to the county."

"Then pray give Mrs. Staveley our compliments and our thanks."

"And Mr. Darcy's compliments also," said Mr. Gardiner, dryly.

"Aye, sir," said Mr. Staveley, with another small bow. "Those travel well in Lambton."

As he departed with instructions for the basket, her aunt reached for the toast. "Well, Lizzy. It seems Derbyshire has not done with us yet."

Elizabeth looked toward the yard, where sun flashed on harness brass as a boy crossed with a roan. She thought of the long park wall, of the south drive, of a portrait's half-finished expression, of a gentleman's hand offered exactly as long as courtesy required and perhaps — perhaps — a shade longer. Her fingers closed once inside her glove before she could prevent them.

"No," she said. "It seems not."

# Chapter 12: The Horse Chestnut

Tuesday came in with a low white heat over Lambton, the kind that made the hills seem nearer and the stones of the main street look older than they were.

Elizabeth had spent Sunday afternoon in the expectation of Tuesday. She had spent Monday in a still worse condition: expecting it with the assistance of leisure. There had been walks with her aunt, a drive with her uncle, a dutiful admiration of two prospects, three lanes, and one bridge, and through every object deserving attention — every tree, gate, signboard, and remembered house of Mrs. Gardiner's girlhood — Tuesday had stood behind the view like a person waiting to be named.

By Tuesday morning, she had grown so accustomed to dread that its arrival seemed almost an impertinence. It should, after two days' residence in her mind, have had the civility to be less fresh.

It had not.

She drew on her gloves, discovered a wrinkle across the left palm, drew one off again, and began a second campaign against the fingers.

"Lizzy," said Mrs. Gardiner, from her place by the sitting-room window, "if you mean to exhaust yourself before Mr. Darcy has paid his call, I must object. It would be awkward to explain."

"I have no such intention. I am only discovering that Derbyshire air is destructive to kid."

"Derbyshire air has many qualities. I had not known that one."

Elizabeth smiled, because smiling was expected, and because her aunt's tenderness had put on humor for her sake. The glove came right at last. She let her hands fall.

Mrs. Gardiner had already determined, with the quiet authority of a woman who understood her niece, that if Miss Darcy liked it they should walk into the town after the call. Elizabeth, she said, would do better in the open air, even in the heat, than in any stiff little sitting-room visit. 

Mr. Gardiner, having devoted Monday morning to the Pemberley water with rods, tackle, and all the moral seriousness proper to a gentleman authorized to fish by another gentleman of ten thousand a year, had returned with one modest trout and three excellent observations on the character of rivers.

"The trout was small," Mrs. Gardiner had said at breakfast, "but your uncle's account of it was very nourishing."

"I have always maintained," Mr. Gardiner replied, "that a fish gains dignity by being difficult to catch."

"And loses it again by being eaten," said Elizabeth.

So they had laughed, and the morning had gone on; and all the while Elizabeth had listened for she knew not what — wheels, footsteps, a voice below, any alteration in the common sounds of the inn.

A little after twelve, the alteration came: a door below, a step upon the stair, Mrs. Staveley’s voice a bit wobblier than usual.

Mrs. Gardiner folded her work, set it aside, and said only, "There." Mr. Gardiner, who had been reading yesterday's newspaper with every appearance of attending to it, set the page on the side table.

Elizabeth stood.

Her gloves were on and straight. The sentence she had been arranging since Sunday — *I am very happy to make your acquaintance, Miss Darcy* — had been practiced so often that it had lost all resemblance to natural speech and now sat in her mind like a visiting-card left by someone she did not know.

A moment later, Mrs. Staveley appeared at the sitting-room door: a neat, capable woman with a cap as white as her apron. Behind her stood Mr. Darcy and a shadow that must be his sister. Elizabeth had time to think of Ramsgate. She had time to rebuke herself for thinking of Ramsgate. She had time to remember that Georgiana Darcy had not chosen to be the subject of anyone's pity.

Mr. Darcy entered first.

This was, Elizabeth thought, a kindness to his sister and a trial to everyone else. He looked as he had not looked on the gravel: no dust on his boots, no road-weariness in his face, nothing disordered but the brief arrest of his eyes when they found hers. Elizabeth felt the room become too small for the breath she had taken. He bowed to Mr. Gardiner first, then to Mrs. Gardiner, then to Elizabeth, with all proper composure; yet the look that met Elizabeth's for the length of a breath was not quite the look of a man paying an ordinary call. It held too much to be ordinary, and too little that could be spoken of.

"Mr. Gardiner. Mrs. Gardiner. Miss Bennet."

Then he turned, and the young lady behind him stepped forward.

Miss Darcy was a willow. Taller than Elizabeth had expected, and formed on a scale that promised, in another year or two, a very handsome woman. At sixteen, she was in that uncertain country between girl and woman where a composed manner may make youth more visible rather than less. Her clear complexion, gentle reserve, and air of being carefully prepared for every possible civility reminded Elizabeth, painfully, of Jane.

Elizabeth liked her at once. Which was inconvenient.

"Mr. Gardiner. Mrs. Gardiner," said Mr. Darcy, and the alteration in his voice was so slight that perhaps no one but Elizabeth, who had nothing to do but notice him, would have heard it. "May I present my sister, Miss Darcy. Georgiana — Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner, of Gracechurch Street; Miss Bennet."

Miss Darcy curtsied. Her eyes glanced from the Gardiners to Elizabeth and away again, then returned with a small, brave effort.

"I am very happy to make your acquaintance," she said.

So. Elizabeth's sentence had escaped and found another mouth.

Mr. Gardiner received the introduction with his usual pleasant ease; Mrs. Gardiner, with all the warmth that could be offered without overwhelming a shy girl. "We are very pleased you have been so good as to come to us," she said. "I hope your journey yesterday did not fatigue you excessively."

"Not me," said Miss Darcy. Then, as if fearing the answer had been too abrupt, she added, "That is — I was not ill from it. Some of our party were less fortunate."

Mr. Darcy's eyes went to his sister, not in correction, but encouragement. Miss Darcy saw it and breathed in a little more deeply.

"We stopped on Sunday at a posting inn where the supper did not agree with everyone. Mr. and Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley have all suffered for it, and the heat of yesterday's road did not improve matters. My companion and I were spared. Mr. Bingley ate everything and is perfectly well, which seems unfair to everyone else."

The last words came out almost under her breath, but there was a hint of mischief in them — a small brightness, quickly offered and almost as quickly recalled.

Mr. Bingley. Elizabeth blinked slowly against the tide of affront toward the name. Jane's sorrow had a face again; and Mr. Darcy stood beside it in her mind, grave, composed, and not to be acquitted. 

Mr. Darcy looked down at Georgiana, the only person in the room who could. "Bingley's constitution has never been governed by ordinary principles."

Miss Darcy's mouth lifted before she could prevent it.

The change was astonishing. Not large; not enough to be called animation. The formal young lady had become, for a second, a sister. Elizabeth saw it and, more dangerously, saw him seeing it. His whole attention had sharpened toward Georgiana, softened around her, and then withdrawn just enough to let her stand by herself.

Not politeness. Not propriety. Feeling, under command.

Elizabeth had not known command could look so much like tenderness.

"Mr. Bingley is at Pemberley, then?" Mrs. Gardiner asked, with no more emphasis than the question required.

"Yes," said Mr. Darcy. "He desired me to offer his compliments."

Elizabeth's fingers curled inside her glove.

Mr. Bingley, within five miles. Mr. Bingley sending compliments, amiable and unconscious, perhaps, while Jane sat at Longbourn with all her sweetness and all her wounds folded neatly out of sight. 

The room seemed, for one instant, to contain not four people but all the winter: Netherfield deserted, London unanswered, Jane's quiet face turned toward the window, Mr. Darcy's letter in Elizabeth's hand declaring, with the severity of a man convinced of his own justice, that he had detached his friend from her sister.

That man was speaking to Mr. Gardiner about her uncle’s morning on the Pemberley water. His manner was easy; his answer exactly judged; his face, if he felt the old accusation pass through the room, gave no sign of it.

No. That was unjust. It was not an accusation passing through the room. It was only passing through Elizabeth.

And had he been unjust? Yes. She would hold to that. Jane's happiness had been treated as a point in another gentleman's calculation; her reserve had been misread, her modesty punished. Nothing in Derbyshire could alter that.

But then Miss Darcy, having been asked by Mrs. Gardiner whether she had often been in Lambton, turned instinctively toward her brother as if the answer belonged to both of them.

"When we were children," Mr. Darcy said, "Georgiana believed Lambton existed chiefly to supply gingerbread and ribbons."

"I was very young," said Miss Darcy, with dignity.

"You were eleven."

"Eleven is young."

"Not when one is demanding blue ribbon with the firmness of a woman of business."

Miss Darcy's blush came with a smile in it. Mrs. Gardiner laughed softly, and Elizabeth, against every principle of self-preservation, nearly laughed too.

He cared for his sister with an attention that had nothing languid in it. Every glance, every small turn of speech, made a place for her to stand. If he loved Mr. Bingley with half so watchful a tenderness — if his friendship had something of this same anxious guardianship in it — then his interference became, not better, but more understandable. A proud man, accustomed to seeing danger before the person he loved saw it; a decisive man, too certain of his own reading; a protective man who mistook silence for indifference because silence, to him, concealed so much.

Not forgiven. Not even softened.

But seen, perhaps, from another side.

Mrs. Gardiner, with the tact that made conversation move as if everyone present had helped arrange it, proposed that if Miss Darcy was not fatigued, they might walk a little. Lambton had changed in some respects and not at all in others; she would like to know which parts Miss Darcy recognized and which parts belonged only to her own memory. Mr. Gardiner declared himself entirely at the disposal of any party that promised shade.

Miss Darcy looked at her brother.

Elizabeth saw the question before he answered it. Not permission exactly. Something older and gentler than permission: *Will this do? Am I right to wish it?*

"If Mrs. Gardiner is not afraid of the heat," he said, "I think the walk would be pleasant."

"I was a Lambton girl," said Mrs. Gardiner. "We were not made of such delicate materials."

Miss Darcy smiled, really smiled, and the matter was settled.

Outside, Lambton received them with a warm curiosity. A woman at the baker's door looked up and curtsied slightly. Mr. Darcy touched his hat. A boy with a hoop drew back against the wall, not in fear but in instant, practiced respect, and stared with open admiration at Miss Darcy's pelisse until his mother, from within the shop, said his name in a tone that restored him to motion. An older man on the opposite side of the street nodded to Mr. Darcy and received a nod in return.

Back in Hertfordshire, Mr. Darcy had been remarked upon because he was rich, proud, handsome, disagreeable, and not given to dancing. In Lambton he was remarked upon hardly at all, and yet. Faces altered as he passed — not startled, not frowning, not greedy for notice. A twitch of a smile here. A respectful lift of the hand there. A shopkeeper's wife inclining her head with something very like pride. They made way for him as one makes way for a person known to belong to the place, not as one makes way for a stranger of consequence.

Miss Darcy walked at first beside Mrs. Gardiner, with Mr. Darcy on her other side. Mr. Gardiner fell in beside Mr. Darcy with some remark on the river, and Elizabeth was left with the agreeable advantage of observing them under cover of lingering to admire the shop windows. The arrangement did not last. Mrs. Gardiner paused twice — once to point out a changed sign, once to identify the house where a former neighbor had lived — and by the second pause, Miss Darcy had turned enough that Elizabeth was included in the conversation almost without anyone deciding it.

"Mrs. Gardiner says there was once a milliner here," Miss Darcy said, indicating a narrow shop now given over to books, prints, and stationery. "But I cannot remember it."

"You are very fortunate," said Elizabeth. "A milliner remembered from childhood can haunt a family for years."

Miss Darcy looked alarmed for half a second, then saw Elizabeth's smile and answered it shyly. 

"Although my aunt may not agree. She has spoken very feelingly of Lambton ribbons."

"They were very fine ribbons," Mrs. Gardiner said. "And very serious matters depended on them."

"Such as?" asked Miss Darcy.

"Whether a girl of twelve should be admired by everyone in church, naturally." Elizabeth included Georgiana in her smile.

Miss Darcy was surprised into a small laugh.

Mr. Darcy looked at Elizabeth then. Only a glance. The others were still smiling over Mrs. Gardiner's ribbons. His attention crossed the little space between them with no word attached. 

Elizabeth looked away first.

They turned from the busier part of the street into a lane that sloped gently toward the churchyard’s far wall. The air changed there. Less stableyard, more grass warmed by sun; less conversation from open doors, more bees in a border of lavender behind a low stone wall. At the end of the lane stood the horse chestnut.

A magnificent tree, its limbs stretched wide, its leaves glossy. Its shadow lay over the path in irregular pieces. The trunk had divided low, as if it had been made expressly to tempt children and then punish them for accepting.

"There," said Mrs. Gardiner, stopping with delight so unguarded that Elizabeth turned to look at her. "Oh, it is so much larger."

Miss Darcy looked from the tree to Mrs. Gardiner. "You know it?"

"Know it? My brother and I nearly lived in it for two summers. Or we thought we did. I daresay we never climbed higher than this." She held her hand scarcely above her shoulder. "At the time, however, we considered ourselves intrepid."

Miss Darcy's eyes brightened. "You climbed it?"

"Repeatedly. And competitively. My brother maintained, without evidence, that he could reach a certain narrow branch before I could. I maintained, with equal justice, that he cheated by beginning before the count was finished."

"Who won?" asked Miss Darcy.

"I did, of course," said Mrs. Gardiner. "It is one of the privileges of memory that it may arrange these matters correctly."

Miss Darcy laughed again, and this time the sound was clear enough that Mr. Darcy's face changed.  His mouth softened. His eyes smiled. The expression was so swift and so unguarded that Elizabeth felt, absurdly, as if she had intruded.

"You did not fall?" Miss Darcy asked.

"Oh, no. The tree was smaller then, and we were sensible children."

Mr. Darcy made a sound that might, in another man, have been called amusement.

Mrs. Gardiner turned to him. "You doubt the general sensibility of Lambton children, Mr. Darcy?"

"I would not presume to question local evidence."

"But you possess contrary evidence?"

"I have one instance."

Miss Darcy looked up at him. "Is this the tree?"

He hesitated.

It was slight. A pause no longer than the shade of a leaf crossing his coat. Yet Elizabeth saw that the story had not been intended for company, or not for this company. Georgiana had asked without calculation. Mrs. Gardiner's memory had opened a door, and Miss Darcy, delighted to find someone else with a childhood in Lambton, had stepped through it.

Mr. Darcy could have closed it. A gentleman with his command of manner could have turned the subject with perfect ease.

He did not.

"This tree," he said. "Unless Lambton has another with the same inclination to punish presumption. Mrs. Reynolds has never forgiven it."

"Mrs. Reynolds has never forgiven *you*," said Miss Darcy, and then blushed at her own boldness.

"Also true."

Mrs. Gardiner's eyes warmed. "Mrs. Reynolds was there?"

"Mrs. Reynolds had brought us into Lambton that morning on some household errand, and arrived after the fact," he said. "Which, from her point of view, was worse. I had been told not to climb it."

"How old were you?" Elizabeth heard herself ask.

His eyes came to hers. "Seven."

"That’s very young," said Miss Darcy, with sisterly loyalty.

"I was old enough to understand the word *no*."

"And young enough," said Mrs. Gardiner, "to consider it an invitation to further thought."

Mr. Darcy inclined his head. "Precisely."

The story, once begun, came with more ease than Elizabeth expected. Not with flourish; Mr. Darcy did not magically become a man of anecdotes. But he told it plainly: the summer morning, the forbidden branch, the conviction — apparently well-founded until the final moment — that the prohibition had underestimated both his abilities and the tree's hospitality. 

Miss Darcy listened with an expression of happy concentration. She must have heard the story before; Elizabeth could tell by the way she waited for certain words. But she loved hearing it from him. Loved, too, perhaps, that he would tell it here, before people not of the family.

"And did you cry?" Mrs. Gardiner asked.

Miss Darcy looked scandalized.

Mr. Darcy looked at Mrs. Gardiner with gravity. "No."

"Ah," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"I roared."

Miss Darcy's hand flew to her mouth. Elizabeth laughed before she could stop herself.

Mrs. Gardiner recovered first. "Then I am relieved. A silent injury is always alarming. A roaring one gives the household useful information."

"That was Mrs. Reynolds's view," said Mr. Darcy. "She informed my father that my lungs were in excellent order, whatever might be said of my judgment."

"And what was said of your judgment?" Elizabeth asked.

His look came to her again, steadier this time. "Very little that was flattering."

It should have been nothing. A commonplace answer. Yet the words landed with more meaning than he had, perhaps, meant to put into them.

Elizabeth turned toward the chestnut leaves.

Miss Darcy, fortunately, had not followed that line of thought. She was looking from Mrs. Gardiner to the tree with renewed interest. "And you truly climbed this one?” she said. "I should like to have tried such a thing.”

There was a small hunger in the sentence, quickly hidden. Not for the climb itself, perhaps, but for the picture of it: a girl racing her brother without masters, without a companion, without the whole great apparatus of improvement and caution that Elizabeth suspected had governed much of Georgiana Darcy's life.

Mrs. Gardiner heard it too. "Then you must imagine me very fleet and my brother very slow. That is how I prefer the story."

Miss Darcy smiled, and the smile stayed.

As they strolled, Miss Darcy asked Mrs. Gardiner whether the churchyard had changed, and whether the lane had always bent so sharply by the wall, and whether Lambton winters had been very severe. She asked Elizabeth if Hertfordshire had many such trees, then looked embarrassed by her own eagerness until Elizabeth said that Hertfordshire possessed trees in abundance, though very few with such a history of moral instruction.

"The fault was not the tree's," said Mr. Darcy.

"No?" said Elizabeth.

"No. The tree performed its office. I misjudged the branch."

There was a pause where there should not have been one. Mrs. Gardiner was speaking to Miss Darcy just ahead of them; Mr. Gardiner attending them genially. The lane was public; a cart had stopped at the turn to let them pass. And yet the sentence seemed to hold between them.

*I misjudged the branch.*

Elizabeth could not answer it.

By the time they turned back toward the inn, the sun had moved just enough to narrow the shadow at their feet. Elizabeth was warm, and more weary than the walk could account for. Miss Darcy's reserve had not vanished; it had only opened, like a door left ajar in a house still mostly closed. Yet through that opening Elizabeth had seen enough to make retreat impossible.

At the inn door, the formal world gathered itself again. Mr. Darcy called for the carriage, but it was already being brought forward.

Mrs. Gardiner thanked Miss Darcy for the pleasure of the call. Mr. Gardiner added his own thanks with a compliment to Lambton shade that made Miss Darcy smile. She began the proper answer, then stopped.

"If you are not otherwise engaged," she said, turning a little toward Mrs. Gardiner and a little, unmistakably, toward Elizabeth, "we should be very happy — I mean, I should be very happy — if you would all dine with us at Pemberley."

Mr. Darcy's head turned.

Not sharply. Nothing in Mr. Darcy's public manner had ever been so ungoverned as sharpness. But the movement was immediate.

"Georgiana," he said, gently.

She looked at him, and the brightness in her face faltered.

Elizabeth saw the falter and resented him for causing it.

He stepped half a pace nearer his sister, enough to include her, not enough to silence her. "I believe we had better not ask Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner to commit themselves to dinner while the house remains in such disorder. Miss Bingley and Mrs. Hurst are not yet recovered, and Hurst is making the best of his confinement with more philosophy than appetite. Tomorrow, perhaps, if Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet are so good as to allow it — tea would be less fatiguing to everyone."

Tea. Not dinner. Not a grand invitation. Not a retreat either.

Miss Darcy's face cleared at once. "Yes. Tea. If you would come. I should like that very much."

There was the girl again, not quite hidden by the heiress.

"We should be very pleased,” Mrs. Gardiner said, her smile warm.

"Very pleased," said Mr. Gardiner. "I shall come prepared to praise the trout again, if required."

Elizabeth said what she must say. "Thank you. I should be happy to come."

Mr. Darcy bowed. 

He had hardly spoken to her all morning. Not directly. Not in any way that could be placed before an aunt and uncle and called particular. Yet as he raised his head, his eyes found hers.

This time she did not look away first.

# Chapter 13: Tea at Pemberley

Wednesday had the polished stillness of a day arranged for display and left too long in the sun.

The road to Pemberley had not altered. That was the trouble. Elizabeth had come to it two days ago with a tourist’s conscience and an aunt’s arm ready at her side, prepared to admire, to be instructed, and to go away. Today she was expected. 

The trees should have had the decency to look different. The stream should have lost some of its assurance. The house, when it rose grand again from its woods and breadth of lawn, should at least have offered some outward proof that Saturday and today were not the same day at all.

It did not.

Stone, water, lawn, gorgeous light: all held their places. Pemberley did not explain itself for her comfort. It left Elizabeth to feel the alteration alone.

Mr. Gardiner, who had spent half the drive maintaining that tea was an admirable institution because it permitted a man to be sociable while admitting he required cake, leaned toward the carriage window as they rounded the final turn.

“Well,” he said, with quiet satisfaction, “I find I admire a house still more when I have fished its water.”

“My dear,” said Mrs. Gardiner, “you admired it Saturday.”

“Saturday, I admired it as architecture. Today I admire it as a patron of trout.”

Elizabeth smiled because her uncle deserved it. His good humor had carried them across several awkward roads before this one. Her hands lay folded in her lap. Her gloves were clean. Her gown was exactly what it ought to be: a morning muslin of no pretension, decent for tea, suitable to a call, and chosen by Mrs. Gardiner with the silent care of a general selecting ground.

Whether it became her had not been discussed. The gown’s duty was not to make her pretty, but to make her correct.

At the door, everything proceeded before discomfort could find a place to stand. A servant appeared as the carriage stopped. Another received Mr. Gardiner’s card. A footman in livery held the grand front door open. The hall was cool, and scented with beeswax, as before, but now late roses, lavender, and clipped green sprigs adorned two narrow side tables and the round oak table in the center. Their scents .

Elizabeth had crossed that hall as a tourist. Mrs. Reynolds had arranged the past around her, and Elizabeth had been permitted to admire from afar. Now she must enter as an active guest, answer greetings, accept tea, and try to discover what sort of countenance a woman ought to wear when visiting the house of a man whose proposal she had refused.

They were shown not into the great drawing-room as she had half feared, but into a smaller drawing-room on the garden side of the house. Smaller by Pemberley measures, that is, which meant that it would have held the Bennets’ best parlor, the Lucas Lodge drawing-room, and perhaps Mr. Collins’s entire sense of consequence without inconvenience. Pale green silk covered the walls above white lower paneling. The curtains, soft cream figured with leaves, were drawn back from high windows. Sun crossed the carpet in broken pieces, gold and green, as the branches moved outside.

Carved chairs polished by generations of hands stood near the windows with a low striped sofa among them, all turned toward the tea table rather than the fire. It was not a low table for books or elbows, but a polished Pembroke table drawn into service, high enough for cups, plates, and Miss Darcy’s careful hands. Roses in a bowl gave off the rich, bruised sweetness of late summer. The tea things were already laid on it: teapot, hot-water urn, cups, sugar, cream, plates, and silver tongs, all arranged as if no hand had ever hesitated over them.

Simple tea, then.

Simple tea at Pemberley, it appeared, required old porcelain, thin bread and butter, preserved apricots in a shell-shaped dish, three kinds of cake, and plates so fine Elizabeth expected them to ring if anyone looked at them too sharply.

Miss Darcy stood beside it.

She was dressed exactly as the occasion required. Hair pinned casually up. A pale gown, beautifully made, with no more ornament than a ribbon at the waist and a narrow edging that rewarded attention without begging for it. She was at home, and therefore more exposed than she had been in Lambton. In the street she had possessed the advantage of motion. Here, in her brother’s house, before a table arranged for hospitality and a company waiting to see how she would use it, she looked very young indeed.

Mr. Darcy stood a little behind her and to one side, letting her lead but there a moment if she needed support. His dark coat, faultless linen, and grave propriety matched the room — exact, costly, restrained, and so well judged that the clothes disappeared into the man.

Miss Caroline Bingley, by contrast, had chosen to be noticed.

She rose from one of the chairs near the tea table with admirable grace, one hand resting on its arm as though the exertion had social consequence. She was pale — truly pale, not fashionably so — and there was a tightness about her mouth that no art could turn into delicacy. Yet she had dressed for contest. Her silk was too rich for the hour, her lace too elaborate for a quiet country tea, the chain at her throat too bright against a complexion not yet recovered from illness. She looked like a woman who had been unwell and had determined to make recovery as expensive as possible.

Miss Darcy came forward first. Her curtsey was exact; her voice, low. Yet when her eyes met Elizabeth’s, a brightness came into them that had not been there when the door opened.

“I am very glad you could come,” she said.

“So am I,” Elizabeth replied, curtseying, and found that it was not only politeness.

Mrs. Gardiner answered with all her easy warmth. Mr. Gardiner bowed, and Miss Darcy’s hands clasped once before her, then released, with the effort of receiving them all properly.

Only then did Miss Bingley advance.

“Miss Elizabeth Bennet,” she said, with a smile that had to travel some distance before reaching civility. “What a very unexpected pleasure.”

There it was: Meryton, Netherfield, Jane’s unanswered hopes, all folded into eight words and a bare curtsey.

Elizabeth returned the civility. “Miss Bingley. I hope your journey has not fatigued you.”

“Quite recovered, I thank you. Or nearly. A little fatigue after the road is nothing. We were all so…  concerned when we heard a Miss Bennet was in the neighborhood.”

A Miss Bennet.

In an instant Elizabeth saw the calculation Miss Bingley must have made. Jane, somehow come into Derbyshire. Jane within reach of Mr. Bingley. Jane’s quiet beauty appearing at Pemberley like a ghost invited to tea. Then the calculation corrected itself. Merely Elizabeth: less alarming, perhaps, but not more welcome. 

There are disappointments, Elizabeth supposed, which even a sickbed cannot make restful.

“Your concern does you credit,” said Elizabeth.

Miss Bingley’s smile thinned.

“Miss Elizabeth,” cried Mr. Bingley, coming forward from somewhere near the windows with all the warmth his sister lacked. His coat was as well cut as Mr. Darcy’s, but worn with none of Mr. Darcy’s gravity; even his cravat seemed to have been tied in better spirits if not perfect style. “This is indeed a pleasure. I had no notion — Darcy told us only yesterday — that you were in Derbyshire. Mrs. Gardiner, Mr. Gardiner, yes? How do you do? I hope you are all well. Your family is well? Miss Bennet is — that is, your sisters are all well?”

He had colored before he finished.

Elizabeth saw it. Miss Bingley saw it. Mr. Darcy, who was bowing to Mrs. Gardiner, paused no longer than a man might pause because another person had spoken too quickly; but Elizabeth saw that too.

Jane had not been invisible. Not to him.

“Very well, thank you,” Elizabeth said, and because she was not cruel, or not cruel in that way, she added, “My eldest sister was quite well when I last heard from Longbourn.”

Mr. Bingley’s color rose higher; his smile came and was checked almost in the same breath. “I am very glad. Very glad indeed.”

“Charles,” said Miss Bingley, “you are keeping Miss Darcy from her table.”

He moved at once, with an apology so ready and good-humored that it might have disarmed anyone whose heart had not been sitting for months beside Jane’s sorrow. Elizabeth looked down at the carpet. Sunlight caught on her shoes.

With only a little shifting of chairs, and only one pause in which no one seemed entirely certain who ought to sit first, they were arranged at last. Mr. Gardiner was given a chair near Mr. Darcy. Mrs. Gardiner placed herself where she could speak to Miss Darcy without seeming to examine her. Elizabeth found herself on the striped sofa nearest the windows, the damask cool through the thin fabric of her gown and ridged beneath her gloved fingertips. Miss Bingley settled nearest the tea table before anyone had asked her to do so.

Miss Darcy looked at the cups. Then at the urn. Then, once, at her brother.

Mr. Darcy did not move. He said, in a tone belonging to the room at large, “Mrs. Reynolds tells me the apricot cakes are excellent today.”

Miss Darcy’s eyes went at once to the dish, with the look of a girl who had been offered the very subject she required. “Yes. Mrs. Annesley thought — I mean, I hope you will try them. They are made with fruit from the south wall.”

“Then we are obliged to Pemberley’s gardens for a second time,” said Mrs. Gardiner. “The basket you sent us yesterday made a luncheon that Mr. Gardiner has spoken of ever since.”

“I spoke of the trout also,” said Mr. Gardiner. “I am a man of balanced gratitude.”

Miss Darcy smiled and reached for the teapot.

“Allow me, dear Georgiana,” said Miss Bingley, leaning forward with languid kindness. “The larger cups are for stronger tea; Mrs. Reynolds always prefers it so when there is company. You are too careful of everyone’s comfort to trouble yourself with every little household habit at once.”

Miss Darcy’s hand paused, two fingers still on the handle of the teapot.

The sentence sounded like assistance and acted like command. It praised Georgiana’s sweetness, corrected her hand, claimed knowledge of Pemberley, and placed Miss Bingley, with one movement of the wrist, between the mistress of the house and her own table.

Mrs. Gardiner did not move beside Elizabeth; she merely became, in some manner impossible to accuse, more attentive.

Mr. Darcy crossed to the table. Not quickly. Not as if anything had happened. He took up the plate of apricot cakes and offered it first to Mrs. Gardiner.

“Mrs. Gardiner, you must tell my sister whether the south wall has done credit to Lambton.”

The plate passed from him to Mrs. Gardiner, and the room altered by an inch. Miss Bingley was no longer advising the hostess. Miss Darcy had her gardens returned to her. Mrs. Gardiner, with an apricot cake in her hand, had been made as necessary to Pemberley’s judgment as any lady in the room.

Elizabeth had never known a plate of cakes could be so well employed.

Mrs. Gardiner accepted one. “I shall consider myself appointed judge.”

Miss Darcy poured. Her hand was steady. The tea fell amber-dark into the cup. Steam rose with the scent of China tea, hot porcelain, sugar, and butter from the cakes. When Elizabeth received her cup, the porcelain was thin through her glove and warm enough to make her adjust her hold at once. Tiny blue flowers circled the saucer, each petal painted with such patience that it seemed unreasonable for any household to possess them in dozens.

Mr. Darcy came to her next with the plate.

Elizabeth chose one of the smallest cakes. Mr. Darcy held the plate perfectly still; she was the one who took too long over the choice. It was crisp at the edge, soft within, and tasted of almond, butter, and the tart sweetness of apricot. Longbourn had cake. Gracechurch Street had excellent cake. Pemberley had cake that appeared to have been born knowing the difference between perfect plenty and excess.

Miss Bingley observed the plate as if it had betrayed her.

“Miss Darcy’s taste is always elegant,” she said. “Even in these smaller matters. I often tell her that when she is mistress here in earnest, she will make Pemberley all that it ought to be.”

Miss Darcy blushed. Not with pleasure. Elizabeth could not like the phrase *in earnest*. A girl might be young, shy, and unsure of her teapot, and still be mistress of her brother’s house already.

“Surely,” said Elizabeth, before she had quite decided to speak, “Pemberley seems already to know what it ought to be.”

Miss Bingley’s gaze flicked to her.

Mr. Darcy’s did as well.

Elizabeth took a sip of tea. It was too hot and very good.

“I mean,” she added, with all possible mildness, “that a house with so much history must guide its inhabitants as much as be guided by them.”

“How prettily said. One might almost think you had been studying great houses, Miss Eliza.”

The old name. The old tone. Netherfield’s drawing-room opened in Elizabeth’s mind. Miss Bingley with her book unread, Miss Bingley walking around the room to secure attention, Miss Bingley asking Mr. Darcy to admire her figure and receiving less than she had arranged for. Elizabeth had once found it absurd. In Pemberley, it was sharper. 

Miss Bingley was measuring territory; not because Elizabeth had conquered any of it, but because Miss Bingley did not know how to enter a room without drawing boundaries.

“I have had excellent instruction,” Elizabeth said.

“Mrs. Reynolds?” said Mr. Bingley, brightly; then, perhaps realizing that he had entered a conversation whose floor was less secure than it looked, he added, “A capital woman, Mrs. Reynolds.”

“She is,” said Mr. Darcy.

Two words. Nothing more. Miss Bingley’s fan shifted once against her wrist; Miss Darcy’s color eased. Elizabeth had not expected to be pleased by Mr. Darcy’s loyalty to his housekeeper. Worse, she could not help being pleased.

Mr. Gardiner, with that practical kindness which made him hard to defeat in company, asked Mr. Bingley whether he had ever fished the Pemberley water. Mr. Bingley turned at once, grateful for any subject with fresh air in it.

“No, never. Darcy has offered it often enough, but I am a disgrace to every sport I attempt. I am too tender-hearted with fish; they perceive my weakness and take advantage of it.”

“Then they cannot complain of your intentions,” said Mr. Gardiner.

“My intentions are always excellent. My execution is generally in need of supervision.”

Mr. Darcy’s mouth almost moved. “So I have observed.”

Mr. Bingley laughed, and the room followed. He had that gift of making agreement easy. Elizabeth remembered Jane’s face when he first asked her to dance. Jane’s unguarded happiness in the carriage after Netherfield. Jane’s brave composure when the letters stopped. The last bit of her cake lost its sweetness.

Mr. Bingley turned to her. “And your family truly remains well? Miss Bennet has not — that is, she did not suffer from the heat this summer? I remember she was once unwell after a ride in bad weather.”

Miss Bingley set down her cup with a sound too soft to be called a correction and too pointed to be an accident.

Elizabeth found herself studying Mr. Bingley more closely than civility required. He was thinner. Not dramatically; no romance-novel wasting, no visible ruin. Only a leanness at the cheek, a little less boyish fullness about the face, as if the months since Netherfield had taken an unexpected toll. His eyes were still kind, and fixed on her with an eagerness too open for perfect civility.

“Jane is very well,” Elizabeth said. “She is not often ill, though she is very often patient.”

The sentence had more edge than she intended. Mr. Bingley did not seem to feel it. Mr. Darcy’s fingers tightened once about his cup; he looked down and did not drink.

Miss Bingley did too. “Miss Bennet was always sweetness itself. So placid. One could never know what she felt, which is a great advantage in society.”

Elizabeth’s last crumb of cake grew dry upon her tongue.

A great advantage. Jane’s reserve, made ornament. Jane’s pain, made proof that none existed.

Before Elizabeth could trust herself to reply, Miss Darcy spoke.

“I think,” she said, her voice small but clear, “that quiet manners do not always mean a quiet heart.”

The room changed.

Not by any movement large enough to be named. Miss Darcy’s eyes dropped to the cups; Miss Bingley stared at her with the astonishment of a person struck by a cushion and uncertain whether to complain.

Mr. Darcy did not smile. He did not praise her. He merely inclined his head towards his sister, once, as if she had said no more and no less than the truth. Miss Darcy’s fingers tightened around the handle of the milk jug; then she set it down without spilling a drop.

“No,” said Mrs. Gardiner, gently. “It is not.”

Elizabeth looked at Miss Darcy, and a warmth rose so sharply beneath her ribs that she had to look away.

“Miss Bennet is very kind,” Mr. Bingley said.

The words were simple enough to be safe, except that they were not safe at all.

“Indeed,” said Miss Bingley, rallying. “All the Bennet ladies are known for lively qualities of one kind or another.”

Not quite an insult. A little veil dropped over the word *lively*, a little pressure placed where Lydia, Kitty, Meryton, and every remembered mortification might be expected to stir.

Mr. Darcy turned to Mr. Gardiner.

“Did you find the lower bend difficult yesterday? Greaves tells me the bank has altered since the spring rain.”

Mr. Gardiner accepted the change of subject with the mildness of a man who had never in his life objected to being useful. “I did, yes. The current turns more sharply than it appears from above. I lost a fly to it, which I mention only because I had been fond of the fly.”

“Tragic,” said Mr. Bingley. “One grows attached to a good fly.”
