“You would not,” said Mr. Darcy. “You would attach it to your sleeve and apologize to it.”

Miss Darcy laughed.

Not the polite small laugh from earlier. A real one, startled out of her by the picture. Mrs. Gardiner smiled into her tea. Even Miss Bingley permitted herself a thin laugh, perhaps because refusal would have been the greater discourtesy.

Elizabeth watched as Mr. Darcy make room for everyone.

He did not command the conversation. He did not perform in it. He gave each person, by turns, something safe to hold. He knew when to turn a sharp room toward fishing, when to let a shy girl’s courage pass without display, when to make hospitality look like accident, and when to say almost nothing at all.

Nothing to Elizabeth that anyone could name.

Enough to Elizabeth that she could name nothing else.

At Netherfield, she had thought his attention proof of disdain.

Here, she began to understand that attention might be service as well as judgment.

As the tea went on, everyone except Miss Bingley took another cake. Miss Darcy asked Mrs. Gardiner whether she had walked often by the church when she was young. Mrs. Gardiner answered with a story of a winter so cold the pump in the yard froze and the boys of the town slid down the lane until the curate complained. Mr. Bingley asked whether Hertfordshire lanes were as good for sliding, and Elizabeth said she could not recommend them for dignity. Miss Bingley remarked that country amusements were wholesome when kept in their proper season. Miss Darcy’s mouth curved over her cup.

By degrees, the room lost its first stiffness.

Or rather, Elizabeth lost her fear that stiffness was all there was.

The chair beneath Miss Bingley’s hand creaked whenever she shifted. Silver tongs clicked against sugar. A bee worried at the window before finding its way out through a raised sash. Somewhere beyond the open windows, a gardener’s shears made a clean little rhythm: snip, pause, snip. 

Pemberley, which had seemed in the hall to wait in judgment, began to sound like a house where ordinary afternoons could happen: cups set down, laughter not quite suppressed, a young lady growing braver by inches.

Miss Darcy had poured the cups without mishap. She had offered the second round of cakes. She had answered Mr. Bingley’s cheerful sallies, Mrs. Gardiner’s kindness, and one of Elizabeth’s quieter questions about music. She was still constrained, still watchful; but she had ceased to look as if the table might accuse her.

Miss Bingley saw it too.

“You must play for us next time, Miss Darcy,” she said. “Your visitors should know how accomplished you are. I always say your performance improves whenever you can be persuaded to overcome your modesty.”

Miss Darcy’s back straightened by a quarter-inch, and the pleasant curve of her mouth vanished.

Elizabeth felt Mr. Darcy’s attention move.

“Miss Darcy spoke of music so prettily at Lambton,” said Mrs. Gardiner, “that I was quite persuaded she must play well.”

Miss Darcy looked at her, startled and grateful.

“I do play,” she said. “But not always well before company.”

“Then you are like every musician of sense,” said Elizabeth. “The instrument behaves worse when watched.”

“Does it?” Miss Darcy asked, leaning a little forward despite herself.

“Certainly. At Longbourn our pianoforte has been known to develop opinions merely because a guest entered the room.”

Mr. Bingley laughed. “I should like to hear such an instrument defend itself.”

He had said it with open pleasure. Only after saying it did it seem he remembered what else Longbourn contained. His smile remained, but his eyes had gone searching again, as if one cheerful sentence might yet bring Jane into the room.

Miss Bingley’s fan flicked open.

It was not hot enough for a fan. It was, however, an excellent object with which to be composed.

Mr. Darcy crossed to the window and lowered the sash by an inch. “The sun is coming round.”

It was addressed to no one. It served everyone. The beam that had been striking Miss Darcy’s hands moved off to the floor.

“Thank you,” said Miss Darcy.

He inclined his head and remained by the window.

Seeing him there, Elizabeth understood with uncomfortable clarity why he had seemed so wrong at Meryton. He had been a great house’s master in rooms that made no place for him. A man trained to breadth, duty, privacy, and consequence, compressed into assemblies and card tables and the easy judgments of a neighborhood that wanted him agreeable on command. 

None of that excused him. It did not excuse insult. It did not excuse Jane. But here, with light behind him and the room arranged around obligations he knew how to meet, he fit.

The knowledge would not sit comfortably.

It was also impossible to unknow.

When the visit had stretched to something near three quarters of an hour, Mrs. Gardiner rose. Miss Darcy rose too quickly, checked herself, and smiled at her own haste to do the proper thing. Elizabeth caught the smile.

“We have trespassed on you long enough,” said Mrs. Gardiner.

“No,” said Miss Darcy, and colored. “I mean — not at all. I am very glad you came.”

Miss Bingley’s smile returned at once. “Miss Darcy is always so affectionate in her attachments. It is one of her sweetest qualities.”

Her fan tapped once against her palm: a small sound, neat as a pin set into silk.

Miss Darcy’s shoulders drew in by the smallest degree.

Elizabeth set down her saucer. The porcelain made no sound.

“We are very glad we came too,” she said. “I had not known Pemberley could be so pleasant indoors.”

Mr. Darcy’s eyes came to hers; for a moment he seemed uncertain whether she had offered praise, reproof, or some uneasy mixture of both. Miss Darcy looked delighted. Mrs. Gardiner’s eyes flickered with private amusement.

Miss Bingley blinked. “You surprise me, Miss Eliza. I should have thought you found the house very pleasant on your first visit.”

“I admired it then,” said Elizabeth. “That is not always the same thing.”

No one spoke for half a second.

Then Mr. Bingley, blessed Mr. Bingley, said with feeling, “Very true. I have admired many things I did not particularly wish to sit on.”

Mr. Gardiner laughed. Mrs. Gardiner smiled. Miss Darcy’s laugh escaped before she could consult anyone about it. Even Mr. Darcy’s lips tilted up.

Miss Bingley shut her fan.

At the door, Miss Darcy came forward before anyone could prompt her.

“If you are not engaged tomorrow,” she said, looking first to Mrs. Gardiner, then to Elizabeth, “perhaps you might come again. Not for tea, if that is too much trouble. Only — there is a walk I think Mrs. Gardiner might enjoy, and Miss Bennet said yesterday that Hertfordshire trees have less moral instruction than ours.”

Mr. Darcy waited until Georgiana had finished, then made the invitation proper.

“If it would be agreeable,” he said, “and if the heat is not too great, we would be honored to show you the lower walk tomorrow afternoon. It is shaded nearly all the way.”

The lower walk. The invitation collected meanings as it stood there: shade, memory, Georgiana’s courage, Mr. Darcy’s voice making room for all of them.

Mrs. Gardiner accepted with the air of a woman who had seen a young girl’s courage hold for nearly an hour and would sooner have disappointed a bishop than refuse it. And, Elizabeth knew from the past week, her aunt was not easily daunted by a walk.

Miss Bingley, still seated, looked down at her closed fan, opened it halfway, and shut it again.

Elizabeth curtsied. “I should like that very much.”

It was true.

That was the trouble.

As they descended the steps and entered the carriage that waited in the bright afternoon, Elizabeth looked back once. Miss Darcy stood at the door, pale gown in the shadow, smiling as if the afternoon had given her more than she had dared ask. Mr. Darcy stood a little behind her, exactly where he had stood all afternoon: near enough to steady, far enough to let her be seen.

Miss Bingley was not visible from the door.

Mr. Bingley was. He stood beside Mr. Darcy now, and when Elizabeth turned, he bowed with all his old warmth and a little less of his old ease.

“Pray remember me to your family,” he said.

Then, after one small pause: “To Miss Bennet especially, if you please.”

Jane, then, had not been left wholly behind in Hertfordshire.

Elizabeth inclined her head. “I shall.”

Mr. Darcy’s hand shifted on the doorframe, then stilled.

She entered the carriage with tea and roses still clinging to her gloves, the taste of apricot lingering where sweetness had gone sharp on her tongue. Pemberley was behind her, but it would not return to being only stone and water and excellent prospects.

Tomorrow, she would come back.

# Chapter 14: The Lower Walk

By Thursday morning, Elizabeth had learned that a second invitation to Pemberley could trouble a person more than the first.

A tempered sun lay over Lambton. A little wind moved through the inn yard. The hills, lush with green, looked ready to reward any party sensible enough to be pleased with them.

Over another fine breakfast at the inn, Mrs. Gardiner read the latest letter from Jane. “The children are well, Mrs. Bennet nervous, and Lydia injured beyond all previous female suffering by the continued absence of Brighton."

“Then all is as we left it," said Mr. Gardiner.

“Not quite," said Mrs. Gardiner. “Mary writes a postscript."

Elizabeth looked up. “Mary?"

“On the subject of self-command."

“That is new in subject only," said Mr. Gardiner. “Mary has always been very ready to command herself in public, and others in private."

Mrs. Gardiner smiled and folded the sheet. “She praises Kitty, praise be. Kitty, it seems, has been practicing."

Elizabeth’s spoon stilled in her tea. “Practicing?"

“The pianoforte. Jane says she plays twenty minutes every morning, and Lydia declares no punishment in the family has ever been so severe."

Mr. Gardiner laughed. “A noble beginning. Most improvements are first recognized by the suffering of others."

Elizabeth smiled, but the image remained with her. Kitty at the instrument, awkward perhaps, resentful perhaps, but staying. Kitty, who had once drifted through every room in Lydia’s wake, now sitting for twenty whole minutes where Jane had persuaded her to sit, making some sound that was not merely echo. Perhaps she was even displacing Mary from the piano stool. The image came with them into the carriage and took a seat next to Elizabeth.

At Pemberley, the house, cool even in the sultry heat, did not trouble itself to be new.

Stone, lawn, and water held their places. Elizabeth had no time to resent them for it, for Miss Darcy was already in the morning room with her brother and came forward at once.

Her soft blue muslin gown was plain enough for walking, fine enough to put Jane’s second-best to shame; her gloves were in one hand, as if she had come a little before she was quite ready and would not retreat to correct herself now.

“I am very glad you could come," she said, and then looked at Elizabeth with a quickness that warmed and discomposed them both.

“So are we," Mrs. Gardiner replied. “You promised us shade, Miss Darcy. In such weather, that is no small recommendation."

“The lower walk is shaded nearly all the way," Miss Darcy said. “And there is a place where the water is cooler. I mean — it sounds cooler. I do not know whether water can sound cool."

“It can in August," said Mrs. Gardiner. “I have heard it do so myself."

Miss Darcy smiled before she could prepare for it.

Mr. Darcy bowed to the Gardiners, then to Elizabeth. Elizabeth met his eyes while she was still smiling. His expression faltered a moment, and then settled back into propriety. 

The small morning room, on the garden side, was pale-paneled and bright with reflected green from the lawn. The windows stood open, and the air carried the smell of warm boxwood and cut grass.

The room might have been all summer ease, had Miss Bingley not been deeper within it, yet seated where no one could fail to discover her. A small book lay open and unread in her hand; a shawl, too light to be necessary, rested about her shoulders. She looked better than the day before, which was to say less ill and more dangerous.

“My dear Georgiana," she said, rising with languid care, “you are very good to exert yourself for the lower walk. I am sure Miss Bennet and Mrs. Gardiner will forgive me if I do not attempt Derbyshire heroics today."

Miss Darcy’s fingers tightened around her gloves. “It is not so difficult a walk," she said, soft. “And it is shaded."

“How fortunate," said Miss Bingley. “Shade is always a consolation to those who are determined upon exertion."

The words were small. Their effect was not. They made a pleasure sound like a defect of judgment.

Mrs. Gardiner’s answer came before Elizabeth’s indignation could arrange itself into speech. “Then we shall be doubly obliged to Miss Darcy for choosing so well. I have always thought the best walks are those that make one forget they are exercise."

Miss Darcy’s eyes lifted gratefully.

Mr. Darcy nodded toward his sister. “If you are ready, Georgiana."

Miss Darcy led the way back into the hall, where gloves and hats were donned. Miss Bingley, having made the walk rustic and herself elegant in one economy of effort, remained within. Elizabeth discovered, as they crossed the hall, that the prospect of the lower walk had improved.

They set out by a side door that opened toward the rear garden.

For the first few minutes the sun lay full on the gravel, bright enough to make the pale house behind them seem whiter when Elizabeth glanced back. Warmed stone scented the air; bees worked somewhere among the borders with a noisy industry that made conversation feel less necessary. 

Mrs. Gardiner lifted her face to the breeze. Mr. Gardiner seemed to be already listening for water. Miss Darcy, who had been careful in the hall, seemed to take easier possession of herself with every step away from it. When the path slipped beneath the beeches, the shade closed over them cool and green, and Elizabeth felt the whole party breathe easier.

Mr. Gardiner, with the instinct of a man who knew that rivers and gentlemen improved on acquaintance, asked almost immediately whether the lower bend he had fished yesterday belonged to the same stream they could just hear through the trees.

“It does," said Mr. Darcy. “The path divides a little way ahead. If you would like to see the turn from above—"

Mr. Gardiner looked a question at his wife.

“My dear," said Mrs. Gardiner, “do not pretend reluctance for our sake." She smiled at Mr. Darcy. “We shall keep Miss Darcy with us, if she will permit it."

Miss Darcy looked from her brother to Mrs. Gardiner, and then to Elizabeth.

“I should like that," she said.

Mr. Darcy’s gaze rested on her for one brief instant. “Then we shall meet you by the lower bridge."

It was correctly said, and nothing more than the arrangement required. Yet Elizabeth felt the cost in it, as one feels a current beneath a smooth piece of water. He would have preferred, perhaps, to remain near enough to interpret every silence and prevent every embarrassment. Instead he bowed, turned with Mr. Gardiner toward the side path, and left his sister to them.

Miss Darcy watched him until the turn of the path took him from view. Then she faced the lower walk with an expression Elizabeth had not yet seen from her: not relief exactly, but pleasure.

Mrs. Gardiner began to speak of the trees. Her kindness took the form of interest in beech roots, path stones, a bird’s nest, the turn of a hill. By the time they had gone twenty paces, she had asked Miss Darcy whether that copper beech had always leaned so, whether the path flooded in winter, and whether the lower walk had been much frequented when Miss Darcy was a child.

“Oh no," Miss Darcy said, and then corrected herself. “That is — I came here sometimes. With my brother, or Mrs. Reynolds. But not often alone."

“Were you forbidden?" Elizabeth asked lightly. The question had risen before discretion could smooth it.

Miss Darcy considered this with great seriousness. “Not forbidden. Only there was generally some reason not to go. Lessons, or weather, or someone thinking another place more suitable."

“Suitable," said Elizabeth, “is a powerful word. It can close more doors than a key."

Miss Darcy looked startled, then smiled in understanding. “My brother is never unkind. But he is sometimes careful before I have known I wished for anything."

The words came softly. The loyalty in them was plain; so was the care with which she had permitted herself to say even that much.

Mrs. Gardiner did not answer too quickly. “Those who love us are often tempted to do our fearing for us."

“Yes," said Miss Darcy. “And my cousin too."

“Colonel Fitzwilliam?" Elizabeth asked.

Miss Darcy nodded. “He laughs at my brother, which is a comfort. But then he generally decides my brother is right."

Mrs. Gardiner laughed gently. Elizabeth could not help smiling.

Miss Darcy seemed surprised at having said so much. Her eyes went at once to the path, and her fingers moved over the seam of one glove.

“I do not mean that I am not grateful."

“No," said Mrs. Gardiner, with a warmth that made denial unnecessary. “Of course you do not."

Under the beeches, the path curved downward. Gravel gave way to packed earth, dry at the center and soft with moss at the edges. Ferns grew in the cooler hollows. The air changed as they descended: less garden sweetness, more damp stone and leaf shade. Somewhere below, water moved over rock, not loudly, but with a steady murmur that seemed to gather the heat from the day and carry it away.

Miss Darcy’s step altered. She began to look ahead rather than at her own feet.

“There is a place," she said, and stopped. She flushed faintly. “It is nothing. Only stones by the water. When the stream is low, one can cross there. Or almost cross. My brother would say it is not a crossing if one must still turn back at the last stone."

“Brothers," said Mrs. Gardiner, a world of annoyance and love in one word.

Miss Darcy’s smile returned. “This way."

She led them from the main path down a smaller one, made perhaps for gardeners or children and tolerated by the estate as a harmless irregularity. A hazel branch leaned across it; Miss Darcy lifted it with an unconscious familiarity. The stream appeared at once — narrow here, bright over shallow stones, with a ferny bank on one side and, on the other, three flat stones set crookedly from the shore.

The place was very small.

That was its charm.

No prospect opened from it. No guest would be brought there to admire Pemberley’s consequence. The house was unseen; the lawn forgotten. The grand water above and below had reduced itself here to a child’s problem and a girl’s secret.

Miss Darcy looked at it as if she feared it might not be worth the showing.

Mrs. Gardiner stepped closer. “Oh, this is delightful."

“Do you think so?"

“Absolutely. When I was young, we had a place nearer town where the water crossed the lane after rain. We considered it a matter of honor to pass without wetting our shoes, which we never did. Or at least, we never told our mother we did."

Miss Darcy’s laugh came lightly, and this time did not disappear at once.

Elizabeth crouched to look at the stones. Spray had darkened the middle of the nearest one; the far edge was green where moss had taken advantage of neglect.

“Is this the famous crossing?" Elizabeth asked.

“Only in a very generous sense," Miss Darcy said. “The third stone is too far unless the water is low."

“Then it is a crossing with principles. It refuses to be attempted at the wrong season."

Miss Darcy considered this, then laughed. “My brother would approve of that description."

Mrs. Gardiner looked from one young woman to the other and wisely interested herself in a fern.

Elizabeth rose, brushing a speck of moss from her glove. “Longbourn has very few tests of this kind. Our dangers are more often chairs left where they ought not be, or sisters practicing scales with more resolution than mercy."

“Your sisters play?"

“Some of them do. Some attempt it. Some consider music an excellent thing in other people."

Miss Darcy smiled. “And you?"

“I perform only when affection, duty, or a shortage of alternatives requires it."

“That sounds very sensible."

“It is cowardice with a better name."

Miss Darcy laughed again — not loudly, but with a quickness that seemed to surprise her. She looked almost at once toward the path by which Mr. Darcy had gone, then checked herself, as if even laughter required permission when it arrived unexpectedly.

Elizabeth saw it.

She did not like how much she minded it.

Miss Darcy was loved. That was beyond question. Protected, instructed, cherished, considered. Two guardians, a companion, masters, servants, a brother whose attention gathered around her at the first sign of unease. Yet here she was, showing two almost-friends a stone by a stream as if it were a pleasure that had to be smuggled out.

No. Not almost-friends. Not yet.

But not strangers.

Mrs. Gardiner had the enviable art of becoming familiar without presuming on it. Elizabeth had been admitted, somehow, not to Georgiana’s confidence, but to the threshold of it; and she found, with a discomfort that was not all discomfort, that she wished to be allowed further in.

Miss Darcy stepped onto the first stone.

“Take care," Elizabeth said, and regretted it at once.

Miss Darcy looked back, not offended, but rueful. “You see? Everyone says that."

Mrs. Gardiner’s eyes met Elizabeth’s with the smallest glint of humor.

Elizabeth made her voice solemn. “Then I withdraw it. I meant to say: advance with all the recklessness proper to a heroine of the lower walk."

Miss Darcy’s lips parted in astonishment and pleasure. She stepped to the second stone.

“That is the easy one," she said. “It is so wide."

“I am relieved to find even heroines are allowed easy stones."

Miss Darcy set the toe of one half-boot carefully on the easy stone. “The third is only possible if the water is lower."

“Then the third is not failure. It is deferred glory."

Miss Darcy laughed, and this time the sound carried above the stream.

The pleasure of it struck Elizabeth. It was not that Miss Darcy became another creature. She did not suddenly chatter, or grow bold, or throw off the years of careful teaching like a shawl too warm for the weather. She was still shy. Still careful. Still glancing back at propriety as if it might have followed her down the path.

But something had opened.

Mrs. Gardiner asked whether the stream froze in winter. As she stepped back safely to shore, Miss Darcy answered that it did, or partly did. Mrs. Reynolds disliked anyone coming down when the bank was hard. Once Colonel Fitzwilliam had said he would teach her to slide and then been reproved by her brother before he had advanced three steps in the plan.

“Did he obey?" asked Elizabeth.

“Colonel Fitzwilliam? No. But he changed the subject so amusingly that my brother was obliged to stop being displeased before he had finished."

“A useful talent."

“Yes," Miss Darcy said. “I have often wished to learn it."

The simplicity of the admission silenced Elizabeth.

She pictured Longbourn. Lydia’s unchecked noise, Kitty’s echoing giggles, Mary’s solemn interruptions, Jane’s gentleness, herself saying too much because she could not bear to say nothing. There was no want of foolishness there. No shortage of contradiction. No careful male authority had ever stood between a Bennet daughter and a ridiculous opinion. Elizabeth had often wished for more restraint in her family. She had not considered that restraint, in abundance, might become another kind of poverty.

A girl such as Miss Darcy did not need another lesson. She needed someone who could laugh first and explain afterward.

And with that thought, absurdly and unmistakably, came Kitty.

Kitty, who was still too easily led, and yet not only led. Kitty, who had begun, however grudgingly, to practice when Jane asked it. Kitty, who would have stepped onto the first stone, squealed at the second, declared the third impossible, attempted it anyway, and then described the whole adventure at dinner with more drama than accuracy.

Kitty would know what to do with a place like this.

The thought was so unexpected that Elizabeth nearly smiled at the stream.

They returned slowly to the main path. Miss Darcy did not talk all the way; that would have been too much to ask of one afternoon. But her silences had changed. They no longer seemed empty spaces waiting to be filled by someone more practiced. They were pauses in which she might, at any moment, decide to offer something.

At the turn by the lower bridge, voices reached them.

Mr. Gardiner appeared first, hat in hand, speaking animatedly, somehow, about water. Mr. Darcy came beside him, attentive, composed, and carrying in his expression a sort of grave satisfaction.

Miss Darcy turned at the sound.

A moment earlier, she had been smiling at something Mrs. Gardiner said about wet shoes and childhood grandeur. The smile had been unguarded. Not brilliant; not noisy; only hers.

When she saw her brother, it gathered itself back into propriety.

Not vanished. Only folded.

Mr. Darcy saw it. His step faltered.

For one breath, brother and sister looked at one another across the little distance where the paths met. Miss Darcy seemed about to apologize for something — having delayed, or wandered, or been pleased beyond the approved measure — but her brother spoke first.

“You found the stepping-stones," he said.

Miss Darcy’s color rose. “They are not truly a crossing."

“No," he replied, with something almost dry in his voice. “But they have always made a very determined attempt."

Miss Darcy looked down, smiling.

Elizabeth, who had meant to enjoy only the kindness of it, found herself enjoying his tone as well.

They walked back toward the house together, the party re-forming by degrees: Mr. Gardiner and Mr. Darcy speaking of the water; Mrs. Gardiner beside Miss Darcy; Elizabeth near enough to hear, not near enough to intrude. The house appeared through the trees in portions — a line of stone, a high window, the pale flash of steps. The formal world reassembled itself with every yard.

Near the garden door, Miss Darcy slowed.

“Perhaps you might stay—" she began, then stopped, as if the words had outrun her training. “Forgive me. You have already given us so much of the afternoon."

Mrs. Gardiner’s smile warmed. “Then we must be careful not to spend all our pleasure at once."

Mr. Darcy looked from his sister to Mrs. Gardiner. “If you would allow us another day, Miss Darcy would be very happy to show you more of the grounds."

Miss Darcy’s eyes flew to him. The brightness returned before she could prevent it.

Elizabeth felt, absurdly, as if he had given the permission to her as well.

Mrs. Gardiner accepted with all proper gratitude. Mr. Gardiner declared himself entirely at Pemberley’s disposal. Elizabeth said what was required, and what was true.

“I should like it very much."

It was true; and on the road back to Lambton, when Kitty returned to her thoughts, Miss Darcy returned with her.

# Chapter 15: After the Lower Walk

The lower walk had followed them indoors.

Georgiana entered the morning room with it still upon her: the quickened color from the air, one glove half-drawn from her fingers, the trace of pleasure she had not yet had time to dismiss. She came in at Darcy’s side and looked, for the space of three steps, younger than she did at the instrument and less young than she did when she was afraid.

Then she saw Miss Bingley.

The brightness did not vanish. That would have been less difficult to bear. It reduced itself by degrees: a checked step, a lowered glance, the glove folded once, and then once again, with unnecessary care.

Something must be done.

Bingley was also in the room, standing near the tea table with all the satisfaction of a man who had discovered that exercise, if endured by others, might still end in cake for himself. Mrs. Reynolds, or some servant more punctual than the party, had anticipated them. The tea stood ready but untouched. Cups waited beside the pot. A plate of small seed-cakes stood where Bingley could admire them without appearing to have arranged himself for that purpose.

“You have returned at the very instant,” he said. “Tea was in danger of becoming punctual without you.”

Georgiana smiled, and the smile nearly recovered itself.

Miss Bingley set down her book, her finger in what looked to be the same spot it had occupied when they left. Darcy did not suppose a page had suffered.

“My dear Georgiana,” she said, “how animated you are. Miss Bennet’s spirits are contagious, I see.”

There it was.

A civil sentence. A sisterly tone. No word that could be challenged without making the challenger absurd.

Georgiana’s fingers closed round her gloves.

Darcy had heard Miss Bingley say such things a hundred times. He had heard them in drawing rooms, at Netherfield, in town, and in this house. They had belonged, he supposed, to the ordinary climate of her conversation: scented, decorative, faintly oppressive, and, for a man with a door always at his disposal, easy to escape.

He had not considered what that climate might do to a girl who had nowhere to go.

“Miss Darcy did find the walk agreeable,” he said.

Miss Bingley’s eyes came to him, quick and assessing. “How fortunate.”

Bingley, who had never in his life willingly permitted an edge to remain an edge, seized the safer half of the remark. “And was it? Agreeable, I mean. I regret missing it, though I cannot pretend to regret being present when tea arrived.”

“Yes,” Georgiana said. “Very pleasant.”

Five minutes ago, at the lower bridge, her pleasure had outrun speech. Now she had returned to a phrase any governess in England might approve.

For a moment, Darcy did not move. He wanted to leave the room.

He reconsidered.

Leaving would have been the old habit, though he would not have called it that. He would have gone to his office, closed the door, considered the matter, written a letter perhaps, made some arrangement perhaps, and returned with a decision polished enough to look like certainty. Georgiana would have remained meanwhile with Miss Bingley’s care, Bingley’s kindness, and her own habit of making herself easier for everyone by becoming smaller.

Darcy crossed to the tea table instead.

“Georgiana,” he said, “will you pour?”

Her eyes lifted to his. He had given her the room, and she knew it.

“Yes.”

Miss Bingley’s hand moved as if to assist. She stopped, but not before Darcy saw the movement.

He took a chair where he could see his sister’s hands and Miss Bingley’s face, which was not the same as studying either. Bingley sat near the cakes. Miss Bingley settled opposite Darcy, one finger still keeping her place in the unread book.

Georgiana poured. Her hand was steady. Not as easy as it had been at the stream, not nearly as bright, but steady.

He had been proud of steadiness for years.

He was no longer certain it was enough.

Georgiana glanced toward the empty chairs by the hearth. “Are Mr. and Mrs. Hurst still unwell?”

Miss Bingley’s mouth arranged itself into solicitude and did not quite succeed. “They remain in their chambers. Louisa’s head is not improved, and Mr. Hurst has discovered that solitude, when accompanied by a sofa and no expectation of conversation, is the finest medicine in Derbyshire.”

“I am sorry for it,” Georgiana said.

“As am I,” said Miss Bingley, with the air of a sister whose own comfort had suffered most by their illness.

“Will the Gardiners come again?” Bingley asked, taking his cup with gratitude disproportionate to tea. “A delightful man, Mr. Gardiner.”

Darcy answered because an answer was required. “Miss Darcy has invited them.”

“I am glad of it.” Bingley smiled, an obvious, wide curve. “Very glad. They are excellent company. And Miss Elizabeth — that is, Miss Bennet — she seemed much pleased with the walk?”

Miss Bingley’s spoon touched the side of her cup. One neat sound.

“Miss Bennet has always been easily pleased by country rambles.”

The old irritation might once have been enough to occupy him. Today, he also observed the sentence as it passed him and struck elsewhere.

Georgiana looked down at the cup she was filling.

There. The woman had found the tender place.

Darcy said, “The lower walk is generally admired.”

Miss Bingley smiled. “Generally, yes.”

“Perhaps—” Georgiana began.

She stopped.

Darcy waited.

Her hand rested beside the teapot. She did not look first at him. She looked at the cups, then at the open window, then at him. “If Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet are not engaged another day, might they perhaps come again? Not only to walk. I mean — if dinner would not be too much trouble.”

Dinner.

The word entered the room with several others in attendance: intimacy, precedence, family, expectation. Miss Elizabeth Bennet seated at his table. Georgiana looking down the length of the table and finding someone who might smile back.

Bingley looked delighted. Of course.

Miss Bingley looked at Darcy.

Georgiana’s color had risen. “Or an evening,” she said, less certainly. “Only if it would be proper. I do not know — I only mean that Mrs. Gardiner spoke so kindly of the neighborhood.”

Darcy set his cup down. He had not drunk from it.

Ramsgate had begun with kindness. And ended with a near-elopement.

No. That was cowardice. Ramsgate had not begun with kindness. It had begun with concealment, vanity, calculation, and his own absence.

Still, the old caution rose at once. It loomed with all its proofs: Georgiana’s age, her fortune, the world’s appetite, his own failure, Wickham’s smile, the sea air of a place he had thought safe because he had arranged it.

He looked at his sister’s face.

He saw no folly there. Only the mortifying courage of asking for a pleasure while already braced to have it refused.

“We may certainly ask,” he said, “whether another visit would be agreeable.”

It was not a promise of dinner. It was not a refusal.

Georgiana heard both halves and chose, for once, the kinder one. “Thank you.”

Thank you.

For permission to ask for guests in her own brother’s house.

Darcy ordered his hand not to clench.

Miss Bingley’s smile returned, softer and therefore more dangerous. “How charming it is to see young people so quickly attached. New friends are always the most delightful before one knows them very well.”

Bingley frowned, not in comprehension, but in discomfort at the air becoming less easy. “I have found them delightful afterward as well, in many cases.”

“That is because you are generous, Charles.”

“I should hope so,” said Bingley. “It is a much pleasanter failing than the opposite.”

Darcy almost smiled. His mind had already turned back to the lower walk.

Georgiana returning with color in her face.

Georgiana laughing before she remembered to ask whether laughter was safe.

Miss Elizabeth Bennet sharing her smile. She had not flattered Georgiana. Nor drawn her out by force. She had not praised her accomplishments, instructed her in confidence, or made the girl’s shyness an object of display.

She had treated Georgiana’s silence not as a failure to be corrected, but as a pace to be matched.

And Georgiana had returned larger than she left.

“Darcy?”

He looked up.

Bingley was watching him with open concern. Miss Bingley watched with something more pointed. Georgiana looked at him with the readiness he knew too well: the anxious preparation to believe his silence must have been caused by something she had done.

“I beg your pardon,” he said. “What did you ask?”

Bingley laughed, too kindly to leave embarrassment in its traces. “Only whether the Gardiners remain long in Lambton.”

“A few days more, I believe.”

“You believe?” Miss Bingley said. “How unlike you to be uncertain, Mr. Darcy.”

He had earned that. He had not been listening.

“Uncertainty is not always avoidable.”

“No,” said Miss Bingley. “But one generally tries.”

Georgiana set down the sugar tongs with unnecessary care.

Darcy turned to his sister. “Did Mrs. Gardiner say whether she had known the old rectory lane?”

Georgiana blinked, then brightened a little at the safe subject. “Yes. She remembered the lane after rain. She said they used to try to cross without wetting their shoes.”

“A universal ambition,” Darcy agreed. “Rarely rewarded.”

“She said they never did,” Georgiana added. “Or at least never told their mother when they did.”

Bingley laughed. “A proper distinction.”

The room shifted by an inch. Not enough to redeem it. Enough to prove that it could be moved.

Darcy took up his cup and drank.

Cold.

Fitzwilliam would have done it better.

The thought came with such force that he nearly set the cup down again. Fitzwilliam would have found some careless speech for Georgiana to answer, some joke broad enough for Bingley and harmless enough for Miss Bingley, and he would have made the room turn before Georgiana knew she had been rescued.

But Fitzwilliam, too, would have watched. After Ramsgate, perhaps more than before. He had a soldier’s cheerfulness and a younger son’s ease, but he did not lack caution. Between them, he and Darcy had arranged masters, companions, journeys, letters, allowances, visits, proper companions, proper music, proper safeguards.

They had also prevented.

They had prevented very well.

Georgiana attended carefully while Bingley explained that he had once attempted to cross a ditch at Netherfield and had been betrayed by a bank “of most respectable appearance and no principles whatsoever.”

She smiled. She did not laugh.

Fitzwilliam had always been the more cheerful guardian, which was not the same thing as being the less cautious one. Between them, they had contrived to make Georgiana safe.

They had made her lonely.

The realization did not arrive like a blow. A blow would have been cleaner. It came like a sound heard through a wall: indistinct at first, then impossible not to hear.

Miss Elizabeth Bennet had laughed with Georgiana, and Georgiana had not been diminished by it.

Liveliness had not made her reckless. Wit had not made her impertinent. Attention had not made her vain. Miss Bennet had not made Georgiana less careful in any way that mattered. She had only made carefulness less onerous for half an hour.

Darcy’s thumb moved once along the handle of his cup.

Miss Bingley was speaking, again. “Of course, Georgiana, if you invite new friends to dinner, you must allow me to advise you on the table. A small party may be more difficult than a large one. Every deficiency is more noticed.”

The spout dipped; tea touched the saucer before Georgiana righted it.

Miss Bingley had not forbidden anything. She had not said Mrs. Gardiner was unsuitable. She had not said Miss Bennet was dangerous. She had only placed dinner among deficiencies and made Georgiana a girl who would need advice before she dared hospitality.

Suitable company.

He had sought suitable company for Georgiana. He had chosen it, approved it, measured it by family, manner, accomplishment, and consequence. He had feared vulgarity, fortune-hunters, unchecked spirits, improper intimacies, the contagion of low connections and heedless girls.

He had not feared refinement.

“Georgiana,” he said.

She looked up at once.

“If we are to ask the Gardiners and Miss Bennet another day, you may decide whether it is to be a walk, tea, or dinner. Mrs. Reynolds will assist with the arrangements.”

Miss Bingley’s eyes sharpened.

Georgiana’s lips parted. “I may decide?”

“Yes.”

It was one syllable. It cost him more than it should have done.

Bingley broke into a grin. “Excellent. I vote for dinner, if votes are collected.”

“They are not,” Darcy said.

“Then I withdraw my vote and retain my opinion.”

Georgiana laughed, carefully.

Darcy did not look at Miss Bingley. He did not trust himself to do it without making too much of what she had done, and too little of what he had permitted. He took another sip of cold tea.

Caroline’s fan opened. “How very generous you are, Mr. Darcy.”

“No,” he said.

The word was out before he had considered it. It was too flat. Bingley looked at him. Georgiana looked at him. Miss Bingley’s fan stopped.

Darcy set down his cup. “My sister is mistress here. It is not generosity to remember it.”

Silence followed.

Georgiana’s color deepened, but not unhappily. “Thank you,” she said again, softly.

Gratitude where there ought to have been only right.

He could not bear it.

He bore it. Swallowed it down with the tepid tea.

Elizabeth Bennet had been in the room for less than an hour the day before and not at all today after the walk, yet the morning room had altered around the fact of her. Bingley was brighter because she existed within reach of Jane. Georgiana was braver because she had laughed with her by water. Caroline was sharper because she saw both things and could not govern them.

Darcy was less certain because Elizabeth Bennet had once told him the truth so completely that he still found pieces of it in rooms she had left.

At last the tea table dispersed by the natural degrees of a household that had performed enough civility for one afternoon. Bingley remembered a letter he had not finished. Miss Bingley declared the heat oppressive. Georgiana gathered the cups together until Darcy asked a servant to take them and saw her relinquish the task without apology.

He stood.

“I must write to Fitzwilliam.”

Georgiana looked up, startled. “Is anything wrong?”

“No.” He made himself say it without impatience. “Nothing is wrong.”

It was true.

It was not sufficient.

Bingley, already halfway to the door, turned back. “Give him my regards. Tell him I have become a serious fisherman by association.”

“I will not tell him that.”

“You may tell him I intend to become one. That is nearly as good and much less falsifiable.”

Darcy inclined his head. It might have been amusement. It might have been fatigue. He could no longer tell.

He left them then — not abruptly, not fast enough to betray haste. Down the corridor, past the open door where the afternoon light crossed the floor, toward the office where estate papers waited in correct piles and a letter from his steward lay exactly where he had left it.

The office looked the same as ever. Ledgers squared off. Pen-knife beside the blotter. The sand was dry in its little silver box. Mr. Greaves’s memorandum concerning the north coppice waited beneath a weight, patient and legible and much more reasonable than any human being in this house.

Darcy shut the door.

For half a minute he did not go to the desk. He heard nothing from the hall.

That had been his arrangement too.

Quiet rooms. Proper women. Suitable company. A brother who loved her and loved her so vigilantly that he had made himself, in all practical matters, another locked door.

He drew out the chair and sat.

*Georgiana is well.*

The sentence presented itself at once, neat as a visiting card.

It was true. She was well. She practiced, rode, read, wrote, took the air, sat with Mrs. Annesley, received Miss Bingley, and answered questions in a voice everyone praised for its sweetness. She was safe from Wickham. Safe from scandal. Safe from folly. Safe from almost everything that had teeth.

He took a sheet of paper.

*Georgiana is well.*

The ink darkened, settled, and made the lie visible.

Not a lie. Darcy would not permit himself that. Fitzwilliam, reading it, would understand the plain sense of the words. Georgiana was not ill. No new danger had appeared. No name had been spoken which must send either of them riding all night.

The sentence was true.

It was not enough.

He thought of Elizabeth on the lower walk, though he had not stood near enough to hear every word. A tilt of her head. A glove darkened by moss. The refusal to make Georgiana’s shyness the subject of the morning. The mercy of treating a nervous girl as a companion and not a project.

At Hunsford she had shown him no mercy at all.

No. That was not true. She had given him the only mercy in her power. She had refused him plainly enough to make self-deception impossible. She had named his offenses. She had left him the labor of believing her.

He had believed her.

Not at once. Not generously. Not without all the bitter little trials by which a proud man attempts to prove a truth false because it is inconvenient.

But he had believed her.

And now, in rooms she had left, the consequences continued to present themselves for signature.

He bent to the letter.

# Chapter 16: At the Instrument

An invitation to dinner followed, written with that exact propriety which makes a favor look like the natural consequence of good manners. The Gardiners had been delighted beyond all prudence; they had meant to show Elizabeth Derbyshire’s walks, prospects, and humbler beauties. But they had never once imagined they might carry her, by invitation, into one of the finest houses in the county. 

Elizabeth had been pleased too—pleased, and a little quieted by the ease with which everything had been settled, as if the day’s unexpected meeting had not altered the world so much as she had feared.

Dinner itself had been, in every sense, divine. Abundant without parade, attentive without officiousness, and conducted with such general good behavior that even Miss Bingley’s civilities could be received without owing her any gratitude. Even the Hursts, whom Elizabeth had not yet seen since Netherfield and whom her aunt and uncle had never met at all, contrived a worn civility—Mrs. Hurst pale and languid, Mr. Hurst heavy-eyed and dutifully silent. Both were so evidently reduced by recent illness that the most exquisite course of dinner seemed to find them without any stomach to honor it. 

If there was constraint, it wore its Sunday face; if there was observation, it kept behind the comfort of candles and well-timed questions. They rose from table with the agreeable fatigue of people who had eaten well and been determined to be pleased with one another.

Afterward, they were shown into the room with the pianoforte.

Elizabeth knew it at once, though evening had altered it. On Saturday, Mrs. Reynolds had called it the music room, and Elizabeth had crossed its threshold as a tourist: free to admire the view of the lawn, the little writing-desk near the window, the fine instrument with Mozart open upon its stand, and then to pass on before any object could ask anything of her. 

Tonight the same room had been made ready for people expected to sit, observe, and be observed. The curtains were drawn back from the tall windows, but the glass had gone black except where candles made duplicate flames upon it. The lawn beyond was no longer a prospect. It was a depth.

The pianoforte stood where she remembered it, angled a little toward the room as well as the windows, its mahogany case catching the candlelight in long polished gleams. The music stand held a tidy stack of sheets rather than the single open page she had seen before. Someone had placed two small candles near it, each with a bright brass shield to throw the light downward upon the keys. Their light made the ivory look whiter than it had by day and made the black keys shine like little bars of sealing wax. The scent of fading rose leaves mingled with the clean wool of the thick carpet underfoot.

The room was grand, but not with the dining-room’s public magnificence. Its elegance was more artful: pale walls, white lower paneling, curtains figured with leaves, and chairs arranged so that music might pretend to be conversation while everyone had an excellent view of the performer. A harp stood beyond the pianoforte, gilded, elegant, and neglected for the evening, its strings catching the light so finely that they seemed less fitted for sound than for display. Near the window, the writing-desk was bare, its inkwell capped. The evening was for the people in the room, not those far away.

Elizabeth's glove brushed the back of the carved elbow-chair Georgiana had indicated for her. The wood was smooth from use rather than new polish; its worn edge had the look of something touched often and without ceremony. The silk of her gown rustled faintly against the cushion. Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner sat to her left, closer to the fire in its pristine white frame, but with their chairs turned enough toward the instrument to make refusal of music impossible.

The lush carpet captured the sound of footsteps almost completely, so that people seemed to arrive in fragments: Mr. Hurst's shoe at the hearth as he settled on the sofa at an angle to the fire and the pianoforte, in visible gratitude for both; Mrs. Hurst’s soft sigh as she joined him, graceful and pale; Miss Bingley's fan opening once with a neat dry snap; Mr. Bingley's low laugh before he had finished crossing the room; the small chime of Mrs. Gardiner's bracelets as she settled her hands in her lap.

Miss Bingley chose one of the remaining chairs with an air of perfect accident, though it left her admirably placed to address either Miss Darcy or her brother. She seemed to have recovered completely. There remained only a fineness about her mouth and a brightness in her eyes that made any lingering illness look less like weakness than like an advantage she had no intention of wasting.

Mr. Bingley's good humor had survived the table, and was harder to resent than indifference would have been. "And Miss Bennet is well?" he had asked at dinner, coloring as if he had named one lady too particularly in the presence of seven witnesses and a roast. Elizabeth had answered as Jane would have answered: "Very well, I thank you." The answer had been both true and insufficient. At the far end of the table Mr. Darcy had looked down at his glass. 

Elizabeth was not disposed to pity him.

Mr. Bingley chose the last cushion on the sofa with the Hursts, half-turned toward the instrument and wholly ready to be pleased.

Mr. Darcy stood by the mantel, one shoulder turned a little from the fire. The light caught the strong line of his profile and left the rest of his face in reserve; he looked neither easy nor inattentive, but as if nothing in the room were likely to escape him.

Miss Darcy started to take the chair between Elizabeth and Miss Bingley, but was interrupted.

"Georgiana," said Miss Bingley, "you *must* play. Our friends have not yet heard you properly."

Properly.

Elizabeth had not known a word could curtsy and command in the same syllable.

Miss Darcy smoothed one hand over her gown. "If anyone wishes it."

"My dear girl, everyone wishes it." Miss Bingley looked around the room with the confidence of a person collecting consent from those who had not offered it. Mrs. Hurst inclined her head. Mr. Bingley smiled encouragement. Mr. Hurst inspected the fire as if music might be endured at that distance. "You cannot imagine that we should allow such an instrument to remain silent."

Miss Darcy's color rose.

Mr. Darcy moved one hand along the mantel, and then stilled it.

"I should be very glad to hear anything you wish to play, Miss Darcy," said Mrs. Gardiner, calm, kind. "But only if she is inclined."

Miss Darcy’s look toward Elizabeth’s aunt was both grateful and not relieved enough.

"I shall play," she said.

Elizabeth disliked Miss Bingley a little more for making the words necessary.

Miss Darcy went to the pianoforte, pale gown, pale hands, and all Pemberley's expectation gathered between her and the keys. Earlier, she had received them in the hall with courage enough to cross it first; she had answered Mrs. Gardiner at dinner on the subject of London masters and Derbyshire practice; she had even smiled when Mr. Gardiner declared that the masters had the worse bargain. Elizabeth had treasured that smile absurdly. 

Here, with the candles set to display her hands and every chair turned toward the instrument, that smile looked in danger.

At the music stand, Miss Darcy made one small halt. Most people would not have seen it. Elizabeth saw it because the girl's hand, which had reached toward a sheet of music, came back and touched the edge of the pianoforte instead, as if the wood were safer than the choice.

Mr. Darcy saw it. His hand, which had been resting on the mantel, closed once and opened again.

"Miss Bennet," he said, with a politeness that made the request appear general rather than urgent, "as you are nearest, would you be so good as to assist my sister with the pages, if she wishes it?"

Miss Darcy looked quickly from her brother to Elizabeth. "If Miss Bennet does not mind."

Elizabeth rose at once. "Not in the least."

Standing beside Miss Darcy, she saw the instrument differently. The candles, so pretty from the chairs, made a little stage of the keys; the music was no longer ornament but demand; every face in the semicircle had become a witness.

The first piece was something of Handel’s, grave and graceful. Miss Darcy played with great delicacy, with flashes of quiet brilliance. There was a sureness in her touch when she forgot the room, and a slight constraint whenever she remembered it. Elizabeth listened with more attention than judgment. She had often been ashamed of Mary's performances because Mary performed as if improvement were a duty owed to the neighborhood. Miss Darcy played as if accomplishment had been placed in her hands long ago, and pleasure somehow omitted from the lesson.

When the piece ended, there was the usual praise. Mr. Bingley's was immediate, warm and imprecise. Mr. Gardiner's was sensible. Mrs. Gardiner's was kindly. Mr. Hurst's was inaudible and perhaps digestive. Miss Bingley's was the most elegant and the least useful.

"Charming, Georgiana. Quite charming. Though you know I always think that piece scarcely does you justice. You have such command when you choose to exert it."

Miss Darcy's fingers closed upon one another in her lap.

Elizabeth, standing beside her, felt her own hand tighten on the edge of the music stand.

Elizabeth pictured Kitty at the old square pianoforte at Longbourn, striking a wrong note and laughing before Mary could instruct her. Kitty had no command whatever. Kitty had noise, complaint, ribbons, loyalty when not led astray, and a sort of playfulness that made her parents smile and forget to scold her. And, lately, courage.

"You promised me the duet," Miss Bingley said.

Miss Darcy's fingers tightened again. She lifted her eyes. "Did I?"

"Did you not? Or if not a promise, something very like it. Louisa, you remember the duet we played in town?"

Mrs. Hurst, whose loyalty was weakened by headache but not extinguished, said, "Very pretty."

"Very pretty, and very much admired," said Miss Bingley. "Georgiana has the principal part perfectly, if only she will not be so timid. I am certain Miss Bennet will forgive us a little display among friends."

Among friends.

Elizabeth felt the phrase arrive and arrange the room around itself. Friends did not compel. Friends did not make refusal costly. Friends, she thought, did not require a girl to prove she deserved their admiration before she might have their ease.

Miss Darcy's hand moved among the pages with more haste than certainty. "I am not sure I recollect it well enough." The sheet of music she sought did not appear.

"Nonsense. You recollect everything once you are seated." Miss Bingley rose. "You may play the second part, if you prefer. It will be nothing—only a little thing."

Only a little thing, said by someone who had already made it too large.

Mr. Bingley shifted in his chair. "Perhaps Georgiana has played enough for one evening."

Miss Bingley smiled at him. "Charles, you are always for sparing everyone every exertion. It is very amiable, but it would leave us with no music, no dancing, no conversation, and no improvement of any kind."

"I am always in favor of conversation," said Mr. Bingley.

Mrs. Hurst laughed faintly, then checked herself with a soft cough into her handkerchief. "Caroline is right. Miss Darcy plays so well that it would be a pity to hide it."

A pity to hide it.

Miss Darcy's gaze dropped to her lap.

Elizabeth saw it then, and anger came too quickly for politeness. Her teeth set; before she could prevent it, her eyes had gone from face to face around the room. The girl was not being asked to play. She was being asked to submit to being admired. There was a difference, and every person in the room who chose not to see it was guilty of some small cruelty. When Elizabeth looked toward the mantel, she found Mr. Darcy very still, his gaze fixed upon Miss Bingley with an expression that wanted only a little less civility to be a glare.

Miss Bingley had reached the piano bench. Her hand rested on the back of Miss Darcy's shoulder with an intimacy that belonged either to sisters or to conquest.

"Shall we begin at the allegro? It shows your hand to advantage."

Miss Darcy said nothing.

A second passed. Another.

Then Mr. Darcy spoke.

"No."

It was not loud. It did not need to be.

Miss Bingley's hand left the shoulder.

The room, which had seemed to lean toward the instrument, grew still.

Mr. Darcy took a step away from the mantel. His face had altered very little, but Elizabeth knew, with a certainty she could not have defended to anyone else, that he was angry.

"My sister has already obliged us very handsomely," he said. "If she wishes to play again, she will say so."

The correction was made before them all, and Elizabeth’s breath caught. The words were not severe; but they restored Miss Darcy to the place from which Miss Bingley had removed her: in possession of herself.

Miss Bingley recovered first. "Of course," she said lightly. "I would not press Georgiana for the world. You know my only fault is wishing to hear her too often."

Mr. Darcy bowed, and allowed the excuse to fail unaided.

That was new.

Elizabeth looked down at her hands to hide what might have been too plainly visible in her face. Gratitude was unsafe. Admiration was unsafe. The wish to laugh at Miss Bingley's defeat was, perhaps, the least dangerous of the three.

Miss Darcy remained at the instrument. Her hands touched the keys, too softly to make a sound.

"Thank you," she said.

It was impossible to tell whether the words were for her brother, for the room, or for the permission to stop. Elizabeth suspected Mr. Darcy knew and was pained by it. He returned to the mantel.

Then Miss Darcy drew another piece from the music pile. It looked impossible to Elizabeth even on the page. The pages were worn at the corners, penciled with small reminders that looked like prayers. She set it before her with a steadiness that seemed to be less ease than determination.

She began at once.

The piece was quick and bright, all order at the surface and difficulty beneath. Elizabeth had not sense enough for judgment; but she could hear the places where a hand might falter, and she could see that Miss Darcy did not. When the tricky passage arrived—a turn that seemed to require three hands and no fear—her fingers flew through it as if they had been waiting there all day.

By the fire, Mr. Darcy made a small sound—no word, no interruption, only a low, breathy “yes.”

Miss Darcy did not pause. Her eyes stayed on the page, intent. But a small smile escaped her, quick as the run itself, and then was gone again into concentration.

She finished with a neat, unhurried certainty—as if the last bar were not a release but a choice—and let her hands rest upon the keys a moment longer than necessary, giving the last chord its due.

Then she looked up, and her gaze went first, as it must, to her brother. The pleasure on his face answered her own; for a moment his gravity almost gave way. When Georgiana turned to Elizabeth, Elizabeth forgot every proper observation and could only breathe, very softly, “Well.” It was not clever; it was not even a sentence; but Miss Darcy seemed to understand it. She smiled—almost grinned—before recollecting herself and turning back to the others.

Mr. Gardiner was beaming. "Miss Darcy, may a very ignorant man ask whether that second piece was German? I have always suspected German music of being cleverer than I am."

The corner of Miss Darcy's mouth tilted up.

"Yes, you are right."

"Then I am safe. I have heard of Mozart."

Mr. Bingley brightened. "As have I, though I cannot pretend he has improved me."

This produced a small laugh from Miss Darcy—not much, but enough to set the conversation on safer ground. Mrs. Gardiner asked about the history of the music. Mr. Bingley applied himself to being pleased with everyone. Mr. Hurst closed his eyes and was either meditating upon art or sleeping with unusual refinement.

Miss Bingley retreated to her sister; their conference proceeded behind the fan and beneath the general conversation. Mrs. Hurst glanced toward the mantel and then back at Miss Bingley. She gently shook her head, and then reached a hand toward her temple, closing her eyes.

As Elizabeth seated herself again at Miss Darcy’s left, Mr. Darcy took the seat on his sister’s other side. He offered no apology, claimed no victory, and Miss Darcy, while answering Mrs. Gardiner, seemed easier for his being there.

Elizabeth had seen the man proud. She had seen him mortified. Now every polite gesture felt like a message she could not decipher.

She looked away.

At the hearth, the fire gave a brisk pop. Mr. Hurst, who had appeared to be meditating with his eyes shut, opened one of them with an expression of such grave reproach—as if the coal had addressed him with unbecoming familiarity—that Mr. Bingley was obliged to look very hard at the carpet.

Miss Darcy laughed—only a soft little sound—and then, as if startled by her own ease, pressed her lips together and looked down at her hands. Elizabeth thought, unexpectedly, of Kitty.

Kitty could never have played so beautifully; she would have been too loud, too certain of being wrong, and very likely to laugh first. But she would not have made Miss Darcy ashamed of wanting music to be easy.

The thought brought Longbourn suddenly near, so that Elizabeth almost missed Mrs. Gardiner speaking to her.

"Lizzy?"

"I beg your pardon."

Her aunt's eyes rested on her, warm and too observant. "We were wondering whether you would sing."

Elizabeth looked toward the pianoforte, then toward Miss Darcy, whose hands had quieted in her lap. The choice offered itself plainly enough.

Elizabeth rose.

"Yes," she said, "if Miss Darcy will be so kind as to choose something very easy, and if everyone promises to expect very little."

Mr. Bingley laughed at once. "That is the best promise ever asked of company. I make it with all my heart."

Miss Darcy looked at Elizabeth. Some uncertainty remained, but she was not shrinking anymore. Elizabeth crossed back to the instrument with her. Side by side, they sifted through the music.

"*Very* easy?" Miss Darcy asked.

"Disgracefully easy."

Miss Darcy smiled in earnest.

Elizabeth glanced beyond the music. Mr. Darcy was watching his sister; yet the warmth in his expression seemed, for one dangerous instant, to include the person who had made her smile.

Together, they chose the song.

# Chapter 17: Pemberley Goes Quiet

By nine the next morning, Pemberley had arranged itself around departure.

Darcy had always disliked the particular confusion of guests leaving a house. Arrival could be made handsome: horses checked at the sweep, servants moving with clear purpose, greetings exchanged beneath the shelter of expectation. 

Departure exposed everything arrival had concealed. Trunks loitered in passages. Footmen crossed the hall carrying rugs, small boxes, books forgotten in bedrooms, bottles of smelling salts, a shawl Miss Bingley had declared lost and then discovered to have been under her own hand. A lady’s maid came down twice to ask whether Mrs. Hurst’s cordial had been packed, and Mr. Hurst, emerging from the breakfast room with his coffee unfinished, expressed his belief that if the cordial had not been packed, it deserved to remain in Derbyshire and recover itself.

Mrs. Hurst did not laugh. She was pale enough to make the journey seem reasonable and languid enough to make every stair in the house an accusation. She had spent ten minutes over dry toast, taken nothing else, and afterward declared, with a hand to her temple, that she should never be well until she was again in her own rooms.

Mr. Hurst looked ready to agree with any opinion that conveyed him nearer London, or nearer a sofa that expected nothing of him.

Miss Bingley was all solicitude.

Darcy had seen Caroline Bingley perform many social duties with skill: admiration, regret, civility, the appearance of interest in matters that did not interest her in the least. Solicitude, this morning, had a brightness at the edges. She was very sorry to leave Pemberley. She could not bear that Louisa should suffer. She hoped dear Georgiana would forgive the abruptness. She must believe Mr. Darcy would understand that a sister’s health admitted of no delay.

He understood several things.

He understood that Louisa Hurst was genuinely unwell, though perhaps less endangered than Caroline’s manner suggested. He understood that Mr. Hurst had no wish to pass another day in a house where he might be expected to admire music, walking, or conversation. He understood that Bingley would have remained, had his family allowed it.

He had planned for that, though he had disguised the plan from himself as prudence. Beneath all his proper intentions had lived the assumption that Bingley would remain another month, perhaps two; that there would be a walk, a quiet hour, some decent interval in which a confession might be approached by degrees.

Louisa’s illness had not granted him that. He could not regret Miss Bingley’s removal; he would not leave Georgiana in her orbit a moment longer than necessity required. But Caroline’s retreat drew Bingley with it, and that meant a carriage ten minutes from the door.

Georgiana stood near the foot of the stairs, composed, correct, and too still. She had come down early, dressed in a pale morning gown with a ribbon at the waist and her hair arranged with more care than the hour required. 

“Dear Georgiana,” Miss Bingley said, taking both her hands. “You must not be dull when we are gone. I depend upon your writing to me — long letters, mind. You must tell me all your improvements, and whether you have conquered that Mozart. I shall be most severe if you neglect your practice.”

Georgiana’s fingers moved once within Caroline’s. “I shall write.”

“And do not over-fatigue yourself with company.” Miss Bingley smiled. “You are not yet used to being so much admired.”

The words were light. Another small wound.

Darcy had made one correction the night before. It had been public, necessary, and, apparently, insufficient. To make another now, in the hall, before servants and trunks and a sister already humiliated by too much notice, would only turn Georgiana’s mortification into a scene and give Caroline the advantage of appearing injured.

“Georgiana,” he said, “Mrs. Reynolds wished to consult you about the blue room flowers before they are sent up.”

His sister looked at him. Relief entered her face and was disciplined almost at once. “Yes. Of course.”

She withdrew with a curtsey that did her credit and made Darcy angrier than clumsiness would have done.

Miss Bingley watched her go. “How sweet she is.”

Darcy did not answer.

Caroline’s smile faltered by no more than a thread. “And so young, after all. One forgets it, seeing her so accomplished.”

“One should not,” Darcy said.

It was not severe. It was also not an invitation to continue.

Bingley, who had been speaking to Mr. Hurst near the door, looked from one to the other with the quick discomfort of a man who had smoothed his sisters’ edges more than once. “Darcy, would you walk with me a moment before we set off? Hurst says the horses will not be ready for ten minutes, which in posting language means either five or twenty.”

Mr. Hurst said, “It simply means they are not ready,” and took another swallow of coffee.

Darcy inclined his head. “Certainly.”

They exited through the side door to the west terrace, its pale stone still cool from the night. Beyond the balustrade, the lawn ran bright and wet toward the trees; cut grass and watered gravel scented the air. A groom crossed the sweep at a distance, leading one of Bingley’s horses. From the open window behind them came Caroline’s voice, lowered but distinct enough to carry the shape of complaint.

Bingley stood with his hands behind his back and looked out across the lawn. He had not, Darcy thought, slept well. His good humor remained, but it sat nearer the surface, ready for use and not quite natural.

“I am sorry to go,” Bingley said. “It is absurd. I have been here only a few days, and half the house has been unwell for most of them, but I am sorry all the same.”

“You must attend your sister.”

“Yes. Louisa will be more comfortable in town. Hurst will be more comfortable when Louisa is comfortable.” He smiled, then lost the smile. “Caroline is determined that her usefulness shall be observed.”

Darcy let that pass.

Bingley turned a pebble under the toe of his boot. “I had hoped to see the Gardiners again before leaving. And Miss Elizabeth.”

Darcy’s hand tightened on the balustrade.

“And Miss Bennet,” Bingley added, too quickly. “That is — to hear of Miss Bennet. Her sister said she was well.”

“Yes.”

A cart sounded somewhere beyond the stable-yard. A servant laughed. Darcy took a deep breath.

He had intended to speak later. He had intended some decent order: a word first, perhaps; then a fuller account; then, if Bingley wished it, every mortifying particular Darcy could honorably give.

There was no time now.

He could write. Wait until Bingley was settled in London, when a friend’s confession might be delivered at a distance.

But truth had already suffered too much from his arrangements.

“Bingley,” he said. “There is something I must tell you.”

Bingley looked at him at once. “Is anything wrong?”

“Yes.”

The word came more easily than the explanation.

Darcy moved a little farther along the terrace, beyond the open window’s easy reach. There was no merit in looking at the view. He did it anyway.

“When you left Netherfield last November, I believed Miss Bennet did not return your regard.”

Bingley’s hands, which had been clasped behind him, shifted once. For a moment he looked only puzzled, as if Darcy had named a subject from another room entirely.

Darcy had known Bingley in spirits, in embarrassment, in disappointment, in all the soft weather of his temper. He had not known him quite so still.

“I told you so,” he continued. “More than once. I used my influence with you — and with your sisters — to prevent your returning to Hertfordshire. I thought I was acting for your benefit.”

The puzzlement left Bingley’s face. Pain started to spread across his countenance.

Darcy turned. His friend had put one hand on the balustrade. Not heavily; only enough that the knuckles showed pale for an instant before he loosened them.

“I was wrong,” Darcy said.

Bingley drew his hand from the stone, then set it back again. “Wrong in what part?”

“In the judgment. In the interference. In the confidence with which I mistook Miss Bennet’s manners.”

The stillness altered. Bingley looked away, across the lawn. “You said you thought she did not care for me.”

“I did.”

“And do you no longer think so?”

“No.”

Bingley turned back to him. He searched Darcy’s face with an expression almost comical in its astonishment, had there been any comedy in the occasion. “But you are an excellent judge of everyone.”

“No.”

The answer was so immediate that Bingley blinked.

Darcy pushed himself to continue. “I have often been a confident judge. Not the same thing.”

Bingley looked down. The toe of his boot moved against the pebble again, then stopped. “What has altered your opinion?”

Darcy did not answer quickly enough.

Bingley looked up. “Miss Elizabeth?”

Darcy let out a breath. “Miss Elizabeth Bennet gave me reason to understand that I had been mistaken.”

That was too little. It was also all he could say. Hunsford, the proposal, the letter, Elizabeth’s accusations — none of it belonged on the terrace at Pemberley with Bingley pale in the morning light and the carriages waiting.

Bingley’s face changed at Elizabeth’s name, but not toward suspicion. Hope, when it came too fast, could look almost like pain.

“Miss Elizabeth would know,” he said.

“Yes.”

“And Miss Bennet — Jane — may have cared.”

Darcy heard the name and accepted the rebuke of it. Bingley had earned the right to say what Darcy had avoided saying for all these months.

“I now believe she did.”

Bingley turned away. His arms came across his chest, and he looked out again over the dew-bright lawn. For once, Darcy did not attempt to read him. He had read Jane Bennet too neatly, too coldly, and had congratulated himself on penetration. He would not now name every movement in his friend’s face and call the naming understanding.

“I thought myself a fool,” Bingley said at last. His voice low, but steady. “At first, when we came away, I thought — well, no matter what I thought. Caroline was so certain. Louisa too. You, as well. I convinced myself I had imagined more than existed, and how could I not? You are all so much cleverer than I.”

“Bingley—”

“No, it is true in many particulars.” He gave a small, unhappy laugh. “I do not mean I am an idiot. I hope not, at least. But you see further than I do. You always have. I thought, if Darcy says there was nothing, then I have been making a romance of ordinary kindness. A man may survive being made a fool of. He ought not, perhaps, arrange his whole life around never being made one again.”

Darcy had no answer worthy of him.

Bingley uncrossed his arms and leaned both hands on the balustrade. “And now you tell me there may have been something.”

“Yes.”

“After all this time.”

“Yes.”

“Good God, Darcy.”

It was not anger, or not only anger. There was injury in it, and bewilderment, and a dawning impatience.

“I had no right,” Darcy said. “I cannot repair the months you have lost. I cannot know what Miss Bennet now feels. I can only tell you that my former judgment should no longer govern yours. If you still wish to know her mind, you must act upon your own judgment.”

Bingley gazed steadily at him then, and the injury sharpened. “My own. Yes. I suppose I ought to have done that before.”

Darcy inclined his head once. It was all he trusted himself to do.

For half a minute they stood with the lawn before them and the open house to their side. Darcy heard the faint sound of wheels on gravel as another carriage was brought round.

Then Bingley said, “Did you mean to tell me before today?”

Darcy’s throat worked against the answer. “Yes.”

“Had you?”

“No,” Darcy said. “Not today. I meant to wait until it could be done better.”

Bingley gaped at him, astonishment returning in spite of everything. “Better?”

“More privately. More completely. Less—”

“Less at the very moment my carriage is being brought round?”

“Yes.”

Bingley considered him for one grave second.

Darcy waited for the anger he deserved. He had imagined Bingley injured; he had imagined him wounded into silence; he had not, until that instant, imagined him raising his voice. The possibility entered him ice-cold and well deserved.

Bingley laughed.

The laugh had no gaiety in it at first. Then, because he was Bingley, some gaiety entered despite everything. “You are the most extraordinary man. You have ruined my peace since last winter, and now you dislike the timing.”

Darcy found himself almost smiling. It hurt.

“I dislike many things about my conduct.”

“That is just as well, for I dislike some of them too.”

“You have every right.”

“I know.” Bingley straightened. “That is new. I generally leave rights to other people; they manage them with more confidence.”

The carriage rolled into view beyond the corner of the house. A footman came down the steps with a rug over one arm.

Bingley watched it, then looked past it, as if the road visible from the sweep might already be the Hertfordshire road. “I must take Louisa to town.”

“Yes.”

“And Caroline.”

“Yes.”

“And Hurst, unless we abandon him by accident at the first inn, which would be unkind to the inn.”

Despite himself, Darcy said, “Very.”

Bingley’s mouth curved and fell again. “But I need not remain in town.”

“No.”

“I may go to Netherfield.”

“If you choose.”

“If *I* choose,” Bingley repeated. The phrase seemed to interest him, as a door might interest a man who has always supposed the wall solid.

Darcy said nothing.

Bingley turned to him. “Does she know? Miss Elizabeth. Does she know you have told me?”

“No.”

“Will you tell her?”

“I do not know.”

Bingley studied him then with more perception than Darcy had ever allowed him credit for, though that too was an old injustice. Bingley had never been stupid. He had only been willing to believe those he loved.

“At some point,” Bingley said, “you may find that a lady likes to be trusted with what so nearly concerns her.”

Darcy looked at him.

Bingley colored. “That sounded wiser than I intended.”

“I believe it was.”

“Then I retract it. I am not fit for wisdom this morning.” He took one breath, then another. “I am angry with you, I think.”

“Yes.”

“And grateful.”

“You should not be.”

“I daresay I shall decide that for myself too.”

There was nothing to say to that.

Bingley held out his hand.

For a moment Darcy only looked at it. Then he took it. Bingley’s grip was warm, brief, and firmer than usual.

“I wish you had been wrong less cleverly,” Bingley said.

“As do I.”

“And I wish I had loved her less obediently.”

Darcy had to look away.

Caroline’s voice reached them from the steps. “Charles! Louisa is in the carriage.”

Bingley released his hand. “Coming.”

They returned together to the portico, where the whole business of leave-taking had arranged itself into public order. Georgiana stood at Darcy’s side. Miss Bingley expressed regret enough for three households; Mrs. Hurst was already settled with cushions; Mr. Hurst entered the carriage with an expression of martyrdom too indolent to be theatrical. Bingley stood last on the step.

He bent over Georgiana’s hand with his old kindness. “You must write to me about the German fellow,” he said. “Mozart. I shall rely upon you to make me seem informed in conversation.”

Georgiana smiled. “I do not think that is possible by letter.”

“Then I am lost. But write anyway.”

She laughed, a little. Darcy was grateful for it.

Soon, the carriages went down the sweep, wheels loud on gravel, then softer past the bend, then gone.

For some time after, Pemberley was full of the noises that follow departure: doors closing, servants carrying away the last signs of occupation, Mrs. Reynolds giving instructions in the hall. The house resumed itself room by room. Cushions were restored. Fires were mended. Curtains drawn back. A maid crossed the music room with the brass candle shields from the pianoforte, and Darcy, passing the door, stopped.

The room looked harmless by daylight.

The mahogany instrument stood in its proper place. The sheets had been stacked. No circle of chairs compelled anyone to sit and admire. The harp threw a narrow line of gold against the wall. Last night’s unhappiness had left no mark upon the furniture.

Georgiana was not in the room.

He found her in the small morning room, seated at the table with a note open before her. She looked up when he entered and folded the paper too quickly.

“From Mrs. Gardiner?” he asked.
