“Yes.” She smoothed the crease. “They cannot come today. Mr. Gardiner has letters to answer, and Mrs. Gardiner thinks Miss Bennet a little fatigued after last evening. They send their compliments and thanks.”

Miss Bennet, fatigued. Perhaps she was. Perhaps the fatigue was his doing, or Caroline’s, or Pemberley’s, or all of them combined. The thought lodged where no useful action could reach it.

Georgiana looked down at the folded note. “She says they may be obliged to return to London sooner than they expected.”

Georgiana kept her attention on the paper. “Not certainly. Only perhaps. Mr. Gardiner has received a great many letters from London.”

The morning had already carried the Bingleys away. Now it threatened the Gardiners too.

Darcy had known, of course, that the Gardiners were travelers. Their presence in Derbyshire had never belonged to him, nor even to Pemberley. They had an inn, a carriage, children in Hertfordshire, business in London, and every claim upon their time that propriety could respect. He had known it; but knowledge, while Miss Bennet stood beside Georgiana at the instrument, had been a fact put away for later.

Later had arrived before noon.

Georgiana folded the note again. “If they are to leave, they will have much to arrange.” Meaning no time for tea, or dinner, or music, or grace.

She said it reasonably. That was the worst of it. She had already begun to make herself equal to the disappointment.

The morning room had always been sufficient for letters, breakfast, estate business, and the small civilities of a quiet house. It had never before been asked to hold a girl’s disappointment and do anything useful with it.

“Did you wish to ask them again?” he said.

Georgiana’s eyes lifted at once, then lowered. “I do not wish to trouble them.”

“That is not what I asked.”

Her fingers slid along the edge of the note. “I did like having them here.”

Darcy gazed past his sister, toward the window.

Georgiana’s voice grew softer. “I thought perhaps — only if Mrs. Gardiner wished it — they might come to tea another day. Or we might call. But if they are leaving tomorrow, perhaps there is no another day.”

There it was: the careful retreat before refusal, the apology built into desire. He had taught her that, not by instruction but by habit. Caution had been the language of their household for years. He had spoken it fluently, and Georgiana had learned to make even pleasure conditional.

“We have not asked a great deal,” he said.

She looked at him then.

“We shall call at Lambton this afternoon. See if Mrs. Gardiner is at home to us.”

Her hand moved across the table and came to rest, lightly, on his.

“Yes,” she said.

The touch, brief and unlooked-for, struck him more sharply than any thanks.

Bingley was not the only person to whom he had given too much guidance and too little trust.

With Bingley he had called it friendship. With Georgiana, protection. The habit was the same. If it was to alter, it would not do so by resolution alone.

It would begin with Lambton.

# Chapter 18: Luncheon at The Rose

By the time Elizabeth had done with the broth and bread at luncheon, the letters from London had done what no road, prospect, or Derbyshire summer had yet managed: they had turned the Gardiners' concerns toward home.

On the table in the private second dining room at the Rose. Three letters from London lay open on the table, or had done; Mr. Gardiner had folded them back and pushed them aside, but they persisted.

The first was from Mr. Granger's own hand, written in a cramped uneven script that made his fever more convincing than any complaint could have done. The second, from the warehouse clerk, was clearer and therefore worse: a customer impatient, goods waiting, and no one in Gracechurch Street with authority enough to make either behave. A third note added little but urgency.

Mr. Gardiner had read all three that morning, written two answers, abandoned one, and come down with the look of a man who has discovered that business allows a holiday only until it chooses to recall him.

Mr. Gardiner must go to London. The only question was whether Mrs. Gardiner and Elizabeth must go with him.

The landlord had served cold fowl and a dish of trout with anxious ceremony, as if good attendance might soften disappointing news. Jane's letter lay apart from the London ones, marked already by Mrs. Gardiner's careful thumb, as if domestic comfort might be kept separate from commercial alarm by the breadth of a table.

"The children are well," her aunt said, for the third time; reassurance had become a household duty. "Jane says Edward has a new tooth, Harriet has made no fresh attempt upon the poultry-yard, and Marianne has attached herself to your mother with such devotion that Mrs. Bennet considers herself both adored and persecuted."

"That is a tolerable account of Longbourn," said Mr. Gardiner.

"It is a very good one," Elizabeth replied. "If Mama has only one child adoring her and no daughters contradicting her at breakfast, she must be in a state of comparative felicity."

Mrs. Gardiner smiled, but it was the smile of one accepting comfort because comfort was required.

Mr. Gardiner turned one of the London letters over, though he had read it twice. "Granger is not a man to exaggerate illness."

"No," said his wife.

"Nor to write three pages when one would serve."

"Which, as you know," said Mrs. Gardiner, "makes the three pages alarming."

Elizabeth looked at the trout and could not have said whether it was good or ill-dressed. She met Mr. Granger twice before, in Gracechurch Street. A dry gentleman with a pen behind his ear, a cough that signified calculation rather than weakness, and a talent for making any commercial matter sound both urgent and dull. If Mr. Granger had taken to his bed, or near enough to it to write for help, it must be dire.

"I had hoped," Mrs. Gardiner said, "that the matter might be one Mr. Fisher could manage. He has been so many years in your service."

"Fisher manages the ledgers brilliantly. But he cannot manage Baxter. It’s taken us years to learn how to do it, and Baxter doesn’t have the advantage of being one of the owners of the business."

"Who is this Baxter?" Elizabeth asked.

"A man who owns cloth, carts, opinions, and an unfortunate belief that all three should arrive precisely when he wishes them to," said Mr. Gardiner. "He has been patient for six years. It seems he has chosen this week to reform his character."

"And Mr. Granger ill." Mrs. Gardiner shook her head. She folded Jane's letter once, then unfolded it again. "We have not been long from Longbourn."

"No," said Mr. Gardiner. "Three weeks, when we set out, if roads, weather, and children permitted. We have had roads and weather. The children, according to Jane, have behaved better than any reasonable traveler has a right to expect."

Elizabeth set her fork down; it struck the plate with a small, neat sound that felt louder than it was.

Another fortnight.

The words ought to have called to mind roads still to be traveled, villages yet unseen, prospects her aunt had named with a girl's bright memory and a woman's moderation. They should not have made her think first of Pemberley.

She was ashamed of the swiftness.

Pemberley was not a destination. It was not even properly an event. It was a house at which she had been received with more kindness than she had deserved, a family into whose concerns she had been accidentally drawn, and a gentleman whose civility had become, in a very inconvenient manner, less easy to dismiss each time she saw it.

Two weeks meant nothing.

It meant everything.

"We shall return with you, of course," Elizabeth said.

She had spoken briskly; that was something. Briskness had always been a useful servant to her, ready to dress reluctance as good sense and feeling as decision. It did not, however, alter the feeling.

Her aunt looked at her, surprise in the tilt of her brows. “Of course? We might well carry on as well on our own, don’t you think?”

“Oh, I don’t know,” said Mr. Gardiner. “Two ladies, alone—”

"My dear," Mrs. Gardiner replied, with the patience of a woman well acquainted with her husband's notions of danger, "we should be in Lambton, not the Sahara. I have three old friends within a quarter-mile and a landlord who has been apologizing since the post-chaise scratched the gatehouse."

Elizabeth cut a piece of fowl with unnecessary precision. "Uncle. You cannot go to London and leave Aunt and me to prosecute a tour by ourselves."

Mrs. Gardiner's mouth opened, shocked. Elizabeth looked down before she could be betrayed into smiling. Smiling would have admitted relief, or pain, or both. She was not sure which had come first.

"It is not that we should be by ourselves in any alarming sense," Mrs. Gardiner said. "We are at Lambton, where I have old acquaintance enough to make solitude difficult. The inn is respectable. The roads we meant to take are no wilderness."

"Still," said Mr. Gardiner, "I do not like leaving you. But I do not like dragging you back to an overheated city and the danger of Granger's fever."

"And I do not like being dragged back," Mrs. Gardiner said, with the candor of a wife too sensible to pretend for the sake of neatness. "But neither do I like going on without you."

Elizabeth placed her fork beside her plate. "Then there is no difficulty. We return."

There. It was said. A proper sentence, composed of duty and good sense. If something in her rebelled at it, that something was not to be consulted. 

Aunt Gardiner refolded Jane's letter, this time exactly along its crease. "Miss Darcy will be disappointed."

The name touched Elizabeth before she could prepare for it; her hand tightened on the stem of her glass, and for a moment all the noise of the room seemed to draw back.

"Oh," she said.

"She wrote so prettily this morning." Mrs. Gardiner set down her glass. "It was a shame to have to tell her no."

Elizabeth turned her own glass once on the tablecloth, and did not look up. Last night. Miss Darcy at the instrument: pale hands, careful courage, the small smile that had escaped after the quick passage and then been caught back like contraband. Miss Darcy laughing at Mr. Gardiner's ignorance of German music. Miss Darcy looking brighter when Elizabeth promised to expect very little.

"She was very kind," she said at last.

"More than kind," said Mrs. Gardiner.

Mr. Gardiner looked from one to the other. "Only Miss Darcy? I seem to recall Mr. Bingley was quite solicitious. And, of course, our esteemed host.” 

Elizabeth did not dare speak of that gentleman. She reached instead for Jane's letter, though she did not need to read it. She needed only the ordinary feel of paper in her hands, Jane's neat familiar script. Jane's hand, neat and even, lay open where Mrs. Gardiner had left the page. *The children continue in excellent health. Mama is in very good spirits, chiefly owing to a new conjecture concerning Mr. Bingley, founded upon nothing whatever. Kitty sends her duty to our aunt and uncle and asks whether the Derbyshire sheep are truly as steep as mountains.*

Kitty. Sheep as steep as mountains. Elizabeth had to smile. It was a small, sad smile.

"I shall write to Miss Darcy," she said. "If Aunt wrote this morning, perhaps the next should come from me. I can thank her for last night and explain that our plans have been altered."

"Kindly," said Mrs. Gardiner.

Elizabeth looked up, into her aunt’s warm, too-observant gaze.

"Yes," Elizabeth said.

It would be easier by letter. A letter permitted gratitude to be measured, regret to be folded into proper phrases, and everything dangerous to be left unsaid. She could be all that was civil at a distance. Distance had become, in a surprisingly short time, one of the most respectable things in the world.

Mr. Gardiner pushed his plate away by an inch. "If I leave tomorrow, you might remain here one more day and follow at a more tolerable pace. Or we may all begin southward together in the morning. There is no reason for my business to make three people uncomfortable at the same rate."

"There is every reason," said Mrs. Gardiner. "One’s family should be uncomfortable together."

"My dear, that is a principle upon which many families have conducted themselves, but I am not certain it has improved society."

Elizabeth rose before the conversation could become too tender to endure. "I will fetch my writing case."

"No need, Lizzy. You haven’t had your coffee. Stay. The ink here is serviceable."

"The pens are not."

Mr. Gardiner looked toward the writing desk near the window. "A grave charge."

"I shall not risk Miss Darcy's opinion of me upon an inn pen. She plays Mozart. She will notice."

Mrs. Gardiner laughed then, and the sound eased the room for a moment.

Elizabeth crossed to the small table near the window where a tray, a chipped blue vase, and the offending pens had been arranged. Outside, Lambton's street moved through its ordinary early afternoon: cart wheels, one child calling to another, two women pausing beneath the inn sign with baskets on their arms. 

She stood there a moment. Ordinary life. In full possession of itself.

She took up a pen. The nib was blunted; she set it aside and took up another, which was worse.

The door opened.

The landlord appeared with a countenance full of importance imperfectly restrained. "Beg pardon, sir. Mr. Darcy and Miss Darcy are below, and ask whether Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet are at home."

Elizabeth's hand closed on the pen.

The nib snapped.

# Chapter 19: A Brave Invitation

In the small dining room, no one spoke for a full second. Then everyone did.

"Mr. Darcy?" said Mr. Gardiner.

"Below?" said Mrs. Gardiner.

"Yes, ma'am," said the landlord, satisfied with the effect. "Miss Darcy is with him."

Elizabeth looked down at the broken pen in her hand as if it had acted without her permission. Ink had not spilled, by some mercy. The point lay upon the tray, small and black and accusing.

Her pulse had collected absurdly in her thumb. A minute earlier, Mr. Darcy had been a thought she must not touch; now he was below stairs, asking to be admitted.

"I beg your pardon," she said to no one in particular.

Mrs. Gardiner rose. "Of course, we are at home, Mr. Pratt. Pray show them into the private parlor. We shall attend them directly."

"Yes, ma'am."

The landlord withdrew.

For long moments, the room became a campaign. Mrs. Gardiner looked toward the glass above the mantel; Elizabeth looked toward the door; Mr. Gardiner looked at both ladies and had the wisdom not to look amused until his back was half turned.

"My cap," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"It is perfectly well.”

"It is perfectly well for luncheon with my husband and niece. It is not perfectly well for Miss Darcy."

"Your cap is better bred than most people we know," said Mr. Gardiner.

"Edward."

Elizabeth crossed to her aunt, untied one ribbon, retied it, and was far too conscious of her own fingers. "There."

"And you, Lizzy?"

"I am very well."

"You have ink on your thumb."

Mr. Gardiner offered his handkerchief with the solemnity of a man presenting a diplomatic instrument. "For the honor of Hertfordshire."

She took it. "I expect Derbyshire has seen worse."

"Not in a private parlor, I hope."

Mrs. Gardiner's eyes met Elizabeth's in the glass. The look lasted only a moment but spoke volumes: compose yourself; do not be ashamed; I see you.

Elizabeth returned the handkerchief and smoothed the front of her gown: the muslin Miss Darcy had already seen at tea, but the short blue jacket changed the silhouette to make it different enough. She hoped.

She smoothed the jacket once more, though there was nothing wrong with it.

"Shall we?" said Mrs. Gardiner.

They went down.

---

As they entered the private parlor, Miss Darcy rose from the sofa near the window.

It was not a large room, but the inn had done the best it could. A saffron carpet covered most of the floor; two chairs sat by the empty grate, two sofas loitered near the window. A round table still bore the landlord's idea of elegance: a bowl of late roses, one dropping petals into the water, and the open casement gave onto the inn yard softened by vine leaves. Voices came and went beyond it.

Mr. Darcy stood near the window. At Elizabeth's entrance, he turned too quickly to have been looking at anything outside with real attention.

Then his attention found her, with an intensity no bow could quite disguise.

He inquired after their health; he expressed regret for having called at an inconvenient hour, though a letter refusing Miss Darcy's invitation had almost been begun upstairs. Yet in the instant before civility restored his face, Elizabeth had seen something unguarded: expectation, checked almost at once; relief, perhaps; and something more singular than mere civility allowed.

The sight went through her before she could summon any answer to it. She felt it first as heat in her face, then as a wish to look away, then as an equally strong wish not to.

Instead, she smiled at Miss Darcy.

That was safer. It was also not safe at all.

Miss Darcy's pleasure at seeing them had escaped before she could make it formal. Her color had risen; her hands were folded together; her curtsey, though correct, had a gladness in it no master could have taught.

"We feared we should miss you," she said.

Elizabeth distrusted how glad she was to be the object of it. "You are very kind,” she said. “We were just speaking of writing to you."

"And so we are fortunate," said Mrs. Gardiner, taking Miss Darcy's offered hands with warm ease. "A letter would have deprived us of the pleasure of thanking you in person for last evening."

Miss Darcy looked pleased and uncertain what to do with so much direct gratitude. "It gave me great pleasure."

Mr. Darcy's gaze moved to his sister. His expression altered in a manner too small to name and too plain to miss. Elizabeth saw pride there, and care too: the care with which he kept pride from becoming a demand.

Mrs. Gardiner took the spot on the sofa nearest Miss Darcy, as if by accident and with too much skill for accident. Mr. Gardiner chose the chair from which he could see everyone and obstruct no one. Elizabeth was left the end of the sofa, which placed her not opposite Mr. Darcy precisely, but near enough that any glance from him had a shorter journey than she liked.

For a few minutes there were inquiries and answers. Mrs. Hurst's health. Mr. Bingley's departure. The road to London. The beauty of the morning. Mr. Gardiner's letters. Here Miss Darcy's attention sharpened, and Mr. Darcy's hand, resting upon the windowsill, stilled.

"Is it true," Miss Darcy began, "that you might be obliged to leave Lambton sooner than you expected?"

Mrs. Gardiner glanced once at Elizabeth. "My husband's partner has been taken ill. The business requires Mr. Gardiner's presence sooner than any of us wished."

Miss Darcy's face softened. "I am sorry to hear it."

"So am I," said Mr. Gardiner, "though less on my own account than on my wife's. She has been promised several Derbyshire prospects and is in danger of receiving only letters from warehouses in exchange."

Miss Darcy smiled. "That *is* a poor exchange."

"A scandalous one," agreed Mrs. Gardiner.

"And the children?" Miss Darcy asked. "They are well?"

"Quite well. We have had another letter from Miss Bennet this morning. They are well settled at Longbourn, and eagerly awaiting a litter of kittens before the week is out."

"Delightful," said Miss Darcy.

A brief look of amusement passed over Mr. Darcy’s face.

“Brother?”

"Pemberley may soon be in the same condition," he said. "Or so my groom has warned me."

"Then Derbyshire and Hertfordshire are in perfect accord," said Mr. Gardiner.

There the conversation might have returned to safe ground. Elizabeth could feel everyone moving toward it: weather, roads, the old church, Mrs. Gardiner's childhood walks. Then Miss Darcy looked down at her hands.

"It seems a pity," she said, "to lose so much of Derbyshire when it was so nearly promised to you."

The sentence was not an invitation. It was a sigh given shape. Elizabeth saw Miss Darcy hear herself and falter. "I mean—only—that Mrs. Gardiner spoke of so many places she remembered. And Miss Bennet has seen very little yet. And if Mr. Gardiner must go—" She stopped.

Mr. Darcy gave her his whole attention, steady as a hand offered but not forced upon her.

Miss Darcy glanced at him and drew a breath. "Oh, I am saying it badly." But she did not retreat. 

"If there were any way for you to remain near Lambton," Miss Darcy said, "I should be very happy."

Mrs. Gardiner leaned toward her by a fraction. "I understand you perfectly," Mrs. Gardiner said, gently enough to make the words kind rather than final.

Elizabeth did not know whether to be grateful or afraid.

For a moment no one supplied the answer Miss Darcy had not quite dared to ask.

Then Mr. Darcy spoke. "Why not?"

The words were quiet. Miss Darcy looked up, not rescued from her own wish, but steadied in it.

"Why should Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet lose the rest of their tour if Mr. Gardiner is obliged to return?” he said. “If they would accept Pemberley as a temporary situation, the arrangement might answer every purpose."

Elizabeth's pulse, which had behaved with tolerable civility through the visit so far, forgot itself. Her hands had closed upon each other before she knew they had moved.

Mr. Darcy continued, and now his voice had altered. It was still formal, but a thread had entered it that made the courtesy feel less general than it sounded. "Pemberley can accommodate such an arrangement without the least inconvenience. My sister would be grateful for your company. Mrs. Gardiner would be within reach of Lambton, and of all the places she wished to revisit. Mr. Gardiner could continue to London with less concern." He paused. "The invitation would, of course, be to Mrs. Gardiner as much as to Miss Bennet—and only for such days as remained convenient within your original plan."

So proper. So complete. So impossible to object to without seeming ungracious, fearful, or both.

Relief stole into Elizabeth before judgment could stop it, and was therefore more alarming than any objection.

Its danger lay in its fairness. With this offer, he had left every person in the room a respectable reason to consent and himself no visible claim upon their gratitude. Mrs. Gardiner's comfort, Mr. Gardiner's peace of mind, Miss Darcy's company, the unfinished Derbyshire tour — all had been considered, arranged, and offered back to them as if his own wishes had no share in it.

And yet she could not believe his wishes had no share in it. She was in danger of thinking well of him. 

Elizabeth gripped her hands, folded very correctly in her lap. They looked composed. They did not make her so.

Miss Darcy added, before her courage could desert her, "I should like it very much. If you wished it. I mean, if it would not be inconvenient."

She stopped, pressed her hands together, and sighed. "Pemberley is so quiet now."

The last sentence did it. Not the logic of carriages and inns and two remaining weeks. Not Mr. Darcy's careful propriety.

Miss Darcy's eyes met Elizabeth's for only a moment, then turned to Mrs. Gardiner. It was not childish pleading. It was a young woman's very careful claim upon pleasure.

Mrs. Gardiner heard it too.

Mr. Gardiner, who had been watching the exchange with the calm seriousness he brought to matters of importance, looked to his wife. "It would certainly relieve me of a concern."

"Yes," said Mrs. Gardiner.

"And it would not lengthen your absence beyond what we had already intended."

"No."

"And Jane's letter is reassuring."

"Yes."

Mrs. Gardiner turned to Mr. Darcy. "You are very good, sir. But we could not impose upon Miss Darcy's kindness."

"You would not impose," said Miss Darcy, more quickly than before. She seemed startled by her forwardness, but did not take the words back. "I should be very glad."

Mr. Darcy looked at Elizabeth then.

Only for an instant. Long enough for Elizabeth to feel that the answer mattered to him in a way no arrangement of rooms and carriages could explain. Then he looked away, as if he had remembered himself.

"It would be a great kindness to my sister," he said to the room.

His voice had changed again on the last two words. My sister. Not a display. Not a tactic.

Elizabeth knew she might still object. She could say that London was safest, that Mrs. Gardiner's duty lay with her children, that a young woman did not remove from an inn to a gentleman's house because the gentleman's sister was lonely and the gentleman himself had learned how to ask without asking. Every sentence would be defensible. Every sentence would be cowardice dressed as prudence.

She said none of them.

Mrs. Gardiner's hand moved in her lap, once, as if closing gently upon a decision.

Elizabeth understood, before her aunt spoke, that she would accept.

She ought to have felt alarm. She did feel alarm. It beat high in her throat and made the room too warm. But pleasure was there too: the sudden image of Pemberley in morning light. Miss Darcy beside her at the piano. Mr. Darcy somewhere in the same house, and herself with every proper reason to remain. The two feelings had come together, and she could not separate them.

"We should be honored," Mrs. Gardiner said, "if Miss Darcy is certain she would not repent such kindness."

Miss Darcy looked as though repentance had never been further from her mind. "I am certain."

Mr. Gardiner smiled. "Well, then. I shall go to London with a lighter conscience than Baxter deserves."

The remark restored the room to motion. Miss Darcy smiled outright, freely, as if she had forgotten to be careful. Mrs. Gardiner began at once upon the practical questions: luggage, rooms, carriages, timing. Mr. Darcy answered with calm precision: Mr. Gardiner's departure, the carriage, the trunks, no haste, no obligation, only what suited Mrs. Gardiner best. Within minutes, what had been impossible before noon had acquired dates, rooms, carriage-times, and a measure of anticipation.

Nothing was to exceed the time they had already meant to spend in Derbyshire. That point was settled plainly, and somehow made the whole thing more difficult to distrust.

Only then did Elizabeth realize how still she had been. She let out her breath carefully, as if too sudden an exhale might reveal too much.

She had wished to stay.

He had made it possible to stay.

It was all perfectly proper.

That was the trouble.

# Chapter 20: Proper Company

The morning after their removal to Pemberley found Elizabeth in possession of several privileges to which no young woman of sense could reasonably object: a chamber near her aunt’s, a bell she did not mean to ring, a writing table placed exactly where the light was best. She had only to rise from a bed whose smooth linen still seemed an extravagance, and she was already at Pemberley. No horses to order, no carriage to endure, no road between anticipation and arrival.

This last circumstance was the most difficult to grasp. Pemberley had, until now, required preparation. It had required a bonnet, a road, a turn in the drive, the gathering of composure while stone and glass rose into view, the knowledge that one must enter, admire, behave. This morning there had been no approach. She had opened her eyes, and Pemberley was before her.

Her chamber was sizable and airy, but not one of the grand rooms. Grandeur might have been resisted. A state bed, a painted ceiling, a prospect arranged for admiration — all these would have reminded her that she was temporary, insignificant, and very properly overwhelmed. But this room, with its pale blue hangings, sober rug, small fire-screen, and view over the rising ground, seemed arranged to put a guest at ease rather than overpower her. 

Her trunk had stood at the foot of the bed for all of half a day. Some quiet and very efficient person had carried it away, after distributing her clothing into drawers and presses that opened smoothly, setting her brushes where they ought to be, and leaving no sign that arrival had required effort at all. Her gloves lay upon the chair where she had left them. A volume, placed there by some considerate hand, waited upon the little table as if young ladies had always been expected to read at Pemberley before breakfast.

Mr. Gardiner had accompanied them to the great house. He had kissed his wife with tenderness, charged Elizabeth to prevent her aunt from climbing every hill in Derbyshire merely because she had climbed it twenty years before, and thanked Mr. Darcy with a frankness which, had it been less sincere, might have been easier for Elizabeth to bear. Mr. Darcy received it as he received everything that morning: with exact attention, no flourish, and an absence of condescension which made gratitude harder to dismiss.

There had been no officious display of rooms, servants, carriages, views, advantages, or obligations. Mrs. Gardiner’s comfort had been consulted before she had time to request it. Elizabeth’s chamber sat very appropriately beside her aunt’s. Miss Darcy had shown them the way herself, shyly pleased and more animated with every door she opened.

By the next morning, Mrs. Gardiner had accepted their situation with a composure Elizabeth envied. Her aunt had written to Longbourn, arranged that Mr. Gardiner should send word from London on his arrival, praised the linen, inquired whether Miss Darcy walked before or after luncheon, and displayed no uneasiness beyond that of a woman who suspected Derbyshire weather of plotting against her bonnet.

Elizabeth, less usefully employed, found herself wandering after breakfast with no better purpose than to discover what sort of place Pemberley became when it was at ease.

As a visitor, she had been conducted. As a caller, she had been received. As a guest, it appeared, she might move freely, quietly, at whatever pace she chose, without being summoned, guided, or corrected. It was a dangerous degree of liberty; she had already proved unequal to it by leaving her gloves upon a chair.

The house altered under such liberties. Rooms she had admired three days ago were now simply in use. A door stood open where a servant had passed. A vase had been moved since yesterday. A chair near a window bore the slight displacement of recent occupation. In an upstairs sitting room, a book lay open upon a table with a paper-knife beside it, abandoned mid-chapter. Elizabeth did not take it up. That would have been too much like confidence. But she read the title as she passed, and touched the margin with one finger before she knew she had done it.

Pemberley was not less grand for these proofs of life. Instead, it was more difficult to resist. For one foolish instant she could imagine going on in just such a manner: passing through halls without being conducted, knowing which room held the morning sun, leaving gloves where some silent hand would restore them, finding windows open and fires laid and every convenience arranged before she had thought to desire it.

The impertinence of the fancy stopped her where she stood.

To imagine admiring Pemberley had once been dangerous enough. To imagine being at ease in it was worse. She could hear her mother’s voice in the very next breath — *Oh, Lizzy! only think what a house!* — and the mortification was so complete that Elizabeth almost laughed aloud. Nothing could more effectually cure a young woman of presumption than discovering Mrs. Bennet already at the end of it, arranging the drawing-room chairs and asking whether the plate was all real silver.

She had meant only to look for Mrs. Gardiner, who had been drawn away by Miss Darcy to consult over some music. Instead, Elizabeth took a wrong turning — or persuaded herself she had — and found herself in the portrait gallery.

Unlike the first time she’d seen it, the gallery was empty, and the Darcys on the walls appeared to have completely recovered from the inconvenience of being exhibited to strangers.

Elizabeth strolled slowly beneath them.

These silent sitters could not object to her. They could not know of Meryton assemblies, ill-judged speeches, mortifying mothers, flirtations, refusals, letters, or younger sisters tempting fate and folly . The living Mr. Darcy knew much of it.

She stopped before a portrait of a young woman in pale silk, one hand resting on the shoulder of a little girl who looked out with solemn eyes. The lady’s expression had that composed sweetness which painters bestowed upon women of rank, whether they possessed it or not. The child, however, had escaped into something nearer truth. She appeared not unhappy, exactly, but watchful; loved, perhaps, and instructed; cherished and alone.

Another Miss Darcy.

No — Georgiana. The correction mattered. The permission had been given after dinner the previous evening, with much color and a courage so evidently gathered for the purpose that Mrs. Gardiner had accepted it as gravely as if it were a treaty. Elizabeth had said the name only once since, and had felt, absurdly, as if she had been trusted with a key.

Not another composed young lady arranged for admiration; not the accomplished musician, not the heiress, not merely the sister guarded by a brother who would have fenced the world out — and had learned, once, how easily a fence might be climbed. But Georgiana: the young woman who had looked almost startled when Mrs. Gardiner praised a country tune; who had brightened at the prospect of kittens; who had admitted, with all the courage of a much bolder creature, that Pemberley was quiet now.

In the distance came the sound of a pianoforte. A phrase began, faltered, and was taken up again from the beginning. A bar was repeated with conscientious care, as if the player had been accused by no one and had nevertheless found herself guilty. 

Elizabeth left the portraits to their propriety and followed the music.

The music-room door stood partly open. She paused before entering, less from hesitation than from an unwillingness to disturb what she had found.

Georgiana sat at the instrument, her profile bent over the music. Mrs. Gardiner stood beside her, one hand resting lightly on the top of the piano, following the troublesome passage as if her attention alone might help it into order. As always, her aunt had found a way to make herself useful.

Elizabeth entered quietly. Georgiana did not seem to notice her, but Aunt Gardiner caught her gaze and smiled a welcome.

Elizabeth settled herself in the deep embrasure of the east window, where a cushioned bench and two pillows made comfort almost indecorously easy. Looking out, she could see the sweep of the hill down to a small stream; looking in, she could see the player and her aunt, both with the glow of morning light around them. 

The gentle scene pressed upon her more than a formal entertainment could have done. No one asked Georgiana to astonish them. No one praised her into discomfort. No one made her accomplishments a tax she owed to the room. Elizabeth took up neither work nor book. For once, idle hands did not feel like negligence; they felt like permission.

When Georgiana recommenced the piece, Mr. Darcy appeared at the door.

Elizabeth knew he had entered before she turned and saw him, for Georgiana’s playing altered by the smallest degree — not worse, but perhaps a bit more careful. He seemed to perceive it too. He did not advance into the room, but remained near the door, listening with an expression Elizabeth could not readily name. Pride was there, certainly. Affection also. But beneath both lay a vigilance which had begun to trouble her.

A brother might delight in a sister’s music. Mr. Darcy seemed always prepared to defend his sister against censure, however slight, and from quarters Elizabeth could not yet see.

Mrs. Gardiner turned the page. Georgiana crossed one of the difficult passages neatly this time, and Mr. Darcy’s face changed. The change would have escaped anyone not already in the habit of watching him; Elizabeth was vexed to discover that the habit had formed without her consent.

When the piece ended, Georgiana looked first to Mrs. Gardiner.

“There,” said Mrs. Gardiner. “Mozart deserves you.”

Georgiana shook her head, but smiled. “Shall I try the sonata next?”

“By all means.”

They looked round as Mr. Darcy entered the room. He bowed to Mrs. Gardiner. “Ladies,” he said. “Pray, continue.” 

Georgiana turned back to the music, sifting the sheets with Mrs. Gardiner to find the new piece. 

Mr. Darcy held a book in one hand, and moved at once toward the chair nearest the east windows, its little round table set beside it as if for his habitual use. Then he saw (too late to pretend he had not seen) that Elizabeth already occupied the window-seat nearest the chair. He paused, no longer than a breath, but it made his decision visible. He came on nevertheless. 

His eyes met Elizabeth’s only long enough to acknowledge her presence. She looked down at her hands and found nothing in them to distract her.

When he sat, the chair placed him at an angle to the window, near enough for speech but turned sufficiently toward the instrument to make silence proper. The light fell cleanly on the open pages, and his profile was presented to Elizabeth as if the arrangement had been intended for his reading alone.

For a little while they attended the music. Then silence, instead of remaining silence, began to acquire weight.

“Miss Darcy seems happy this morning,” Elizabeth said, keeping her voice low enough not to travel beyond him.

Mr. Darcy’s attention did not leave his book. “You are kind to think so.”

“No—kindness is easy to grant; I am not in the habit of granting what is easy.”

He turned then, enough to show that he had understood more than the surface of her remark. Elizabeth wished, too late, that she had not begun so near the heart of the matter. But Georgiana was bending over the music with her aunt, pointing to some favorite passage, and the sight gave courage to what had only been thought.

“Miss Darcy must have had many excellent instructors,” Elizabeth said.

“She has been fortunate in them.”

“And in her brother.”

His expression closed by a degree. “I hope she has not been unfortunate.”

“No,” said Elizabeth. “No one could think that.”

The correction was too quick. Mr. Darcy’s eyes lifted from the page to her face, and his hand stilled upon the book, as if the answer had struck him more forcibly than praise. 

Elizabeth looked away, at Georgiana. “But excellent instructors cannot be everything.”

Mr. Darcy did not answer at once.

Georgiana began another piece: something lighter than the last, with a turn in it that seemed to please her, though she played it twice before she trusted it. The morning had arranged itself into a picture of perfect propriety: music, open door, Mrs. Gardiner within easy hearing, and Mr. Darcy no nearer than courtesy allowed. Nothing in the world could be safer, yet Elizabeth felt strangely without defence.

“No,” Mr. Darcy said at last. “They cannot.”

The answer was too measured to be agreement.

Elizabeth glanced at him, and away again. “You think I am presuming.”

“I think,” he said, and stopped, as if rejecting the first answer that had offered itself. “My sister has been fortunate in Mrs. Annesley.” The lady sat happily knitting by the little fire, the sort of presence one ceased to notice until one required it.

“I do not doubt it. Mrs. Annesley appears all kindness and sense.”

“She is both.”

“And yet she cannot be sixteen for Miss Darcy.”

He looked at her then—quite at her; and she felt, at once, that she had drawn him from every other thought.

Elizabeth had not meant to say it so plainly. The sentence had come while she was watching Georgiana’s hands: so accomplished, so careful, so young. But having said it, she would not recall it.

Mr. Darcy’s face altered very little, one of his most inconvenient habits.

“She is not much alone,” he said.

Elizabeth felt the justice of the answer, and its insufficiency. “That is not quite what I meant.”

At the pianoforte Georgiana paused. Mrs. Gardiner turned the page back, and the passage began again. Elizabeth waited until the music covered them.

“I am sure she has every attention,” she said. “More than most young women could hope for. But attention is not the same thing as company.”

Mr. Darcy’s hand, which had rested beside the book, closed on the carved arm of the chair.

Elizabeth marked it at once, and knew she ought to stop. She continued.

“When I was a girl,” she said, “I was improved by the society of my sisters, though not, perhaps, in any way a governess would have thought worth praising. I learned little method from them, and less moderation. But I was made less solemn by it. Less watched. There is a kind of ease that cannot be taught by older people, however much they love us.”

He looked away from her, toward Georgiana.

For several bars there was no answer. Elizabeth began to think he would not give one. Then he said, “Ease is desirable. It is not always safe.”

So that was his first principle: not pride, but fear.

Elizabeth might have pitied him for it, had fear been less tyrannical in its conclusions. Instead it irritated her, because it was too reasonable to be dismissed and too narrow to be accepted.

“No,” she said. “Not always.”

“You will understand,” he said, with a formality that did not quite hide the effort beneath it, “that my sister’s situation requires some caution.”

“I do understand it.”

“Do you?”

The question was low, yet it struck more sharply for its quietness.

Georgiana had bent closer to the music; a curl had slipped against her cheek. Her whole attention seemed fixed upon the keys. Elizabeth understood then that Mr. Darcy’s caution did not begin with rank, or fortune, or the ordinary hazards of a young heiress. It began with one man’s name.

*Mr. Wickham.*

The name entered the room as surely as if it had been spoken.

Elizabeth clasped her hands tightly. What if it had been Lydia in Wickham’s sights? 

Perhaps it had been.

Brighton.

“I understand more than I once did,” she said.

He turned back to her. Something crossed his face. Pain, quickly restrained.

“I beg your pardon,” he said. “My question was ungenerous.”

“It was not unjust.”

“I did not mean to imply—”

“That I have no right to speak? No. I did not take it so.”

This was not wholly true. It had been exactly how she had taken it for the first half-second. The second half had been less comfortable.

Mr. Darcy seemed to hear both halves.

“I know you speak from kindness to her,” he said.

The words were civil, and therefore more provoking. They granted motive and refused judgment.

Elizabeth drew a breath and let her eyes return to Georgiana, who was trying the same difficult turn once more with patient obedience. “Then let me speak from kindness a little further, and you may think me impertinent after.”

His jaw tightened once, and settled again. “I shall endeavor to postpone it.”

It was so unexpected, and so grave in delivery, that Elizabeth nearly smiled. 

“Miss Darcy might benefit from a companion nearer her own age.”

There. The thought, once a half-formed conviction, had become a sentence. It stood between them with shocking solidity.

Mr. Darcy said nothing.

Elizabeth wished he would object at once. Silence gave too much room to imagination. It allowed her to hear every possible answer: *Impossible. Unsuitable. Presumptuous. A Bennet? Untenable.*

“A companion,” he said.

“Yes.”

“Young ladies of her age are not always—” He stopped again, but this time the word he had withheld was plain enough.

Safe.

Elizabeth heard it and could not entirely condemn him for it.

“Not always,” she said. “But neither are older ladies always perceptive. Nor accomplished ones always kind. Miss Bingley was very proper company.”

Mr. Darcy’s head turned a fraction, a small, involuntary recoil, checked almost at once.

Elizabeth had not meant to use Miss Bingley as a weapon. Or perhaps she had. It was difficult to be certain of one’s virtue in the middle of an argument.

“She is not an example I would choose for my sister,” he said.

“No. But she is an example the world would choose for her.”

His answer did not come.

Elizabeth pressed on before courage could cool into prudence.

“A young woman may be guarded from impropriety and still be made miserable by what everyone calls suitable. I do not say Miss Darcy is miserable. I only say—” She stopped, because only say had become the refuge of people saying too much. “I only wonder whether she has enough easy society.”

Georgiana started the passage over again.

Mr. Darcy’s expression turned severe; his eyes remained elsewhere. “You have someone in mind.”

It was not a question.

Elizabeth could have retreated there. She could have said only that the principle seemed worth considering. She could have left Kitty unnamed and returned to Mrs. Gardiner with her dignity less endangered.

But Kitty’s face came to her with painful vividness: Kitty sulking in the parlor, Kitty beginning three sentences before finishing the fourth, Kitty standing in her father’s library with courage enough to be frightened, Kitty laughing too loudly over something foolish because no one had taught her how to be pleased in moderation.

Kitty, who was not Lydia.

“My sister Catherine,” Elizabeth said.

Mr. Darcy’s hand left the chair.

It was not a large movement. It did not need to be.

Elizabeth felt the old mortification rise at once, hot and familiar. There was the answer before he had spoken it. Not Miss Catherine. Not a Bennet. Not one of those younger sisters whose want of propriety had once been written of with such pitiless justice.

She lifted her chin by a degree. “You need not look so alarmed, sir. I have not ordered her trunk.”

His eyes returned to hers. “I did not mean—”

“No. Of course not.”

That was unfair, and she knew it while saying it. The knowledge did not improve her temper.

Georgiana stumbled over a note. Both Elizabeth and Mr. Darcy turned toward her at once. Mrs. Gardiner murmured something kind; Georgiana gave a little laugh of apology and began the measure again.

The shared motion had checked them more effectively than politeness.

When Mr. Darcy spoke again, his voice was lower. “Miss Catherine is very young.”

“She is the same age as Miss Darcy.”

He let out a short, impatient breath.

“I do not claim she is wise,” Elizabeth pushed on. “I do not claim she is accomplished. I do not even claim she would not say something foolish before the first hour was over. But she has warmth. She has spirits. And she is not—”

She stopped.

She had nearly said *she is not Lydia*. To speak the sentence aloud would be to injure Kitty in defending her and Lydia in condemning her. It would also confess too much to Mr. Darcy of Longbourn’s inward divisions.

Elizabeth tried again. “She might like Miss Darcy for herself.”

The words, once spoken, revealed the true center of her argument.

Mr. Darcy’s gaze moved to Georgiana, who was nodding agreement at something Mrs. Gardiner had said over the music.

“For herself,” he repeated, almost too soft for Elizabeth to hear.

His expression altered—not enough for triumph, but enough to show that the argument had found its mark.

Elizabeth saw it and was foolish enough to hope.

Then he said, “And if Miss Catherine should be led again by livelier spirits than her own?”

Hope withdrew.

Elizabeth tilted her head, and looked directly at him. “At Pemberley?”

“At Pemberley, in Lambton, at Longbourn, anywhere. Influence is not confined to the house in which it begins.”

“That is a very comprehensive objection.”

“It is an honest one.”

The civility of that answer cost her more than a sharper one would have done.

Mr. Darcy’s face tightened. “Miss Bennet—”

“No, pray do not apologize. You are responsible for your sister. You must consider what is best for her.”

“I must.”

“And I am responsible for thinking well of mine where I can.”

The music stopped.

It was not the end of the piece. Georgiana had only paused to ask Mrs. Gardiner something about the fingering, but the break came at the worst possible instant. Elizabeth’s last sentence seemed to remain in the open air, where anyone might have collected it.

Mrs. Gardiner turned another page with admirable composure. “I think here, my dear. Though you must not trust me beyond counting to four.”

Miss Darcy laughed. 

The piece began again.

Elizabeth had time to recover herself. She did not recover.

Mr. Darcy, for his part, looked as if he had found himself in the wrong and could not yet discover the honorable path out of it. The acknowledgment checked her resentment, though it did not wholly disarm it.

“If not Kitty,” Elizabeth said, surprising herself by the steadiness of her voice, “then someone else. That was my true meaning. A respectable young woman from Lambton, perhaps. Someone known to Mrs. Reynolds. Someone whose family is steady, whose manners are modest, whose situation would make the invitation a kindness rather than a condescension.”

Mr. Darcy looked more alarmed at this than he had at Kitty.

Elizabeth stared at him. “Worse?”

The word escaped before she could dress it properly.

His brows drew together. “Not worse.”

“Only impossible in a different direction.”

“That is not what I said.”

“It is what you looked.”

He took that with more justice than most men would have done.

“A local girl,” he said carefully, “would be known here.”

Elizabeth waited.

“Known by the servants. By every family in Lambton. Her father’s shop, or farm, or connexions — whatever they were — would enter with her before she had crossed the threshold.”

“That need not make her wretched.”

“It might.” His eyes remained on Georgiana. “If she were treated as companion, she might be thought beneath friendship. If treated as friend, she might become an object of envy before she had done anything to deserve it.”

Elizabeth had not expected that answer.

It was not contempt. It was worse. It was consideration.

“You have thought of everything,” she said.

“No.”

The word was immediate.

He looked at Georgiana.

“I have thought of too many dangers,” he said. “That is not the same thing.”

Elizabeth’s anger lost its footing.

She did not know what to do with an honest man.

Another man might have made more of it. Mr. Darcy seemed almost displeased to have allowed the words out. But they changed the air between them more than any apology could have done.

Elizabeth found she could not answer at once.

In the silence, Georgiana finished the piece. Mrs. Gardiner praised her with exactly enough warmth. Georgiana rose from the bench and turned toward them, her face brighter than it had been when Elizabeth entered.

“Brother,” she said, “did you hear? I did not lose myself at the second turn.”

“Very good.”

The two simple words transformed Georgiana. She looked younger, shone brighter.

She laughed and came away from the instrument with the music still in her hand. “I am afraid you have discovered that I possess more courage than command,” she said to Mrs. Gardiner.

“Then I have discovered a very common accomplishment. My dear Georgiana, if you truly wish to hear young ladies perform with more courage than command, you need only invite Lizzy to sing before dinner.”

“Aunt!” cried Elizabeth; such an attack could not go unanswered.

“I have said nothing against your singing. I have only described it with family accuracy.”

Georgiana’s giggle came freer this time. Even Mr. Darcy made a sound that might have been interpreted as a chuckle.

Elizabeth received the attack with all the dignity available. “You see how I am treated in Derbyshire, Georgiana. I had hoped Pemberley might be a refuge from family truth.”

“Pemberley has never been secure against truth,” said Mr. Darcy.

The words were mild. They might have meant nothing. But Elizabeth made the mistake of looking at him. He was not smiling now, nor was he stern; his countenance had settled into something thoughtful and proud and uneasy. 

He had not yielded. Still, she had the mortifying comfort of believing that he had heard her.

# Chapter 21: An Acquaintance First

The breakfast-room at Pemberley was at its best before the house had agreed what the day should become. The morning light entered low and pale across the Turkey carpet; the silver on the sideboard gave it back without brilliance; the servants moved with that quiet exactness which made comfort appear almost accidental. There was toast under a cover, coffee steaming in the urn, a dish of eggs before the fire, and no one at the table but Georgiana. Mrs. Annesley had gone to Lambton the evening before to sit with her sister, who was ill.

Darcy stopped on the threshold.

He had not expected her so early. Yesterday — the first full morning with Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet under the roof — Georgiana had come down ten minutes later than her usual hour, glancing toward the door as if expecting someone worth waiting for. She had adjusted her schedule to Caroline's from duty, during the Bingley party's stay. This was different. Georgiana, who had once been punctual from obedience, had become unpunctual from hope.

Yet here she was, seated at the end of the table with a cup of chocolate before her and a shawl of Norwich silk drawn close about her shoulders. She would not wear it when the guests came down — she had taught herself that much, after Mrs. Annesley had gently observed that shawls with fringe were not kind to nervous fingers in company. But at breakfast, before the day required her to be composed, she allowed herself this one soft thing.

"Good morning," he said.

She looked up quickly, and the look had in it the old startle and something newer beneath it. Pleasure, perhaps. Expectation not disappointed.

"Did you sleep badly?"

"No." She considered, as if truth required more exactness than comfort. "Not badly. I was thinking."

He had not slept well himself. Miss Bennet's words from the evening before had followed him upstairs and had not had the grace to leave him at his door.

Darcy took his usual chair. A servant moved at once to attend him; he accepted coffee, refused eggs, accepted toast because refusal would occasion another movement, and waited until the man had withdrawn. The door closed. The room settled.

Georgiana looked at her chocolate.

He had meant to say nothing. He had meant to allow her all the ease she could claim before the guests appeared. But her hands were too still upon the table, and Georgiana at sixteen ought not to have hands so practiced in stillness.

"What were you thinking of?"

She colored at once.

This did not reassure him either.

"Only—" She stopped. Her fingers found the fringe of her shawl and worked it once before letting go. "Only that the house feels different."

Darcy said nothing.

It did. He had known it since the first evening, and more dangerously since yesterday. Pemberley had altered before under company. Bingley brought noise and good humor; Caroline brought arrangement; the Hursts brought trunks, complaints, and a degree of indolence that settled into the furniture before they did. His aunt's visits filled rooms with solemn consequence before she had crossed their thresholds. But this was not that.

Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet had not occupied Pemberley. They had entered it, moved through it, and somehow left it altered.

The breakfast-room knew it. The second cup placed beside Georgiana's was not for Mrs. Annesley. The chair at Darcy's left had not been moved, yet he knew, with mortifying precision, that Miss Bennet had sat there yesterday and had argued with his sister about whether Derbyshire butter deserved its reputation. She had not argued solemnly. Georgiana had laughed over it.

"Different in what way?" he asked.

Georgiana lifted her eyes to him. "Pleasanter."

"Yes," he said, and heard how little the word contained.

"It is pleasant to have someone younger in the house."

Someone whose presence did not turn every sentence into a lesson.

Not a companion. Not a master of music, of classics, of riding. Not Mrs. Annesley, whose kindness was a steady lamp and whose age, though not great, belonged to another country. Not Caroline, whose accomplishments had entered every room before her and left no air for anyone else.

Someone younger.

Darcy sipped his coffee. 

"Miss Bennet has been kind to you."

"Yes." Georgiana's answer came at once. "Kind, but also—" She searched for the word, finally settling on, "She speaks as if I may answer."

The coffee had cooled. He had not tasted it.

"Do others not?"

"They do." She was distressed by the implication at once. "Mrs. Annesley always does. And you do, Fitzwilliam."

"But?" he said.

Georgiana bent her head. "But Miss Bennet seems to expect that I will."

So slight an alteration, and yet the whole balance had shifted.

He had not meant to make a life in which his sister had forgotten to expect anything. He had not meant protection to become a room so carefully furnished that its occupant did not know whether any chair was meant to be used. He had given her masters, a companion, music, books, even London — as if noise and crowds could cure what his caution had made. Every attention he could conceive and every caution he could afford.

He turned his cup a quarter-inch upon its saucer.

*Attention is not the same as company.*

Miss Bennet's voice, returning with unwelcome clarity.

He broke the edge of his toast with his knife.

"That is a valuable quality in a friend," he said.

Georgiana looked up.

"A friend," she repeated.

"An acquaintance," he corrected, too quickly. "At first."

Her eyes moved back to her plate, but not before he had seen the hope there. It was small, almost ashamed of itself.

"Of course."

He had done it again. Given the gift with one hand and measured the danger of it with the other.

Darcy pushed his toast aside.

"And Mrs. Gardiner?”

"Oh, Mrs. Gardiner is so easy. She is never—" Georgiana stopped — the same abrupt retreat he had seen a hundred times this past year when she feared she had spoken past her allowance. "Never waiting to be pleased,” she finished.

Darcy was obliged to look away for a moment. Through the window, the prospect was fair and dry. Another hot day.

"Mrs. Gardiner has much sense," he said.

"And Miss Bennet has spirits." Georgiana considered this, then added conscientiously, "Not noisy spirits. Not like—" She stopped again.

Darcy knew who had almost been named. Liveliness, in Georgiana's experience, had worn a handsome face and an easy laugh, and it had nearly destroyed her. The terror of Ramsgate had not faded; it had only learned to be quiet.

But not all liveliness was ruin.

The sentence sounded like Miss Bennet. He was not pleased to discover how thoroughly her opinions had colonized his own.

Georgiana's fingers returned to the fringe of her shawl.

Darcy set down his knife.

He had not meant to raise this at breakfast. He had not meant to raise it at all until he had spoken with Mrs. Gardiner. But Georgiana's hands were still, and her hope was visible, and the morning would not wait for a better-furnished intention. 

"What if we invited a young lady," he said. 

Georgia gave him all her attention

"Someone nearer your own age. She might visit for a time. Some time, if the arrangement proved satisfactory."

Her eyes widened. "Who?"

He had not expected the question so soon. Foolishly.

"A daughter of one of your friends?” Eagerness quickened her voice, and sharpened it. "Or—someone from Lambton?"

"No."

"Then—" She stopped, uncertain.

Darcy's left hand gripped the side seam of his trousers, beneath the edge of the table, where she could not see it.

"Miss Catherine Bennet," he said.

Georgiana's lips parted.

"Kitty?" She leaned back a shade, but did not retreat. "Miss Elizabeth called her Kitty when she told us of Jane's letter yesterday afternoon. She said no one at Longbourn could remain Miss Catherine for above four minutes togethe."

Darcy should not have found that pleasing.

He did.

"What else did Miss Bennet tell you?"

Georgiana's face brightened. "Just in the past week, Kitty had devised a way of keeping Mrs. Gardiner's two eldest children from quarreling over battledore and shuttlecock. They would not obey the nurse, because each believed the other had begun it. Miss Catherine told them there must be a court."

His brows rose.

"Yes, a court. She made the youngest child the judge, because he could not understand the matter and would therefore be impartial. The others were obliged to argue their case before him, and every time they interrupted one another she made them bow to the judge and begin again."

He could just picture it. Despite himself, Darcy laughed.

Georgiana laughed too, softly and with relief, as if his amusement had given her permission for her own.

"And did this produce justice?"

"I do not think so. Miss Elizabeth said the judge ate part of the evidence."

"The evidence?"

"A piece of cake. It had some bearing on the quarrel. It wasn’t clear how."

"Then he was well chosen. Many judges have disposed of evidence less agreeably."

Georgiana smiled down at her chocolate. "Miss Catherine made them all laugh, at least. And then they forgot to quarrel."

There was the point, as plainly as if Elizabeth had placed it before him in writing: He had been guarding against the wrong peril.

Kitty Bennet, whom he had not troubled himself to distinguish from her youngest sister, had managed to keep children from discord with nothing but invention and a piece of cake. She had governed, not by authority, but by wit. It was not polish. It was not a quality Caroline Bingley would have praised, or, having recognized it, have thought worth praising. It was something less safe on paper and more valuable in a room.

"And Miss Elizabeth Bennet thought well of it?" he asked.

"She did. Though she did say that Kitty was much improved by having persons smaller than herself to command, for it prevented her from being the smallest person in every argument."

He could hear Elizabeth in the line — the tenderness under the wit and the apology under the tenderness.

*My sister Catherine.*

Miss Bennet's chin had lifted when she said it. Not *a Bennet*.  Not one of the family he had judged and written of and judged again.

He had thought only of the dangers.

"Would you like such a person to visit?" he asked.

Georgiana's smile vanished. Not from reluctance. From astonishment.

"Here?"

"If it could be arranged properly."

"At Pemberley?"

"Yes."

"To visit me?"

The last words were very quiet.

Darcy had thought himself prepared. He was not.

"Yes," he said. "If you wished it."

Her eyes dropped. She pressed her lips together. "Miss Catherine might expect—"

"What?"

"That I should be entertaining. Or accomplished. Or very grand."

"I do not think Miss Catherine sounds very grand herself."

He heard his own answer. Too prompt. Too grim.

Georgina glanced up at him. "Is she very improper?"

The question was so direct that he could not protect himself with formality.

"I do not know her well."

"But you are afraid she may be."

He did not answer.

"Because of what happened before," she said.

The urn hissed softly. A footman passed somewhere beyond the door. The house continued. Darcy did not.

They did not speak of Ramsgate. They had spoken of it at first in broken necessities: arrangements, dismissals, letters, what must be done, what must never be said. Then silence had become mercy, and mercy had hardened into law.

Georgiana's hands folded together in her lap.

"I know you are afraid for me," she said. There was no reproach in it. That was worse.

Darcy rose and went to the sideboard, though he wanted nothing there. He stood with his back to her long enough to master his face.

*There is a kind of ease that cannot be taught by older people, however much they love us.*

He returned to his chair.

"Miss Catherine Bennet is young," he said. "Her manner has not always recommended her — though I cannot name a particular fault beyond it. But Miss Bennet believes she has warmth and better sense than has always been allowed to show."

"Miss Bennet would know."

The simple trust of it pierced him.

"She is not infallible," he said, as though a man who had misjudged every Bennet in the county were qualified to say so.

"No." Georgiana hesitated. "But she sees people."

He had no answer to that.

Georgiana pondered a moment. Her hand rose to the table to reach for her cocoa. It paused. She tilted her head in that way women do when they are calculating.

"If Miss Catherine came, Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Elizabeth might stay longer."

Darcy did not move. He was aware, with perfect clarity, that his sister had just said aloud what he had not permitted himself even to think.

"I did not mean—that is, Mrs. Gardiner would be less solitary. And Miss Bennet would have her sister. And if Miss Catherine were uncomfortable, Miss Bennet could help her."

"Georgiana," he said carefully, "Miss Bennet's remaining at Pemberley cannot be the object of any invitation."

"I know." Her voice was small.

"But if Miss Catherine were invited for your sake, and if Mrs. Gardiner approved the arrangement, and if Mr. Bennet consented — then it might be considered."

"Considered," she repeated. Then, more quietly, "But do you wish it?"

He could not refuse her that.

"Yes."

She looked toward the windows, where the morning had strengthened by degrees and now reached clear across the carpet. The light brightened the silk fringe where it lay across her arm.

"A friend," she said, almost to herself.

The color in her face. The smile she was trying to restrain. He thought of all the years in which he had congratulated himself on keeping danger at a distance. He thought of the distance itself.

"Mrs. Gardiner must be consulted," he said.

Georgiana sat straighter. "This morning?"

"If the opportunity offers."

"And if she thinks it unwise?"

"Then we shall attend to her judgment."

Georgiana nodded. It was a reasonable answer. It was also a blow, and he saw her receive it with all the gentleness he had taught her.

He could not bear the success of his own instruction.

"But if she sees no objection," he said, "you may write a note to Miss Catherine — to be enclosed with mine to Mr. Bennet."

Georgiana's breath caught.

Her eyes shone. "Today?"

"Today, if Mrs. Gardiner agrees."

The door opened before she could answer. A servant entered to replenish the coffee, and the breakfast-room became, in an instant, a public place again. Georgiana lowered her eyes to her plate. Darcy took up his cup. 

Mrs. Gardiner would come down soon. Miss Bennet would come with her, perhaps laughing at some private exchange upon the stairs, perhaps grave from a letter, perhaps entirely herself in a way that continued to undo every defense he had arranged against her.

He would not speak to Miss Bennet first. He would not turn this into a triumph for her argument, nor into a confession of his own. He would do the thing properly, if it was to be done at all.

Mrs. Gardiner first. Then his own letter to Mr. Bennet, and Miss Bennet's — with Georgiana's note folded inside hers.

Pemberley had admitted one Bennet under its roof and been altered by it.

He was about to invite another.

# Chapter 22: Letters North and South

The daily post came late to Pemberley. Elizabeth had already learned this from the servants — who mentioned it as one mentions weather, without apology — and from the quality of the waiting, which was different here than at Longbourn. At home, the post was a skirmish: Mrs. Bennet's nerves engaged at the sound of the boy's step, Lydia snatching at anything addressed in a hand she recognized, Mary not looking up from her book but listening with both ears. At Pemberley, letters arrived on a tray. They arrived clean. No one scrambled.

It was, Elizabeth thought, the sort of household in which one might write important letters without anyone making a scene about it.

She was about to discover whether this was true.

The summons came around mid-morning, delivered by a servant whose composure could not quite disguise that he was carrying something unusual: an invitation from Mr. Darcy for Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Bennet to join him at their convenience in his office. 

Not the morning room. Not the drawing room.

Mrs. Gardiner received this intelligence with the calm of a woman who had been entirely at her ease at Pemberley and was not unwilling to be diverted. "His office," she repeated, setting down her teacup. "That sounds rather more deliberate than a breakfast-table remark." She regarded her niece. "Do you know what this is about, Lizzy?"

"I may have some idea."

"Then I shall rely on your countenance to tell me when to be alarmed."

They followed the servant through a corridor Elizabeth had not yet entered — narrower than the gallery, paneled in darker oak, with the practical quiet of a house turned toward its own business. The door stood open. Mr. Darcy sat behind his desk.

Elizabeth could scarcely have supposed she should ever see this room. This was the part of Pemberley that faces the estate, not its visitors. She had pictured something — one could not help it — a library in miniature, perhaps, or a gentleman's retreat arranged for admiration. What she found was much plainer than that and harder to dismiss. 

The desk was large but not ornamental. Papers lay in ordered stacks. The inkstand had been used recently; a quill rested beside it with the nib still dark. Bookshelves lined two walls, but these were working shelves — ledgers mixed with volumes, a folio of maps, estate plans rolled and tied with cord. Through the window, the prospect gave onto the nearer grounds: not the grand south view that visitors admired, but the working landscape of stable-yard, kitchen garden wall, and the lane toward the home farm.

This was not display. It was responsibility, reduced to paper and ink. Here tenants became names, repairs became figures, and good intentions either acquired dates and signatures or were nothing at all.

Georgiana was already seated in one of three comfortably worn wooden chairs placed before the desk. She wore a morning dress of white muslin and a look of determined composure that was betrayed, entirely, by her hands. They were pressed together in her lap with such fierce stillness that the effort showed in her wrists.

"Mrs. Gardiner," Mr. Darcy said, rising. "Miss Bennet. Thank you for coming."

He was formal. Of course he was formal. Elizabeth might have expected nothing else and yet found herself surprised by the degree of it. He had arranged chairs. He had cleared space on the desk.

Mrs. Gardiner took the offered chair in the center with the ease of a woman who could sit in any room in England and look as if she belonged there. Elizabeth sat in the other and found that it placed her at an angle to the desk — near enough to see Mr. Darcy's face but turned slightly toward Georgiana, as if the arrangement had been designed to prevent anyone from looking at anyone else too directly.

"I wished to consult you," Mr. Darcy said, "on a matter concerning my sister."

Elizabeth kept her eyes on Georgiana. It was easier than looking at anyone else.

"It has been suggested," Mr. Darcy began — and looked at Elizabeth.

The glance lasted no longer than a breath. He corrected it at once, returning his gaze to Mrs. Gardiner with the air of a man who had taken a wrong turning and wished everyone to understand that the road was entirely to blame. But the damage was done. Mrs. Gardiner's expression did not change, which was itself a change.

"It has been observed," he continued, more carefully, "that Georgiana would benefit from the society of a young woman nearer her own age. Miss Bingley's recent visit has made plain that accomplished company is not always — that is to say, the qualities most valued in a drawing room are not invariably those which best serve a young woman's spirits."

He stopped. The sentence had cost him something. Elizabeth could hear the seams.

"My sister has expressed a wish to invite Miss Catherine Bennet to Pemberley."

Georgiana's hands tightened in her lap.

Mrs. Gardiner took a breath in, and did not look at Elizabeth. "Miss Catherine," she said, in the considering tone of a woman who wished to be certain she had heard correctly before allowing herself an opinion.

"For a visit of some weeks," Mr. Darcy said. "Under proper supervision, of course. With your approval and Mr. Bennet's consent."

"I see." Mrs. Gardiner was quiet for a moment. Her gaze moved to Georgiana, whose color had risen, and then — with the unerring instinct of a woman who had raised four children and observed two nieces at close quarters for twenty years — to Elizabeth.

"Lizzy," she said. "Is this what you want for your sister?"

The question was simple. And ruthless.

*For your sister.* Not: for yourself. Mrs. Gardiner had not asked the other question — not here, not in front of everyone. But Elizabeth heard it anyway, like a note struck and then damped.

"Yes," Elizabeth said. "I do."

"Catherine is lively," Mrs. Gardiner observed. "And not always discreet."

Elizabeth drew a breath. "She is warm. She notices when people are uncomfortable and tries to mend it — not always gracefully, but sincerely. This past spring was not easy at Longbourn, and Kitty bore her share of it without complaint and without making herself the subject. This month, she has taken on the Gardiner children when Jane could not manage everything alone, and she did it without being asked."

She paused. "She has a steadiness, and a goodness of heart. She will make Georgiana laugh. She will not make her feel watched. Or alone. She has earned my trust, and I think she will earn Georgiana's.”

To her aunt, she added, "She is not Lydia." 

"No," Mrs. Gardiner said quietly. "She is not." 

Elizabeth's throat was tight. She had not known, until she heard herself say them, how much she meant every word.

Mrs. Gardiner regarded her for a moment. Then she turned back to Mr. Darcy.

"If Catherine is to come, she must be properly escorted."

"I shall write to Mr. Bennet directly," Mr. Darcy said. "And offer my carriage for the journey."

He did not look at Elizabeth when he said it. He did not make the offer appear a gallantry. That restraint did more to disturb her than gallantry would have done.

"Very good. A defined visit — three weeks, perhaps, to begin. If it does not answer, the arrangement can be ended with no injury to anyone's pride. If it does answer —" Mrs. Gardiner smiled. "We shall all be pleasantly surprised."

"I shall not be surprised," Georgiana said, very softly.

Every head turned to her.

"That is —" She faltered. "I only meant from what Miss Elizabeth has told me of Kitty already, and it all sounded — it sounded exactly right."

Mr. Darcy's expression changed. Not much. Not enough for anyone who did not already know the precise geography of his reserve. Elizabeth felt it at once — a small, treacherous softening — and Mrs. Gardiner, with the accuracy of a beloved aunt, felt *that*.

Elizabeth's hands, which had every right to betray her, remained perfectly still. That surprised her more than anything else in the room.

---

The letters were planned before anyone left the room. Mr. Darcy would write formally to Mr. Bennet. Georgiana would write a note to Kitty, to be enclosed in Elizabeth's letter to her father. Elizabeth would write to her father — separately, in her own hand — and a note to Jane.

Mrs. Gardiner would write to Mrs. Bennet.

"Someone must," her aunt said, with the resigned valor of a woman who has prevented many impending domestic disasters. "If your mother were to hear this news from anyone but her own sister, I shall never hear the end of it. Neither, I fear, will Derbyshire."

Elizabeth returned to her room to write.

The letter to her father was simple enough in principle and agonizing in practice. She was writing to Mr. Bennet in favor of Mr. Darcy's household. She was recommending that her father send one of his younger daughters into the care of a man whom, at the Netherfield ball, she had declared—very much to her own satisfaction at the time—the last gentleman in England with whom she could ever be prevailed upon to dance.

She dipped her pen. She tapped the ink out, and set the pen down. She picked it up, and dipped it again.

*My Dear Father—*

*You will, I hope, forgive the liberty I take in writing on a subject which has lately afforded you so much amusement at my expense; but as the comfort of one of your daughters is concerned, I shall venture to be serious for once.*

*Mr. Darcy has proposed to invite Catherine to Pemberley for a few weeks, as a companion to his sister, Miss Georgiana Darcy. Mrs. Gardiner approves the plan, provided Catherine comes properly attended; and Mr. Darcy offers his carriage for the journey.*

*If you see no objection, you will oblige me very much by allowing her to accept. I have found Miss Darcy gentle and anxious to do right; and I believe Kitty’s good humour would be of real service to her.*

*I am, &c.* 

The letter to Jane was even more difficult. Elizabeth managed to write three sides—three pages of a single sheet. She described Georgiana's music, the beauty of the Derbyshire hills, the charm of Mrs. Gardiner's renewed acquaintance with Lambton, the excellence of the coffee. She mentioned Kitty's prospective visit as a practical arrangement for Miss Darcy's companionship. She said nothing whatsoever about Mr. Darcy.

She read it back. Georgiana appeared in nearly every paragraph. Mr. Darcy appeared nowhere.

That would have to do.

---

On the fifth morning after the packet left Pemberley, an express arrived. 

Elizabeth knew it for an express before she broke the seal. The handwriting was Jane's, but the exorbitant postage had all the look of her mother’s economy.

*My dearest Lizzy,*

*I hardly know where to begin —*

Not a promising opening, Elizabeth reflected, from a sister whose composure could survive nearly anything.

The letter was long. It was also, in places, very nearly funny, though Jane had plainly not intended it to be. Elizabeth read it at the window of the morning room, with the Derbyshire sun warming the paper, while Georgiana practiced scales and Mrs. Gardiner sat calmly mending a stocking as if the entire county of Hertfordshire had not apparently lost its mind.

Jane reported the following:

Mrs. Bennet, upon receiving her portion of the packet, had required salts, a glass of wine, and the immediate attendance of Hill; and in the agitation of the moment had insisted — before the whole household — that she could not possibly allow any daughter to be sent so far without a mother at hand. She had then gone on, with equal warmth, to wonder why Catherine was to be chosen, when Jane was at home.

Lydia had wailed through breakfast, dinner, and supper for two consecutive days; and the Gardiner children, who are not yet formed to such exertions of patience, suffered accordingly. The weather, happily, was fair, and those who could contrive a walk or an errand were much abroad; but indoors there was little respite from Lydia’s accounts of her hardships, and of the great house and great situation for which she had been destined, and which—by some perverse arrangement of things—had fallen to the one sister who would not know what to do with it.

Kitty did not know what to think and had said so, repeatedly, to anyone who would listen and several people who would not.
