At least one mystery was quickly solved. Mr. Gardiner had stopped at Longbourn for an evening on his way to London and had given a partial account of the Pemberley arrangements, which had satisfied no one and alarmed everyone differently.

The Gardiner children, hearing that their cousin Lizzie might remain in Derbyshire even longer, had declared they would not be left alone with Lydia and Mary when Jane went out in the evenings, as Mary read to them from Fordyce and Lydia made them act out scenes from novels she would not name.

*Aunt Gardiner writes to Mama that you are "very well situated,"* Jane continued, *which I am sure she meant kindly; but the phrase has not quieted me as much as she perhaps intended.*

Elizabeth set the letter down a moment. What had Jane taken from that phrase?

What had their mother?

*I do not wish to distress you, Lizzy, but your letter has made me anxious. Are you quite comfortable at Pemberley? You write pages about Miss Darcy and the land and the pianoforte, and say nothing of Mr. Darcy himself. I cannot forget what you told me of his behavior in Kent, nor can I be easy if you are sacrificing your own peace to help a young girl you have known barely a fortnight.*

*If you are easy, write only that, and I shall believe you. But if you are not, do not make Miss Darcy's comfort of more consequence than your own. Kitty can manage without Pemberley; Miss Darcy has those who love her. We cannot spare you to a duty that is not yours.*

Elizabeth folded the letter. She pressed her hands flat upon the table and sat very still.

Jane had read the silence louder than the words. 

*Are you quite comfortable at Pemberley?*

No. Her room was comfortable enough; her thoughts were not.

Worse, she had begun to defend Pemberley without being asked. She had begun to write of its people as if her opinion might matter to them. And even if she had not mentioned him in her letter, the master of the house was always present.

She picked up the letter and read it again.

---

Two days later, Mrs. Gardiner and Mrs. Annesley had taken Georgiana into Lambton for the afternoon — ribbons, or sheet music, or some errand that required three women and a carriage — and Elizabeth was alone in the morning room when the regular post brought two letters from Longbourn.

The first was addressed to Mr. Darcy in a hand Elizabeth recognized as her father's. It was sealed with the Bennet arms, which Mr. Bennet used so rarely that finding the seal must have required Hill, a candle, and twenty minutes of searching through the library desk.

The second was addressed to her.

There was nothing for Mrs. Gardiner. That was either an oversight or a declaration.

*My dear Lizzy,*

*I have received Mr. Darcy's very civil letter, and your own — with Miss Darcy's kind note to your sister enclosed. I found your letter interesting for what it contained and more interesting for what it did not.*

*I shall bring Catherine to Pemberley myself. She has agreed, after the requisite tears, that she can be ready by Thursday. I shall deliver her into Mr. Darcy's care in good order and retrieve her in three weeks if she has not been sent home sooner.*

*I confess some curiosity to see the place that has wrought such a change in my Lizzy's opinions. I recall a time, not very distant, when a certain gentleman's name could not be mentioned in your hearing without provoking a very decided opinion — but I shall not finish that thought, as I perceive you would rather I did not.*

*Your affectionate father*

Elizabeth read it twice. She read the last line a third time. Then she set it down on the table beside Jane's earlier letter and regarded both as a woman might regard two witnesses who had given testimony she could not refute.

Her father was coming to Pemberley.

Her father, who missed nothing and said everything, was coming to the house where she had been living for a week, at the invitation of a man she had once despised, in defense of a sister she had once been ashamed of. And he would remember every opinion she had ever been pleased to pronounce.

And Mr. Darcy, who had once named her father among the family's failings, would be watching.

The man himself appeared at the morning-room door. He held his own letter from Mr. Bennet — opened, read — and something else: a folded sheet, smaller, written in a round uncertain hand.

"Mr. Bennet has given his consent," he said. His voice was formal. His expression was something else entirely. Elizabeth dared not look long enough to determine what.

"He tells me he is coming himself,” she said to his boots.

Mr. Darcy received this in silence. His eyes lowered briefly to the letter in his hand, and when he looked up again there was, about his mouth, the faintest movement.

“It might have been Mrs. Bennet,” he said, after a moment.

Elizabeth’s gaze jumped to his face. Was he laughing? 

“I have ordered the carriage sent to Longbourn,” he continued. “The coachmen will be instructed to make all reasonable haste.”

"My father does not deliberate when he has decided."

"So I gather." He looked down at the smaller letter in his hand. "Miss Catherine has written to Georgiana."

He held it out.

*Dear Miss Darcy,*

*I am very much obliged to you for your kind letter and for the honor of your invitation. I have never been to Derbyshire but Elizabeth says it is very beautiful and that you are fond of music, which I am too, though not so accomplished as I should like. I hope I shall not be too noisy for Pemberley. Elizabeth says it is a very fine house and I shall try to deserve it.*

*Your obedient servant,*

*Catherine Bennet*

*P.S. — Elizabeth also says you have kittens. I should very much like to see them if it is not too much trouble.*

Elizabeth looked up. Mr. Darcy was watching her face.

"She will do very well," he said.

He did not smile. He did not make a compliment of it. The concession was plain, and therefore worth more.

She handed the letter back. Their fingers did not touch.

"Georgiana will be delighted," she said — and heard, too late, how certain she sounded. As if she had any right to be certain of Miss Darcy’s happiness. As if Pemberley’s pleasures had become matters in which she might speak with confidence.

"Yes," he said. "I think she will."

Jane's words rang in her ears.

*Are you quite comfortable at Pemberley?*

No.

Comfort had very little to do with it.

# Chapter 23: The Carriage from Longbourn

By noon Pemberley had reached that condition of readiness which, in a smaller house, would have passed for agitation and, in this one, expressed itself chiefly through the absence of any visible disturbance.

Elizabeth, who had been learning Pemberley’s habits by degrees, found the effect more oppressive than if half the servants had been running through the passages with linens under their arms. At Longbourn, an expected arrival announced itself everywhere: in her mother’s voice on the stairs, in Hill’s compressed lips, in the wrong cloth laid and removed and laid again, in some younger sister sent on three errands and completing none of them. Here, a chamber was aired, hot water ordered, a fire laid and not lit, a tray adjusted by half an inch, and the house went on as if preparation were a natural property of polished wood and well-trained feet.

Kitty was coming to Pemberley.

The words had looked improbable in a letter. They had become no more manageable in expectation.

In the music room, Mrs. Gardiner sat near the garden-side window with her work in her lap, making a tolerable show of attending to it. Georgiana occupied the chair nearest her, a square of embroidery in her lap that had not advanced by a single stitch since Elizabeth entered the room. Mr. Darcy stood near the mantel with the air of a man who had been placed there for composure and intended to remain until composure had served its sentence.

He had already said, twice, that the carriage could not be expected before one. He had asked Mrs. Reynolds whether the blue room had been aired, though Elizabeth was certain Mrs. Reynolds had aired it already yesterday. He had inquired whether Miss Catherine would prefer tea upstairs on arrival, and had accepted Mrs. Gardiner’s assurance that no young lady, after so long a journey, should be required to know what she preferred until she had washed her face.

“The first kindness to a traveler,” Mrs. Gardiner had said, “is to ask nothing of her for twenty minutes.”

Mr. Darcy had bowed as if she had settled a point of estate law.

Elizabeth had been tempted to laugh. But she could not. She had written the letter that was bringing Kitty here; she had argued, persuaded, promised. If the day went wrong, it would not be Pemberley that had failed, or Georgiana, or even Kitty. It would be Elizabeth, who had asked too much of all of them.

Georgiana's hands had left her needle and lay folded in her lap with that unnatural stillness. A month ago Elizabeth might have taken that stillness for composure. Pemberley had taught her better. Georgiana could arrange herself beautifully; she could sit upright, answer correctly, and keep every finger in its proper place; but hope without occupation looked, Elizabeth thought, very like dread.

“You need not look so solemn, my dear,” Mrs. Gardiner said to Georgiana, without lifting her eyes from her work. “Catherine will be more frightened of you than you can possibly be of her.”

Georgiana lifted her chin — a small, brief motion so like her brother that Elizabeth nearly did laugh. "I am not frightened,” she said.

“No. Of course not. I should have said: our Kitty has had two days in a carriage to imagine Pemberley, and no one imagines sensibly after the first forty miles.”

Elizabeth kept her expression in place by main force. Her aunt was right. Kitty had had two days to imagine Pemberley, and imagination, left to itself, could build something terrifying. She had written, in her round uncertain hand, that she hoped she should not be too noisy for Pemberley.

Too noisy for Pemberley.

Elizabeth had not known whether to laugh or weep over that line. Since then, the words had followed her through two mornings and one very unsatisfactory night.

The door opened. 

It wasn’t the carriage. A servant entered and crossed at once to Mr. Darcy, who turned before the man had spoken, as if he had been expecting not this servant, exactly, but this sort of interruption.

"From Robinson, sir," the man said, low, and held out a folded slip.

Mr. Darcy took it. His eyes moved over it; the paper did not require much reading. "How long ago?"

"Ten minutes, sir."

"The west stall?"

"Yes, sir."

"Tell him I shall come down if there is any change. And send Tom to the lower drive. He is to watch for the carriage and bring word before it reaches the sweep."

"Yes, sir."

The servant withdrew.

Mrs. Gardiner looked up then. “Nothing amiss, I hope?”

“No, indeed. One of the mares is near her time.” Mr. Darcy folded the note with more precision than so small a paper deserved. “My stablemaster is cautious.”

“And you are more so,” said Mrs. Gardiner.

He inclined his head, accepting the charge without contest. “Where livestock is concerned, caution seldom offends me.”

It was said to the room; nevertheless Elizabeth heard in it something of the man she had seen in his office. His responsibility had a shape: messages carried to him softly; decisions made at once; comfort ordered for others; an eye kept upon the drive.

Mr. Darcy slipped the note into his coat pocket and seemed, for the first time that morning, not master of the whole of his countenance. He did not pace. He did not fidget. He only looked once toward the window, though the stables were not visible from it, and then back toward his sister.

“If you wish to play, Georgiana, pray do not wait on our account.”

Georgiana started. “I had not meant—”

“I know.”

Something in the brevity of it released her. She rose, crossed to the instrument, and opened it with the care of a person lifting a lid from water that might reflect too much. She started with a few scales and broken chords, and then began playing a piece Elizabeth had heard before but could not name. Georgiana stopped after six bars and returned to the beginning. Her hands settled as the music took them.

Mr. Darcy listened for half a minute; then, as if decision were a relief in itself, he stepped away from the mantel.

“Mrs. Gardiner—if you will excuse me. I shall go to the west stable.”

Mrs. Gardiner looked up with a smile and a little inclination of the head.

Elizabeth remained only a few minutes longer, till she could not but feel that every moment added to her awkwardness. Mrs. Gardiner’s needle moved steadily. Georgiana repeated the troublesome passage, not badly, but with the fixed attention of one who had given her nerves a task and expected obedience from them.

Elizabeth rose. “I shall take a turn,” she said to the room.

Mrs. Gardiner lifted her gaze to Elizabeth and dropped it again, smiling to herself over her work. Georgiana, at the pianoforte, took no notice.

Elizabeth went out through the glass door to the veranda.

The air held the dry warmth of a summer day not yet resolved into heat. Beyond the terrace, the lawn fell away toward trees, their leaves dappled and moving in a light wind. As she paced, the music inside faded, and the sounds without came forward. At the far edge of the veranda she could see the edge of the drive, a pale curve appearing between the limes, vanishing behind a thicker stand of beeches, then emerging lower down where the land opened.

Below, near the turn where the trees thickened, a boy in short trousers came running into view, stopped, shaded his eyes, then turned toward the house. He saw Elizabeth on the veranda and altered course at once.

“Miss Bennet,” he called, checking himself into something like decorum when he reached the steps. “Beg pardon, miss. The carriage is on the lower drive.”

Elizabeth’s hand, which had been resting on the stone balustrade, closed once. Something tightened below her ribs, brief and unreasonable, as if the news had pressed the air from a room that had no walls.

“Thank you.”

The boy bobbed and ran on toward the side entrance, where messages entered Pemberley as cleanly as letters on a tray.

Elizabeth did not go back to the music room.

She ran down the steps.

The carriage came on through the trees: a dark gloss between the limes, a flash of wheel, the lifted heads of horses that had come a long way and knew they were nearly done. Then it cleared the beeches and was fully before her — Pemberley's own carriage returning with Longbourn inside it.

Elizabeth had thought she was prepared for the sight. She was not.

The horses held a controlled trot. A postilion sat straight. Road dust lay along the lower panels and had gathered in the creases and even touched the Darcy crest on the door. The carriage was elegant, well-sprung, and far too composed for the cargo it carried. It stopped before the steps with a final crunch of gravel that sounded, to Elizabeth's foolish ear, like the end of one arrangement and the beginning of another.

She was at the step before the footman reached the door.

He opened it. Mr. Bennet descended first.

He had traveled better than Elizabeth expected and worse than he would have admitted. His coat was brushed, his neckcloth tolerable, his hat at the correct angle. But there was road dust on one cuff, and his face, when he turned toward the house, had the faintly narrowed look of a man who had been jostled for too many miles and was determined to make amusement pay for the inconvenience.

“Well, Lizzy,” he said, looking up at Pemberley, “I begin to perceive that Derbyshire has arguments.”

“Only now, Papa?”

“I reserved judgment. It is the privilege of age to arrive late at conclusions and call it prudence.”

He kissed her cheek. The kiss was brief, dry, and familiar enough to undo her more than tenderness would have done. 

Then Kitty appeared at the carriage door.

For a full second she did not move.

She had both hands on the frame, one booted foot extended toward the step, her eyes fixed upon the house with a kind of helpless attention. A ribbon had worked loose from her bonnet. Her traveling gown, though neat, bore every mile between Hertfordshire and Derbyshire. She looked like a girl who had been told she was wanted and had spent the whole road north preparing to be found wanting.

Elizabeth went to her, and reached up and caught her sleeve.

“Come down, Kitty. The gravel will not examine you.”

Kitty’s gaze snapped to her. Relief crossed her face so plainly that Elizabeth felt the force of it in her own throat.

“Oh, Lizzy.”

It was not a greeting. It was an appeal.

On the other side of the step, the coachman held out his hand. Kitty took it and came down with more haste than grace, then stood beside her on the sweep, clutching a small reticule as if it contained instructions for survival.

At the back of the carriage, footmen began to remove the luggage. A groom moved to the horses’ heads. Somewhere behind them, the house door opened and steps sounded in the hall, but no one came out in a rush

Kitty’s gaze moved from the open doorway to the windows, then back to Elizabeth. “Is Mr. Darcy not at home?”

“He is, and Miss Darcy, as well.”

Kitty looked more frightened, not less. “Have we come at a bad time? I thought — I do not mean that anyone should have — only, I thought perhaps—”

“That the whole house would come out upon the gravel to receive you?” Mr. Bennet supplied, removing his gloves finger by finger. “In great houses, Kitty, affection is permitted to wait upon hot water.”

Kitty stared at him.

Elizabeth laid a hand very lightly on her sister’s sleeve. “They are being considerate. No one expects you to be received formally with the dust of the road still on your face. Here’s Mrs. Reynolds, who will show you up; Aunt Gardiner is waiting; and when you are comfortable, Miss Darcy will be very glad to see you.”

Kitty drew one breath, then another. “So they are not — that is, she is not disappointed?”

“She has not had time to be disappointed,” said Mr. Bennet. “You have not yet spoken.”

“Papa,” Kitty said—her usual intonation of affront, but fainter.

“An advantage, my dear. Preserve it until you have washed.”

Kitty almost smiled. Not quite, but at least she was less near tears than she had been a moment before.

At that instant, Mrs. Reynolds arrived, composed, capable, as unperturbed as if she greeted dusty Bennets every Tuesday.

“Mr. Bennet. Miss Catherine. Welcome to Pemberley.” Her curtsey was perfect. “Your rooms are quite ready. Mrs. Gardiner is upstairs and asked to be told the moment you arrived.”

“Thank you,” Kitty said quickly; then, as if the words had escaped too soon and required improvement, “I am very much obliged — I mean, we are — Papa, are we?”

“We are in a state of obligation so extensive I hardly know where to begin,” said Mr. Bennet. “Lead on, Mrs. Reynolds. My daughter is anxious to become presentable before anyone of consequence discovers her.”

“Papa,” Kitty whispered, mortified.

But Mrs. Reynolds’s mouth had softened at the corner. “Miss Catherine has had a long journey, sir. Pemberley does not stand on ceremony with road dust.”

This was so exactly the right sentence that Elizabeth could have embraced her, had Mrs. Reynolds been an embraceable woman, which she very much was not.

They crossed the threshold.

The hall had altered since Elizabeth first entered it and not altered at all. Beeswax, cool stone, flowers on the side table, the round oak table at the center. Kitty’s step faltered. Elizabeth slowed with her.

“There — we are in,” she murmured.

Kitty looked at the ceiling. “A hall like a church.”

“But it cannot hear you.”

“That you know of.”

The answer was so quick, and so nearly Kitty herself, that Elizabeth turned to look at her. Kitty had spoken without thinking. Elizabeth could not help smiling. Kitty saw it and looked alarmed at her own success.

Before Elizabeth could answer, Mr. Darcy appeared from the passage leading toward the back of the house.

He came in without haste, but Elizabeth saw at once that he had not been waiting idly. There was a trace of the outdoors about him — a faint darkening of dust at the edge of one boot and the look of a man whose attention had been recalled from one duty and set upon another before the first had entirely released him.

“Mr. Bennet,” he said, bowing. “Miss Catherine. I am very glad to see you safely arrived.”

Mr. Bennet returned the bow with all necessary gravity. “You are very good, sir. Your carriage has conveyed us in such comfort that I begin to fear I shall be unfit for common roads.”

“I hope not. I should be sorry to have done Hertfordshire an injury.”

Mr. Bennet’s brows lifted by the smallest measure. The first probe had been answered.

Mr. Darcy turned to Kitty. “Mrs. Reynolds will show you to your room. My sister will be happy to receive you when you are rested from the journey.”

Kitty, who had curtseyed with tolerable composure, lifted her eyes to him and dropped them again. "Thank you, sir. I am — very grateful for the invitation.”

“The kindness is yours,” he said.

Not mere civility, but a sentence placed where she could stand upon it.

Something in Kitty steadied. A small settling, like a breath completed.

Mr. Darcy stepped back, giving Mrs. Reynolds the way. He did not look at Elizabeth until Mr. Bennet and Kitty had begun up the stairs. When he did, the look was brief enough to be nothing and direct enough to be impossible to mistake.

Elizabeth felt it like a hand laid, without warning, upon the very thought she had been striving to keep silent. To be seen so plainly was not comfort; it was exposure.

She had meant only to bring Kitty into safety, and to bring Georgiana some comfort. She had not meant to bring her there—to Pemberley, to him, under his eye—as if Longbourn were a thing to be weighed and found wanting.

Her pride rose first; then, more unwillingly, a sharper sensation behind it. What if, in fact, he looked not to judge, but to understand?

Then he turned, because another servant had appeared at the far end of the hall, and Pemberley, having admitted Longbourn, resumed all its other claims upon him.

---

Three-quarters of an hour later, Kitty was ready to come down, her gown changed, her hair re-pinned, and her face scrubbed into greater anxiety.

Elizabeth could not blame her. Washing removed dust, not expectation.

The smaller drawing room on the garden side was familiar by now: the pale green silk, the cream curtains drawn back from the high windows, the polished table set near the chairs by the light. The first time, she had entered it wondering what countenance a woman ought to wear when visiting the house of a man whose proposal she had refused. Today, she entered beside a sister who was likely wondering, with equal urgency, what countenance a girl ought to wear when invited to be useful in a house that seemed to require nothing.

The tea things were already laid.

Same as before: the thin porcelain, the bread and butter, the cakes, the silver tongs, the shell-shaped dish with preserved fruit, all the careful plenty of Pemberley’s version of simplicity. But today, place for another cup had been set where Kitty would not have to reach across anyone. The sofa nearest the windows had been drawn a little forward, as if to make room for two sisters to sit together without appearing to have arranged themselves for mutual defense.

Mr. Darcy stood behind his sister and a little to the side, exactly as he had stood on that first afternoon, near enough to steady, far enough to let her be seen. But this time Elizabeth understood the position. She had perceived something of it then; today, with Kitty trembling beside her, she saw how much labor such stillness concealed.

Mr. Darcy took a step forward. “Georgiana—allow me to present Mr. Bennet and Miss Catherine Bennet.”

Georgiana came forward, made a slight curtsey, and said, “Mr. Bennet.” Then she turned to Kitty. “Miss Catherine,” she said, and stopped.

Kitty curtseyed. “Miss Darcy.”

They looked at one another with all the solemnity of two young ladies who had each been told the other was very much to be liked and found the instruction too important for ease. Elizabeth remained at Kitty’s side, near enough that her sister’s skirt brushed hers.

“I am very glad you are come,” said Miss Darcy.

“I am very much obliged to you for inviting me.”

Georgiana’s hands clasped once, then released. “I hope your journey was not too fatiguing.”

“No — yes — I mean, it was long, but the carriage was very comfortable, and Papa says I must never complain of a road after being carried over one in such a manner, because it would show ingratitude and poor taste both.”

Mr. Bennet, who had allowed himself to be directed to the chair near Mr. Darcy with the air of a man accepting the least uncomfortable position in an experiment, said, “I stand by the principle.”

Georgiana smiled. It was small, but it was real enough to reach Kitty, who looked quickly at Elizabeth as if to ask whether anything had gone wrong.

As they were seated, Elizabeth took the place beside Kitty on the sofa, near enough for support and not so near as to appear to offer it. Miss Darcy returned to the tea table with the gravity of one undertaking a serious public office, and Mrs. Gardiner placed herself near enough to be useful without seeming necessary.

Elizabeth shifted nearer Kitty on the sofa. “You are doing very well,” she said under the cover of Mrs. Gardiner asking after Mr. Bennet’s journey.

“I have only spoken nonsense.”

“No more than is customary in drawing rooms.”

Kitty's breath hitched, not quite a laugh.

Mr. Darcy did not take his own cup at once; instead, he turned to Mr. Bennet with the plate of cakes and a gravity which made pastry appear a matter of hospitality rather than appetite.

“I understand you are fond of reading, sir. The library is at your disposal while you remain. Though I should warn you, the catalogue has lately fallen into a state my steward calls temporary and Mrs. Reynolds calls provoking.”

Mr. Bennet regarded him over the rim of his cup.

“A gentleman who offers another gentleman a library before asking his opinion of the weather has made a promising beginning.”

Mr. Darcy’s answer came after precisely the right interval. “Then I am fortunate to have begun correctly.”

“Do not be too confident. The catalogue may yet condemn you.”

“I shall submit to judgment.”

“An admirable resolution. Few men do, when books are involved.”

Elizabeth took her cup because Miss Darcy offered it; otherwise she might have forgotten that tea existed.

He had prepared.

He had thought of her father before her father entered the room. He had considered what might make him comfortable, what might turn his wit toward pleasure rather than injury, what might occupy him without inviting him to make sport of anyone weaker. He had prepared for Mr. Bennet as he had prepared occupation for Georgiana, sport for Mr. Gardiner, and likely even shade for the lower walk.

The thought ought to have disturbed her. Instead, finding no argument ready to her hand, Elizabeth turned to Kitty and chose the smaller duty before her.

Kitty had taken one of the smaller cakes and was holding it in both hands without eating. Georgiana, seated now with her own cake, seemed to be searching among possible subjects and finding each too delicate to introduce.

Elizabeth gave Kitty the slightest nudge with her knee. “The kittens,” she murmured.

Kitty’s eyes widened — not in alarm this time, but recollection. She turned back to Georgiana with such earnestness that the cake in her hand was forgotten entirely.

“I have thought of the kittens nearly the whole way north,” she said. “I hope that is not very foolish.”

Georgiana’s face altered. The careful hostess vanished for half a second, and the girl who had written the invitation looked out.

“No,” she said. “I have been waiting to show them to you. Robinson says they may be handled soon.”

Kitty’s shoulders lowered by an inch. “Are they very small?”

“Four are, Robinson says. One is larger and thinks himself above the others.”

“That is often the way in families,” said Mr. Bennet.

“Papa,” said Kitty, before remembering where she was.

Mr. Bennet looked entirely unrepentant. Georgiana’s mouth curved — not quite a laugh, more the beginning of one — and her eyes went to her brother. His face softened by the smallest degree. The laugh escaped her after all, quickly checked, but enough that Mrs. Gardiner looked down into her tea to conceal a smile.

Mr. Bennet raised his cup. Over its rim, his eyes found Kitty — and stayed, with something quieter than wit. The steady look of a father who had noticed his child doing well.

Kitty’s shoulders settled. The cake, forgotten until now, received its first small bite.

A servant appeared in the doorway.

Mr. Darcy turned at once.

The man did not enter fully. “Beg pardon, sir. Robinson asks whether you would come to the west stable when convenient.”

“Is anything amiss?”

“No, sir. Only what’s expected. He says it may be soon.”

Mr. Darcy set down his untouched cup. “One of the mares is near her time,” he said to Mr. Bennet. “Pray excuse me.”

He bowed and crossed toward the door.

Kitty had leaned closer to Elizabeth, her cake plate empty, her face turned toward Miss Darcy as the two of them considered the relative dignity of kittens. Elizabeth, glancing down, saw her sister’s fingers loosening at last around the plate.

Then she looked up.

Mr. Darcy had paused at the door. He had turned back.

His gaze rested for a moment on Kitty, then on Elizabeth beside her. For one startling instant, Elizabeth understood them as he might: the woman he had once asked to enter his life, and the sister attached to every objection he had once laid before her, both admitted now beneath his roof.

There was no triumph in his face. No surprise. No trace of the judgment she had feared.

Elizabeth was the first to lower her eyes.

# Chapter 24: The West Stable

Pemberley had survived the arrival of Mr. Bennet and Kitty without any outward sign of distress. No servant looked alarmed; no chair had been displaced; no ancient Darcy had stepped from a portrait to object. Yet by ten o'clock the next morning, Elizabeth suspected the house had begun, in its quiet Derbyshire way, to reconsider some of its arrangements.

Breakfast did not break up so much as divide itself. Georgiana had spoken of the foal and the kittens with a banked excitement that made refusal impossible; Kitty had received the intelligence as if Pemberley had been built for no other purpose; and Mrs. Gardiner, after one glance at Mr. Bennet and another at Mrs. Annesley, had declared that the young ladies might surely be trusted to admire young animals under suitable guidance.

Suitable guidance, Elizabeth soon discovered, meant herself.

Her father, Mrs. Gardiner, and Mrs. Annesley remained at the table over coffee, the cups drawn nearer, their voices lowered into that tone by which grown people pretend they are discussing nothing of consequence. Kitty and Georgiana had gone to exchange slippers for half-boots; Elizabeth, detained by the last of her coffee and already clad appropriately, heard only enough to understand the subject — *comfortable arrangements*, *a few days more*, *young ladies together*. At Longbourn, such a discussion would have been shouted through three doors and answered by the person least concerned. At Pemberley, it advanced by degrees, folded into civil sentences and set down beside the coffee-pot.

Kitty knew nothing of this. Kitty knew only that Miss Darcy had said the kittens might be seen after breakfast.

"Are we truly to go?" she asked when they were collected in the hall, her bonnet tied too loosely and one glove still unbuttoned. "I mean, if it is not troublesome. It is not troublesome, is it? Only Miss Darcy said Robinson said—"

“I did say it,” Georgiana said. “And I thought—I hoped—you might call me Georgiana. Robinson told my brother they were old enough to be taken up, if one were careful.”

"Then we shall be careful," said Kitty with great solemnity. She looked down at her gloves as if care might be found among the buttons. "Lizzy, am I straight?"

"Perfect," said Elizabeth.

Kitty made a face at her, then remembered where she was and tried to turn the expression into composure. Georgiana saw the attempt and looked away, but the corner of her mouth betrayed her.

That small betrayal did more for Kitty than any praise could have done. Her shoulders settled. 

They went out by the side door, where a groom had been sent to show the way. The air came warm and clean across the yard, carrying the smell of cut grass and something sharper beneath it — horse, leather, the sun already laying its hand upon the stone.

The west stable was stone-built and orderly, handsome without the least ambition to impress.  Its broad doors stood open on a cool passage, a dark line of stalls, brass hooks, rubbed leather, fresh straw, and brooms set ready for the next use. Swallows cut low under the eaves. Somewhere a horse struck one hoof against wood, and the sound carried through the open door to fade among the walls of the yard.

Kitty stopped as they crossed the gravel. "It is so much larger than our stable at Longbourn."

Georgiana considered this. "I suppose it must be."

"Oh! I did not mean — only everything here is larger than Longbourn. Except Mama's voice. Nothing is larger than that."

Kitty's mouth closed with a little click, as if she had tried to catch the words after they had escaped. Georgiana's composure slipped — not quite lost, not quite held.

Elizabeth, walking a little behind them, looked down at the gravel and trusted the morning air to arrange her countenance.

"My aunt's voice," Georgiana said, after a moment's consideration, "is not loud, but it does travel."

Kitty turned to her. "Lady Catherine?"

Georgiana nodded.

Kitty considered this with great seriousness. “Lizzy once said Lady Catherine could make a compliment sound like an order and an order sound like a sermon.”

“Did she?” Georgiana asked. She glanced back at Elizabeth.

Excellent. “Kitty,” she said warningly.

“But you did say it.”

“I did not expect to be quoted in Derbyshire.”

Georgiana’s answer came after a moment, small but certain. “It is an apt description.”

Kitty turned back long enough to give Elizabeth one triumphant lift of the brows.

She had spent years correcting Kitty: lowering a voice, checking a step, smoothing a folly before it could catch someone else's notice. She had called this sisterly duty and had not always been wrong. 

But correction, even when necessary, had a way of filling a room before the corrected person could enter it. Here, with Georgiana beside her, Kitty was not being corrected. She was being received, imperfectly and with interest.

Elizabeth's hand closed at her side. She did not know why that should sting. She only knew it did — a quick, unreasonable tightness, gone before she could name it, as if she had been shown that something she had carried for years could have been lighter all along.

Just inside the entrance to the west stable they found Mr. Darcy.

He stood inside the open doorway of the first loose box, one hand resting on the top rail, his head turned toward Robinson, who was speaking in the low, practical tones of a man whose whole life had been spent among creatures that did not take kindly to noise. Behind them, the mare stood in the deeper straw, her dark head lowered toward something hidden by the rail, Mr. Darcy’s shadow, and the angle of her own body. One ear turned back and forward; the straw beneath her shifted once, softly.

Mr. Darcy had not heard them at once. Or, having heard, he had not turned before Robinson finished. His sleeves were rolled to the forearm; his coat hung from one of the brass hooks near the door; and there was straw caught in the seam of one boot. His hat was in his hand. He looked less like the formal master of a great house than like a man to whom every living thing in it might, by one route or another, become a responsibility.

The thought came too near admiration. Elizabeth made herself attend to what the mare had been guarding.

It was a ridiculous creature. Its legs appeared to have been designed for some taller animal and delivered by mistake; the knees all knob and angle, the narrow lengths below them too delicate for the undertaking, the hooves no larger than teacups and set down with great uncertainty in the straw. Its coat still had the soft, brushed-up look of something not yet finished, darker along the spine and pale where the morning light found the fine hair at its neck. It smelled faintly of warm milk and clean straw, with the sharper animal scent of the mare behind it.

The foal shifted one foot, then another, and seemed surprised to discover both belonged to him. A small breath came through its nose — not quite a snort, not quite a sigh — and the mare turned one ear back at once. Elizabeth had the absurd thought that if she laid a hand against its neck, she would feel the whole creature trembling with the effort of being alive.

It regarded the world from beneath a narrow blaze with all the suspicion of a young gentleman introduced too early to company.

Georgiana stopped only a foot from the door. Her hands clasped together.

Mr. Darcy turned then. At the sight of his sister, his face changed — a loosening around the eyes, a warmth that did not reach his mouth.

"Georgiana," he said. "You are come at the right time. Robinson tells me our new gentleman has stood only twice before and objected to both occasions."

"He does not look like a gentleman," Kitty said as she bumped up against Georgiana’s shoulder to see better. She put one gloved hand over her mouth. "I beg your pardon."

Robinson’s weathered face scarcely moved, but the lines beside his eyes deepened. Mr. Darcy looked back at the foal.

"True," he said gravely. "At present he looks like an arrangement of legs with an opinion."

Kitty’s gloved hand remained over her mouth, but the giggle escaped around it. Georgiana looked at her first in happy astonishment, as if she had not known such a thing could be said aloud; then amusement caught her too. She turned at once to her brother, not quite asking permission and not quite able to help herself.

"You may come nearer, if you like. Not too near the mare. She is patient, but patience is not consent."

The stable smelled of straw and warm animal and the faint medicinal sharpness of some wash Robinson had used. Dust moved in the light from the high window. The mare turned her head and examined them with one dark liquid eye, then dismissed them. The foal, in an attempt to place itself behind its mother, discovered that its hind legs had gone in two directions at once and needed a moment to find their way beneath him.

"Oh," Kitty whispered, all caution forgotten. "He is splendidly foolish."

Georgiana regarded the foal as if foolishness had been unexpectedly admitted among the virtues.

"He was born before dawn?" she asked.

"A little after three," Mr. Darcy said. "Robinson had the better part of the labor. I had the honor of being informed after all useful work was done."

"Sir did come down," Robinson said.

"To stand in the way with a lantern."

"To hold it steady," said Robinson, which, from his mouth, appeared to be a significant compliment.

Elizabeth pressed her thumbnail into the pad of her finger. A man might be praised at an assembly, admired in a drawing room, commended for fortune, consequence, manners, or person; but there was a different kind of knowledge in hearing a servant defend his usefulness where flattery would be absurd. 

“Many gentlemen would have held it less steadily,” Elizabeth said.

He turned his head toward her. "You set the standard low, Miss Bennet."

"I have four sisters, sir. I have seen lanterns, teacups, tempers, and entire schemes dropped under less provocation."

Kitty, who had bent closer to see the foal's narrow knees, said, "I never dropped a lantern."

"No," said Elizabeth. "You once dropped a candlestick."

"It was not lit. Or not by the time it reached the floor."

"For which we are all grateful."

Georgiana laughed again — quieter this time, but real. The mare shifted, her weight rocking the straw beneath her. Robinson lifted one hand, not sharply, only enough to remind them that the west stable was not a theater. Kitty slipped back at once.

"I am sorry," she said to the mare, not to Robinson.

Robinson's mouth twitched. "No harm done, miss."

Mr. Darcy’s attention returned to Kitty. An adjustment crossed his face — not the whole of his judgment revised, that would have been too easy, but one line of it moved, the way a pen is lifted and set down in a different place.

“The kittens are farther along the stable,” he said. “Robinson’s boy can show you. They are less easily offended than the mare, though I am told one has already formed a high opinion of his consequence.”

“That must be the large one,” Georgiana said, forgetting to be shy in her certainty. “Robinson said there was one larger than the rest.”

Kitty turned to her with full attention. "How much larger?"

"I do not know, exactly. Larger in manner, at least."

"Then we must see him before he becomes unbearable."

This time Georgiana's smile did not ask permission.

Mr. Darcy remained by the foal as they moved away. Elizabeth was conscious of leaving him — of the space opening between them, of the shift from the warm milky scent to cooler musty air in the passage — and then, after they passed into the box the boy led them to, she was not conscious of him at all.

The kittens had been given a wooden box lined with old sacking in a far corner, though their respect for its boundaries was already in question. Their mother, a striped barn cat of narrow face and austere principles, lay curled along one side and opened one yellow eye upon the visitors. Seeing no immediate incompetence, she closed it again. Five kittens lay in a heap against her belly, the whole arrangement at first glance indistinguishable from a small, breathing muff.

Then the heap moved.

Kitty was lost.

She went down upon her knees in the straw with no thought for her gown. Her face lit — a flush of pleasure so unguarded that Elizabeth had to look away from it, down at the straw, where chaff clung to the hem of her own skirt.

Georgiana lowered herself more carefully beside her, arranging her skirts in the manner of a young lady who had never been encouraged to sit on stable straw and had decided that the attempt required strategy. Kitty waited until she was settled before reaching toward the box.

"May we touch them?"

The groom, a boy of perhaps twelve who had been watching them with interest, said, "Robinson says gentle, miss. And mind the queen. She don't like surprises."

"The queen?" Georgiana repeated.

"Stable cat, miss."

Kitty considered the mother cat. "She does look as if she had a court."

The boy grinned and retreated, his duty discharged.

Kitty extended one finger. The mother cat opened her eye again. Kitty stopped. The two regarded one another with the mutual seriousness of sovereign and petitioner.

"You see," Kitty said to Georgiana in a low voice, "one must introduce oneself first. Cats are not like dogs. Dogs have already forgiven you for whatever you are about to do."

Georgiana bent nearer. "How do you introduce yourself to a cat?"

"Slowly. And without hope."

Elizabeth, standing behind them, felt her mouth pull. It was strange to see Kitty certain of anything. Stranger still to see Georgiana receiving instruction from her with such gravity.

The queen permitted Kitty's finger to approach, sniffed it thoroughly, then endured one touch upon the head with the expression of a sovereign tolerating a foreign ambassador. Georgiana, watching, copied the movement. The cat allowed it.

"Oh," Georgiana said. The sound was soft, private. Her fingertip rested on the cat's brow.

Kitty looked pleased with her success. "There. Now she knows you."

"Does she approve?"

"Not yet. But she has not dismissed us."

This distinction appeared to satisfy Georgiana.

The kittens, less invested in ceremony, began to wake. One gray head emerged; one black paw stretched and vanished. A pale creature rolled backward into its sibling and remained there, affronted. Then the largest kitten, a smoky gray with white paws and a chest far too grand for his size, climbed directly over two of the others and placed himself at the front of the box.

Kitty drew in a breath. "Oh, there he is."

Georgiana leaned closer. "That is the one."

"The tyrant?"

"I had not called him that."

"No, but he has."

The kitten opened his mouth. No sound came out. He tried again and produced a squeak that did not at all support his pretensions.

Kitty pressed her lips together. "Poor thing. He believes himself a lion and has been betrayed by his voice."

Georgiana gasped. "He does not know."

"Of course he knows. That is why he is angry."

The kitten, proving it, shoved one paw into the face of the smallest tabby and attempted to climb the side of the box. His back feet found no purchase. He slid down backward into the heap. The pale kitten, now beneath him, objected with a more convincing squeak.

Kitty looked at Georgiana with solemn concern. "We must intervene. He is trampling his subjects."

Georgiana's mouth trembled. Her fingers curled in her lap, and she bent nearer the box as if the kitten required all her attention.

Kitty turned back to the box and lowered her voice into the grave tone she had used, Elizabeth remembered, when judging quarrels between the Gardiner children.

“Miss Darcy—Georgiana. I believe this is a matter for law.”

"Law?"

"Certainly. We cannot permit one kitten to climb upon all the rest merely because he has wider paws."

Georgiana's eyes went wide with attention. "What law applies?"

Kitty considered. "The law against being unbearable."

"Is there such a law?"

"There ought to be.” She looked back. “Lizzy, is there?"

Elizabeth folded her hands behind her. "If there were, half the country would be transported."

Kitty nodded. "Then we must begin modestly. With kittens."

Georgiana bent over the box. The tyrant had recovered and was now attempting to seize Kitty's glove. Kitty let him catch one finger and shook it with great delicacy, greeting a very small and badly mannered gentleman.

"Sir," she said, "your conduct has been observed."

The kitten bit her glove.

"Ah. No remorse."

Georgiana squeaked.

"He must be named," Kitty said. "One cannot reform a creature without naming him. What is the proudest name you know?"

Georgiana looked up, startled by the question. Elizabeth kept her eyes on the kittens and her thoughts to herself.

Kitty, who did not wait long for answers, said, "At Longbourn we would call him General, because Lydia thinks every officer must be obeyed and Mary thinks every general ought to be studied. But he is too small for General."

"Colonel?" Georgiana offered.

Elizabeth's gaze did not move. The word had brushed near too many histories, but not the dangerous one. Colonel Fitzwilliam might have laughed at such promotion.

The kitten planted one white paw on the edge of the box, swayed with great consequence, and then sat down hard upon his brother. "No. A colonel would have better balance."

"Captain?"

At last, the kitten succeeded in climbing over the side of the box, landed shoulder-first in the straw, shook its head against the indignity of the ground, and marched away from his brothers and sisters.

Kitty caught him with both hands. "Captain is too generous. He has deserted his men."

Georgiana peered at the kitten, which made a blind, indignant pass at her nose.

"Then you must take charge of him," Kitty said, and settled him carefully in Georgiana’s lap. "He respects you more."

"I do not think he respects anyone."

"That is why he must practice."

Georgiana curved herself around him. The tyrant, discovering himself enthroned, ceased fighting and began to purr with an authority far beyond his size.

"Oh," Georgiana said, in quite another voice.

The pleasure in it was so naked that Elizabeth turned away.

She had not meant to look for Mr. Darcy.

Nevertheless, she looked.

He had left Robinson and the foal and was walking toward them, his coat retrieved and slung over one arm, his pace unhurried. The light from the yard fell behind him, touching the rolled linen at his forearms and the dark hair loosened slightly by the stable air; and Elizabeth, who had been admiring his conduct, was startled to find herself admiring the man.

Elizabeth stepped back from the girls before he reached her. Her pulse was doing something unnecessary.

Mr. Darcy stopped beside her — not quite so near as he might have done, and not so far as prudence required.

For some seconds neither spoke. Kitty was explaining to Georgiana that the kitten must be held against one's bodice since he considered himself a person of rank, because persons of rank always expected support. Georgiana listened with an attention no music-master had ever commanded.

As Georgiana bent over the kitten, Mr. Darcy’s face altered—not subtly this time, and not quickly hidden. The stern lines of his mouth softened; the care about his eyes eased. For a moment, Elizabeth saw, without disguise, how dearly his sister was loved.

"Your sister has a talent for command," he said.

Kitty was rescuing the pale kitten from being sat upon. "Only where kittens and children are concerned. We do not yet know whether it extends to persons of reason."

"That may be an advantage. Persons of reason are often less easily improved."

She turned back to him and found his attention not on her, but on Kitty. No mockery touched the words. If anything, they were spoken with the gravity of a man who had spent too many years attempting to improve persons of reason and had found kittens a more promising field.

"She likes to be useful," Elizabeth said.

It was not praise, exactly, and for that reason it cost her more.

"That she is."

No compliment. No flourish. He gave the words exactly as much weight as they could bear, and no more.

She had defended Kitty often enough; she had not known how much she had needed another person to see what she defended. The admission — even silent, even only to herself — left her exposed in a way she did not care for.

The tyrant kitten, dissatisfied with rank when it required stillness, attempted to climb Georgiana's sleeve. Kitty intervened.

"You cannot be a captain," she told him. "You have no discipline."

Georgiana's head bent over the kitten. Her shoulders shook once.

Elizabeth felt it land in him — a stillness that deepened rather than broke.

"Mrs. Gardiner spoke yesterday of the Lambton fair."

The change of subject was so abrupt that Elizabeth might have missed its meaning had she not been standing close enough to catch the care beneath it.

"She remembers it fondly," Elizabeth said.

"I believe it is held later this week.” His eyes remained on Georgiana and Kitty. "It is not large. There is some livestock, a few stalls, more ribbons than any county can require, and enough noise to make one appreciate the road home."

Elizabeth smiled before she could stop herself. "An irresistible description."

"I did not intend to recommend it too highly."

"No. That would be rash."

He took the rebuke, if it was one, with a slight inclination of the head. "It may be too much. Georgiana has not cared for it before,” he said. “But I am not certain that before is a useful guide.”

Elizabeth did not answer at once.

There had been a time, not many days ago, when such a sentence would have vexed her. Now she heard in it not refusal, but the effort to reconsider an old certainty.

“Then perhaps she may wish to be asked,” she said.

He turned to her then, and whatever he had meant to say seemed, for a moment, to have deserted him.

Not long. Long enough. She felt the warmth of it on her face like sun through a window, and looked away.

"I shall ask her," he said.

He had permitted the kittens. He would ask about the fair. He was learning, not to become careless, but to let joy make a claim. Elizabeth, standing beside him with her hands clasped too tightly behind her back, understood that the proof she had wanted was here, and that it asked something of her she was not yet ready to name.

A squeak of protest came from the straw. The tyrant had escaped Georgiana's lap, conquered Kitty's knees, and placed his front paws upon the ribbon that hung from her bonnet.

"Sir," Kitty cried, "that is not yours."

The kitten, unmoved by property law, pulled.

Georgiana reached to help. The ribbon gave way in Kitty's hand; the kitten tumbled backward into the straw with the air of a commander betrayed by his own cavalry. Kitty made a sound of outrage so perfectly proportioned to the size of the offense that Georgiana lost all command of herself.

She laughed.

Not prettily. Not properly. Happily.

# Chapter 25: A Father's Judgment

By the time the ladies withdrew after dinner, Pemberley had begun to receive Kitty Bennet without visible alarm.

This was either a compliment or a capitulation.

For her second dinner in the great house, Elizabeth’s sister had come down with her ribbons ironed, her hair arranged with more patience than usual, and an expression of determined propriety. She had taken her place beside Georgiana without knocking the chair, the table, or her own courage out of order. She had answered Mr. Darcy when he spoke to her.

During the meal, the conversation had turned, by Mrs. Gardiner's gentle management, to the country around Lambton; and Georgiana, who had been quiet for almost three minutes, asked whether Longbourn had any hills of consequence. Kitty opened her mouth, shut it, considered, and said that Hertfordshire had some slopes which Mary called improving and Lydia called inconvenient. Georgiana's smile appeared at once. Kitty saw it and grew, not louder, but steadier.

Mr. Bennet, at Mr. Darcy’s right hand, observed this through the mild, half-absent air by which he generally concealed attention. He had spoken with Kitty before dinner. Kitty had found Elizabeth while she was fastening her necklace.

"Papa asked whether I liked it here. Not liked it, exactly. Whether I was comfortable. And whether Miss Darcy — Georgiana — was kind. And whether I understood that I was not to be troublesome. Only he did not say troublesome. He said — I forget what he said, but I knew what he meant." Kitty's fingers had gone to the ribbon in her hair. "And then he asked whether I wished to stay."

"And what did you say?"

"I said I did. If I might. If you stayed too. Or Aunt Gardiner. Or Mrs. Annesley, but she’s always here, I suppose. I did not mean I should be wild." She had colored. "I told him Georgiana wished me to see whether the gray kitten is still a tyrant tomorrow."

"And did Papa consider that a sufficient reason?"

Kitty's chin had come up. "It is a very good reason. Papa said it was the first political question in which I had shown any steadiness."

Elizabeth had laughed then, but the answer had remained with her through dinner, settling somewhere beneath the brighter noise of knives, glass, and civil conversation.

Her father had asked Kitty what she wanted.

A daughter might reasonably be asked whether she wished to remain in a house not her own. Yet Elizabeth knew too well how often decisions at Longbourn were made around Kitty: to silence Lydia, to soothe Mrs. Bennet, to preserve peace for an hour, to prevent some greater folly. Kitty had been carried by stronger wills, louder wishes, nearer tempers. Tonight, she had been asked.

And having been asked, she sat beside Miss Darcy with the dignity of a person who knew her answer mattered.

When the ladies rose to take their leave, Mr. Darcy held the drawing-room door for them as they passed. Elizabeth, last but one, found herself obliged to go near him. He was all due attention: the hand upon the door, the slight inclination, the controlled serenity of a host. But his eyes, when they met hers, carried something stronger than courtesy. The stable had not been forgotten. Nor the moment beside her, not quite near enough to be improper, not far enough to be safe.

She passed him with a step that was nearly steady.

In the drawing room, Kitty and Georgiana took up a place near the table, where a work-box had been set out and abandoned earlier. Kitty picked up a length of ribbon, then put it down again, perhaps remembering the tyrant kitten's claims upon property. Georgiana bent over some piece of sewing with great seriousness and immediately threaded the wrong size of needle. Kitty glanced once toward Mrs. Annesley.

Mrs. Annesley, at the far end of the table, smiled and returned to her own work.

No permission was spoken. None was needed. Kitty leaned closer and murmured something Elizabeth could not hear. Georgiana listened as if the management of a needle had become the most interesting subject in Derbyshire.

Mrs. Gardiner, seated beside Elizabeth, said very softly, "Your sister seems happier tonight."

"Yes."

"And less careful about it."

Elizabeth looked down at her hands. She had found no occupation for them and did not quite know what to do with them. "My father spoke with her before dinner."

"I know."

"Did he tell you?"

"Kitty did, in effect. She has the air of one who has passed an examination and cannot be persuaded not to mention the school."

Across the room, Kitty was choosing thread from the work-box, an embroidery frame newly set up beside her.

The catch beneath Elizabeth’s ribs returned — the same she had felt in the stable. "I feared she could not be steady."

Mrs. Gardiner's reply was gentle. "She has not had much occasion to try."

There was nothing to answer to that.

The fire had settled into its coals, and the candles nearest the work-table threw the girls' shadows long across the carpet. Georgiana paused over a stitch. Kitty glanced at her work.

"It is a common rebellion," Kitty said. "Thread never wishes to go where it is told."

Georgiana turned the needle between her fingers. "Does anything?"

Kitty considered this. "Dogs. Sometimes."

"Not kittens."

"Never kittens."

The exchange steadied them both.

It did not steady Elizabeth. She reached for the nearest book upon the table and discovered it was a volume of sermons. The first sentence informed her that the discipline of the mind began with command of wandering thoughts. She snapped the book shut.

Mrs. Gardiner did not laugh. That was a kindness.

The gentlemen were still at table. There were many subjects upon which two gentlemen might speak over port: estates, roads, the weather, Mr. Gardiner's absence, the merits of Derbyshire stone, the general inconvenience of daughters. Her father could spend ten minutes on a label if the vintage displeased him. Mr. Darcy, she was certain, could discuss a farm boundary or a tenant's roof with such precision that time itself would feel obliged to stand properly measured.

That was not, she was sure, what they were speaking about.

She did not fear that her father would be rude. He could be provoking, indolent, negligent, and, upon occasion, unfair; but he was not a fool. Nor did she fear that Mr. Darcy would be offended by a father's questions. He had borne worse from her.

No. What unsettled her was the knowledge that they might be speaking of Kitty — and perhaps, without saying her name, of Elizabeth. Of leaving two daughters under Mr. Darcy's roof. Of trust, which could be made to sound like household management if gentlemen tried very hard.

And beneath all that, the thought she did not permit to stand upright: that her father was judging the man she had judged, misjudged, resisted, watched, defended, and begun — begun, only begun — to need near her.

The door opened. Mr. Bennet entered first, Mr. Darcy behind him, both perfectly composed. Too composed. Elizabeth knew her father well enough to understand that idleness did not always mean inattention, and Mr. Darcy well enough, now, to know that restraint could conceal more movement than another man's speech.

Coffee was brought. Cups were distributed. Mr. Bennet waved his away with thanks. Mr. Darcy spoke to Mrs. Gardiner about the roads near Lambton. Georgiana showed Kitty a stitch; Kitty corrected it with care. Elizabeth's cup grew cold between her hands.

At length her father turned to her. "Have you finished your coffee, Lizzy?"

"I have not started it."

"Then you will not miss it." He rose. "Mr. Darcy has been good enough to lend me his library for a time. Come and keep me company."

Kitty, startled, looked from her father to Elizabeth and then back again.

Elizabeth set down the untouched cup and rose. Mr. Darcy rose as they did. She was aware of Mrs. Gardiner's eyes upon her, of Georgiana's anxious stillness, of Kitty's parted lips. Mr. Bennet went ahead. As Elizabeth passed near Mr. Darcy, she could hear the slight shift of his breath before he spoke.

"My father has not exhausted you, I hope," she said quickly, because she could not afford to hear whatever he might say first.

"No," he said. Then, after a pause, "He is very much your father."

It should have amused her. It did. It also made her heart behave foolishly.

"That is not always taken as praise."

"In this instance, it was intended as such."

His face gave little away, but not nothing. The warmth was there, held carefully back — not, she thought, because her father was in the corridor, but, perhaps, because he was not yet certain she would welcome it.

She had not, before.

"Thank you," she said.

Mr. Darcy said nothing, and she went past him.

The library was near — only the next room but one. Its door stood half open. She knew the room. She had walked through it with Mrs. Reynolds on that first morning, when the books had stood at odd angles, as if lately read, the window-seat cushion had been sat on, and the owner had been thought to be miles and miles away. She had been in it since — once, when Mr. Darcy had shown her uncle a set of county maps. But she had not been in it like this, at night, with the candles lit and her father waiting. Tonight it seemed less a library than a witness.

Her father crossed to the hearth, picked up a book from the table, and turned it over without reading its title. "Shut the door, if you please."

Elizabeth did as he asked, and then moved to the chair opposite his, in front of the banked fireplace. He took his spectacles off, and turned them over in his hand.

"I have spoken with Kitty."

"So she told me."

"Did she give a faithful report?"

"That depends upon what you asked."

"Then no." He leaned back, with the slow satisfaction of a man who had found the chair adequate to his need. "She informed me, among other things, that Miss Darcy's cat is a monarch, that one kitten is destined for tyranny, and that she herself may be of some use in preventing civil disorder."

"So I observed."

"I feared as much." His mouth twitched. "She also told me that Miss Darcy is kind, that Mrs. Annesley is always near without being tiresome, that Mrs. Gardiner knows everything before anyone says it, and that you smile more here than lately at Longbourn."

Elizabeth's color rose. "Kitty is impertinent."

"Frequently. In this case, she may also be accurate."

"Papa."

"Yes, yes. We shall return to your crimes in a moment." He leaned back. "First, Kitty. She wishes to stay."

"Yes."

"She does not wish it merely because she has her own room here, or because there are kittens, or because Miss Darcy owns more ribbons than her sisters?"

"I do not think so."

"No." He tapped the spectacles lightly against his palm. "Nor do I. She spoke of Miss Darcy before she spoke of the kittens. That is either a good sign or a severe indictment of the kittens."

Elizabeth smiled despite herself.

"I have also spoken with Mr. Darcy."

"So I gathered."

"A composed young man. Difficult to provoke. I was obliged to work harder than is good for my health."

"What did you ask him?"

"What a father ought to ask when he proposes to leave one daughter in another gentleman's house and finds another daughter already far too much at ease there."

The words were lightly spoken. They struck with inconvenient force.

Elizabeth folded her hands in her lap. "And were you satisfied?"

"As far as any father may be satisfied." He looked about the room. "Mrs. Annesley appears to be a sensible woman. Your aunt is satisfied for the present. Miss Darcy's desire for Kitty's company is plain enough, and Mr. Darcy's understanding of the arrangement is neither careless nor obscure. Kitty will not be left to govern herself." He paused. "Your aunt, of course, cannot remain indefinitely.”

Elizabeth could not deny it. "Aunt Gardiner is still within the time she expected to be away."

"For the present."

"Yes."

"When your aunt must leave, the matter will want settling again. I will not have it drift." He tapped the spectacles lightly against his knee. "But while she remains, I see no reason to drag Kitty south tomorrow simply to prove that I can." He paused. "She may remain."

The relief came at once, and too strongly. It was not only for Kitty.

"She will be very happy."

"So I believe. I shall attempt not to resent Derbyshire for succeeding where Hertfordshire has often failed." He put on his spectacles, sighed, and removed them again. He pulled out his handkerchief and started wiping the glass. "You may remain as well, while your aunt does."

“Was this in question?”

"Was it not?"

"I am here with Aunt Gardiner."

"An admirable answer to a question no one asked." His voice gentled, though the sharp edge remained. "Lizzy, do you wish to return with me?"

She had expected the question. She had not expected the room to go so still around it.

"Kitty should not be unsettled again so soon."

"Kitty has been discussed."

"Miss Darcy depends upon the arrangement."

"Miss Darcy has a brother, a companion, a house full of servants, and, for the present, your aunt."

"But Kitty—"

"Will remain." He held her gaze. "I asked whether you wished to return."

Elizabeth looked away first. The library shelves rose dark and orderly beyond him. By candlelight the spines looked different — warmer, closer, the gilt worn soft on the volumes nearest the hearth. A book lay face-down on the arm of the chair she had not taken, its place held without a marker, as if the reader meant to return before the candle burned out. 

"I am comfortable here," she said.

"A dangerous confession. Pemberley has undone stronger minds."

"It has done no such thing."

"No? Then I must have mistaken the evidence."

She stood, because sitting had become intolerable, and went to the nearest shelf. The books there were bound in calf and arranged by a logic she could not immediately discern. Her finger touched one spine and withdrew. "You have been speaking with Mr. Darcy too long. You are both becoming fond of evidence."

"Mr. Darcy is fond of evidence. I am fond of conclusions reached with as little exertion as possible."

"Then you may conclude that I am useful here."

"I have." His answer was low enough that she turned. "You are useful to Kitty. You are useful to Miss Darcy. You are useful, I suspect, to Mr. Darcy, though he did not have the presumption to say so."

Heat came into her face at once. "Papa."

"Do not look so severe, Lizzy. I have not accused you of a crime."

"You imply—"

"I imply that a father may observe his favorite daughter without immediately being charged with imagination."

She tried to answer. Wit came, saw the room, and retreated.

"I have no wish to carry you off, if you are truly easy here. But I have still less wish to leave you here merely because you think everyone else requires it."

"I do not."

"Very good. Then say so."

Elizabeth looked at him helplessly. It was an old look they shared, though the cause was new. At Longbourn, he had often invited her to share his amusement and left her to carry what amusement did not mend. She had loved him for his wit and resented him for its uses, sometimes in the same hour. Tonight he was trying to do something more difficult than laugh at the world.

She could not punish him for being late when he had finally come.

"I do not wish to leave tomorrow," she said.

Not all the truth, but enough of it to stand.

Mr. Bennet nodded once. "Then you shall not."

"Thank you."

"Do not thank me too warmly. I am leaving one daughter in Derbyshire nearly smitten by the livestock and permitting another to remain in the house of a man whose library has already led her into dangerous partiality."

"Partiality for books?"

"Among other hazards."

"Papa."

"Ah, that familiar tone. I am reassured. You are still my Lizzy." He rose and came to stand beside her at the shelf. For a moment they both looked at the books and not at each other. "Mr. Darcy spoke well."

Elizabeth's throat tightened for no reason she was willing to examine. "Did he?"

"He spoke plainly. That is rare." Mr. Bennet took down a volume, glanced at the title, and replaced it. "He made no display of generosity. He did not pretend there was no inconvenience. He described the arrangement, the limits of it, and the persons responsible for it. A dull performance, in the best possible sense."

Elizabeth smiled faintly. "You approve of dullness?"

"In young men, I approve of it exceedingly when the alternative is charm."

The word *charm* landed between them with another name beneath it. Wickham was not spoken.

Mr. Bennet looked at her over the rims of spectacles he had finally put on. "I have not forgotten, Lizzy, that our family has lately had cause to distrust charming young men and careless fathers."

She went still.

He said it dryly, almost lightly, but not quite. The confession in it was small, and very like him. He would not unfold guilt upon the table. He would set down a corner of it and pretend it was a bookmark.

"Nor have I," she said.

"Then I shall try, for once, to be neither charming nor careless." He drew a breath, seeming mildly displeased by the exertion of sincerity. "Kitty may remain because she wishes it, because she is properly attended, and because I believe it may do her good. You may remain because your aunt remains, because the arrangement is respectable, and because you choose it."

The last words found her.

*Because you choose it.*

Her fingers found the cuff of her sleeve. Every reason she had given herself for remaining was true, and together they made a wall she could stand behind that she had constructed herself.

Her father had opened a door in it.

"I do choose it," she said, too softly.

Mr. Bennet heard. Of course he heard; he had always heard what amused him. Tonight he heard what mattered.

"Then stay, Lizzy," he said. "Only know why you stay."

The sentence should have relieved her. It did not. It followed her when they left the library and returned to the drawing room, where Kitty looked up with all her future held anxiously in her face.

"Well?" Kitty said, forgetting every lesson in composure at once.

Mr. Bennet folded his spectacles and put them away. "I have examined the evidence and find that Derbyshire may endure you a little longer."

Kitty rose so quickly that her needle rolled off the table. "Papa!"

"Do not embrace me with a needle in the neighborhood."

"I have not got it."

"That is even less comforting."

Georgiana had gone pink with pleasure. Mrs. Annesley retrieved the needle; Mrs. Gardiner looked from Mr. Bennet to Elizabeth and asked nothing. 

Mr. Darcy, who had stood on Elizabeth’s entrance, folded himself back into the wingback chair, held in that careful way she had begun to recognize — the restraint that meant he was feeling more than he intended to show.

Kitty turned at once to Georgiana. "Then we may see whether Mr. Grayfur is still a tyrant tomorrow."

"If the queen permits it," Georgiana said.

"She must. Papa has examined the evidence."

Mr. Bennet bowed slightly toward Miss Darcy. "I leave my daughter in your care, Miss Darcy, and your cat's governance. I trust one of you will prove equal to the charge."

Georgiana, startled and pleased into courage, answered, "We shall try, sir."

Mr. Darcy's eyes moved to his sister. His face did not soften as openly as it had in the stable, but Elizabeth saw the effort it cost him not to show too much before them all. Then his gaze came to her, and something in his face gave way. Not much. The corner of his mouth, a softening he did not catch in time.

# Chapter 26: The Fair at Lambton

The next morning, Mr. Bennet departed after an early breakfast, before the dew was off the lawns. At late breakfast, the table had not changed, but everyone seemed aware of the chair he no longer occupied — Kitty most of all. 

Determined not to be low, she praised the marmalade. Georgiana asked whether marmalade was always so highly considered in Hertfordshire. Mrs. Gardiner, with admirable gravity, gave a short account of orange preserves, London tradesmen, and Kitty’s mother’s opinion that no breakfast could be respectable without fruit.

Mr. Darcy listened as if breakfast talk, once admitted to his table, deserved the courtesy of being heard. Elizabeth had learned not to mistake it for dullness. He did not always speak; he registered. Georgiana's guarded eagerness, Kitty's determined cheerfulness, Mrs. Gardiner's careful effort to keep the talk easy — he seemed to see them all and interfere with none until interference would serve.

"There is a fair at Lambton today," he said when Mrs. Gardiner had finished.

He had mentioned it before, at the stables while Kitty and Georgiana were occupied with the kittens. He had described it then as if testing whether ordinary pleasure might be permitted.

Kitty set down her knife. "A fair?"

"A small one."

"How small?"

"Large enough to be called a fair by those who wish to attend it, and a nuisance by those who do not."

Georgiana's eyes went at once to Kitty, whose face had brightened in the most complete betrayal of all resolutions to be composed.

"Is it like Meryton market?" Kitty asked.

"Less military, I expect,” said Elizabeth.

"Less dominated by officers," Mrs. Gardiner added, with a glance at Elizabeth that carried more history than her words.

Kitty colored, but did not retreat. "Then it may be better."

Mr. Darcy's gaze moved from Kitty to Georgiana. "There are stalls in the market-place and along the green; some farmers bring stock, though not in any great number; there are sellers from Bakewell and Matlock, ribbons and straw work, gingerbread, tools, harness, toy-sellers, printed ballads, a fiddler by the inn, and games for children on the green. I have known it since I was a boy."

The last sentence altered the table. It was not said to invite tenderness; perhaps that was why it invited it. Elizabeth looked at him before she could prevent herself. He had not been describing amusement only, but a piece of the estate's life, one of those small public observances by which a great house acknowledged the country around it.

Kitty asked him, "And do you still like it?"

Mr. Darcy considered his answer with unnecessary seriousness. "In moderation."

Kitty considered this. "That is how Mary likes everything."

"Then Miss Mary Bennet and I are of one mind on more subjects than I had supposed."

How different such a sentence would once have sounded from him; how impossible it would once have been for him to say it at all.

Mrs. Gardiner's expression held a little of memory and a little of caution. "When I attended the Lambton fair, it was not always the quietest of places. Has it changed much?"

"In some respects, not much," said Mr. Darcy. "There is still a part of it best left to those who enjoy noise for its own sake. But in the earlier hours, and along the market-place and green, it is orderly enough. Mrs. Annesley means to walk there later with her sister's family, I believe, so we must not claim her attendance."

Georgiana's disappointment showed before she corrected it. "Her sister's youngest child has had a fever."

“And is now recovered enough to be shown the fair,” said Mr. Darcy. “Mrs. Annesley has been promised that privilege.”

Mrs. Gardiner smiled. "Then we must not rob her of it."

"We need not go," Georgiana said at once.

Kitty looked from Georgiana to Mr. Darcy, and then, with an effort which did her credit, looked down at her plate. The whole little battle was there: the wish to plead, the memory that pleading was Lydia's province, and the choice of silence because she had begun to value being trusted.

Mr. Darcy's gaze moved from Kitty's lowered eyes to Georgiana's face.

"If Mrs. Gardiner approves," he said, "and if you would like to see it, I can take you for an hour after luncheon."

Georgiana's answer came only through the lifting of her head.

Kitty forgot caution. "Oh, yes — if Aunt Gardiner approves. I mean, if Miss Darcy wishes it."

Georgiana smiled. "I should like it."

Elizabeth turned her cup a little in its saucer. She had no reason to be moved by so small a concession. Young people went to fairs; guardians permitted it; gentlemen accompanied sisters. Yet nothing in Mr. Darcy's manner suggested he thought it small. He had measured the noise, the crowd, Kitty's eagerness, Georgiana's shyness, Mrs. Annesley's absence, and his own authority — and chosen liberty.

It was not the kind of proof which announced itself. It merely changed what the day would be.

“Elizabeth?” her aunt asked. “What do you say?”

Elizabeth glanced up from the cup she had been turning. “Yes,” she said, and found the word less steady than she meant it to be. “If we keep to the earlier part, I think it may do very well.”

"Then we shall all go," said Mrs. Gardiner.

Kitty smiled as if Derbyshire had justified itself at last.

---

The road into the village was busier than Elizabeth had yet seen it: carts drawn to the side; farmers in their better coats; children darting where children ought not; women pausing in pairs to inspect goods and each other with equal care.

The green had been given over to booths, trestles, and small temporary wonders. A fiddler stood near the inn door, tightening a peg while a few children waited hopefully. From another direction came the smell of gingerbread, horses, damp straw, and hot metal from a brazier where a man repaired pots.

Mr. Darcy, who had ridden beside the carriage, dismounted before the servant had fully lowered the step.

Kitty came down from the carriage carefully, though delight kept breaking through. Georgiana landed beside her, gloved hands folded, looking not frightened exactly, but aware of every motion around her.

"It is very busy," she said.

"It is," Kitty answered. "But not all at once, if one looks at one thing at a time."

Elizabeth turned, surprised and pleased.

Georgiana considered this. "Then what should we look at first?"

"Something that cannot run away."

"The ribbons, then," said Elizabeth.

"Or the gingerbread," said Kitty.

“The gingerbread seller has too many admirers already,” Georgiana said.

"True. Ribbons are steadier."

Mr. Darcy, who had come to Georgiana's other side, did not smile openly. "Then we begin with ribbons."

They did not begin with ribbons. They began with being stopped three times before they reached the green. Mr. Darcy was greeted first by a farmer whose lease, Elizabeth gathered, had lately been renewed. A miller's wife wished him to know how proud she was that her eldest boy had been taken on at the stables. Then an older man, his back bent nearly double, removed his hat and forgot whatever he had meant to say.
