Mr. Darcy supplied it for him.

"Your granddaughter, she is recovered?"

"Aye, sir. That she is. Running about again, and her mother says more trouble than the fever."

"I am glad to hear it."

"Aye, sir. We are much beholden."

Mr. Darcy inclined his head. “A promising sign.”

Elizabeth walked on with Mrs. Gardiner.

"Lizzy," her aunt said in a low voice, "you are very silent."

"I am observing."

"That is often the most dangerous form of silence."

Elizabeth might have answered, but Kitty called to them from a stall where lengths of ribbon were displayed with all the triumph of scarlet, blue, green, and yellow that sun and cheap dye could produce. Georgiana stood beside her, looking at the happy confusion of colors with the solemnity of a person choosing between moral principles.

"This one," Kitty said, holding up a blue ribbon, "is nearly the color of the gray kitten's eyes."

Georgiana touched the ribbon. "It is pretty."

"Too bright for a tyrant," Elizabeth said. "He would require something sterner."

"Black velvet," Georgiana said, "if his government is to continue."

Kitty laughed; Georgiana pressed her lips together, but the laugh escaped all the same. The woman behind the stall, a broad-faced person with sharp eyes and a warm manner, looked from one young lady to the other with satisfaction.

"Miss Darcy has found merry company today," she said.

Georgiana’s color rose, but she answered with proper composure. “Yes. Miss Catherine is very kind.”

Kitty, perhaps to spare her any further notice, held up another ribbon. “This darker blue, perhaps?”

After the young ladies had obtained both the blue and the purple, they moved to the next stall, stuffed with well-made but poorly painted wooden toys.

"That was well done," Mrs. Gardiner said.

Elizabeth looked after Kitty and Georgiana. “It was,” she said, softer than she intended.

As they moved on, Mr. Darcy rejoined them. Elizabeth was conscious of the arrangement at once: Kitty and Georgiana ahead under Mrs. Gardiner's eye, herself and Mr. Darcy following at a proper distance. The world around them supplied witnesses by the dozen.

Every few steps someone greeted him. Not with the boldness of intimacy, nor with fear. Not with the formal respect his consequence always commanded, but with a confidence that seemed founded on long experience.

He knew names — not every name, but enough, and the names he did not know he did not pretend to know. He asked one woman whether her husband had found the new gate easier for the cart. He told a boy not to lead a pony through the thickest part of the crowd and was obeyed after only a little resentment. He stood aside for an older woman with a basket before she had to ask. He refused a painted wooden whistle Georgiana could not possibly want, but bought two small carved animals from the same boy after learning the boy's father had made them during a wet week when the hay could not be cut.

One went to Georgiana, a stiff-legged horse. The other he gave to Kitty, after a pause, with such formality that Kitty received it as if it had been a royal appointment.

"For me?"

"If you will accept it."

"It is a cow," Kitty said, delighted.

"So I believe."

"I shall call it Mr. Bennet."

Elizabeth laughed before she could help it. Mr. Darcy looked away for a moment, but the corner of his mouth betrayed him.

"An honor I shall not report to your father unless compelled," he said.

Kitty tucked the wooden cow into her reticule, where it made a most inconvenient bulge.

They reached a stall set a little back from the busiest part of the green, shaded by a canvas awning and crowded with straw bonnets, chip hats, ribbons, and artificial flowers. Several women were already there, turning brims, comparing trims, and discovering defects with the air of those who expected defects to lower the price. Georgiana came to a stop before a plain straw bonnet with a narrow brim and a pale ribbon about the crown.

Kitty noticed at once. "That one would suit you." She lifted it by the brim.

"Would it?"

"Yes. It is not too smart."

Elizabeth, drawn by a display of printed cottons at the next stall, had moved a little down the row. The two stalls stood almost together, separated only by a hanging length of muslin and a narrow passage; she could still see the girls when she turned her head, but Kitty, intent upon the bonnet, had lost sight of her.

"I mean, it would not look as if you had tried to be someone else,” Kitty went on. “It would look like you, only at a fair."

Elizabeth looked down at the printed cotton before her and pressed her lips together. It was affection, she reminded herself, and nearly a compliment.

The shopkeeper chuckled. "There's a recommendation I have not heard before. 'Like yourself, only at a fair.' I shall put it on a sign."

Elizabeth glanced back in time to see Georgiana smile, though her hand still hovered short of the bonnet. Mrs. Gardiner had stepped aside to admire a length of lace, and Mr. Darcy, a few steps beyond the hats, had been claimed by a tenant who wished to present a niece visiting from Chesterfield. The introduction was performed with the gravity of a court ceremony: the man beaming, the young woman curtseying once and then hovering as if uncertain whether a second curtsey might be required.

Georgiana looked behind her. "Where is Miss Elizabeth?"

Kitty answered without thought, turning the bonnet this way and that.

"With Mr. Darcy, I believe. You’re right, she will know."

The words were nothing. They were everything. They came with no emphasis, no arch look, no awareness that they might be heard by anyone beyond Georgiana. Kitty spoke as she might have said Elizabeth was with Mrs. Gardiner, or at the bookstall, or near the carriage. *With Mr. Darcy* had become, in Kitty's mouth, a location.

The shopkeeper’s attention fixed on Kitty. At the far end of the stall, another woman, who had been comparing artificial flowers, lifted her eyes and then lowered them again with an expression of sudden industry.

"Is that so?" said the shopkeeper, not quite to Kitty.

Kitty, now attending only to the brim, said, "Yes, I think so. They were just behind us."

Georgiana, catching more in the shopkeeper's tone than in the words, lowered her hand.

At the far end of the stall, the other woman gave a small, satisfied sound. "A fine thing," she said, "if it were so."

Kitty looked up. "If what were so?"

"Nothing, miss," said the shopkeeper, with the practiced innocence of one who had meant everything. "Only that Pemberley has been long without a lady to choose bonnets for young ladies. Miss Darcy is as sweet as May, but a house wants more than sweetness, does it not?"

"Aye," said the other woman. "And Mr. Darcy wants—"

She stopped.

Elizabeth, from the neighboring stall, had heard every word. She turned back at once; she could not let this continue.

"Indeed," she said, because civility demanded a tone and her own pride supplied the steadiness of it, "we are only choosing a bonnet."

"To be sure, miss," said the shopkeeper.

The woman at the flowers, less easily silenced, smiled at Elizabeth with a warmth that was almost maternal. "Only that it does a place good to see young ladies about it. Miss Darcy looks well today. Some company suits a house."

Elizabeth's gaze moved to Georgiana, then to Kitty, then — fatally — to Mr. Darcy, who had now entered the stall.

He had heard enough—of that she was certain. All expression left him except courtesy. It was done so quickly, so cleanly that no one unaccustomed to him would have detected a change. His posture did not stiffen; his voice did not cool by any degree that could be measured; he merely became the gentleman of Pemberley in public, exact in attention and inaccessible behind it.

"Georgiana," he said, "if you like the bonnet, you should have it."

Georgiana looked uncertain. "I wanted Miss Elizabeth's opinion."

Mr. Darcy turned to Elizabeth. "What do you advise?"

There was no impropriety in the request. There was no warmth in it either — not that anyone else could claim. He gave her the safest possible place in the exchange and removed himself from it.

Elizabeth took the bonnet from Kitty because her hands required employment. She examined the straw, the ribbon, the proportion of brim to crown, as if bonnets had become the single test of judgment left in the world.

"It is well chosen," she said. "Kitty is right. A very fair bonnet."

Georgiana laughed, relieved. Kitty laughed too, and the shopkeeper declared she would shave off an extra shilling for so philosophical a recommendation.

Mr. Darcy paid for the bonnet. He paid what was asked. Elizabeth noticed that absurd detail and hated herself for noticing. Had he paid more, she might have called it display. Paying exactly, she called distance. Every action offered two meanings, and the one she chose revealed too much of her own heart.

Mrs. Gardiner returned from the lace with her acquaintance and saw enough in Elizabeth's face to look twice, but not enough to understand. Georgiana thanked everyone, and, with a quiet decisiveness, put it on at once, handing her own bonnet to Mrs. Gardiner. 

“The crowd is increasing,” Mr. Darcy said. “We had better return toward the carriage.”

It was perfectly sensible. Elizabeth heard retreat.

They went on. The fair had not altered. The fiddler had begun to play. Children still ran; women still bargained; two dogs engaged in a disagreement beneath a cart and were scolded by three people who owned neither of them. Georgiana touched the ribbon of her new bonnet when she thought no one observed, and when Kitty declared it a triumph, she smiled with real pleasure.

Elizabeth saw all of it at a distance of three feet and several miles.

Mr. Darcy did not walk beside her again. He took Mrs. Gardiner’s arm instead. Once, when a boy carrying a tray of gingerbread came too near, Mr. Darcy checked his own step and Mrs. Gardiner’s, leaving Elizabeth room to pass untouched. Once, when a woman at the next booth looked from Mr. Darcy to Elizabeth with the beginning of a smile, he turned away before the smile could finish. Once his gaze came to Elizabeth's face and glanced off again, as if he dared not know her there.

He might be shielding her from public presumption, sparing her the mortification of an assumption she had not invited. Or he might be withdrawing because the assumption itself was unwelcome. She could build either reading from the same evidence.

That was the terror.

She walked on. At Hunsford she had been so certain. She had read his pride, his contempt, his injury to Jane with such confidence. Then his letter had exposed the certainty itself as the deepest error. She had believed herself humbled enough, since then, to be fair.

But fairness had become desire without asking leave.

The Lambton people, with their half-finished sentences and hopeful looks, had placed her where she had not dared place herself. They had imagined her into Pemberley. They had given Mr. Darcy a happiness and made her the instrument of it. And some unguarded part of her had answered *yes* before judgment could object.

And now Mr. Darcy had become correct — impeccably, impenetrably correct.

A man may be correct from kindness. A man may be correct from embarrassment. A man may be correct from regret that others have presumed too much.

Which man was Mr. Darcy? And what was Elizabeth, if she could be so uncertain of him — and so little certain of herself?

They returned to the carriage before the hour was quite gone. Mr. Darcy handed them all in. His manner was all that it ought to be. No one could have accused him of coldness. No one could have claimed warmth.

Kitty carried the carved cow, a paper of gingerbread, and enough observations to sustain the whole road back. Georgiana wore the straw bonnet and touched its ribbon more than once. Mrs. Gardiner was pleased with the outing, with Georgiana, with Kitty, and with Derbyshire in general.

Elizabeth sat opposite Georgiana. Kitty, beside her, began at once to argue that Mr. Bennet the cow had a statesmanlike countenance. Georgiana defended her carved horse from charges of excessive dignity. Mrs. Gardiner smiled indulgently and said both animals must be judged when they had been longer acquainted with Pemberley.

Elizabeth responded only when required. She answered once when Kitty appealed to her. Mrs. Gardiner, under cover of adjusting her glove, studied her niece's profile and said nothing. Elizabeth looked out as Lambton drew back from the carriage windows and the road rose toward the park.

Only one person did not accept her performance.

Mr. Darcy rode beside them for part of the way, close enough that when the road narrowed and the carriage slowed, Elizabeth could see him through the open window. He was not looking at Georgiana's bonnet or Kitty's animated hands or Mrs. Gardiner's amused face. He was looking at her.

She turned away, but already the concern on his face had become another piece of evidence she did not know how to read.

By the time Pemberley's trees closed around the road, Elizabeth had arranged her countenance into something nearly like ease. It cost her more than she had expected.

He had done nothing wrong, and she was less certain than ever.

The carriage stopped. Mr. Darcy again was at the door before the servant had fully lowered the step. He handed down Mrs. Gardiner, then Georgiana, then Kitty. When Elizabeth's turn came, his hand was steady beneath hers.

"Miss Bennet," he said. Only that.

She looked at him. What she wanted to see and what was true had become, at some point during the afternoon, indistinguishable. She had been wrong about this man once with total confidence. She could not afford to be wrong again. And she could no longer tell which error she was making.

"Mr. Darcy."

She withdrew her hand and went inside with the others.

# Chapter 27: A Letter From Jane

Pemberley had not altered while they were gone.

That was the unkindness of it.

The same doors opened. The same servants appeared with the same noiseless precision. The same cool hall received them from Lambton as if nothing in the world had been disturbed by a bonnet, a shopkeeper’s smile, half a sentence left unfinished, and Mr. Darcy’s hand beneath hers at the carriage step.

Elizabeth entered with the others and understood, with a clarity almost worse than confusion, that the disturbance was entirely her own.

Mr. Darcy did not follow them in. Having handed them down with all proper attention, he took his horse’s bridle from the groom and said something to Georgiana of Robinson, the west stable, and the new foal. A perfectly natural excuse. Also, Elizabeth discovered, another fact capable of four meanings.

Kitty was first across the threshold, still bright with the afternoon, one hand guarding the reticule in which Mr. Bennet the wooden cow had acquired all the consequence of a family relic. The fair came in with her. It clung to her pelisse in a trace of gingerbread; it had left dust along Mrs. Gardiner’s hem and a faint crease in Georgiana’s new bonnet ribbon. Somewhere outside, his horse’s hooves crossed the stones toward the stable-yard, each strike too distinct in the quiet.

Within doors, every footfall was softened, every voice lowered, every object already restored to its proper place.

Elizabeth had no proper place to restore herself to.

The hall offered no opinion. Its floors shone, its doors closed softly, and every servant had the mercy to notice nothing. Such discretion ought to have been kindness. Instead it left Elizabeth alone with the one witness she could not dismiss: herself.

The witness was exacting. It knew what must be done. Kitty’s delight must be answered. Miss Darcy’s bonnet must be praised. Mrs. Gardiner must not be alarmed. Mr. Darcy’s absence must not be examined, though every hoofbeat outside seemed determined to offer itself as evidence.

Elizabeth did each thing in turn. She smiled at the wooden cow. She admired the bonnet. She answered her aunt with enough sense that no one required the answer twice.

It was not composure.

It was obedience to the shape of composure.

A footman approached with the salver. Mrs. Gardiner glanced at the direction of the uppermost letter and took it with a smile. “From Gracechurch Street. Your uncle has remembered that his wife exists, which is always agreeable.”

The next letter was offered to Elizabeth.

Jane’s hand.

For one brief, blessed second, everything steadied. Even when Mrs. Bennet had dictated half a page and Lydia had seized the pen to add underlinings, Jane’s hand remained composed. It belonged to home; to candor without severity; to affection which did not press to be understood before it had been offered.

Elizabeth closed her fingers round it too quickly.

“From Longbourn?” Mrs. Gardiner asked.

“Yes. From Jane.”

“How fortunate. You will have news to carry us through the evening.”

Elizabeth meant to answer. She could not immediately find the words. Mr. Darcy entered then from the outer door, his hat in one hand, the faint scent of horse and summer air coming in with him before the house swallowed both. He spoke first to Georgiana — something of Robinson’s verdict upon the foal, perhaps, or of whether the bonnet should be sent upstairs — but Elizabeth could feel the care with which he did not look toward her.

It should have relieved her.

It did not.

“I believe,” she said, holding the letter as if it required all her attention, “I have a small headache. The sun, perhaps. Nothing of consequence.”

Mrs. Gardiner’s concern sharpened at once; Kitty looked stricken; Georgiana turned from her brother, her question to him left unfinished.

“Only a very slight one,” Elizabeth said, before their kindness could make a scene of it. “I shall read Jane’s letter and be restored to perfect sense.”

Mr. Darcy had ceased speaking. His face showed concern, quickly checked.

His face showed concern, quickly checked. Not coldness. Not warmth that might be observed. Something held in such strict custody that she could not tell whether it had been meant for her or for the room.

“Miss Bennet,” he said, “I hope you will soon be better.”

“Thank you.”

Two words. They had managed a whole conversation of two words.

She went upstairs before she could decide whether that was better or worse.

---

The bedroom was easier than she could bear.

Afternoon light lay softly across the carpet. A glass of water waited by the washstand; the bell-pull hung within easy reach; the upholstered chair near the window had been placed at just such an angle as might invite rest.

She had fled to be alone and found herself surrounded by consideration.

Jane would fix it. 

She laid her bonnet and gloves on the bed with less care than either deserved, crossed to the washstand, and pressed cool water to her temples. Then she sank into the chair by the window.

Jane’s letter smelled faintly of home.

Elizabeth held it a moment longer. The paper had warmed a little beneath her fingers. As if steadiness might be borrowed from paper, she smoothed her thumb once over Jane’s direction.

She unfolded the sheet.

*My dearest Lizzy,*

*I hardly know how to write what has happened, though I have written the words twice already and still cannot make them look less extraordinary.*

The comfort of Jane’s hand vanished at once.

She read on.

*Mr. Bingley is returned to Netherfield.*

The words struck before meaning could follow.

There were more words. Elizabeth saw them. She did not at first understand them. She read on, too quickly now, searching for the shape of the event before the fact itself could settle.

*He came yesterday, quite unexpectedly. We had heard nothing of his intention, though my mother insists she had felt all morning that something particular would occur, and that her nerves had foretold it with remarkable accuracy. Papa says her nerves are entitled to the credit of every event in the county, since they are never quiet enough to miss one.*

The sentence had her father’s dry turn in it, but it drew no laugh. Elizabeth read past it with her pulse still beating too high.

*He called at Longbourn before dinner. I will not pretend I was not surprised, or that I behaved with all the composure I wished. He looked very much as he used to look, and yet not quite so. There was a steadiness in him which I do not know how to describe. He asked whether I had lately heard from you, and whether you and my aunt remained at Pemberley. He spoke warmly of Derbyshire.*

Warmly of Derbyshire.

Elizabeth lowered the letter. Beyond the window, the rising ground climbed steadily away from the house, offering no road, no answer, no visible proof of any journey begun from Pemberley.

Mr. Bingley had been here. He and Mr. Darcy must have had hours together — at dinner, after dinner, in all the ordinary intervals by which gentlemen contrived to be private without seeming to arrange it. Then Mr. Bingley had gone south. Now he was returned to Netherfield.

Nothing in that proved anything. That was precisely why it would not leave her alone.

*Today he came again. Before dinner, he had spoken to Papa.*

Elizabeth’s hand tightened on the page. There was only one meaning to that, and yet she read on as if Jane might soften it, explain it, make it less enormous.

Jane’s next line was very short.

*Lizzy, I am very happy.*

Elizabeth’s hand went to her mouth. For a moment, there was nothing in her but joy.

Jane was happy.

Jane, who had smiled at Netherfield when she had been feverish and still tried to make everyone comfortable. Jane, who had borne disappointment with such gentleness that others had mistaken it for indifference. Jane, whose good opinion had been used against her by those who did not deserve it. Happy.

Mr. Bingley had returned. Mr. Bingley had spoken. Mr. Bingley had not drifted forever in some London drawing room, warm-hearted and weak, while his sisters arranged his future and his friend’s error stood between him and the woman he loved.

Jane was happy.

A laugh rose then, unsteady and bright, with tears close behind it.

Then joy, having filled the room, began to make space for other things.

She had not been there.

That was the first wound. Not that Jane was happy — never that — but that Jane’s happiness had entered Longbourn without her. Netherfield had opened again. Bingley had crossed the drive. Mr. Bennet had been spoken to. Jane had accepted. And Elizabeth had been miles away, learning of it only after happiness had already taken its seat among them.

She had received it by post. 

She read the rest with an attention sharpened almost to pain.

Jane was restrained. Jane was bewildered. Jane did not write like a woman who had expected to be rewarded. She wrote like one who feared that happiness might vanish if described too boldly.

*I cannot account for the suddenness of his return. He says he has long wished to come back, and that he has been very foolish. You know him well enough to imagine the kindness of his manner; indeed, I think there is more kindness in it than ever, for it seems less anxious to please everybody and more desirous of speaking truly.*

Less anxious to please everybody.

Had Mr. Bingley become more himself, or had someone restored him to himself? Had absence taught him to know his own heart? Had his sisters’ designs exhausted themselves? Had London at last become dull?

Or had Mr. Darcy told him?

The thought was not new. It had been waiting beneath every line. It rose now because Jane, with all her mildness, had written the only sentence capable of summoning it.

*He has been very foolish.*

Elizabeth heard, without wishing to hear, another letter.

*I had often seen him in love before.*

The words came back with their old cold confidence. At Hunsford, Mr. Darcy had been certain of Mr. Bingley’s heart, certain of Jane’s reticence, certain of his own right to divide them. Elizabeth had hated him for it — and then hated herself for having been so certain, too.

If Mr. Bingley had returned because Mr. Darcy had spoken, then Mr. Darcy had corrected in humility what he had once done in certainty.

If Mr. Bingley had returned for some other reason, then Elizabeth had wished Mr. Darcy into virtue without evidence.

There lay the humiliation.

*My mother is in such spirits as you can imagine, though perhaps you cannot, for imagination has limits and Mama’s spirits have none. She has already mentioned lace, settlements, gowns, Mrs. Long’s astonishment, and the probability of my being the happiest creature in the world no fewer than twelve times. Papa has been very kind. He says Mr. Bingley is a sensible young man because he has chosen the best woman in England and foolish because he took so long to discover it.*

Elizabeth smiled through fresh tears.

*Lydia remains out of humor. She says there is no merit in being married if one must be engaged in so quiet a way, and that she would have liked at least a carriage upset or a duel. Mary has observed that those who marry from the purest motives need not desire violent distinction. Mama told her not to be tiresome on such a day.*

Jane wrote that Kitty’s absence had been much felt, that their mother had lamented it in one breath and rejoiced in it in the next, because “dear Kitty must learn elegance at Pemberley, though she is cruelly missing all the wedding talk.” Jane hoped Kitty was well, hoped Miss Darcy would not tire of her, hoped Elizabeth would tell her everything of Derbyshire when they were together again.

Then, near the end:

*I wish you were here. Not because I would recall you from what is proper or pleasant, but because there are some joys one longs first to tell to the person who has known the sorrow. I think you will understand me even when I hardly understand myself.*

Elizabeth’s tears came then in earnest.

She understood Jane’s restraint, her gratitude, her wonder. She understood, too, the kindness of that sentence: Jane wished her there, but did not call her home.

The letter had brought happiness from Longbourn. It had not brought Elizabeth back to it.

Elizabeth folded the letter once along its crease and then opened it again immediately, as if evidence might rearrange itself under inspection.

It did not.

Jane was happy. Mr. Bingley had returned. He had been very foolish. He had spoken to Papa.

And still there was no proof of Mr. Darcy.

She rose too quickly. The room shifted, though whether from the headache she had invented or the one she had produced, she could not tell. She crossed to the washstand, dipped her fingers in the water, and touched them to her temples again. The water was cool. It did not answer any question.

If Mr. Darcy had acted, what then?

Nothing, she told herself. Nothing must follow. Jane’s happiness was Jane’s. Mr. Bingley’s choice was Mr. Bingley’s. Mr. Darcy’s conscience, if conscience had moved him, belonged to him alone. Elizabeth had no right to build a private claim upon another person’s repentance.

But if he had acted and said nothing — if he had repaired the injury without presenting the repair at her feet — then the proof was cleaner than gratitude. It asked nothing. It stood at a distance. It did not say, *See what I have done; love me for it.*

That was unbearable.

She had been wrong before. At Hunsford, she had been magnificently, eloquently wrong. Wrong with arguments, wrong with proofs, wrong with the whole force of injured pride and lively intelligence. She had believed herself discerning because she had been offended. She had believed herself just because she had been angry.

And since then? Since Pemberley? His altered manner — if it had altered. His care for Georgiana. His civility to the Gardiners. His patient attention to Kitty. His handing liberty to his sister in small portions, and looking as if each portion cost him something dear. She had called that proof. She had watched, weighed, considered, corrected herself.

And still she did not know whether she was seeing more clearly, or only wanting more dangerously.

A knock sounded.

“Lizzy?” Mrs. Gardiner’s voice came through the door. “May I come in?”

Elizabeth folded the letter at last. “Yes. Of course.”

Mrs. Gardiner entered with a glass in one hand and concern in her face. She took in Elizabeth’s eyes, the letter, the abandoned gloves, and the chair pushed back at an angle. She set the lemonade on the small table beside it.

“I brought you lemonade,” she said. “It is a very poor remedy for a headache, but an excellent excuse for bringing oneself into a room.”

Elizabeth managed an unsteady laugh. “Then it is better than most remedies.” 

Her aunt set the lemonade on the small table beside Elizabeth. “My dear,” she said, “what has happened?”

Elizabeth’s fingers closed round Jane’s letter until the paper creased faintly beneath her thumb. There could be no keeping such news from her aunt. Nor did she wish to keep it. “Good news. Jane.” She tried to smile as the words deserved. “The best news.”

She held out the letter, then drew it back. Some parts were Jane’s before they were anyone else’s. “Mr. Bingley has returned to Netherfield.”

Her aunt’s hand went to her heart. “Oh, Lizzy.”

“He spoke to my father.”

The delight in her aunt’s face was immediate and unguarded. “He has proposed?”

“Yes.”

“And Jane?”

“Very happy.”

“My dear girl.” Her aunt came to her then, not with exclamation, not with a hundred questions, but with both hands extended. Elizabeth took them and was drawn, for one moment, into an embrace that smelled of lavender, road dust, and gingerbread. “What joy. What perfect joy for her.”

“Yes,” Elizabeth said into her aunt’s shoulder. “It is.” Her breath caught.

Her aunt held her a little longer and then stepped back enough to look at her. “My dear, I cannot lay all this at joy’s door. You were not easy before Jane’s letter.”

Elizabeth gave a watery laugh. “Then I am worse than I thought, for I cannot even receive happiness properly.”

“That is nonsense, and you know it.”

“I wish I knew it.”

Elizabeth turned away. She took up Jane’s letter, folded it, and aligned the edges with unnecessary care. “Mr. Bingley was lately here.”

“Yes.”

“He left Pemberley, and now he is at Netherfield.”

Mrs. Gardiner waited.

Elizabeth could not say, *Mr. Darcy separated them once.*

What she said was, “He and Mr. Darcy were in the same house for days before he went. They must have spoken of many things.” Of Jane, her mind supplied at once. “That is all I know.”

Mrs. Gardiner did not answer at once.

“It is not enough to mean anything,” Elizabeth said quickly. “Mr. Bingley may have returned from his own conviction. He ought to have done. Perhaps he did. Perhaps he needed no one to tell him what he felt. Perhaps men of large fortune go from Derbyshire to Hertfordshire and propose the next day from no cause at all except the excellence of Hertfordshire roads.”

“Come, Lizzy. Sit down.” Her aunt guided her gently into the chair, then drew the dressing-table chair close and sat.

“I do not know anything,” Elizabeth said.

There it was. Flat and ugly and true.

Mrs. Gardiner studied her for a moment. “You know that Jane is happy.”

“Yes.”

“And that Mr. Bingley has chosen her.”

“Yes.”

“And that whatever brought him there, his speaking to your father was his own act.”

Elizabeth nodded. “Yes.”

“Those are not small things.”

“No.”

“But they are not the whole of what troubles you.”

Elizabeth looked down at the letter. Jane’s happiness should have been able to fill the whole room. Instead it had become a lamp turned upon every hidden corner of Elizabeth’s mind.

“Aunt,” she said, “have you ever been afraid that you have corrected one error only by falling into another?”

Mrs. Gardiner did not smile. “Often. It is one of the few amusements of growing older.”

Despite herself, Elizabeth almost smiled; some of the tightness in her chest eased.

“I believed Mr. Darcy proud and ungenerous,” Elizabeth said. “In some things, I was right. In more, I was wrong. Since coming here, I have seen —” She stopped again because the words would not remain general. “I have seen him act otherwise than I expected.”

“Yes,” Mrs. Gardiner said.

“And I have tried to be fair.”

“Yes.”

“And perhaps I have become something else.”

Her aunt’s hand came to rest on the back of the chair.

Elizabeth’s mouth twisted. “Partial.”

The word hung there. It was not confession, but it was nearer to one than Elizabeth had meant to come.

“Partiality is not always an enemy to judgment.”

“It has been to mine before.”

“Ah. With Mr. Wickham.”

Elizabeth flinched. Mrs. Gardiner saw it, but did not retract the name. There was mercy in that, too. Wickham should not be allowed the dignity of being unspeakable.

“I liked Mr. Wickham because he made liking him easy,” Elizabeth said. “I disliked Mr. Darcy because he made disliking him easy. There was sense in neither judgment, only pleasure. One pleasure was being flattered. The other was being offended.”

Mrs. Gardiner was silent a moment. “And what pleasure is there now?”

Elizabeth did not answer.

A sound came from below: a door closing, a man’s step in the passage, then quiet again. Pemberley had a way of making even distant sounds seem deliberate.

Mrs. Gardiner followed the direction of her attention.

“I saw Mr. Darcy as I came up,” she said.

Elizabeth went still.

“He asked after you.”

She could not trust herself to speak.

“He wished to know whether you had recovered from the fatigue of Lambton.” Mrs. Gardiner paused. “Or whether anything there had made you uneasy.” Her brow drew together, as if the wording had puzzled her.

Elizabeth’s face warmed at once.

*Anything there.*

Not the crowd. Not the heat. Not the length of the walk. *Anything*.

The word carried her straight to Lambton: to the bonnet-stall, the woman’s smile, Mr. Darcy’s careful withdrawal afterward. Whether it meant concern, apology, or only civility, she could not tell.

“How very considerate,” she said.

Mrs. Gardiner’s brows rose slightly.

Elizabeth heard herself and wished to snatch the words back. They had come out too polished, too brittle.

She tried again. “I was not made uneasy by Lambton. Not — that is, not by anything of consequence.”

Mrs. Gardiner did not press her. “He did not come himself. That was well done of him, I think.”

Elizabeth’s answer came more softly than she intended. “Yes.”

“He appeared concerned.”

Elizabeth had not prepared herself for that word.

Mrs. Gardiner held her gaze with calm. “I am not matchmaking. Do not prepare a speech. I am observing that he appeared concerned, and that he chose not to force his concern upon you. Those are both facts.”

Facts. Elizabeth had wanted facts. She had asked, silently and often, for proof. She had not considered that proof might be made of restraint; that it might arrive by post, addressed in Jane’s hand, and say nothing of Mr. Darcy at all.

Mrs. Gardiner rose. “You should not sit here until dinner.”

“I do not mean to be ungrateful for Jane’s happiness by wandering about like a tragic heroine.”

“I would not advise tragedy. It requires better lighting and more obedient hair.”

Elizabeth looked at her aunt in astonishment, then laughed.

“There,” said Mrs. Gardiner. “That is improved. Walk, Lizzy. Not far, and not where you will lose yourself. But walk. Thoughts are apt to grow very large in a closed room.”

Elizabeth looked toward the window. The late light lay across the lawn, soft and slanting. Beyond it, the paths dropped toward the wilds, where Georgiana had once shown them a secret and where Elizabeth had first seen something of Pemberley that was not grandeur.

“Perhaps I will.”

---

The air outside was cool beneath the beeches, where moss softened the path’s edge and the first damp breath of the stream rose through the ferny hollows. A rabbit broke from the fern at Elizabeth’s approach and vanished under the trees. The noise and color of the Lambton fair seemed to belong to another day.

Elizabeth walked without choosing the direction. Her feet, being wiser than her mind, took her toward the lower path.

The gravel gave way to firmer earth. Branches drew nearer. The house, when she looked back, stood in partial view between the trees, not softened exactly, but made less declarative by distance. It had once intimidated her even in imagination. Then she had seen it and found it beautiful in a way that did not boast of its own importance. Then she had met its master in its grounds and learned that Pemberley’s restraint answered only one question about him.

The path bent again, and the house disappeared.

She remembered Georgiana walking here, offering names of shrubs and small histories of improvements as if each sentence were a coin she had saved and was nervous to spend. 

Georgiana’s laugh had been rarer then. Since then, he had opened doors.

Not all at once. Never theatrically. He had permitted a fair. He had invited Kitty. He had made Georgiana’s pleasure possible, then withdrawn the instant local hopes placed Elizabeth where no one had asked whether she wished to stand.

Had he withdrawn for her sake, not his own?

The thought stopped her where she stood.

A bird rustled the leaves above her. She looked up and saw nothing but motion after the fact.

Might he have been protecting her? The thought was both reasonable and dangerous. It fit the evidence too well. His immediate correctness, his care not to leave her exposed at the bonnet-stall, his refusal afterward to walk at her side, his inquiry through Mrs. Gardiner instead of coming himself — a pattern could be made of it.

A man may be correct from kindness.

Or from embarrassment.

Or from regret.

Elizabeth drew a breath and found it unsteady. 

She resumed walking.

The path descended a little, and the water grew louder, though still unseen. Pemberley did not announce all its beauties. It let them arrive by turns, as if the visitor’s heart might be trusted to keep pace.

Elizabeth walked faster.

A twig caught at the hem of her gown. She freed it with more force than necessary and then stood with the fabric in her hand, breathing as if she had been the one caught.

What did she want?

Elizabeth had never thought herself timid in the matter of wanting. She had refused Mr. Collins against every practical argument, and Mr. Darcy against every worldly one. She knew how to reject what her heart could not accept.

It was the accepting that frightened her. Nor could she pretend that feeling, because inconvenient, did not exist.

What did she want?

She wanted Jane happy. That was simple.

She wanted Mr. Bingley worthy of Jane’s happiness. That was only just.

She wanted Mr. Darcy to have told him. There. It was said nowhere, but it existed. She wanted Mr. Darcy to have done the difficult, quiet thing. She wanted his reserve at the fair to have been protection rather than retreat. She wanted his inquiry to mean concern, not mere civility. She wanted the evidence to point where her heart had already gone.

Her heart.

Elizabeth gave a short laugh that startled a nearby blackbird from the undergrowth. It scolded her from a safer branch, and she could not blame it.

Very well. Her heart, since euphemism had failed.

It had not declared itself grandly. It had not asked permission. It had done far worse: it had made evidence of his restraint, his kindness, his respect, his patience. It had built, piece by piece, an argument for him; and because Elizabeth understood arguments, she had mistaken construction for caution.

Fairness had become desire without asking leave.

The water appeared through the trees, silvered in the late sun. Elizabeth stopped at the turn. She remembered standing not far from here with Georgiana, listening as she explained the stepping-stones and blushed at having made so much of so small a place.

She smoothed open the letter again.

She read only one line.

*Lizzy, I am very happy.*

“Then let it be enough,” Elizabeth said aloud. Her voice sounded strange in the trees.

Be happy for Jane. Be happy without dragging Mr. Darcy into every corner of it.

But he was already there.

She knew enough of who he was to be afraid. He was proud. He was honorable. He could be severe. He could be wrong with alarming strength. He loved deeply and guarded fiercely. He had learned, or was learning, that protection could become imprisonment and judgment could become injury. He listened. He altered. Not always gracefully, not always easily, but enough that she could no longer pretend not to see it.

The question was whether she could trust herself where he was concerned.

She folded Jane’s letter again, slower this time.

She had come out for certainty and found none. Only the shape of her own wish, clearer than before and far more difficult to dismiss.

She wanted him to be good. She wanted his goodness to have reached Jane. She wanted it enough to distrust herself for wanting it.

Elizabeth stood until the light shifted and the first coolness of evening entered the shade. Dinner was soon. Mr. Darcy would be there, and Mr. Bingley’s name would be spoken. Elizabeth would have to sit at table with one and hear the other without betraying herself.

The return path was shorter, or else her thoughts had spent themselves into a shape that could be carried. The house reappeared by degrees. First the upper windows, bright with reflected sky. Then the stone front. Then the steps, the door, the settled order of a place that could contain fairs, letters, misjudgments, and dinner without altering its expression.

Elizabeth envied it.

By the time she reached her room, the maid had come to lay out a fresh gown. Elizabeth set Jane’s letter on the dressing table beside her gloves and stood alone before the glass.

Her face looked like hers. That was something. Her eyes were a little too bright, but candlelight would forgive much. She smoothed one curl, failed to improve it, and let it be.

She picked it up once more, then hesitated. It was not a letter for dinner tables or eager questions. She set it beneath her handkerchief, where it would be safe and near and not visible to the first person who entered.

Jane was happy.

Mr. Darcy would be at dinner.

Elizabeth rang for her maid.

# Chapter 28: At Dinner

At dinner, every place was correct.

Darcy had come to depend upon that fact with a confidence so old it had ceased to be confidence at all. The table knew its business. The silver lay at its proper angles; the candles were trimmed; the servants moved without collision or display; each chair had been drawn out to receive the person for whom it was intended. Pemberley, when summoned to order, obeyed.

Miss Elizabeth Bennet did not.

She entered with Mrs. Gardiner and Miss Catherine a moment before Georgiana, and nothing in her conduct could have supplied an accusation. She was dressed for dinner. Her color was a little heightened. She spoke to Mrs. Annesley with composure, smiled when Kitty turned to ask whether she and Georgiana might attempt a duet after dinner, and took her place at table without mistaking chair, glass, or expectation.

There was no impropriety in her.

There was no ease.

Darcy stood until the ladies were seated and then took his own chair. At his left hand, Miss Elizabeth unfolded her napkin with hands that did not tremble. That was worse. If she had trembled, he might have known where to begin with concern. Instead there was only precision: the napkin laid across her lap, the glass touched and released, the glance that traveled past him across the table and found safe lodging with her aunt.

Not with him. She was close enough that he could hear the silk of her sleeve shift against the chair. He would have preferred distance.

This afternoon, he had asked Mrs. Gardiner whether the excursion to Lambton had over-fatigued her niece. He had tried to make the question what any host might ask. He had failed. Mrs. Gardiner's pause before she answered had told him so.

*Whether anything there had made Miss Bennet uneasy* — those had been his words, or near enough to them to condemn him. He had not been able to say less. He could not permit himself to say more.

At Lambton, he had understood immediately what the women at the stall implied. He had seen — or believed he had seen — Miss Elizabeth’s composure arrive a moment after it was needed. He had felt, in one hot instant of public exposure, the whole reckless shape of his own hope placed before her by strangers who had no right to touch it. 

Afterward he had done what propriety demanded. He had withdrawn. He had given her distance. He had placed propriety between them like a shield.

But perhaps such protection, in his hands, had looked like retreat.

"Mr. Darcy," Mrs. Gardiner said, "Georgiana tells me the west stable has acquired a new object of tyranny."

Georgiana colored at once. Kitty looked delighted.

"The stable, I understand," said Darcy, recalled to his duty, "has acquired a kitten of decided opinions. Whether tyranny is yet proved, I leave to those with greater experience of its government."

"Mr. Grayfur is certainly a tyrant," Kitty said. "He struck Mr. Bennet this afternoon."

Darcy looked blankly at her for a moment. Then he remembered.

"The wooden cow from the fair," Kitty added, though she need not have. He had bought the thing himself.

"Ah," said Mrs. Gardiner, with admirable gravity. "Then the assault was political."

Georgiana laughed softly, and Kitty recovered enough to give a full account of the offense: Mr. Grayfur had pushed the cow from a low shelf, not once but twice, and then sat upon the fallen statesman with every appearance of having conquered the county. Mrs. Annesley declared that such conduct could not be encouraged. Georgiana proposed that perhaps Mr. Grayfur mistook the cow for a rival.

Miss Elizabeth smiled.

Darcy saw it because he had been trying not to look at her.

The smile was true, but it vanished almost before it had done its work. She answered Kitty when called upon, added that Mr. Bennet had often endured worse treatment from members of his own household, and let the table laugh. It was precisely the sort of thing she might have said yesterday. Yet yesterday, the remark would have carried her with it. Tonight it seemed to cost her something.

Darcy broke the trout on his plate into pieces he did not taste. She had pleaded a headache this afternoon and gone upstairs. Whatever had passed in the interval, she had not come down to dinner the same.

"Oh, Lizzy," Kitty said, "I forgot — did you have a letter from Jane?"

Miss Elizabeth's hand paused on her glass. "Yes."

"What does she say? Did Jane say whether Mama has forgiven me for being away?"

Miss Elizabeth turned to her sister. "Forgiven you? I believe Mama has nearly arranged your future elegance from Derbyshire."

Kitty brightened. "Then she misses me."

"Exceedingly. She misses you, praises you, laments you, and believes Pemberley will improve you beyond recognition."

"I do not want to be beyond recognition," Kitty said, alarmed. "Only a little improved."

Mrs. Gardiner looked down at her plate. Miss Elizabeth's mouth curved, but the quickness did not stay. She gazed at Kitty with affection, and beyond affection, fatigue.

Darcy's hand tightened once around his fork and released.

"Was there other news from Longbourn?" Georgiana asked. She put the question with care, as if she knew letters from home might contain either happiness or disturbance and wished to be ready for both.

Miss Elizabeth's gaze dropped to her glass. For one second the whole table seemed to wait without knowing that it waited.

"Yes," she said. "Very happy news."

Mrs. Gardiner's expression softened at once. She knew, then. Darcy looked from her to Miss Elizabeth and felt the first clean line of understanding draw itself through him.

Bingley.

"Jane is to be married," Miss Elizabeth said.

Kitty's cry escaped before she could make it ladylike. "Married!"

Georgiana's face opened into surprised delight. Mrs. Annesley smiled. Mrs. Gardiner, who must have already spent her first joy upstairs, received the news again for the sake of the table, warm and composed.

"To Mr. Bingley?" Kitty demanded.

"Yes. He has returned to Netherfield Park."

He had told Bingley.

The thought arrived with the name. He had told him before Bingley left Pemberley — badly perhaps, too late certainly, but plainly. That he had separated him from Miss Bennet on insufficient evidence. That he had been wrong. He had given no instruction to return. He had told the truth and left Bingley to be himself.

Bingley, being at last in possession of the truth, had gone south. And now Jane Bennet was engaged.

Miss Elizabeth had given Jane's happiness its proper dignity, and it had cost her. On the other side of the table, Kitty was half out of her chair in delight.

"Jane engaged! Oh, Lizzy, and I am not there!"

"That is Jane's only complaint," Miss Elizabeth said. "She wishes you were with her."

"And you."

Miss Elizabeth's hand found her glass. She did not drink. "And me."

Darcy could not speak. To congratulate her at once would be proper. To delay would be marked. To say what rose in him — that Miss Bennet deserved every happiness, that Bingley had been a fool, that he himself had been a worse one — would be impossible.

Mrs. Gardiner saved the table. "Your sister writes happily, Lizzy?"

"Very happily. Bewildered, I think, but happy."

"Jane bewildered," Kitty said, "must look almost exactly like Jane composed."

That drew Miss Elizabeth's smile again, and this time it reached her eyes, though not for him. "Very nearly."

Georgiana asked whether Miss Bennet had known Mr. Bingley long. Kitty answered with the authority of a younger sister who had suffered through every history in the county: Mr. Bingley had taken Netherfield last autumn, had given a ball, had danced with Jane more than anyone, had gone away very cruelly — here she stopped, remembering the table — and had now come back sensibly.

"Very sensibly," Mrs. Gardiner said.

Darcy lifted his glass and drank.

He had thought himself prepared for this. He had imagined, in those hours after Bingley's departure, every possible consequence of his confession — except the one in which Miss Elizabeth sat beside him and heard the name of his friend with sorrow under her joy.

He had repaired what he could. But repair, it appeared, did not return a thing to the state before injury. It displayed the break more clearly.

He set down his empty glass.

"But was not Mr. Bingley just here?" Kitty asked.

There it was.

Georgiana glanced toward her brother, only mildly curious. Mrs. Annesley looked to Mrs. Gardiner, as if the geography of gentlemen's movements were not a subject on which she presumed to judge. Mrs. Gardiner became very still.

Miss Elizabeth did not look at Darcy.

That was answer enough.

"Yes," Georgiana said. "He was here not a fortnight since."

"Then he must have gone straight to Netherfield," Kitty said. "How romantic. Unless he stopped somewhere. Did he stop somewhere?"

No one answered. The question had been tossed into the table like a ribbon, bright and harmless. Darcy saw the knot beneath it.

He could say it.

He could say, *I told him.*

Three words. They would account for Bingley's return. They would tell Miss Elizabeth that he had not left Jane's happiness to accident or convenience or the slow correction of time. They would say he had known himself wrong and acted, at last, before being forced.

They would also ask her to see him acting.

The old impulse rose with horrifying ease: to defend, to explain, to set out the facts in order and trust that once arranged they must acquit him. He had done it once in a letter that shook with pride even where it told the truth. He would not do it at his own table, over Jane Bennet's happiness, with Elizabeth Bennet unable to escape him.

"Bingley," he said, and stopped.

Miss Elizabeth's hands went still beside her plate.

He turned to her because he could not help it. He had not meant to meet her eyes. He had not meant to make of the crowded table a place with only two people in it. But there she was, near enough to touch, pale with self-command, eyes bright and guarded, understanding too much and not enough.

"Bingley acted freely," he said.

Silence followed.

It was an absurd sentence. He knew it as soon as it was spoken. Bingley was of age, of fortune, of cheerful temper, and accountable to no one at the table. Of course he had acted freely. Kitty looked puzzled. Georgiana blinked. Mrs. Annesley, mercifully, reached for her glass. Mrs. Gardiner glanced at Miss Elizabeth and then away.

Miss Elizabeth understood.

That was worse.

Her face changed by almost nothing. Darcy would not have seen it six months ago. He saw it now: the slight stilling about her mouth, the way her fingers rested flat against the tablecloth instead of folding, the withdrawal not of attention but of safety. She had heard the confession inside the evasion. She had heard, perhaps, that he wanted her to hear it.

He had claimed without claiming. Even his restraint had betrayed him.

Kitty recovered first. "Well, I am glad he acted freely if it made Jane happy."

"So are we all," said Mrs. Gardiner, with gentle emphasis.

"Indeed," Georgiana said. "I am very glad for Miss Bennet."

"Thank you," Miss Elizabeth answered. She turned to Georgiana with real warmth, and Darcy watched what he had not earned: the softness she could still give his sister. "Jane will be pleased by your kindness."

"I hope she will be very happy," Georgiana said.

"She will," Kitty declared. "Jane is always happy when people are good, and Mr. Bingley is very good when his sisters do not carry him off."

Mrs. Gardiner coughed once into her napkin.

Darcy deserved that.

Miss Elizabeth's color rose. "Kitty."

"What? He is. He was very good before he went away, except for going away."

"It is sometimes possible," Mrs. Gardiner said, "to be good and foolish in the same season."

Kitty considered this. "Then perhaps he has changed seasons."

The table accepted the phrase with more gratitude than it deserved because it let in laughter. Darcy gave what smile he could. Georgiana smiled in earnest. Miss Elizabeth's lips moved, but the effort was too visible to him.

Dinner continued. Conversation turned, by Mrs. Gardiner's careful hand, to roads and the likelihood that Mrs. Bennet would already have informed every acquaintance within walking distance. Kitty supplied three possible speeches their mother might have made to Mrs. Long. Georgiana laughed at the second and looked guilty for laughing at the third. Mrs. Annesley contributed that happy news traveled fastest where it was least required to travel at all.

Darcy performed the part assigned to him. He spoke when needed. He asked Mrs. Gardiner whether she had found the road from Lambton dusty that afternoon. He answered Georgiana when she asked whether the south lawn might be dry enough tomorrow for walking. He did not look at Miss Elizabeth unless politeness demanded it, and politeness, which had once seemed a comforting structure, became a series of traps.

When the ladies withdrew, he rose with the rest. When Miss Elizabeth turned away from him, preceding him to the door, the movement stirred the faint scent of lavender and summer air from her gown. He had no right to notice it. He noticed it and hated himself for wanting the smallest sign that she did not hate his noticing.

None came.

In the drawing room, candles stood reflected in the dark windows. Mrs. Gardiner took her usual chair. Mrs. Annesley settled with her work. Georgiana and Kitty, released from dinner's formality but not from its news, drew together near the pianoforte and whispered with an animation that had Jane's happiness, Longbourn, and wedding clothes all tangled in it. The promised duet would have to wait.

Miss Elizabeth sat beside her aunt.

Not far from him. Not near.

Darcy stood by the mantel for longer than was necessary and learned nothing from the fire. He could not remain there like a man awaiting judgment. He took a chair. Then he did what he had forbidden himself all evening and let himself look at her.

She was tired.

The brightness in her eyes was not vivacity. Her smile, when Kitty exclaimed over some imagined bridal ribbon, arrived a moment late. Once, when Georgiana asked whether Miss Bennet preferred blue or white, Miss Elizabeth answered sensibly and then looked toward the window as if the dark glass had spoken her name.

He had thought patience required silence. He had not understood that silence could resemble abandonment.

He could tell her. Not here; not like this. But soon. A few words only. Not as a claim. Not as a plea. Simply the truth. She need not thank him. She need not forgive him. She need only be released from whatever question had drawn that strained quiet over her face.

He knew, even as he formed the argument, that it was arranged for his own comfort. It assumed the question was one he could answer.

Georgiana rose and went to the window.

"The sky is quite clear," she said, with mild surprise.

Kitty sprang up at once. "Is it? Oh, let me see. At Longbourn one must go almost into the shrubbery to see anything properly, and then Mama says we shall catch cold or be murdered by damp."

"By damp?" Georgiana asked.

"It is one of the less romantic dangers," Miss Elizabeth said.

There. A little of herself. Thin, but real.

Darcy stood before he had decided to move. The room had become too close. The candles, the reflected faces in the glass, the cheerful voices pressing upon what could not be spoken — all of it seemed suddenly unfit for the truth and equally unfit for concealment.

"The evening is mild," he said. "If Mrs. Gardiner approves, we might take a short turn on the lawn. The stars are often clearer away from the windows."

Kitty looked as if he had offered a private ball.

"May we?" she asked Mrs. Gardiner. "Only for a little while? I have not seen Derbyshire stars."

"They are the same stars, Kitty," Miss Elizabeth said.

"Yes, but from a better county."

Georgiana laughed outright.

Mrs. Gardiner looked from Kitty's eagerness to Georgiana's hopeful face, then to Darcy. There was inquiry in the glance, and caution, and something like permission withheld until he understood its terms. He inclined his head, slightly. A group walk. The lawn. The house in sight. Nothing more.

"For a few minutes," Mrs. Gardiner said. "If shawls are fetched."

Kitty clapped her hands once and then remembered herself. "Lizzy, you will come."

It was not a question.

Miss Elizabeth went still.

For a moment Darcy thought she would refuse. He saw the refusal gather: not in discourtesy, but in weariness. She had endured the fair, the letter, the table, his blunder. He had asked for one more act of composure and called it air.

Then her gaze moved to Georgiana, who had turned from the window with all her hope carefully held. It moved to Kitty, who had already placed Jane's happiness among the constellations and required her sister to witness the arrangement. Last, and only because he was standing in the path of it, her gaze came to him.

"Yes," she said.

The word was perfectly civil. Empty.

Darcy bowed because there was nothing else he could safely do.

He had asked for air.

He had not known he was asking for hope.

# Chapter 29: Under the Stars

"Yes," Elizabeth said.

It was a small word, and therefore more easily managed than any larger one. It committed her to nothing beyond a few minutes upon the lawn. It did not say she wished for air. It did not say she wished for company. It did not say she wished for anything at all.

Kitty, however, received it as a promise of general happiness. "Oh, excellent! Georgiana, you must show me where the stars are best seen. I am certain Pemberley has a particular place for it."

"I do not know that we have a particular place," Georgiana said, but with such pleasure at being consulted that the uncertainty became almost eagerness. "The south lawn is open."

"Then we shall make it particular by going there.”

Mrs. Gardiner rose with a look that took in Kitty's delight, Georgiana's quiet animation, Mr. Darcy's stillness, and Elizabeth's face. Elizabeth did not know what her aunt saw there; she only knew that Mrs. Gardiner's expression softened, and that softness was almost worse than inquiry.

Elizabeth did not look at Mr. Darcy. She had been not looking at him all evening, and the effort of it had grown no easier with practice.

"Shawls, then," said Mrs. Gardiner. "If we are to admire the heavens, we must not do so at the expense of our throats."

Mrs. Annesley raised a hand, as if to excuse herself from any further exertion. "You must admire them for me. I have had all the exercise my spirits can support today.”

"Your relations have worn you quite out," Kitty said with sympathy, then colored as if she had perhaps spoken too plainly.

Mrs. Annesley’s smile deepened. "My relations have the advantage of being numerous.”

"Then we shall admire the stars for you,” Kitty said, recovering. "Though I am not always perfect with their names.”

"That is because the stars have very poor names,” Elizabeth said, because something was required of her, and wit was easier than truth. "They would be remembered more often if anyone had thought to call them after people one knew.”

The shawls arrived, and the party went out by the terrace doors. The night received them coolly but not coldly. The house behind them showed here and there a square of warm gold; before them the lawn fell away into a gentler darkness, and above it the sky opened, clear and deep, with the stars set high beyond all management.

Elizabeth drew one breath and then another. The sight checked her. There was a greatness in the sky which no drawing room could answer; the stars seemed at once remote and piercingly near, and for a moment all the anxieties of rooms and voices were held at a distance.

But only at a distance. The air was easier than the room had been; it made fewer demands. It did not require her to answer congratulations, or accept kindness, or sit near a man who said very little and meant too much by it.

Kitty stopped almost at once. "Oh."

Georgiana stopped beside her. "Are they different?"

"They are higher," Kitty said. "Or brighter? Or perhaps I am only looking up properly. At Longbourn there are trees and chimneys and Mama saying one must not stand in the damp."

"They are the same stars, Kitty," Elizabeth said.

"Yes, Lizzy, but from a better county."

Georgiana turned her head. "A better county?"

Kitty didn’t take her eyes off the stars. "I did not mean Hertfordshire is a bad county. Only that Derbyshire is a better one for seeing things. Mountains and lawns and all that. Hertfordshire is very good for officers."

"That is not a recommendation," Elizabeth said.

"No," Kitty agreed, with feeling. Then, remembering herself, "At least, not always."

Georgiana looked from Kitty to Elizabeth with the careful expression of one who wished to understand the joke without being obliged to ask for its history. "I do not think counties can alter the stars."

Kitty's chin lifted a very little. "I did not say alter."

"No. Of course not. I only meant—"

"I know very well what I meant," Kitty said, and then looked as if she did not know it half so well as she would have liked.

The silence that followed was tiny, but complete. It might have passed unnoticed at a larger party; here, under the open sky and with every feeling already sharpened, it seemed to Elizabeth as visible as the moon.

Georgiana drew her shawl closer about her, as if she had been the one corrected.

Mr. Darcy moved. It was not much — half a step, perhaps, toward his sister. Then he checked himself and was still. But the half-step had carried him nearer to Elizabeth, and she felt the closeness of it like a change in temperature.

Georgiana seemed to feel his movement without looking at him. She straightened a little, her hands still holding the shawl close. "I beg your pardon. I did not mean to correct you.”

Kitty’s chin dropped. "Oh! You did not. Or if you did, I dare say I required it. Lydia says I am always saying things wrong, though she says so when she is saying things worse."

"Then perhaps," Georgiana said, very softly, "Hertfordshire has the same stars under worse management."

Kitty stared at her.

"I only meant—" Georgiana began, but the words trailed away.

"Yes," Kitty said, in delight. "That is exactly what I meant."

The thing was mended almost before it had torn. A mistake, a sting, a blush, an apology; then laughter standing close behind it, admitted without trial. Kitty moved nearer to Georgiana and began explaining, with more charity than accuracy, the various defects of Longbourn's shrubbery as an observatory. Georgiana listened as if no observatory had ever been more deserving of study.

Elizabeth watched them and felt something within her twist.

It was not envy. Or if it was, it was envy of the simplest sort: that two girls might misunderstand each other, be sorry, and go on. That affection might be so lightly bruised and so easily restored. That no letter need be written, no pride defeated, no history revised.

Mrs. Gardiner had drawn Kitty and Georgiana a few steps ahead, pointing out how the Plough led the eye toward the Pole Star. Their voices carried back softly — instruction, question, laughter — and left behind them a silence that belonged only to Elizabeth and the man beside her.

"They are very happy together," Mr. Darcy said. He had not moved again since the half-step, but the night air carried warmth from his coat — clean linen and something that was only him — and his voice reached her with the intimacy of something said in a smaller place.

"Yes," she said. "They are."

"I am glad of it."

That might have been all. It ought to have been all. The words were harmless, and true, and entirely suitable to the situation. But he said them with such care that Elizabeth heard not only gladness in them, but cost. He had once feared Kitty. He had feared Longbourn, Lydia, noise, influence, disorder — everything that might touch his sister and alter her. Now Kitty stood beneath his stars and made Georgiana laugh.

Elizabeth did not know whether she wished to forgive him for that too.

"Miss Bennet," he said, "is the air too cool? We need not remain if you are fatigued."

There it was again: care made perfectly correct, kindness offered in such a manner that it could not be refused without seeming petulant nor accepted without seeming weak.

Elizabeth said nothing.

Kitty and Georgiana moved a few paces farther, still arguing amiably over whether a star could be said to belong to a county if every county saw it. 

Elizabeth glanced back at the house. The windows still showed their squares of warm gold. The drawing room had been too bright for truth; the lawn, it seemed, the sky might be dark enough for every thought she had forbidden herself to have.

"No," she said. "The air is not too cool."

"Then perhaps the walk was ill-timed."

"It was very kindly meant."

Something changed in his voice at that. Elizabeth heard it, and resented hearing.

"I did not mean to impose upon you," he said.

"No. You are very careful not to impose."

The sentence came out more sharply than she had intended. Or perhaps exactly as sharply as some ungoverned part of her had intended and the rest of her had failed to prevent.

Mr. Darcy accepted it. He did not defend himself. He only stood with that grave attention which had, at dinner, made every silence feel inhabited.

Elizabeth ought to have stopped. She knew it. 

Instead she said, "Did you speak to him?"

Mr. Darcy did not pretend not to understand.

"Yes."

One word. The same word she had given Kitty, and just as little protection against what followed.

Elizabeth's hand tightened in the fringe of her shawl. "Before he returned to Netherfield?"

"Yes."

The lawn seemed to grow very still. From some distance came Kitty's voice — "No, but if Hertfordshire sees it too, then it is a shared star, which is very different" — and Georgiana's soft, uncertain laugh.

"You told him," she said.

"I told him what I should have told him long before."

Her breath caught, and she was angry with it for doing so.

Mr. Darcy looked toward the girls, then back to her. "But that is all I did. Bingley acted for himself."

She knew it was true by the care with which he said it, and by the very refusal of triumph in his voice. He would not take Jane's happiness into his hands. He would not make a claim upon it. He would not even let Elizabeth thank him, if gratitude had been possible.

It was noble. It was insufferable.

"How very convenient," she said.

She had already said too much. She said more. "You were willing enough to tell me what you had done when it condemned you."

The breath he let out was careful, a slow, controlled thing, as if steadying himself.

Elizabeth wished, with a violence that startled her, that he would be angry. If he were angry, she might meet him with anger and know what to do with herself. If he defended himself, she might retort. If he was proud, she might despise him.

He was none of these things.

"I did not mean to conceal it."

"No?" The laugh that escaped her was not pleasant. "Then you have achieved concealment by accident, which must be a relief to a gentleman so seldom careless."

He was quiet for a moment. When he spoke, it was not with pain, though pain was there. It was with understanding, and that was unendurable.

"I deserved that," he said.

"No," Elizabeth said at once, because he had not. Or because he had. Or because his readiness to accept it deprived her of the poor satisfaction of having been unjust. "No, you did not. That is—"

She stopped. She could not apologize without breaking; she could not continue without cruelty.

Mr. Darcy did not help her. Perhaps he could not. Perhaps he had learned, too late and too well, not to seize upon any opening she had not meant to give.

The shadow had made truth possible. It had not made truth kind.

"Lizzy?" Kitty called.

Elizabeth turned too quickly. Her shawl slipped from her shoulders. Mr. Darcy caught it before it touched the grass. He held it out without a word, not toward her shoulders, only toward her hand.

She took it. Their fingers met — bare, brief. Elizabeth pulled the shawl against herself as if she had been burned.

Kitty stood beside Georgiana, both of them looking back. The easy line between them had gone uncertain again. What had they seen?

Mrs. Gardiner came forward.

"I believe," she said, in the calm tone that could restore a room, a table, or an entire family to order, "that the stars will remain where they are until tomorrow. The air is growing cool, and Mrs. Annesley will begin to think we have gone in search of another county."

Kitty accepted the rescue first. "Only if it has better stars."

"That would be difficult to determine tonight," Mrs. Gardiner said. "Come."

They turned back toward the house.

For a few steps, no one spoke. The gravel sounded too clearly underfoot. Kitty and Georgiana walked behind them now. Mrs. Gardiner kept near Elizabeth without touching her. Mr. Darcy followed at a proper distance, and that propriety, too, accused them both.

Elizabeth could feel the heat in her face though the night had cooled. She had asked the question. She had received the answer. She had wanted him to have done something; she had wanted him not to have done it for credit; she had wanted him to tell her without making her grateful; she had wanted, impossibly, not to have to want anything from him at all.

At the terrace, Kitty and Georgiana caught up to them. "Georgiana, I did not mean Derbyshire owns the stars,” Kitty said. "Only that it manages them badly."

In the light from the windowed door, Georgiana's smile returned, small but real. "Perhaps only in the shrubbery."

Kitty laughed, relieved.

Elizabeth could not bear it.

"I am very tired," she said, and the words were addressed to no one in particular because if she looked at her aunt she would be understood, and if she looked at Mr. Darcy she would be undone. "Pray excuse me."

"Lizzy—" Kitty began.

"I shall only go in."

She did not wait for more. She went through the bright door ahead of them all, into the candles and warmth and ordinary safety of Pemberley, and carried the night in with her.

# Chapter 30: News from London

The heat had declared itself before breakfast was cleared.

It shimmered upon the lawns, and on Pemberley's stone. Even inside, with the curtains half-drawn, the air was close. Outside, the trees promised shade at a distance and required a walk to earn it.

Elizabeth had come down prepared for Mr. Darcy. She had meant to lie awake and think; sleep, indifferent to her plans, had taken her at once. She had woken with nothing resolved, and had dressed with care, which she told herself was due to the house. 

She had chosen a ribbon with care, which she told herself was due to the heat. She had rehearsed no speech, which was true, because speech would have implied intention; but she had carried downstairs a readiness so complete that it might have passed, in a less honest court, for innocence.

Mr. Darcy was not at breakfast.

Georgiana supplied the explanation. "My brother was called out early to speak with Mr. Hardwick. He manages the south farms, I believe."

"Estate business," said Mrs. Annesley, giving the phrase its proper weight and thereby closing any road to inquiry.

"How early must gentlemen begin estate business?” Kitty asked. “Earlier than breakfast seems unreasonable. Unless the farms are very far away."

"They are not so far," Georgiana said. "But Mr. Hardwick likes the morning."

"Then Mr. Hardwick has a character fit for improvement," Kitty said.

Georgiana laughed, and Kitty, encouraged by success, began at once upon the disadvantages of mornings at Longbourn, where Mama required news before there was any. Mrs. Annesley smiled over her cup. Mrs. Gardiner listened, added two words where two words were useful, and let the girls discover that yesterday's little fracture had mended without ceremony.

Elizabeth buttered a piece of toast she did not want.

Estate business. Of course. Nothing could be more proper. Nothing could be more Mr. Darcy. If he had been proud, she might have been cool. If he had been wounded, she might have been sorrier than she wished. But he had gone to the south farms.

It was so perfectly done that she could have struck him for it, had he been present to be struck.

The thought restored her a little. It was difficult to be entirely overcome while imagining Mr. Darcy brought to justice by a slice of toast.

Across the table, Mrs. Gardiner's gaze landed on her. It did not remain. That was worse than inquiry. Mrs. Gardiner never stared when observation would do.

When breakfast ended, Kitty and Georgiana went to the music room to look for suitable duets, and Mrs. Annesley followed.

Elizabeth was preparing to follow, for no better reason than that motion was preferable to being left behind, when Mrs. Gardiner laid a hand upon her arm.

"Will you walk with me, Lizzy?"

Mrs. Gardiner's voice was mild. The question could have meant nothing more than a wish for air before the day grew hotter, though the day had already done all it could in that direction. But her hand remained for one extra second, and Elizabeth understood that air was not the object.

"With pleasure," she said too brightly. "If pleasure may be had out of doors today."

"A little shade may be found, I think."

"Then we shall pursue it with all the ardor usually reserved for accomplishments and rich husbands."

Mrs. Gardiner only smiled. "Come."

They went out by a side door, where the borders were arranged with that excellent Pemberley taste which made art appear modest and effort appear natural. The house stood behind them in full morning authority. Before them, the garden fell away in ordered beds and clipped edges, then loosened by degrees toward the wider park.

It was intolerably handsome. It did not look like a place from which one ought to run. It looked like stone, water, shade, order, and consequence; and if people were foolish enough to lose their peace among such things, the fault could not be laid upon the architecture.

Mrs. Gardiner opened her parasol. Elizabeth had refused hers, not two minutes before, from some principle she could not now identify. Pride, likely. Pride had managed worse investments.

They walked in silence until the first turn brought them under the partial shade of a lime walk.

"Your uncle's letter came yesterday with Jane's," Mrs. Gardiner said. "I did not speak of it last night. There was enough in one letter to occupy us all."

Elizabeth’s step shortened. "Is my uncle well?"

"Quite well. He writes in excellent spirits, and with his usual indignation against roads, inns, and any horse that has not learned to consult his convenience. There is no alarm."

They passed a bed of late roses. Their scent came up heavy in the heat, less sweet than insistent.

"The matter that called your uncle home has not resolved as quickly as he hoped. I need not leave this hour, nor tomorrow morning, but I must return within a few days."

Elizabeth knew that visits ended. Gracechurch Street had a household that wanted its mistress. Mr. Gardiner had partners, ledgers, warehouses, ships, men who wrote letters and expected answers. Pemberley was not an island. It had only felt like one.

She made herself say, "Of course. My uncle must not be inconvenienced."

"No."

"And you have already been away longer than you first intended."

"A little."

"That is the danger of Derbyshire. It seduces the traveler into delays and then pretends the mountains are to blame."

"Derbyshire has a great deal to answer for."

The reply was kind. It was also exact. Elizabeth heard both and disliked the second for making the first safer.

"I suppose," she said, too quickly, "the real question is Kitty."

Mrs. Gardiner did not answer at once.

"Whether she may remain, I mean. If you must return, and if my father has permitted her visit, and if Georgiana still wishes for her company. I should not like Kitty to be hurried away when she is doing so well. It would grieve her. And Georgiana too, I think."

"I think it would grieve them both."

"And the household is so very proper that impropriety would perish of embarrassment before it reached the staircase."

"That may be among Pemberley's lesser advertised virtues."

Elizabeth tried to laugh and failed to like the sound. "Then Kitty's remaining is possible."

"Likely, I should say. Not settled until Mr. Darcy has been spoken to, and not to be treated as nothing, but likely."

"Mr. Darcy has been all consideration," she said.

It was too stiff. Mrs. Gardiner adjusted her parasol. "He has," she said.

They walked on. The shade broke. The path opened into sun, and the light gave no mercy to Elizabeth’s unshaded eyes. Every stone in the path declared itself. Every border, every turn, every choice of direction stood visible.

"Kitty is the simpler question," Mrs. Gardiner said.

Elizabeth stopped.

Mrs. Gardiner stopped too, but not at once. She took two more steps, then turned back, giving Elizabeth those two steps in which to decide whether she would pretend not to understand.

Elizabeth had no strength for pretense. Not a graceful one.

"Because she is younger."

"In part."

"And because she is useful to Georgiana."

"Yes."

"And because no one at Lambton has troubled themselves to imagine Kitty mistress of Pemberley by Michaelmas."

The words came out before Elizabeth had decided to permit them. They were too sharp. They were also true enough to be vulgar.
