Mrs. Gardiner did not reprove her. "That is one reason."

Elizabeth looked away toward the house. From this angle, Pemberley showed a row of high windows and a sweep of pale wall, all sun and composure. 

"I beg your pardon," Elizabeth said. "That was ill said."

"It was plainly said."

"Plainness is not always a virtue."

"No. But it is sometimes useful."

Elizabeth resumed walking because standing still had begun to feel like confession. 

"One must sometimes consider what reasonable people will see," her aunt continued, "especially after unreasonable people have helped point them in the proper direction."

Elizabeth found no answer. Lambton returned to her in pieces: women with ribbons and baskets, smiles too warm to be innocent, a place made for her before she had chosen to stand in it. Last night she had asked a question beneath the stars and punished the answer she had wanted. This morning Mr. Darcy had removed himself, and she had minded it.

"Do you wish to stay?" Mrs. Gardiner asked.

No one could accuse her aunt of cruelty. She spoke the question as she might have offered a chair to a tired guest. Yet Elizabeth felt its full pressure.

"There is Kitty—"

"Lizzy."

Mrs. Gardiner had not raised her voice. Elizabeth shut her mouth. 

She looked down at the gravel. The stones were pale and hot and numerous. A person might count them for the rest of the morning and learn nothing.

"I do not know."

It was not true enough.

Mrs. Gardiner waited.

Elizabeth pressed one gloved hand over the other. The kid was damp at the palm. "I do not know what I know. That is nearer the truth, though not much more useful."

"It may be useful enough to begin with."

"No, it is absurd. I have prided myself all my life on knowing my own mind, and now my own mind behaves like Lydia with a new bonnet — declaring one thing because it is pretty, another because it is forbidden, and a third because someone else has admired it first."

Mrs. Gardiner's mouth moved. She did not smile.

Elizabeth was grateful for that restraint and resented needing it.

"I thought," she said, and stopped.

What had she thought? That Mr. Darcy would remain proud enough to dislike? That Jane's happiness would settle the matter neatly on one side or the other? 

She had arranged him, in every private supposition, so that she might keep the advantage. He had disobliged her by becoming difficult to arrange at all.

"I thought I had more time," she said. "To know whether I am judging him, or only being led by every circumstance that makes him appear—"

She stopped.

"Appear?" Mrs. Gardiner said.

Elizabeth lifted her eyes. "Good."

Mrs. Gardiner tilted her parasol a little lower, bringing its shade over Elizabeth's shoulder. The gesture was small. It undid her more than speech.

"I cannot leave yet," Elizabeth said. "Not yet. I ought to be able to. It would be simpler. It would be safer. But I cannot turn this visit into — into—"

"Into a decision?"

That was worse than any word she had been avoiding, because it was not romantic. It was practical. It had edges.

"Yes."

Mrs. Gardiner put her hand on Elizabeth's arm. "My dear Lizzy," she said.

That was nearly fatal. Had her aunt continued — had she offered consolation, advice, hope, warning, any of the useful things older and wiser women have at command — Elizabeth might have met it. The unfinished tenderness was harder. It permitted tears without demanding them.

Elizabeth did not cry properly. She would have denied it if accused. But her eyes filled, and for half a minute the hot gravel blurred into a pale seam at her feet.

Mrs. Gardiner allowed the half minute. No more.

"Time is not indefinite," she said.

Mrs. Gardiner's face remained composed, but there was a firmness in it which belonged neither to Gracechurch Street nor to Pemberley, but to character. Elizabeth had seen it before in small domestic forms: in the management of tired children, in a quiet refusal to let Mrs. Bennet govern a visit by nerves, in the decision that a carriage would stop before a driver admitted fatigue. It was kindness with a spine.

"You must be fair to yourself," Mrs. Gardiner said. "If you wish to stay because you require time to understand your own mind, say so honestly, at least to yourself. Do not dress it entirely in Kitty's frock."

Elizabeth laughed, wet-eyed, despite herself.

"And you must be fair to him."

The laugh died at once.

"Mr. Darcy has not pressed you. He has, so far as I can judge, taken some pains not to do so. That restraint does him credit. It also cannot be expected to bear every burden forever."

"No."

"If you remain, it will not be the same visit it was yesterday."

"To him?"

"To him. To you. To any person with eyes enough to see what Lambton saw before either of you wished it seen." 

"I do not say this to frighten you with gossip," Mrs. Gardiner continued. "People talk where there is sense and where there is none. We cannot live by their tongues. But neither can we pretend a thing has no shape because we dislike the name others give it."

The music room windows were not visible from where they stood, but she imagined them: Kitty at the instrument, Georgiana beside her, Mrs. Annesley near enough to guide, far enough to let friendship happen. A proper arrangement. A good one.

Elizabeth had wanted to shelter behind it. She saw that now.

"What will you tell Mr. Darcy?" she asked.

"That my husband's business requires my return within a few days, and that Kitty's visit must be settled plainly."

"And of me?"

Mrs. Gardiner's hand left her arm. "That is for you to decide before I must say anything that cannot be unsaid."

The answer was merciful and merciless.

"I hate practical matters," Elizabeth said.

"No, you do not. You hate them when they tell the truth sooner than feeling is ready."

"That is an excellent reason to hate them."

"It is. But not a useful one."

For the first time that morning, Elizabeth's laugh sounded like herself.

They began walking again. The shade had shifted while they stood. The path ahead ran in full sun for several yards before turning beneath another line of trees. It looked short. It looked exposed. It looked unavoidable.

Elizabeth stepped onto the hot gravel.

The heat came up through the soles of her shoes. Behind her stood the house she could not honestly leave. Before her lay a turn she could not see beyond. Beside her walked the aunt who would not choose for her.

She had a few days.

She had less time than she wanted.

She had enough time to make delay impossible.

# Chapter 31: The Second Letter

The estate office was blessedly cool. Darcy had ridden back from the south farms later than he intended, with Mr. Hardwick’s figures in his pocket and the dust of three lanes upon his boots. The difficulty at Stony Close had proved, after two hours’ examination, to be neither the tenant’s negligence nor the steward’s oversight, but a drainage fault so plain once found that every man present had looked at the ditch with an expression of personal injury. 

Darcy had known what to do with it. He had stood with Hardwick over the plan, marked the field boundary, named the mason, fixed the order of the work, and left behind him a tenant relieved, a steward instructed, and a problem reduced to dates and labor.

That was the mercy of land. It did not cease to be difficult, but it consented, eventually, to particulars.

The small paneled room was dim after the fields, the shutters drawn against the blazing afternoon heat. On the desk, the silver weight held the day’s letters in place; beside them lay a folded map, two ledgers, a sealed packet from his London man of business, and a note from the housekeeper concerning linen for the west rooms. The pen-knife stood where it always stood, next to the blotter. Someone — Mrs. Reynolds, or a maid acting on her orders — had left a glass of barley water on the side table, already beading in the warmth.

Rents, repairs, servants, walls, horses, weather, names. A whole life reduced to paper, and therefore capable of being touched.

Mrs. Reynolds appeared at the door before he could sit.

"Mrs. Gardiner has asked whether you might spare her a few minutes, sir. She said it would not take long, but she did ask whether you had come in yet."

Darcy laid his gloves on the desk. He was still in his riding coat, his boots dusty, his neckcloth loosened from the heat. The drawing room was out of the question.

"Ask her to come here, if she would not mind the informality."

"Very good, sir."

He drank off the barley water while he waited. It was tepid and faintly sweet and did nothing for the unease that had settled beneath his ribs at the word *asked*. Nothing in Mrs. Gardiner’s manner had ever suggested fuss.

She came in a few minutes later, shown to the door by a housemaid who curtseyed and withdrew. Mrs. Gardiner took in the room — the shuttered dimness, the papers, the master of the house in his riding dust — and did not remark upon any of it.

That, more than the request, told him the matter was not casual.

"Mrs. Gardiner. I beg your pardon for receiving you here. I am not fit for the better rooms."

"Not at all," she said. "I am grateful for your time."

The courtesy was warm. It did not comfort him.

"Will you sit?"

"If you please."

He set a chair for her. She accepted it without ceremony. Darcy sat in his father's chair. Every instinct in him preferred standing — but a host sat when his guest sat.

"I have had a letter from my husband,” Mrs. Gardiner said.

Darcy’s attention sharpened.

"He is well,” she added at once. "There is no alarm on that score. But the business which called him back to town has not resolved as he hoped. I must return within a few days.”

A few days.

The phrase did not alter the office. The map remained half-unfolded. The inkstand stood where it had stood. A fly bumped with dull persistence against the upper pane of the window. Somewhere beyond the stable yard one man called to another, and received an answer. The room did not mark it.

Darcy inclined his head. "I am sorry your stay must end so soon.”

"So am I.”

She said it simply. That made it worse. Had she complained, he could have answered her. Regret gave him nothing to meet.

"I hope Mr. Gardiner has not been inconvenienced by our keeping you so long.”

"My husband,” Mrs. Gardiner said, and there was a warmth in it that did not soften the point, "has survived worse inconveniences than being deprived of my company in Derbyshire.”

Darcy almost smiled. Almost.

"But I must go,” she continued. "And before I do, Kitty’s visit must be settled plainly.”

Plainly.

"Of course,” he said. "Miss Catherine is very welcome to remain, if Mr. Bennet approves and if Mrs. Annesley is willing to continue in her charge. My sister would be happy in it.”

"I believe she would.”

"I shall write to Mr. Bennet at once.”

"Thank you. That will be necessary.”

Necessary. Not sufficient.

Darcy looked down at the map before him. Stony Close, East Meadow, the boundary coppice, the low field where the water gathered. Lines, names, answers. He had spent the morning with men who said what they meant because ditch-water permitted no delicacy.

Mrs. Gardiner was quiet for a moment. "Mr. Darcy, I am sensible of your kindness to my nieces."

He did not move. Praise, from Mrs. Gardiner, was never ornament. It usually preceded a truth.

"I hope,” he said carefully, "that kindness has not made anything difficult.”

"That is what I wish to prevent.”

The fly struck the pane again.

"Elizabeth,” Mrs. Gardiner said, "is under your roof. She is grateful for your sister’s friendship, for your hospitality, and for every proper consideration she has received here.”

Every proper consideration. He heard the mercy in the adjective. He also heard its warning. Something in his chest shifted, as if a weight had been set down on the wrong side of a balance.

"I would not,” he said, "have her feel pressed by any obligation to Pemberley.”

"No. I believe that.”

Her belief should have relieved him. Instead it placed responsibility in his hands more exactly than suspicion could have done.

"Yet gratitude,” Mrs. Gardiner said, "may become a pressure even where no one intends it. So may kindness.” She paused. "So may silence.”

Darcy's hand closed once upon the arm of his chair.

"I do not speak for Elizabeth,” she said. "She must speak for herself, or not speak, as she chooses. But my return changes the shape of matters. I thought it right that you should know it before dinner, rather than after.”

He inclined his head. Alarm touched him, swift and plain, before composure closed over it.

Mrs. Gardiner rose. He rose with her.

"She is not to be hurried,” Darcy said.

"No.”

"Nor left uncertain.”

Mrs. Gardiner’s expression altered by the smallest degree. Not surprise, but, perhaps, the recognition that he had reached the same boundary by his own road.

"No,” she said. "Nor that.”

"Thank you,” he said.

The words were inadequate. They were also all he had.

He bowed. She returned it, with all proper composure, and left him in the estate office among his papers.

---

Dinner did not fail. That was among the cruelties of a well-ordered house.

The soup was clear, the fish excellent, the removes faultless. Servants entered and withdrew; glasses were filled. Mrs. Annesley asked Georgiana some quiet question about a new duet; Kitty supplied the answer before Georgiana could, and Georgiana let her, smiling, because that was how they were now. Mrs. Gardiner spoke of the heat with perfect ease. At his left hand, Elizabeth tasted her soup, smiled at her sister, and did not look at him. The candlelight caught her cheek, leaving her eyes uncertain.

Darcy answered when he was spoken to.

He did not look at Elizabeth too long. He did not avoid looking at her so completely that avoidance became its own confession. He passed a dish to Mrs. Gardiner, asked Mrs. Annesley whether the music room had been too warm that afternoon, and responded to Kitty’s opinion of Mr. Hardwick’s morning habits with enough gravity to make Georgiana shake her head and smile.

Every duty was discharged. Every form observed.

Nothing in the room looked altered. The chairs stood where they had always stood. The silver carried the light as it had done last week, last month, all his life. The portraits above the sideboard observed the family and its guests with ancestral indifference. If a man had believed that certain sentences could change the air of a house, dinner proved him wrong. The forms continued. The candles burned. Miss Elizabeth Bennet lifted her glass, set it down again, and did not know that within a few days she might be gone.

Or perhaps she did know.

That was worse.

---

After dinner he went first to the billiard room, because Bingley had once told him that no man ever improved a difficulty by walking directly toward it. Bingley had meant cards. Or billiards. His philosophy was not always transferable, but his warmth had a way of surviving misapplication.

Darcy stood three minutes beside the table and did not take up a cue.

Then he went to the library.

The library knew too much of him. The estate office held business; the library held continuance. His father’s books. Georgiana’s shawl left on a chair. The globe he had turned as a boy until the seas went round too quickly and his tutor told him no empire had ever been governed by dizziness. The desk at which he had written to Colonel Fitzwilliam, to his steward, to Bingley, to men who wanted answers and received them.

He lit two more candles and sat.

There were several honorable courses in theory. Theory was generous that way.

He might speak to her. He might ask Mrs. Gardiner's permission, request a few minutes in the morning room, say only what must be said. The thought came first because desire was not deadened by discipline; it only learned to enter by respectable doors.

No.

The word was so immediate that he set down the pen he had not realized he had taken up.

She was in his house. Her aunt must soon leave. Every wall of Pemberley would stand behind him if he spoke. Every courtesy he had offered might seem to gather at his back and form an argument.

He would not make his house an accomplice.

Then he might say nothing. That had been honor, had it not? To leave her unpressed, to let his conduct speak only so far as she wished to hear it, to take pains that she should never have to wonder whether Jane's happiness had been offered as a debt.

But Mrs. Gardiner had been right. Silence could become pressure too. It could say he had retreated. It could leave Elizabeth to depart without knowing where his feelings lay.

Darcy drew a sheet of paper toward him.

At Hunsford, he had spoken because he believed himself entitled to an answer. Since then, he had held his tongue because he feared every word might become entitlement again. Neither extreme was honesty. One had demanded too much of her; the other asked too much of silence.

The pen waited in his hand.

He wrote her name.

*Miss Elizabeth Bennet,*

It looked too formal, and not formal enough. Formal enough for the world. Not enough for the distance he owed her. Too much distance for the truth.

He took another sheet.

*Madam,*

No.

He had called her madam in anger and defense, though the anger had been wounded pride and the defense had been, in part, true. The word still carried the smell of Rosings: damp paths, early light, the stiff violence of self-command, his own close hand filling every space because he had been unable to say less.

He set the sheet aside.

*Miss Bennet,*

Better. Safer. Painful.

*I would not have you think—*

Too defensive. It began with himself.

*You may perhaps have wondered—*

Cowardly. It asked her to admit curiosity before he had earned the right to notice it.

He drew another sheet.

*Miss Bennet,*

He stopped.

The first letter had defended. It had explained, argued, arranged the facts in order and demanded justice from a woman he had already injured. It had been true. That was not enough to save it from pride.

*I cannot allow my silence to be mistaken for indifference.*

Too blunt? Too much? Too little?

He left it.

The next sentence would not come. He rose, walked to the shelves, and returned without knowing which books he had passed. He sat again.

*My feelings are unchanged.*

He did not write them at once. He let them stand in his mind until they lost the first violence of hope and became merely true.

If he wrote only that, it would be a demand. If he did not write it, the letter had no purpose. He had learned, too late but not too late for this, that truth was not made honorable by being incomplete.

*My feelings are unchanged, except in this: that I know better than I once did how little right they give me to disturb your peace.*

The sentence remained on the page, black, exact, intolerable.

He sat back.

It was not all. It was perhaps too much. It was certainly not all he wanted to say.

He wanted to say that he loved her still, and differently, and more truly for the months in which hope had been denied the easy work of imagination and forced him instead to observe, to wait, to suffer correction. 

He wanted to say that the woman who had refused him had been more present to him than any woman who had ever smiled. 

He wanted to say that every proof he had tried to give had taught him that proof itself was not the point. She was free, and because she was free, anything she gave would be grace.

He wrote none of that. What he wanted had no place in a letter she might open alone, in a house where his name was on the walls.

*I ask for no answer.*

Then stopped, because even that might be false. He wanted an answer as a starving man wanted bread. Want was not the measure of what might be asked.

He amended the line:

*I ask for no answer now, nor for any kindness you do not freely wish to give.*

There was no eloquence in it. Good.

Eloquence had served him badly.

The clock in the hall struck eleven. He had not heard the quarter hours.

He wrote slowly then, not the whole of what he felt, but the shape around it. That he had not wished to press her while she remained his guest. That her sister’s happiness was not his to offer as evidence. That if he had erred in silence, it was not from indifference. That he would accept, without resentment and without further intrusion, whatever her silence or speech might tell him.

He wrote no blessing.

He paused over that. The first letter had ended with *God bless you*. It had been sincere; it had also been final, a dismissal disguised as prayer. He would not borrow it now.

He signed his name because a letter required a name, and because there was no humility in pretending she did not know the hand.

Fitzwilliam Darcy.

Then he sanded the page, folded it, and sealed it before courage could degrade into revision.

He had written what was true.

He had not written what he loved.

# Chapter 32: Recognition

The morning had made no promise of coolness. It came in pale and already warm at the edges, lying across the curtains in heavy sheets and turning the white counterpane into something too bright for comfort. Elizabeth had slept little, and had waked with that peculiar conviction, produced by a restless night, that every thought which had visited her at two o’clock must be sensible because it had been suffered through.

Her gown for traveling was not laid out; she had not gone so far. She had swept up the papers, pens and books and collected them on the small table by the window. These were not decisions. They were merely preparations of a sort too small to accuse her.

Mrs. Gardiner must depart. That fact had stood beside Elizabeth’s bed all night with more authority than any argument. Kitty might remain. Georgiana would wish it; Mrs. Annesley would make it proper; Mr. Bennet had already sanctioned what was necessary, and Kitty, now among kittens, music, and a young lady who cared what she became would not easily be restored to Longbourn without feeling herself injured by Providence.

But Elizabeth was not Kitty.

Her aunt's voice returned, dry and exact: *Do not dress it entirely in Kitty's frock.* She had been cutting the cloth all night.

She tied one ribbon, untied it, and let the bonnet fall back upon the chair. She had not decided to walk. Her hands had.

To remain at Pemberley after her aunt’s departure would be no longer the consequence of road, weather, invitation, sisterly convenience, or any of those useful circumstances by which inclination may be disguised from its owner. It would be choice. It would be declaration before declaration had been offered. It would say, to every sensible observer in Pemberley, and most cruelly to herself, that she had preferred uncertainty near Mr. Darcy to safety at a distance.

And if she were wrong?

The question had worn itself smooth by repetition. It no longer startled; it occupied. She had been wrong once, and wrong with such confidence that confidence itself had become suspect. To go away would be painful. To stay might be presumption. Pain, at least, was respectable. Presumption had no proper defense.

She opened the drawer wider and took out her gloves.

A knock came at the door. A maid entered, made her curtsey, and held out a folded letter on a small salver.

"A letter for you, miss."

She took it and did not move. The gloves remained in her left hand. The letter lay in her right.

She knew the hand.

There was no reason the room should alter; it did not. The bed stood unmade only by her own restlessness. The washstand, the chair, the open drawer, the offending bonnet — all continued in their places with admirable indifference.

The paper was folded with exactness. The seal bore no extravagance. Her name was written in a form neither intimate nor cold.

*Miss Bennet.*

Not *Madam*.

The absurd relief of that first observation nearly overcame her. She set the gloves down, then picked them up again because she had set them upon the wrong chair, though no one in the world could have cared. Her fingers found the edge of the seal, and stopped.

No.

Not here. Not inside his walls, with the house around her and Pemberley itself seemingly built into the very paper. If he meant to wound, she would not be wounded under his roof. If he meant to explain, she would not receive explanation where the house itself might do half her feeling for her.

And if he meant neither?

That possibility she could not examine indoors.

Elizabeth put on her bonnet. The ribbons would not tie properly; she tied them badly and let them be. She took her gloves because her hands required employment, not because she intended to wear them. The letter she slipped into a pocket. She crossed the room, descended the stairs, and passed through a side door that a footman opened before she had quite reached it.

No one stopped her. That was Pemberley too: a house in which attention did not become interference.

She went first to the formal garden, because it was nearest and because a gravel path offered the comfort of direction. The beds lay in full summer order, their edges clipped, their colors arranged with that art which made itself invisible. Beyond them the land fell away, and farther the trees gathered in darker ranks, their shade still some distance off.

She walked until the house was behind her shoulder and the windows no longer seemed capable of seeing. A stone bench stood near the turn of the path. Sitting was impossible. She drew on one glove, then took it off — the paper would not feel right through kid — and pressed the letter flat against her skirt.

She broke the seal.

The first line was simple.

*I cannot allow my silence to be mistaken for indifference.*

Elizabeth read it once without breathing, and then again with such attention that the words ceased for a moment to be words and became only marks, black and exact upon the page.

Not indifference.

She had expected, though she had not admitted it, some reference to her manner at dinner, or to what she had asked under the stars, or to the sharpness with which she had told him that his interference had cost Jane a year of her happiness. She had expected pain to answer pain. 

She had expected dignity. She had expected, if not accusation, then at least the proud suffering of a man too honorable to complain and too injured to be easy.

This was none of those things.

She walked on.

The gravel gave way to a narrower packed-earth path, still kept, but less commanded. Trees stood nearer here, starting to crowd. The grass beyond them was not trimmed into obedience. Elizabeth walked, and stopped to read, and walked again.

He wrote that his feelings were unchanged.

Here she stopped.

The word did not strike as Hunsford had struck. There was no violence in it, no demand entering before permission, no expectation that she must hear because he had resolved to speak. The sentence stood with a gravity that made no advance. His feelings were unchanged — except, he wrote, that he knew better than he once had how little right they gave him to disturb her peace.

Elizabeth pressed the page flatter against the palm of her glove.

At Hunsford, love had come armed with every objection to herself, her family, and her connections, and had seemed to expect gratitude for surviving them. Here, love stood at a distance and named the distance as her due.

She had been prepared to defend herself. It was difficult to defend oneself against a man who had made no attack.

She walked farther down the path.

The next paragraph she read too quickly and was obliged to return to it, angry at herself for cowardice. It concerned Jane. Not much — less than she had feared, less than another man might have claimed — but enough.

He had been wrong. That was how he put it. Wrong in judging Jane’s heart; wrong in allowing that judgment to govern another’s happiness. Mr. Bingley had known the truth before he left Pemberley. What followed was Mr. Bingley’s own act, as it ought always to have been.

No parade of usefulness. No account of difficulty. No description of Bingley’s distress, or his own sacrifice, or the delicacy of restoring what he had injured. Jane's happiness entered the letter only to be freed from any claim he might make on it.

Elizabeth folded the sheet along its crease, opened it again, and read the paragraph a third time.

*As it ought always to have been.*

The words found an older wound but did not press it. They only showed where it had been.

Jane's letter, all restrained joy and tremulous certainty. Bingley's easy warmth at Pemberley, his readiness to be pleased, his heart so much more transparent than the people nearest him had honored. Mr. Darcy at dinner saying, with care enough to make the sentence almost painful, that Bingley had acted freely.

He had been telling the truth then. More than the truth, she thought. He had been refusing to use it.

The path curved toward a little rise where the trees thinned. From there she could see, not the whole house, but part of it: one long pale front through branches, steady in the morning light, without triumph. Pemberley did not look like a trap. That was inconvenient.

She read on.

He had not spoken because she was his guest. He had not wished that any kindness shown under his roof should seem to gather weight against her. He had erred, he wrote, if his silence had caused pain; but he hoped she would believe that pain to be the result of restraint, not indifference. He asked for no answer now, nor for any kindness she did not freely wish to give.

*Freely.* The word was not ornament. At Hunsford, any kindness she might have given would have been claimed rather than received. Here he asked only for what she chose to give.

Elizabeth sat down at last, not upon a bench but upon a low stone edging half lost in moss. The impropriety of the seat would have interested her on another morning. On this one, it only placed her closer to the ground.

*No answer now.*

Not *God bless you*. Not farewell. He had told her what he felt and required nothing in return.

His first letter returned so clearly that for a moment she was again in the lane at Hunsford, damp grass under her shoes and mortification in every line.

The first had said, in effect: You have been wrong, and I will show you where.

The second: I have been wrong, and I will not use my amendment to claim you.

Elizabeth bent over the page. A breeze moved one corner of it; she held it down with her thumb.

She had asked silently and often for proof. She had imagined proof, perhaps, as something large enough to relieve her of the work of choosing — a single act so unmistakable that doubt would become ungrateful and acceptance merely sensible. She had wanted, if she was honest, to be spared the harder business of believing her own eyes.

No such rescue had come.

There had been only a man who bowed to her uncle without condescension. Who had stood in a stable doorway and watched Kitty Bennet put a kitten into Georgiana's uncertain hands, and had not moved to prevent it, though every cautious instinct must have told him to — and had heard Georgiana laugh. Whose face had done something Elizabeth could not name at the time but could name now. 

Who stood under the stars and did not defend himself when defense would have been easier than silence.

Proof had not arrived like thunder. It had accumulated like daylight.

And she had seen it.

That was the thought from which she had been hiding. She had known he was not what she had once believed him. She had known it in pieces long before this morning. And the word *love*, though powerful, explained less than she had once believed. She had loved Wickham’s story and found it false. She had loved her own cleverness and found it insufficient.

But she had seen Mr. Darcy, day after day, in particulars too small and too undramatic to have been wished for. She had seen him when he was not speaking to her. She had seen him as master, brother, friend, host, man corrected. She had seen him refuse triumph.

If she could not trust that, what could she trust?

In the gallery, weeks ago, she had stood before his portrait and felt something she could not name. Now she could. It was recognition: the slow, costly business of looking long enough to see truly.

Elizabeth stood.

She had expected, perhaps, that certainty would feel like happiness. What came first was steadiness — serious, and a little frightening. And then, after a few steps, something very close to relief.

She folded the letter carefully.

For several minutes she walked without purpose except movement. The path had carried her farther than she intended. A field opened to her right; the formal garden was out of sight; the house had vanished behind trees. She might have laughed at herself then, had she been less near tears. She had come out to be free of Pemberley and had walked deeper into it.

No. Not into Pemberley.

Into truth.

A dangerously solemn phrase, and she objected to it at once. Truth, in her experience, was seldom so grand. It was more often a maid with a salver; a folded sheet; a gentleman not saying what he might have said; a young girl laughing over kittens; an aunt laying one hand briefly upon one’s arm and asking a question too kind to evade.

She turned back.

The return path seemed shorter, as return paths often do when one walks them willingly. The trees loosened. The house reappeared in fragments — a chimney, a pale line of stone, a window flashed by sun.

At the edge of the formal garden she stopped.

Mr. Darcy stood near the turn of the walk.

He was not posed for waiting. That was her first absurd, exact thought. He had no hat in his hand, no attitude of picturesque suffering, no appearance of a gentleman arranged by Providence beneath a tree for the convenience of a lady’s altered feelings. He stood quite still, as a man stands who has walked from one errand and failed to proceed to the next. His attention was on the path by which she had come; when he saw her, he did not advance.

She crossed the last of the gravel toward him. He did not move. He did not look away, but stood with that serious, still attention that asked nothing and concealed nothing. She was aware of the letter in her hand. She was aware of being seen. She walked anyway.

He bowed. His attention, after the bow, went to the middle distance — giving her, she understood, whatever space the letter in her hand required.

"Miss Bennet."

"Mr. Darcy."

The forms had never sounded so thin, nor so necessary.

His eyes dropped once to the letter, then returned to her face. "I had not meant to wait," he said. "And then I did.”

“Yes,” she said. “I know.”

The answer moved him more than accusation might have done. She saw it in the care with which he held himself still.

"I was going to the stables," he said, which was so evidently true and so entirely incomplete that, on any other morning, she might have enjoyed it.

There was her Mr. Darcy — not hers, not yet, and the presumption of the word nearly undid her — but there was the man himself: precise enough to be honest about even the failure of an errand.

She enjoyed it. She would not have thought herself capable of enjoying anything, an hour ago.

A little courage returned with a shape she recognized. "And have the stables offended you, sir, or only failed to be sufficiently absorbing?"

His gaze changed. Only just.

"They are blameless.” He looked at her then, rather than past her. “The fault is mine."

"A generous confession. Stables are seldom in a position to defend themselves."

For one moment, almost nothing stood between them but summer air and the foolishness she had offered him. He received it — not with laughter, but with the expression of a man who had not expected to be given anything at all. Then he looked again at the letter in her hand, and the moment deepened.

"If my letter has distressed you," he said, "I am sorry for it.” He took a step back. “If you would prefer that I leave you—"

"No."

It was too quick. It was also true.

He stopped. His hand, at his side, closed and opened. 

She had meant to proceed with dignity. She had meant to say, with all the elegance proper to a woman who had been given the rare opportunity of correcting a former error, that his letter had affected her, that she valued his confidence, that her sentiments had undergone so material a change. 

No. She would not borrow from old speeches and dress them in new sincerity.

What she wanted to say was this: I have been afraid of my own judgment, and you have given it back to me — and asked nothing for the giving.

What she said was, "You were right to write first.”

His brows drew together. 

"At Hunsford," she said, "the letter came after. This time it came before.”

His composure suffered then. His breath changed, and his eyes, fixed on her with a steadiness that did not presume, made speech more difficult than silence.

"I hoped only that it would not add to your uneasiness."

"It did."

He flinched before he could prevent it.

Elizabeth shook her head. "No — not in that way. It made me uneasy because I had been trying very hard to be sensible, and your letter gave me no assistance at all in continuing so."

"I am sorry."

"You need not be. I have been sensible in several directions this morning and liked none of them."

A sound escaped him, too brief for laughter and too warm for anything else.

She took a step nearer. The gravel shifted under her shoe. It seemed an impertinent sound for so grave a moment.

"You said you asked for no answer now," she said.

"I did."

"And for no kindness I did not freely wish to give."

"Yes."

"Then you must allow me the same freedom in answering as you allowed me in reading."

He bowed his head. "Always."

Always. He had no right to say it, and every right; or perhaps she had given him the right by wishing to hear it.

Elizabeth looked toward the house. Its windows caught the sun; behind one of them, Mrs. Gardiner might be folding a letter, or Kitty might be singing as Georgiana played, or Mrs. Reynolds might be directing linen to rooms that had no notion how many futures they contained. Ordinary life continued with indecent calm.

She turned back to him.

"I cannot thank you for Jane’s happiness," she said. "That belongs to Jane and Mr. Bingley."

His mouth tightened once, briefly. Accepted and deserved. "It does."

"But I can thank you for knowing it."

He closed his eyes. When he opened them, she had the oddest sensation that he had expected refusal in so many forms that gratitude, properly limited, had become the most dangerous kindness.

"You were wrong about her," Elizabeth said.

"I was."

"And I was wrong about you."

He said nothing.

"I have had more practice admitting it," she added, because gravity without a little rescue was intolerable. "Though I cannot claim to have improved in grace."

"Your grace has never been the quality I most admired."

"No? Then I am spared the effort of cultivating it."

This time he did smile. Slight, unguarded, and gone too soon.

"Miss Bennet," he said, and the name, which had been safe upon the page, trembled at the edge of something unsafe aloud. "Elizabeth. If I may."

She did not answer with words. She did not look away.

"My feelings are as I wrote them," he said. "They have not changed, except as correction and time have taught me how little they can deserve by merely existing. I love you. I have loved you badly, proudly, and in ignorance. I hope I may love you better. If you cannot return it, say so, and I will not trouble you again. But if there is any hope—"

"There is more than hope."

The sentence came before prudence had dressed it. Elizabeth heard herself, understood herself, and for once did not repent the speed of either.

"Elizabeth," he said again.

"Yes," she said. "If that was the question. Yes."

He took one step, then stopped, asking still without words.

She held out her hand.

His closed around it. His hand was bare — he had been going to the stables, she remembered, or had said so — and warm from the sun. Her own hand was ungloved because she had carried her gloves all morning as props for a composure she had never achieved. The impropriety was slight. 

The sensation was not.

She was conscious of the pressure of his hand around hers — around her — and of the sun on her back, and of Pemberley behind him, waiting.

For several moments they stood so. 

At last she said, because someone must rescue them from perfect happiness before it became embarrassing, "We should go in."

"We should."

"Mrs. Gardiner will be wondering."

"I doubt Mrs. Gardiner wonders without profit."

"Usefully, I expect."

He offered his arm.

Elizabeth looked at it, then at him. "You are certain this is not gratitude?"

His face grew serious at once. "Are you?"

She considered the question. Then she placed her hand upon his arm.

"Quite certain. Gratitude would have made me more solemn."

Mr. Darcy laughed then — not much, but enough; enough that the sound belonged to the morning and not to memory.

She had left the house alone to read the truth.

They turned toward the house together.

# Chapter 33: The Drawing Room

Elizabeth knew the light inside Pemberley at mid-morning now. It came through tall windows with the steady goodwill of a house that had been admitting summer for two hundred years and saw no reason to make a spectacle of it. The entrance hall received its portion without comment; the passage beyond received somewhat less; and by the time it reached the morning room, it had become the sort of light in which ordinary things — a writing desk, a chair drawn near the window, an aunt's face turning from a letter — looked no grander and no smaller than themselves.

Elizabeth crossed the threshold with Mr. Darcy beside her and found that the house did not remark upon it.

The house simply received them. The hall smelled of beeswax and warm dust and the distinct mineral stillness of old stone on a summer day, and none of it had altered because she had said yes on a gravel path that morning.

She had expected — not trumpets. She would have objected to trumpets. But some small shift in the furniture of the world, perhaps, to acknowledge that the furniture of her life had been rearranged entirely.

Mr. Darcy — William, he’d said she might call him — paused at the foot of the staircase. His hand was still near her arm — not touching it, not quite, but near enough that the air between them felt occupied.

"I should speak with Mrs. Reynolds," he said.

"Yes."

"And you will want —"

"My aunt. Yes."

They stood for a moment in the kind of silence that is not uncomfortable but is not, strictly speaking, silence, since it contains the sound of two people deciding how to be ordinary again after being extraordinary for half a morning. Then Mr. Darcy bowed — the slightest movement, nearly nothing — and turned away.

He crossed the hall, turned the corner, and disappeared. She pressed a hand against her ribs, breathed once, and went to find Mrs. Gardiner.

She found her in the morning room. The girls must be at the pianoforte in the music room, and conversation required a door between itself and music. She sat near the window with a letter open in her lap and her reading spectacles pushed up onto her forehead, which gave her the appearance of a woman interrupted between two thoughts and not yet committed to either.

She glanced up when Elizabeth entered. Then she took a longer look.

"Lizzy?"

"Aunt."

Mrs. Gardiner removed her spectacles, folded them, and set them on the table beside her letter with a precision that reminded Elizabeth, with a small shock, of her father. People who read a great deal, she supposed, developed the same habits with spectacles.

"Sit down," Mrs. Gardiner said.

The chair was firm and placed at an angle that allowed her to see both her aunt and the window, beyond which the day continued in its unembarrassed sunshine without the slightest intention of being solemn.

She had planned, on the walk from the entrance hall, to say it gracefully. She had composed a sentence involving the words *material change* and *sentiments* and perhaps *understanding*, which was the proper word for the thing that had just happened to her, though it failed entirely to describe it. She had planned to be calm, measured, and above all witty, because wit was the armor she reached for first and last and always.

What she said was, "Mr. Darcy has asked me to marry him, and I have said yes."

The sentence lay in the room like a stone dropped into still water. Not thrown — dropped. It made no splash. It simply sank.

Mrs. Gardiner was quiet for three seconds.

In the first second, her aunt's face did not change.

In the second, something behind it moved — not surprise, not satisfaction, not the knowing warmth of a woman who had seen it coming, but something closer to the expression of a person who has been holding a door steady against a wind and feels the wind stop.

In the third second, Mrs. Gardiner said, "Yes. I rather thought you might."

The clock on the mantel ticked once into the silence.

"You are not astonished."

"I am a great many things at this moment, Lizzy. Astonished is not among them."

"You might at least pretend."

"I might," Mrs. Gardiner agreed. "But you told me the truth just now, without a single clever word. I think you deserve the same.”

The kindness of that sentence — precise, unornamented, and aimed with the quiet accuracy that was her aunt's gift — nearly undid Elizabeth entirely. She looked down at her hands, which were folded in her lap in the attitude of a woman with nothing to confess, and found that they were steady. 

"How long have you known?" Elizabeth asked.

"Known is too strong a word. Hoped, perhaps. Observed." Mrs. Gardiner paused. "Since the evening he stood in the drawing room and watched you with Georgiana, and his face — but that is his story to tell, not mine."

"And you said nothing."

"What would you have me say? 'Lizzy, I believe Mr. Darcy is in love with you, and I believe you are in love with him, and I believe you will discover it approximately three weeks after it has become obvious to everyone else in the house'? That seemed indelicate."

Elizabeth laughed — the first real one all morning — and it surprised them both. "I would have denied it," she said.

"Vigorously."

"With evidence."

"Incorrect evidence, but offered with conviction. Yes." Mrs. Gardiner smiled. It was the smile Elizabeth had watched all her life and never fully understood until now — the one that said *I have been where you are, and I will not diminish it by saying so.* "Are you happy, Lizzy?"

The question was simple. The answer should have been simple.

"I am —" Elizabeth began, and stopped. She tried again. "I think I may be happy. It does not feel quite as I expected."

Mrs. Gardiner nodded once.

"I thought it would feel more like certainty."

"And what does it feel like?"

Elizabeth shifted in the chair. "Like having set down something very heavy that I did not know I was carrying."

Mrs. Gardiner reached across the space between their chairs and laid her hand on Elizabeth's arm. The gesture was brief, but the warmth of it stayed after her aunt's hand withdrew.

"And Mr. Gardiner's letter?" Elizabeth said, because someone must return them to practical ground. "Your departure?"

"I suspect Mr. Gardiner's letter can accommodate a slight delay in its urgency." Mrs. Gardiner's eyes were bright. "I shall have to write him a very different letter now. He will be insufferable. He told me in Lambton that he liked Mr. Darcy's manner, and he has been waiting ever since for an opportunity to be right about something."

Elizabeth opened her mouth, closed it, and shook her head. "I cannot decide whether you are comforting me or teasing me."

"Lizzy, I have been married fifteen years. I assure you it is possible to do both at once."

They sat together for a time after that, not speaking much, not needing to. The morning room held them with the quiet hospitality of a space meant for small parties and private conversation, and the light through the window moved across the floor with the unhurried progress of an afternoon that had nowhere particular to be.

At last Mrs. Gardiner said, "You will want to tell the girls."

"Yes."

"Shall I come with you?"

Elizabeth shook her head. "No. I think I should like to walk back through the house by myself for a moment.”

She rose, straightened her skirt, and paused at the door.

"Aunt."

"Yes?"

"Thank you for asking whether I wished to stay."

Her aunt tilted her head and gave it a little shake. "You were always going to stay, Lizzy. You simply needed someone to give you permission to know it."

---

The girls were in the drawing room.

Kitty sat at one end of the sofa, a piece of sheet music set aside on the cushion beside her, looking as though she had exhausted its possibilities some time ago. Georgiana sat beside her with a book open in her lap, reading with the absorption of a young woman who is not reading at all but is using the book as a screen behind which to think. Mrs. Annesley occupied her customary chair near the window, her embroidery progressing at the steady rate of a woman whose hands had learned to work without consulting her attention.

And Mr. Darcy. William.

He stood near the fireplace — unlit, in summer — one hand resting on the mantelpiece and the other at his side. He had changed his coat; the riding coat was gone, and in its place something darker, neater. Which meant he had been to his rooms, which meant he had stood before a glass and considered how he wished to appear in this room at this hour, and had decided upon a dark blue coat and a composed face and the fireplace.

When she stepped into the room, his eyes snapped to her.

Elizabeth had been watched by him before. She had been watched at Netherfield, where his gaze had felt like judgment. She had been watched at Rosings. She had been watched across dining tables and drawing rooms and garden paths, and she had learned to feel his attention the way one feels weather — a pressure system, constant, not always comfortable, impossible to ignore.

This was different. This was the face of a man who had offered everything he had and been accepted and did not yet know what to do with his hands.

She almost smiled. She crossed the room and sat down in the chair nearest the sofa, and as she sat, Kitty’s head turned.

Kitty's gift — Elizabeth had not always recognized it as a gift — was speed. She read rooms the way other people read print: left to right, top to bottom, the essential meaning extracted before the full sentence had been parsed. She took one look at Elizabeth, one at Mr. Darcy, and her face broke open like a window flung wide.

"Lizzy!"

"Kitty —"

"Lizzy, you are — are you — has he —"

She was already off the sofa, crossing the room. She caught Elizabeth's hands — both of them — and squeezed with a force that was slightly painful and entirely welcome.

"I *knew* it," she said. "I told Georgiana — I said, last week, I said *something has changed*, and she said I was imagining things, but I was not imagining things, I was —"

"Kitty." Elizabeth freed one hand and placed it over Kitty's, which were still gripping and showed no signs of stopping. "Yes."

"Yes?"

"Yes."

Kitty's eyes filled. She tucked herself against Elizabeth's side and held on. Elizabeth wrapped an arm around her.

Georgiana had risen from the sofa. She stood quite still, her hands clasped before her — a girl who had been taught, by circumstance and temperament, that wanting something too visibly was a form of risk.

Elizabeth held out her free hand.

Georgiana took it. Her fingers were cool and trembled once before steadying.

Her eyes found him.

From the fireplace, Mr. Darcy watched. His hand, which had rested so carefully on the mantelpiece, tightened once against the marble and then released. He looked from Georgiana's hand in Elizabeth's to Kitty's flushed face, and something in him seemed to settle.

---

Someone — Mrs. Annesley, probably — had quietly sent for a luncheon tray. Elizabeth ate bread and cold ham standing at the window without tasting either, and realized it was the first food she had taken all day.

Mr. Darcy was at the writing desk in the corner of the drawing room, his hand resting on the inkstand as though he had been arranging necessities in his mind for some time.

"I will write to your father," he said.

"Yes." Elizabeth crossed to the desk and drew out the second chair. The desk’s surface was wide, polished, and orderly in the way that his surfaces tended to be — everything aligned, everything in its place, the blotter centered with a precision that was either admirable or mildly alarming, depending upon one's feelings about blotters. "And I must write to Jane."

The pen was already cut. She drew a sheet of paper from the tray, dipped the nib, and wrote:

*My dearest Jane —*

*I have news that I think will surprise you, though I begin to suspect it will surprise no one but myself.*

The rest came quickly — honestly, without cleverness, because she was done dressing truth in wit when simplicity would serve.

Her father's letter was harder.

She sat with the pen in her hand and the blank page before her and thought about what she would say to a man who had stood in the library at Pemberley and told her to *know why you stay*. 

He had asked with love and expected — what? That she would stay for Kitty's sake. For duty. For the sensible reasons that a sensible daughter offers a sensible father. He would not have expected this.

The truth was not complicated. It was only difficult to say to a man who had spent twenty years hiding behind a newspaper rather than say difficult things himself. *I stayed because I wished to. I stay because I choose to. The reason is not duty, Papa. The reason is that I have found a man I can trust — not because he has never erred, but because he has erred and corrected and did not ask me to pretend the error away.*

She would not write that — not in those words. But she would write something near it, and he would read it in his library with his spectacles on and his newspaper folded and the decanter at his elbow, and he would understand. Not everything, perhaps. But enough.

"There is Lady Catherine to consider as well," Mr. Darcy said, from the other end of the desk. His pen had stopped.

"She will not be pleased," Elizabeth said, which was so enormous an understatement that it briefly achieved comedy.

"No."

"Maybe we should invite another cousin of mine. To soften the blow."

Mr. Darcy glanced at her. "I was not aware that cousins served a medicinal purpose."

"Or of yours," she said.

He was quiet for a moment. Then: "I have not thought enough about what Anne might wish." He looked down at his unfinished letter. The ink had gathered darkly at the end of one word. After a moment, he blotted it.

Kitty's voice drifted in from the sofa, where she was telling Georgiana something about wedding clothes with the happy authority of a girl who had never attended a wedding but held very firm opinions about sleeves.

"Mama will be impossible," Elizabeth said, turning back to her letter.

"I expect Mrs. Bennet will be —"

"Rapturous. Incandescent. Vindicated." Elizabeth heard her mother's voice in her own head with the vivid clarity of a theatrical performance that required no stage. *Three daughters settled! And one of them at Pemberley! Oh, Mr. Bennet, did I not say — did I not always say —* "She will credit herself entirely."

"Naturally."

"She will tell every woman in Meryton."

"I would expect nothing less."

"She will write to Lady Lucas before my father finishes reading your letter.”

Mr. Darcy's pen resumed its progress across the page. "Then I had better write short."

She took in the bent head, the careful hand, the gravity with which he had delivered that small terrible joke, and knew she had chosen well.

---

The dinner bell found them still in the drawing room. The room had altered while no one attended to it: Kitty's ribbon lay across the sofa arm, Georgiana's book had slid facedown on the cushion, and the ink on all the letters had dried.

They went to dress, and then reassembled and moved to the dining room in the loose procession of a household too small for ceremony but too well-bred to abandon it entirely. Mr. Darcy offered Mrs. Gardiner his arm. Georgiana followed, with Kitty beside her, talking — Kitty was always talking now, and the sound of it didn’t grate but belonged, the way birdsong belongs to a garden not because it is beautiful but because it would be wrong without it.

Georgiana paused just inside the door. She had been quiet through the afternoon — not withdrawn, not unhappy, but quiet. Now she looked at Elizabeth, and at the table beyond, and at the chair at the foot where she had sat every evening since she was old enough to sit at dinner.

"Would you sit here?" Georgiana asked. Her voice was soft, but steady. "I should like to see you here."

Elizabeth opened her mouth to protest — it was too soon, it was not proper, she was not yet anything but a guest with a promise — but Georgiana had already moved to a chair along the side with the settled grace of a young woman who had made a decision and did not intend to discuss it. She looked, not diminished, but relieved, as if the place she had given up had not been lost but filled.

At the head of the table, Mr. Darcy's hand stilled on the back of his chair. He looked first at Georgiana — a look so brief Elizabeth might have missed it — and then at Elizabeth. Then he nodded, once.

Elizabeth sat at the foot of Mr. Darcy's table.

The chair was the same as every other chair in the room — mahogany, upholstered, neither especially comfortable nor especially grand. It did not feel like a throne. It felt like a chair. But from this chair the room arranged itself differently. She could see all of them at once: Mr. Darcy at the far end, too composed by half; Mrs. Gardiner to his right, amusement tucked into the corner of her mouth; Mrs. Annesley next to Mrs. Gardiner with her hands folded and her attention everywhere; Georgiana on his left, and beside her Kitty, one dark ribbon nearly touching one pale one, their whispers threaded through the clink of spoons and glass.

Mrs. Annesley praised the soup. Kitty said something about the bread. Georgiana said something to Mrs. Gardiner across the table — a murmured word that made Mrs. Gardiner — Elizabeth's aunt, and soon to be Georgiana's by whatever bonds affection made before law had caught up — smile.

Steam rose from the soup between them and blurred him for one brief, ridiculous instant, and she thought: *I will see this face across this table for the rest of my life.* The thought did not frighten her. It settled somewhere beneath her ribs, warm and strange. The linen lay cool beneath her wrist; the silver, when she touched it, had already taken the warmth of her fingers, as if the house had decided she belonged here before she had. 

The bread broke under Kitty's hand with a soft crackle that sounded, impossibly, like home — not Longbourn, not yet Pemberley, but something being made between the two. A candle near Mrs. Annesley bent its flame once, then steadied, and the smell of beeswax and soup and the faint green breath of the grounds through an open window was so ordinary, so unremarkable, that Elizabeth had to look down at her plate for a moment before she could trust her face.

The dining room at Pemberley was not large, for a great house. The table could seat twenty and held six. Outside, the grounds stretched into evening, and beyond them the road that led south to Longbourn, where a different table waited in a different life.

Elizabeth picked up her spoon.

Halfway through the soup, Mr. Darcy caught Elizabeth's eye across the cloth. His face held the last of the evening light, and for once she did not have to ask what it meant.

Elizabeth lifted her glass.

A moment later, Mr. Darcy lifted his. No toast was spoken. None was required.

The wine was light and sharp on her tongue. Near Georgiana, Kitty began another sentence before she had finished the last; Georgiana's laugh, small and astonished by itself, slipped into the candlelight.

It was enough. It was, she found, exactly enough.
